Daniel


1) Opening & consent
Anna: Hi Daniel, thanks for joining. Can you hear me okay?
Daniel: Yeah, hi Anna. I can hear you. Can you hear me?
Anna: Yes, I can. Great. Before we start, just a couple of quick things. This is an interview about your experiences with career support, including human coaching and AI tools for career questions. It’s voluntary, you can skip any question, and you can stop anytime. With your permission, Im recording it to record audio for transcription. Is that okay?
Daniel: Yeah, that’s okay. As long as it’s anonymous, right?
Anna: Yes, exactly. We’ll anonymize everything and use a pseudonym like “Daniel,” and remove identifying details. Are there any topics you’d prefer not to discuss?
Daniel: I think it’s fine. Just… not too much family stuff. Career stuff is okay.
Anna: Totally. We’ll keep it to career-related experiences. If anything feels too personal, tell me and we’ll move on. I’m starting the recording now.
Daniel: Okay.
2) Participant context & career situation
Anna: To start, where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you dealing with right now?
Daniel: Okay, um… I’m 24, I’m a UX/UI designer. I’m in Germany now, in Berlin. I moved here almost three years ago. And right now I’m kind of stuck in this loop of short projects and freelance gigs like three weeks here  two months there. I can’t land stable work.
Anna: When you say stable, what does that mean for you?
Daniel: Like a proper contract. Not just we’ll pay you as a freelancer, good luck. I mean full-time, or at least part-time but long-term. And honestly, stability also means health insurance not being this constant anxiety. Here it’s… a whole universe. And I’m tired of always applying, always proving myself, always rewriting case studies.
Anna: What’s been the most significant decision or moment for you in the last 6 to12 months?
Daniel: Honestly… whether I should stay in UX or pivot to something differentt. Like product, or maybe UI-only, or even something outside design. Because the junior UX market feels saturated, and also the German language requirement blocks me a lot.
Anna: How often do you see German required in roles you look at?
Daniel: A lot. Like more than half. Even if the job description is in English, they write German is a plus, but then in interviews they ask, Can you work with stakeholders in German? And I’m like… ich kann ein bisschen. But not enough for workshops.
Anna: How would you describe your German level?
Daniel: Maybe B1-ish, but not a clean B1. I can do appointments, Anmeldung, doctor stuff. But professionally, I freeze. English is fine.
Anna: And you moved from another country? Where from?
Daniel: Serbia originally. I studied there and then moved here for career and life reasons. I also had a girlfriend here at the time, but that ended. I stayed anyway.
Anna: Does your residency situation affect the job search?
Daniel: Yes and no. I have a legal residence permit tied to work, but it’s not super fragile right now. Still, employers sometimes ask, and you can feel hesitation. Even when I say, “No sponsorship,” they still look unsure. It’s just extra friction. And for me it adds pressure because I want a contract not only for money, but also for paperwork stability.
Anna: What kind of work have you been doing in your projects?
Daniel: Mostly early-stage startups. One was fintech, like a dashboard redesign. Another was e-commerce, improving checkout. And then a meditation app where it was more UI polish. Sometimes I do end-to-end research planning, interviews, journey maps, wireframes, prototyping in Figma, handoff. But in small projects it’s often like we need UI by next Friday, so you don’t do proper research, you just guess. And that bothers me.
Anna: What bothers you about it?
Daniel: Because I want to be a real UX designer, not just someone making pretty screens. I want to justify decisions, test, measure. But the projects I get are more like survival mode.
Anna: How do you usually search for work?
Daniel: LinkedIn mainly. Also company career pages. Sometimes Wellfound. In Germany I tried StepStone, but it’s very German. And I go to some meetups—UX meetups, design systems meetups. I tried portfolio reviews too.
Anna: What has networking been like for you here?
Daniel: Mixed. People are friendly at meetups, but it’s hard to convert to referrals. You talk, you connect, and then… nothing. And I’m not super outgoing. I can talk, but it costs energy.
Anna: What kind of support do you feel you need most right now?
Daniel: Clarity and strategy. Like, am I applying to the right roles? Is my portfolio wrong? Am I too generalist? Should I specialize? And also confidence. After many rejections, I start thinking I’m the problem. But I also know the market is tough. So it’s both.
3) Career support in general
Anna: When you need career support like practical or emotional then who or what do you usually turn to?
Daniel: Friends first. I have one friend who’s a developer. He’s good for keep going, but he doesn’t understand design hiring. I talk to my sister back home sometimes, but she’s like, Just get any job, which isn’t helpful hahaha. 
Anna: Yeah, my mother does the same hahaha. I understand. What about more formal support like career services, mentors, coaches?
Daniel: I tried a career service once, like an integration center thing. They helped with German CV format. Lebensrough? Basic stuff. It felt generic and more for traditional roles. And then I used ADPList, which is more design-specific.
Anna: Any other resources?
Daniel: YouTube, honestly. Portfolio tips, case study tips. Medium articles too, but it’s too much content. And sometimes I use ChatGPT for rewriting bullet points or prepping for interviews.
Anna: What tends to feel genuinely helpful?
Daniel: Something actionable. Like, change this section of your case study, or your headline is unclear, or here are keywords you’re missing.If it’s concrete, I can do it. If it’s motivational talk, it feels nice for a moment, but then I still don’t know what to do.
Anna: And what feels unhelpful?
Daniel: Advice that ignores context. Like, just network more, without understanding language barriers or how drained I am. Or just do a personal project, when I already did three and I also need money. Context-blind advice.
4) Human coaching: experience & trust
Anna: You mentioned ADPList. Can you walk me through your experience? How many sessions did you did, what you expected, and how it went overall?
Daniel: I used ADPList maybe six sessions over about a year, with different mentors. I expected portfolio feedback, career direction, maybe interview prep.
Anna: What was the experience like in most sessions?
Daniel: Most were okay. Not horrible. But not a wow. A lot were surface-level. Like they glance at the portfolio and say looks good, and then they talk about their own career a lot. And I’m sitting there thinking, okay, but what do I do tomorrow? You know?
Anna: Can you describe one session that felt neutral or disappointing what happened?
Daniel: Yeah. One mentor was a senior designer at a huge tech company. I booked him because I thought he’d know about hiring. He was clearly smart, but he was rushing. He kept checking another screen. And he kept saying, where is your impact? Where are your metrics? And I said, i was freelance, I don’t have metrics. And he was like, then create metrics. And I was like… how? I can’t invent numbers. And he said, you can approximate.That triggered a trust issue for me. I don’t want to lie.
Anna: What did that do to your trust in him?
Daniel: It dropped. I trusted his competence in big-tech storytelling, but I didn’t trust his alignment with me, and I didn’t trust the ethics of what he was suggesting. Also he didn’t ask questions about my situation, Germany, language, anything. It was like a template advice.
Anna: Was there anything about safety or confidentiality that mattered in that moment?
Daniel: Yeah, because it’s a stranger. You’re already a bit vulnerable. And when someone is distracted, you don’t feel safe to open up. So you keep it superficial.
Anna: And you said one session felt truly helpful. Can you describe that one?
Daniel: That was with a mentor who worked at a mid-size German company. She was also an immigrant, from Brazil. And I think it mattered because she understood the stress. She started by asking questions: my story, what I tried, what I want, constraints. She asked about my German level, and how I present myself in interviews. She also asked what stability means for me like contract, team, insurance. It felt structured.
Anna: What did she do that built trust for you?
Daniel: She was fully present. She took notes and told me she’s taking notes. She shared her screen and made a little structure, goal constraints strengths, gaps, actions. Then she gave feedback in a gentle way. She said my portfolio is visually strong, but the case studies read like a story without decisions. And she gave me a framework: problem, role, options considered, decision, result, reflection. Then she told me to pick two projects and rewrite them using that.
Anna: Did anything change after that session?
Daniel: Yes. I rewrote one case study. I changed my LinkedIn headline. She suggested I apply to “Product Designer” roles instead of “UX/UI Designer,” because in Germany the naming is different. She also told me to add a line like Deutsch B1 working on it instead of hiding it. That felt honest.
Anna: If you think about trust in human mentors, what are your personal trust triggers?
Daniel: Competence, empathy, structure. And humility. If someone says, i don’t know that’s fine. If they pretend, I hate it. And being fully present matters a lot.
Anna: And what makes you distrust?
Daniel: Overconfidence, generic advice, distraction. Also when mentors push their own path like it’s universal. And sometimes the power imbalance, like they treat take-home tasks as nothing, but for me it’s unpaid time I can’t afford.
5) AI tools: experience & trust
Anna: Now let’s talk about AI tools like ChatGPT. How do you use them for career-related questions?
Daniel: Mostly for rewriting. I paste bullet points and ask it to make them more concise or more impact-oriented. Sometimes I ask for keyword ideas from job descriptions. It’s like a writing assistant.
Anna: Can you give a concrete example where it helped?
Daniel: I had a bullet like designed onboarding screens for fintech app. Very flat. ChatGPT suggested stronger phrasing like redesigned onboarding flow by simplifying steps and improving clarity. It also gave me versions without fake metrics, if I ask it not to invent numbers. That helped me upgrade the tone.
Anna: And a moment where it failed or felt untrustworthy?
Daniel: One time I asked how to answer Why did you move to Germany? and it gave me this super polished, fake-sounding story about german innovation culture, and it didn’t sound like me. It also assumed a visa type incorrectly, like it just made an assumption. That reminded me that I don't know my life.
Anna: Do you have privacy concerns when using it?
Daniel: Yes. I don’t paste sensitive info. I remove company names sometimes, or I generalize. I’m cautious.
Anna: When you think about “trust” in AI, what does trust mean for you?
Daniel: Accuracy and boundaries. It should be able to say it’s unsure, but it rarely does. It always gives an answer. And I trust it more when it explains the changes, like why a sentence is stronger.
Anna: Do you ever use it for emotional support during the job search?
Daniel: Sometimes. When I get rejected I ask how to cope. It’s comforting in a shallow way because it doesn’t judge me. But it doesn’t replace a friend.
6) Human vs AI comparison via tasks
Anna: I’m going to name a few tasks, and for each one tell me whether you’d prefer a human coach, AI, or both, and why. For improving CV or LinkedIn wording?
Daniel: AI first. It’s fast, cheap, and I can iterate without feeling embarrassed. Then maybe a human to check if it sounds authentic and aligned.
Anna: For deciding what roles to apply for UX, product designer, something else?
Daniel: Human or hybrid. AI gives generic lists. A human can ask about my preferences and constraints, and also understand Germany context. But it has to be a good match.
Anna: For interview preparation?
Daniel: Hybrid. AI for practicing phrasing and structure, human for feedback on how I come across, tone, confidence, if my story lands.
Anna: For values clarification, figuring out what you really want?
Daniel: Human. AI gets cliche there. A human can hold silence and challenge me in a useful way.
Anna: For handling rejection and motivation?
Daniel: Honestly, friends. But if it’s between AI and coach, then human. AI can help at 2 a.m. with calming words, but it’s limited.
Anna: I want to add a quick rating moment. From 0 to 10, how would you rate your trust in human career coaches or mentors for your situation?
Daniel: I’d say 6.
Anna: Why 6?
Daniel: Because humans can be great, and I experienced that. But it’s inconsistent. So I trust the concept, not automatically the person.
Anna: And your trust in AI tools like ChatGPT, 0 to 10?
Daniel: Maybe 5.
Anna: Why 5?
Daniel: It’s useful, but it can hallucinate and miss context. I trust it for writing support, not for truth.
Anna: Now effectiveness. How effective has human coaching been for your situation overall, 0 to 10?
Daniel: Effectiveness… maybe 4.
Anna: Why 4?
Daniel: Because only one session really changed things. The rest felt like noise. And I still don’t have stable work, so overall impact is low.
Anna: And effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, 0 to 10?
Daniel: Maybe 6.
Anna: Why higher?
Daniel: Because it actually improved the output my CV language, cover letters, and speed. I got more replies when my documents were clearer. It doesn’t solve direction, but it helps a lot with production.


7) Trust–effectiveness link
Anna: In your experience, how do trust and effectiveness connect? Do you have examples where you trusted someone but it wasn’t effective, or where something was effective but you didn’t really trust it?
Daniel: Yeah, both. Trusted but not effective: I had a mentor who was very kind and validating. I felt safe, I trusted her as a person. But she didn’t give concrete actions. I left feeling warm, but nothing changed.
Anna: And effective but not trusted?
Daniel: The big tech mentor. His advice had useful parts impact, decision-making story, stronger narrative. But I didn’t trust him because he seemed okay with exaggerating and he was distracted. I still took some tips, but it cost energy. If you don’t trust, you second-guess everything, so it’s harder to act.
Anna: So distrust creates friction?
Daniel: Exactly. Trust makes implementation faster. Distrust makes me overthink. Trust is like a multiplier.
Anna: And with AI, do you see the same pattern?
Daniel: Yeah. AI can be effective but not trusted, like it writes something good but I don’t trust it’s authentic or accurate. And trusted but not effective is like emotional comfort—safe, but not changing anything long-term.
8) Preferences & hybrid model reaction
Anna: Imagine a hybrid model where AI helps you prepare and organize things, and a human coach steps in for deeper conversations and decision points. What’s your reaction?
Daniel: That sounds good. AI can handle the annoying part—rewriting, templates, checklists. And the human can do the meaning part and also verify reality.
Anna: Verify what?
Daniel: Context. Like what companies actually require German, what roles are truly junior-friendly, what hiring expectations look like here. AI might list companies, but a human in Germany can reality-check.
Anna: What would make you trust a hybrid system like that?
Daniel: Transparency about what AI does and doesn’t do. Privacy. And quality of matching for the human part. If the human part is random like ADPList sometimes, hybrid doesn’t solve it.
Anna: Matching seems central for you.
Daniel: Yeah. My one good mentor worked because she understood the Germany market and immigrant stress. If a system matches me with someone who gets those constraints, trust goes up.
Anna: Any concerns about hybrid models?
Daniel: That AI becomes too central and flattens nuance. Or the human just repeats what AI says, then why pay. And also I worry about incentives, like upselling or pushing me into a path that benefits the platform.
Anna: What would reduce that concern?
Daniel: Clear boundaries, maybe evidence like testimonials, and making it easy to switch mentors without shame. If switching is normalized, it’s easier to find a real fit.
Anna: How does that compare to your experience with ADPList?
Daniel: ADPList feels like speed dating. Profiles are vague, quality varies. Also I spent the first ten minutes explaining my context every time. That repetition is exhausting and it affects trust.
9) Closing
Anna: We’re almost at the end. If you could tell people designing career support tools what they should understand about someone in your situation, what would you say?
Daniel: Don’t underestimate admin stress or insurance, contracts, Anmeldung. It affects risk. People say take a risk, but here risk is tied to paperwork. And junior designers need very specific feedback, not like your portfolio is good also I want to not feel judged for language or being foreign. When someone acknowledges it’s harder, I trust them more, but then they also need to help me act.
Anna: If you could design the perfect support session for yourself next week, what would it look like?
Daniel: AI first i upload my CV and two job descriptions, it gives adjustments and a checklist. Then human: 45 minutes to decide my positioning and narrative, and to commit to a plan. And maybe a short follow-up with next steps for accountability.
Anna: What would make you walk away thinking, this was effective?
Daniel: If I have a plan I actually believe in, and I feel less scattered. Ideally it leads to interviews, but even without that, effectiveness is knowing what to do and why.
Anna: Anything you want to add before we stop?
Daniel: Not really. I’m curious what patterns you’ll find, but yeah. That’s it.
Anna: Thanks, Daniel. I’m stopping the recording now. And just to reiterate, everything will be anonymized. Thanks again for your time.
Daniel: Thank you. Bye.
Anna: Bye.


























































Simon




1. Opening and consent


Anna: Hi Simon, can you hear me well?


Simon: Yes, I can hear you. Can you hear me too?


Anna: Yes, perfectly. Thanks for joining. Before we start, I want to quickly go through consent. Is it okay if I m recording the audio of this interview? The recording is only for transcription, it will be anonymized, and you can skip any question or stop at any time.


Simon: Yes, that is okay. You can record.


Anna: Great, thanks. Just to confirm, we will use a pseudonym for you, and anything identifying like company name or specific people, we will keep generic. If I ask something that feels too specific, you can just say pass.


Simon: Yeah alright 


Anna: also the topic is trust and perceived effectiveness in AI based career coaching versus human career coaching. There are no right answers. I am interested in your experience and your thinking. Does that sound okay?


Simon: Yes, totally.


Anna: Perfect. I will start the recording now. Okay, recording is on. To warm up, how are you doing today?


Simon: Um, fine. A bit tired because I was debugging something since morning, but yeah, okay hahahah 


Anna: Sounds very on brand for a machine learning engineer. hahaha . Okay, let us start.
2) Participant context and career situation


Anna: To begin, where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now or have recently faced?


Simon: Okay. So i  am 24, and i am finishing my Master degree in Germany. I have a physics background, like my Bachelor was physics, and then I moved into more data science and machine learning during my Master. Right now I work in a Munich startup in a student role. The title is something like AI product engineer, but it is basically a mix of ML engineering and product work. And the main career thing is that I already signed a full-time offer with the same startup. So, in about two months after graduation I start full time, and it also has a Blue Card pathway. So in that sense the big decision is kind of done, but I still have like smaller questions: what direction do I want long term, how do I build toward product management, and how do I handle the German bureaucracy without losing my mind hahaha. 


Anna: Oh, German bureaucracy is crazy right. When you say the big decision is done, can you tell me what made that decision significant for you?


Simon: It is like, it locks in the next chapter. I moved here from Brazil two years ago for the Master. So the decision is not only job, it is also, do I stay in Germany, do I build a life here, do I commit to the visa path, all those things. Munich is expensive, rent is crazy, and at the same time career wise it is a good place for tech, at least for what I do. So the offer is not just money, it is stability, residence permit, being able to plan. Also it confirms that my pivot from physics into ML actually worked. That felt big.


Anna: What was most difficult about it, in the last months? Was it uncertainty, lack of information, pressure, fear of mistakes, something else?


Simon: A mix. Uncertainty about the market, because everyone talks about layoffs, and also in Germany there is like this vibe of, you need to be very qualified, very formal, like your CV must be perfect. Also pressure from timeline. My student residence permit is tied to finishing studies, and then you have this job-seeking period, and you have to do paperwork, and the Ausländerbehörde appointments are… yeah. So I had a time pressure, like if I do not get an offer fast enough, it becomes messy. And fear of mistakes, yes. Like, if I accept the wrong role, maybe I get stuck writing code forever, and I already know I want to move more toward product. So I wanted a role that gives me credibility technically but also lets me touch product.


Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed during that time?


Simon: Practical and emotional, both. Practical as in how do I position myself, how do I explain the physics background, how do I write a CV that fits the German style, how do I prepare for ML interviews, and also how do I negotiate, like salary, contract terms, and Blue Card stuff. Emotional support is more like am I making a mistake moving countries, what if I fail, what if I do not fit in. But I am a bit self-directed, so I mostly tried to build my own structure. Still, I used tools and people.


Anna: When you say self-directed, what does that look like for you in practice?


Simon: I set my own milestones. Like, I created a spreadsheet with companies, roles, deadlines, status. I decided, okay, every week I apply to X roles, every weekend I work on portfolio projects, and I go to meetups to get referrals. Also I have a clear picture of what I want next. Like, short term I am okay doing hands-on ML. Mid term I want to be closer to product decisions, user needs, and strategy. I do not want to be coding all day forever. And because I know that, I choose activities that push me there, like I ask to join product meetings, or I do user story writing sometimes, even if my job is technical.


Anna: That is helpful. Can you describe the startup a bit, in a generic way, and what you do there day to day?


Simon: Sure. It is a B2B startup, Munich-based, around industrial IT and predictive maintenance. So we have sensor data from machines, and we build models to predict failures or optimize maintenance schedules. My tasks are mixed. Some days I do data pipelines, like cleaning time series, feature engineering. Some days I train models and evaluate them, and make them production-ready, like monitoring, drift checks, that stuff. And because we are small, I also do product things, like talking to the product manager, translating client pain points into model requirements, writing internal docs, and sometimes presenting results to clients. It is very fast. Like, you ship fast, then you fix. Not always perfect.


Anna: What was your path into that role? How did you find it and what did the hiring process look like?


Simon: I found it through LinkedIn plus a meetup connection. I went to a machine learning meetup in Munich, like one of those evening events with pizza, and I talked to someone who later became my internal referral. Then I applied on LinkedIn, and the process was: first a recruiter screen, then a technical interview with an ML engineer, then a take-home assignment. The assignment was like, here is a dataset, build a small model, explain your choices, and write a short report. Then I had a final interview with the CTO plus product manager, more like system design and product sense: how would you deploy this, how would you handle feedback from customers, how do you prioritize. And also some behavioral questions.


Anna: How did you feel in those steps? Any part particularly challenging?


Simon: The take-home was time-consuming. Also the product sense questions, because I came from physics and like pure engineering mindset. In physics you are trained to be correct, not necessarily to be pragmatic. In startup you need to say, okay, we cannot be perfect, we need something that works and creates value. So I had to learn to talk in that language. Also speaking English is fine for me, but sometimes German comes in, like documents or bureaucracy, and then you feel less confident.


Anna: What languages do you use day to day?


Simon: At work mostly English. Some colleagues speak German in side conversations, but meetings are English because we have international people. My German is like B1-ish, maybe. I can do basics like Anmeldung, or at the doctor, but professional German, no. And outside work, in Munich, you can survive in English, but for bureaucracy you need German or someone who helps.


Anna: What was your relocation story, briefly? What brought you to Germany?


Simon: I wanted a strong Master program in data and AI, and Germany was attractive because tuition is low compared to like the US or UK. Also I liked the idea of being in Europe, like more stability. I applied to programs, got into one in Munich, moved here in 2024. The first months were intense: finding a room, Anmeldung, opening bank account, health insurance, like Krankenkasse, all that. And then adapting to winter. Hahaha. Brazil to Munich winter is… yeah.


Anna:  Hahaha. Yes. And you said you have a full-time offer starting in two months after graduation. What does the Blue Card pathway mean to you personally?


Simon: It means I can stop being stressed all the time. Because the visa situation affects everything. Like, you cannot sign a long rent contract if you are unsure. You cannot plan travel easily. You always think, what if the appointment is late. So Blue Card is like a feeling of legitimacy. Also it is a signal that my salary and contract are in a good range. But it is also paperwork, and it depends on rules, and rules can change. So it is not pure comfort, but it is better than being on student status.


Anna: Okay. That gives me a good overview. Let us move to how you usually get career support.


3) Career support in general


Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?


Simon: Um, first myself  like, I read a lot, I do research. Then I talk to a few people: friends who are also in tech, some classmates, and one mentor I have informally. Not like official mentoring, but a guy I met at university who works as a data scientist, maybe 5 years older, and we talk sometimes. I also used the university career center once, like they had a CV check. And then honestly I use AI tools a lot, mainly ChatGPT, for writing and structuring.


Anna: What tends to actually help you, and what tends to feel unhelpful or frustrating?


Simon: Helpful is concrete feedback. Like, if someone reads my CV and says, this line is unclear, this is too long, this does not show impact. Or if they tell me, for this role you need to emphasize A and B. Also helpful is reality checks about the market, like, do not apply to only big tech, also look at middlestrandt or look at startups. Unhelpful is generic advice like, follow your passion, or network more, or just be confident. Like, yeah, okay, but what do I do tomorrow. Also unhelpful is when people give advice that does not fit my context as an immigrant in Germany. Like, someone might say, just walk in and talk to the manager, but in Germany that is weird


Anna: If you think about the support you got during your job search, what were the top channels for you?


Simon: LinkedIn for sure, because it is easy and you see roles. Referrals through meetups helped a lot. GitHub and portfolio also mattered, because in ML, having projects that show you can do end-to-end is important. And also my university network, like professors or labs, not super strong but some.


Anna: Did you use any other resources, like online communities, forums, or events?


Simon: Yes, meetups, like Munich ML meetup or something like that i dont remember the name of it , had a very funny one actually , also some AI product events. And I read blogs, like on interview prep, and watched YouTube videos for system design. But you need to filter because a lot of content is US market focused. In Germany interviews are sometimes different. But the ML fundamentals are universal.


Anna: When you evaluate career advice in general, what makes you take it seriously?


Simon: Two things. One is competence, like does the person actually know what they are talking about, have they done it. The other is fit, like do they understand my goals and constraints. If advice is competent but not fitting, it can still be interesting, but I might not follow it. And I also value honesty about uncertainty. Like, if someone says, it depends, and then explains what it depends on, I trust that more than someone being very sure.


Anna: That is a nice transition into trust. Next I will ask specifically about human coaching, but you mentioned you have not had offline coaching. So we will talk about expectations.
4) Human coaching experience and trust 


Anna: Have you ever had an offline or online career coaching session with a professional coach, like a paid coach or a structured program?


Simon: No, not in the classic sense. I never paid a coach or went to a private career coach. I only used the university career center, but that was more like a service, not coaching. It was like, here are tips, fixed appointment, very practical.


Anna: Okay. So if you imagine a human career coach, what do you expect they would do with you? What would you want from them, given your situation?


Simon: I would want them to help me with strategy and positioning. Like, how do I present myself as someone who is technical but wants to move toward product. Also I would want them to help me identify gaps: what skills do I need, what experiences do I need. And maybe accountability, like, if I say I will do X, then they check. Also maybe help with negotiation and career planning. And honestly, emotional support too, like someone who can listen and reflect back, because moving countries and building a career is stressful.


Anna: What would make you trust a human coach?


Simon: Hhm let me think… Competence i guess. Like, they should have experience with tech careers. If it is a general coach who only coached like marketing people, I would worry. Also kindness or empathy,or  like when they should listen properly, not rush not judge and confidentiality. Because career topics can be sensitive, like visa status, salary, insecurities. I need to feel safe that they do not share it or use it in a weird way. Thats all i think. No  structure also. Structure yes. Like, I like a plan. I do not want just a conversation, I want some framework, goals, follow-ups. And also feeling seen, like they understand my background and my specific context.


Anna: When you say feeling seen, how would you notice that in a session?


Simon: The questions they ask. If they ask generic questions like, what are your strengths, and then they give a generic summary, I would not feel seen. But if they pick up on details, like, you said you liked bridging technical and business, tell me more, and then they connect it to a plan, then I feel they actually listened. Also if they remember what I said last time, like small details.


Anna: What would be red flags for you with a human coach?


Simon: Sales vibe. Like if in the first 10 minutes they push their package, or they say, you need 10 sessions, pay now, I would not like that. Also false certainty, like, I guarantee you will get a job in 2 weeks. That is unrealistic. Another red flag is if they do not understand tech context and still act like they do. Like mixing up basic concepts, or giving advice that is clearly not relevant. Also confidentiality doubts, like if they talk about other clients in too much detail. And also if they are too moralizing, like judging your choices.


Anna: If you think about your own attitude, you said you generally trust career coaches and believe they often help. Where does that belief come from, if you have not used one yourself?


Simon: From observing friends and also from the idea that talking to an experienced person can help you see blind spots. Like, coaching is basically structured reflection plus expertise. I have seen friends who had coaches for job changes, and they got clarity and made decisions faster. Also, I guess I am open to the concept. I do not think coaches are magic, but I think they can help if they are good and if the client is engaged. I would consider it in the future, maybe when I want to make a bigger transition, like from engineering to product management.


Anna: You also mentioned you have considered becoming a career coach later for technical specialists. Can you tell me what you mean by that?


Simon: Yeah. Like, in tech, a lot of people struggle with interviews, or with presenting themselves, or with deciding between paths like research, engineering, product, management. And someone who has been through it could help, especially if they know how to prepare for coding interviews, ML interviews, system design, and also how to communicate impact. I could imagine doing that later, as a side thing, like helping people with CVs, interview prep, career strategy in tech. But not now. Now I need to build my own career first. 


Anna: Makes sense. If you were to choose a coach, what would you look for in their background or credentials?


Simon: I would look for someone who has worked in tech, ideally in Germany or Europe, because context matters. Maybe someone who has been an engineer and then moved to product, because that is my goal. Also I would look for testimonials, but carefully, because testimonials can be marketing. I would look for a first session that feels like value, not just sales. And maybe some proof of methodology, like how they work, what is the process. And I would prefer someone who can speak English, obviously, and maybe some German knowledge could help with local context.


Anna: Okay. Let us switch to AI tools, where you do have experience. 


5) AI tools experience and trust 


Anna: Can you tell me about your experience using AI tools for career-related questions? What have you used, and for what?


Simon: Mainly ChatGPT. I also tried Claude once, but mostly сhat because it is convenient and I already have a routine. I used it for rewriting my CV, rewriting bullet points, translating things into a more professional English. Also for LinkedIn summary, like how to position myself. And for interview prep: generating likely questions, practicing answers, and also explaining ML concepts in a way that is interview-friendly. Like, if I know the math, but I need to explain it clearly. It helped a lot, honestly.


Anna: You said it helped you get the job by rewriting your CV. Can you walk me through that episode? What exactly did you do and what changed?


Simon: Yes. So initially my CV was very academic. Physics style. Like, lots of theory, not enough impact. And also the language was not good, because I wrote in a kind of formal, but not business-friendly way. I took my CV text, removed personal details, and pasted bullet points into сhat. I asked something like: rewrite these bullets for an AI product engineer role in a German startup, emphasize impact, use action verbs, keep it concise, quantify where possible. And it gave me a version that was much more readable.


Anna: What was different in the rewritten version?


Simon: It was more outcome focused. Like, instead of saying, implemented a neural network, it would say, built a predictive model that reduced error by X, or improved detection of anomalies. It also suggested a structure: first a summary, then skills, then experience, then projects. And it helped me translate academic projects into product language. Like, I did a thesis project on time series forecasting, and chat helped me frame it as, developed forecasting pipeline for industrial sensor data, evaluated models, deployed prototype. That kind of framing.


Anna: How did you decide what to keep and what to change?


Simon: I did not just copy paste. I compared it with job descriptions and with other CV examples. I took the good parts, and I edited. Also I checked for accuracy, because sometimes it invents metrics. Like it will say, improved performance by 30 percent, but I did not measure that. So I had to be careful. I used it like a draft generator, not as truth.


Anna: Did you notice any emotional impact from that process? Like confidence, stress level?


Simon: Yes, it reduced stress, because the blank page problem is real. Like, you know you did stuff, but writing it in a good way is hard. When chat gave me a draft, I felt, okay, I can work with this. It also made me more confident to apply, because I felt my CV was not embarrassing hahaha. And I did get more interview invitations after improving it, so that reinforced it.


Anna: Can you share a moment where ChatGPT truly helped beyond writing? Something like a decision, or preparation, or clarity?


Simon: Interview prep. There was one interview where they asked product sense questions. Like, how do you decide what model to deploy, how do you trade off accuracy versus latency, how do you communicate uncertainty to a client. I practiced with chat by giving it the context and asking it to act like an interviewer. And then I responded, and it gave feedback, like, you focused too much on technical details, mention business impact, mention monitoring, mention user trust. That helped me. In the actual interview, I was more structured. And I think that contributed to the offer.


Anna: Do you remember a specific piece of feedback that stuck?


Simon: Yes. It said something like, always connect model decisions to stakeholder needs. Like, if the client needs reliability, talk about robustness and monitoring, not just metrics. And also, do not oversell, be clear about limitations. That is aligned with how Germans like things, I think, like realistic. So I used that.


Anna: And can you share a moment when ChatGPT failed you or felt unhelpful?


Simon: Definitely. Sometimes it gives generic output. Like, you ask, how do I move from ML engineer to product manager, and it gives the same list: learn business, talk to stakeholders, build roadmap. It is true but too generic. Another failure was hallucination. I asked it about Blue Card requirements and timelines, and it confidently gave wrong information. Like it mixed up salary thresholds and said something about documents that was not correct for my case. I noticed because I cross-checked with official sources and with my company HR. So that was a big reminder: do not trust it for legal specifics.


Anna: How did that affect your trust?


Simon: It lowered it for that domain. Like, I trust it for writing and brainstorming, but for legal or visa stuff, I treat it as a rough starting point at most. If it speaks with too much certainty in areas where rules change, I get suspicious.


Anna: Any other concerns?


Simon: Privacy. Like, CV contains personal info. I tried to remove name, address, company names, but still, you share your career story. So I am careful. I do not paste full contracts or visa letters. Also sometimes it makes assumptions about my background, like it will assume I am a software engineer with 5 years experience, because the prompt is vague. Then the answer is not tailored.


Anna: How do you verify AI outputs in general?


Simon: I do a few things. For factual claims, I ask for sources or I cross-check with credible websites. For writing, I check for tone, and I make sure it is truthful. For interview prep, I compare with real interview guides and with feedback from humans. Also I sometimes run the same question with a different phrasing, to see if the answer is consistent. If it changes drastically, I know it is shaky.


Anna: What makes it feel trustworthy to you, like in the moment?


Simon: When it reflects my context correctly. Like, when I give it details and it uses them. Also when it shows nuance, like, it does not just say do X, but says, here are options, here are trade-offs. And when it admits uncertainty, like, I am not sure, or it depends. That increases trust. If it is too confident, I trust less.


Anna: That is a good point. Let us move into comparison.


6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks 


Anna: I want to compare human coaching and AI tools via specific career tasks. I will name a task, and I would like you to tell me which you would prefer for that task and why. Does that work?


Simon: Yes.


Anna: First task: rewriting a CV and LinkedIn profile for a specific role in Germany. What would you prefer, human coach or AI tool?


Simon: For first draft and iteration, AI tool. It is fast and you can do many versions. But for final polish, especially if it is high-stakes, I would like a human review, ideally someone who knows the market. Because AI can make it sound good, but maybe it misses local expectations, like German CV style, or it can create too much fluff. So I would do AI first, then human second.


Anna: What is the advantage of AI there, in your experience?


Simon: Speed. You can ask 20 times, rewrite this bullet, shorten, make it more impact-focused, tailor to job description. A human would get tired, or it would be expensive. Also AI is non-judgmental, you can ask stupid questions hahaha


Anna: Agreed. And what is the advantage of a human there?


Simon: Context and honesty. A human can say, this claim sounds unrealistic, or this project is not relevant, remove it. Also humans can pick up subtle things, like this sounds arrogant, or this is too humble. And they can ask you follow-up questions to get more detail.


Anna: Second task: choosing between two offers, like startup versus corporate, or Germany versus another country. Who would you prefer?


Simon: Human. Because that is more values and trade offs. AI can help list pros and cons, but a human can challenge you, like, you say you want stability but you choose startup, why. A human can also understand emotions, like fear, identity. AI is more mechanical.


Anna: Third task: interview preparation, technical and behavioral. What would you prefer?


Simon: Hybrid. AI for drilling questions and structuring answers, human for realistic feedback and for understanding how an interviewer in that company thinks. Like, a human who has done ML interviews can say, your answer is too theoretical, or you missed the point.


Anna: Fourth task: dealing with German bureaucracy and visa stress, like residence permit, Blue Card steps. What would you prefer?


Simon: Human, or official sources, not AI. Hahaha. AI is too risky there. It can give outdated info. A human like HR or a relocation consultant or someone who went through it is better. AI can maybe help translate a letter or explain vocabulary, like what does this title? Auf title? I dont remember hahaha what does it mean, but not advice.


Anna: Okay. I will now embed a quick micro-poll. I will ask you to rate some things from 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust or not effective at all, and 10 is complete trust or extremely effective. There is no correct number. I care about why. Is that okay?


Simon: Yes.


Anna: First rating: trust in human career coaches, even though you have not used one. Based on your expectations. What number from 0 to 10?


Simon: I would say 7.


Anna: Why 7 and not lower?


Simon: Because I think many coaches genuinely want to help and have experience. The human element, empathy, confidentiality, that creates trust for me. Also if someone has a reputation and credentials, I would trust them. So baseline is positive.


Anna: Why not higher than 7?


Simon: Because variability. Some coaches are great, some are not. And without personal experience, I cannot say 9 or 10. Also, there is always the risk of generic advice or misfit. And sometimes coaching can be more about motivation than real strategy. For me, I want substance. So 7 feels like, I am open and generally trusting, but not naive.


Anna: Second rating: trust in AI tools for career support, like ChatGPT, for your use. 0 to 10.


Simon: For trust, maybe 6.


Anna: Why 6?


Simon: Because it is useful and often accurate in writing and general guidance, but it can be delulu and it lacks true understanding of my life. So I trust it conditionally. I trust it as a tool, not as an authority. Also privacy concerns reduce trust a bit.


Anna: What would make it a 7 or 8 for you?


Simon: Better transparency about sources, like if it shows where it got info, and if it has strong guardrails like, for legal topics it refuses or says it is uncertain. Also if there was a mode that guarantees not storing data, or a clear privacy contract, that would increase trust.


Anna: Third rating: perceived effectiveness of human coaching for your situation, expected effectiveness. 0 to 10.


Simon: I would say 7 again.


Anna: What does effectiveness mean to you here?


Simon: Effectiveness is outcomes. Like, does it help me make decisions, create action steps, build confidence, actually improve results like interviews passed or better offers. For human coaching, I expect it can be effective if the coach is good and fits my goals. They can help with strategy and accountability. So 7.


Anna: Why not 9?


Simon: Because it is not magic. Also I am already quite structured. A coach might not add huge value for me in basic things. It would be more effective in specific transitions, like moving to product. But in general, I think it is helpful, not transformational always.


Anna: Fourth rating: perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, based on your experience. 0 to 10.


Simon: For effectiveness, I would say 8.


Anna: Why 8?


Simon: Because it actually produced concrete outcomes for me. It improved my CV, it helped me prepare, and I got the job. Also it saves time every day. So in effectiveness terms, it is high. Even if I do not fully trust it, it is effective for certain tasks.


Anna: Why not 10?


Simon: Because it fails in areas like legal specifics, and it can be generic. And it cannot replace a human for deep reflection or accountability. Also, sometimes it makes me overthink. Like, you ask too many questions and you get too many options. 


Anna: That is very clear. Thank you. Let us go deeper into trust and effectiveness as separate things, because you already started to distinguish them.


7) Trust-effectiveness link 


Anna: When you think about trust, what does it mean for you in this context? And separately, what does effectiveness mean? You started defining effectiveness, but I want to hear it in your own words.


Simon: Okay. Trust is like, do I believe the source is competent, and do I feel safe using it. Competence means it gives good guidance, it understands context, it does not mislead. Safe means confidentiality, not being judged, not being exploited, like not being manipulated. Also trust includes empathy for humans, like feeling understood. For AI, empathy is not real, but you can still feel comfortable if it is respectful and consistent.


Effectiveness is results. Like, after using it, do I have more clarity, do I take action, do I make a decision faster, do I perform better in interviews, do I get offers, do I feel more confident. It is more measurable, at least in my head.


Anna: How do trust and effectiveness connect for you? For example, if you trust something more, does it automatically become more effective?


Simon: Not automatically, but it changes how I use it. If I trust a coach, I will share more details, and then they can tailor better, so it becomes more effective. If I do not trust, I hold back, and then the advice is generic, so less effective. But there is also the opposite: something can be effective even if I do not fully trust it, like AI. I do not fully trust it, but it still helps, because I use it in a limited way.


Anna: Can you give an example of low trust but some effectiveness, from your experience?


Simon: Yes. When I used chat to generate interview questions for the startup, I did not trust it fully because it might not know the company. But it still produced a structured list: ML fundamentals, system design, product questions, behavioral. Even if some questions were not exactly asked, the structure helped me prepare. So effectiveness was there, but trust was moderate.


Anna: And can you give an example of high trust but low effectiveness, even hypothetically?


Simon: Hypothetically, yes. Imagine a human coach who is very empathetic, very supportive, you feel safe, you trust them personally. But they do not have tech context and they give advice like, just show your passion, or network more, or do a generic resume template. Then you might feel good emotionally, high trust, but outcomes are not improved. So low effectiveness for my goals.


Anna: That is a strong distinction. Can the opposite happen, high effectiveness but low trust?


Simon: Yes, similar to AI. Or even with a human. Like, you could have a very direct reviewer, maybe a hiring manager, who is harsh, you do not feel emotionally safe, but they give very precise feedback that improves your CV. So you do not trust them as a person, but the advice is effective. That can happen.


Anna: What about trust in terms of confidentiality. How important is that in career conversations?


Simon: Very important. Especially as an immigrant. Visa status, salary, insecurity about language, these are vulnerable topics. With a human coach, I would need a clear confidentiality agreement. With AI, confidentiality is more like data privacy. If I think my data could be stored or used, I will limit what I share. And then effectiveness goes down because it lacks context.


Anna: That is interesting. So your trust influences how much context you provide, and that influences effectiveness.


Simon: Exactly.


Anna: Are there any moments where you felt you had to choose between trust and convenience?


Simon: Yes. Convenience is AI. You can do it anytime, late night, quick. Trust is more human, but it costs time and money. Sometimes I choose convenience. Like, at 1 am, I am not going to call a coach. I will ask chat. Even if trust is lower, it is available.


Anna: In those moments, do you notice any boundaries you set with the AI?


Simon: Yes, I keep it at the level of content, not personal secrets. Like, I talk about how to phrase things, how to structure, but I do not paste personal documents. Also I remind myself that it can be wrong, so I treat it as suggestions. I think that boundary helps maintain both trust and effectiveness in the limited domain.


Anna: What would make you stop using an AI tool for career support?


Simon: If it repeatedly gives wrong info in a way that harms me. Like, if I follow advice and it leads to a bad outcome. Also if there was a data scandal, like my data is leaked. Or if it becomes too manipulative, like it pushes certain services. Basically if the risk becomes high.


Anna: And what would make you stop using a human coach, hypothetically?


Simon: Same vibe i guess  if they break confidentiality, if they push sales, if they do not listen, if they give generic advice, if they pretend certainty, or if they do not respect my autonomy. I do not want someone to tell me what to do, I want someone to support my decision-making.


Anna: Great. Now I want to explore your preferences and the idea of hybrid models.


8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction


Anna: Imagine a hybrid model where AI helps with preparation, structuring, and follow-up, and a human coach helps with deep reflection, accountability, and high-stakes decisions. What is your initial reaction?


Simon: That sounds like the best combination, in theory. Because it uses AI where it is strong: repetitive tasks, drafts, brainstorming, 24/7. And it uses humans where they are strong: understanding context, emotions, and making you commit to actions. I think hybrid can also reduce cost, because you do not need a human for every small thing.


Anna: What parts of that hybrid idea feel attractive to you personally?


Simon: The workflow. Like, I could use AI to prepare before a session: write my thoughts, clarify my questions, generate options. Then in the human session, we go deeper, and we decide. After that, AI could help me execute: write emails, update CV, plan steps, reminders. That would be very efficient.


Anna: What parts feel risky or unclear?


Simon: Data flow. Like, does the human coach see what I wrote to AI. How is it stored. Who owns the data. Also accountability. If AI gives a plan and the human gives another plan, what is the source of truth. Also quality control: if the AI output is wrong, does the human correct it, or do you end up trusting wrong stuff. And the boundary of roles: when do you escalate to human, when do you stay with AI.


Anna: If you could design the hybrid model for someone like you, what would be the rules for when AI leads versus when a human leads?


Simon: AI leads when tasks are low emotional and more technical: rewriting text, summarizing job descriptions, preparing interview question lists, generating templates, translating. Human leads when tasks involve values, identity, long-term decisions, or sensitive topics like negotiation, visa strategy, or conflict at work. Also human leads when I feel stuck emotionally, like anxiety or self-doubt, because AI can be supportive but it is not real empathy.


Anna: What would make you trust a hybrid platform more?


Simon: Transparency. Like, clear explanation of what AI does, what human does, how data is handled, how matching works if there is matching. Also clear limitations: do not promise guarantees. And quality of humans: vetted coaches with relevant backgrounds. Also, I would like the AI part to show sources when it claims facts, and to separate facts from suggestions.


Anna: Would you personally pay for such a hybrid service?


Simon: Maybe, yes, if I am at a transition point. Right now I already have a job offer, so I do not urgently need it. But for the move to product management, I might pay, because that is strategic and it is harder. If the hybrid service can provide a coach who actually did that path, plus AI to support execution, that is valuable.


Anna: What would be a deal-breaker?


Simon: If it feels like a funnel to sell courses, or if it is too generic. Also if privacy is unclear. And if the coaches are not credible. Like, if they are generalists, it would not fit. I need someone who understands technical roles.


Anna: How would you want the coach to interact with you, style-wise?


Simon: Calm, structured, not too motivational. I prefer someone who asks good questions and also gives concrete feedback. Like, a mix of coaching and consulting. Some coaches are purely reflective, but I also like directness.


Anna: If the platform offered AI-only, human-only, and hybrid, what would you choose for different situations?


Simon: AI-only for quick tasks like cover letter drafts, bullet rewrites, interview question practice. Human-only for big decisions and personal topics. Hybrid for structured career planning over time, where you want both reflection and execution support.


Anna: Great. We are close to closing. I want to ask one more thing: looking back, what do you think was the most effective support you used in your job search, and why?


9) Closing


Simon: Most effective was actually a combination: chat for writing and prep, plus human input from my referral and from my manager. Like, the referral helped me understand what the startup needed, what to emphasize. Chat helped me produce materials fast and practice. So effectiveness came from combining resources. The AI tool gave me speed, but humans gave me credibility.


Anna: If you had to describe in one sentence what trust means for you in this topic, what would you say?


Simon: Trust means I feel safe sharing enough context, and I believe the guidance is competent and honest not like manipulative to get money out of me hahaha. 


Anna: And one sentence for effectiveness?


Simon: Effectiveness means it leads to concrete progress


Anna: Thank you.  Is there anything you expected me to ask but I did not, or anything you want to add?


Simon: Maybe just that for early-career people in tech, the barrier is often not knowledge, it is communication. Like, you can be good, but you cannot present it. AI helps with that a lot. But humans help with confidence and with navigating systems. So both matter.


Anna: That is a valuable point. Thank you. Just to close formally, do you have any questions for me about the study or what happens next?


Simon: No, I think it is clear. You will anonymize and use it for research.


Anna: Exactly. We will transcribe, remove identifying details, and use it as part of thematic analysis. If later you want to withdraw, you can message me. Thank you for your time and for being so thoughtful!


Simon: Sure. Thank you. It was interesting.


Anna: Thank you so much i will stop the recording now.

2) Participant context and career situation

So, I am internal. I work on go to market and brand, and also campaign stuff, and some content, and internal stakeholder management. And right now my big question is: I want to transition from marketing into product management, or like a bridge, product marketing into PM. Not just, okay, we have an offer and we market it nicely. It is like owning the problem, and iterating. I want to be closer to that.
Anna: And what is pushing that now, in this last period?

In the last, I would say, six to nine months, I was staffed on a project, not as a consultant but kind of supporting with marketing, for a digital product BCG was building internally for a client team. It was like a prototype, and we had a product owner from the consulting side, and designers, and some engineers. And I was invited because they needed positioning and messaging, yes, but then I ended up doing more. Like, I was the person who translated user feedback into changes in the onboarding flow. I wrote the first version of the user stories. I ran a workshop with internal users. And I felt, um, it sounds dramatic, but I felt alive. Like, this is the thing. And then I went back to my usual marketing cycle, where it is like, we polish a deck, we align on wording for two weeks, and I was like, why does this feel empty now?
Anna: So there was a comparison experience.
Lisa: Exactly. And also, I had a moment with a senior stakeholder, like a partner, who said something like, you are so good at making this sound great, but what is the actual thing? And it was not mean. But it hit me. Because I was like, yes, I can make things sound great, but I also want to influence what the thing is. Not just the story. And then I started reading about PM, talking to people, and then coaching.
Anna: What made the decision difficult?
Lisa: Several things. One is credibility. In Germany, the job market for PM can be picky, like they want experience. Titles matter a bit. Also language expectations. Some PM roles say English is fine, but then you see, okay, German required. And inside BCG, there is internal politics. Like, internal mobility is possible, but it is not like you click a button and suddenly you are a PM. There are product roles in BCG X, in Platinion, in digital ventures, and also in internal knowledge teams, but you need sponsorship, and you need to show evidence. People will ask, have you shipped? Have you owned a backlog? Have you done discovery? And my CV says marketing. So that is the problem.
Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed?
Lisa: Structure. And honesty. And someone to, eehm, challenge me, but also not make me feel stupid. Because inside BCG, the environment is high pace and feedback culture is direct. It is polished, but it is also intense. You get feedback like, this is not sharp, this is not client ready. And I can handle that at work, but for my career, I needed someone who can hold the uncertainty with me. Like, am I making a mistake? Is this a fantasy? And then also practical support: CV, LinkedIn, target roles, experiments, interviews.
Anna: When you say uncertainty, what was the emotional part for you?
Lisa: The fear of losing status. And also, fear of disappointing myself. Like, I worked hard to get into BCG. It sounds silly because I am not even a consultant, but still, it is a known brand. And then to say, I want to switch, it is like, okay, am I ungrateful? And also fear of being rejected. Because PM interviews can be brutal, and I do not have the typical story. So, anxiety, yes.
Anna: Thank you.And are you considering switching internally, externally, or both?
Lisa: Both. Internally, because it is easier in some ways, like you already have the brand and network. But also harder because people know you as marketing Lisa. Externally, because Berlin ecosystem has many product roles, Munich too but different, more enterprise, more automotive, more B2B. And I am based in Munich. So, I am exploring both.
3) Career support in general
