Lisa: Um, friends, definitely. I have two friends who are product managers. One is in a startup in Berlin, one is in a big corporate in Munich. I also talk to my boyfriend, but he is in finance, so he is supportive but not super helpful on the content. And then at BCG, there are mentors, like informal, not official. I asked one woman in BCG  for a coffee chat. And I used online resources, like articles, some podcasts, and AI tools, yes. And then human coaching, which is the main thing for me.
Anna: What has actually helped, and what felt unhelpful?
Lisa: Helpful is when people give examples practical. Like, not just, follow your passion, but like, okay, here is how I moved from function A to function B. Here is the story I told. Here is how I positioned my experience. That helps. Unhelpful is generic advice. Like, network more. Okay, yes, but how? And also, some advice was too confident. Like, just apply to PM roles, it will work out. And I was like, hmm, no, you do not see my actual profile and the market.
Anna: And within BCG, how is it to ask for career support?
Lisa: It depends. People are nice, but everyone is busy. Staffing cycles are fast, projects change, priorities change. And there is a culture of being composed. So, you can say, I am thinking about development, but to say, I feel lost, is harder. Also, there is sometimes a fear that if you say you want to leave your function, your manager will think you are not committed. So you have to navigate that. It is like, you share selectively.
Anna: Aha. And you mentioned AI tools as part of your support mix. We will come to that later.
Lisa: Yes.
4) Human coaching experience and trust
Anna: You said you do human coaching. Can you tell me how you started, and what that process looks like?
Lisa: Yes. So, I found my coach via LinkedIn, through a friend recommendation. A friend of mine worked with her, and she said, this woman is good, she is structured, she understands corporate, and she is not, um, like a motivational speaker type. And I was like, okay, that sounds like what I need. I checked her LinkedIn, she had experience in tech and some consulting background, and she had coached people into product roles. So I booked a first call.
Anna: What was the first call like?
Lisa: It was like a chemistry check. She asked about my situation, my goal, what I tried already. She was calm, very direct, and she did not promise magic. She said, we can work together, but it will be work, and we will measure progress. And that already created trust for me because it felt honest. Not like, you will be PM in six weeks. 
Anna: And what is the format now?
Lisa: We meet every two weeks, like therapy in cadence and depth. It is on video call, usually 60 minutes. And we have a process. Every session starts with a project audit. She asks, what did you do in the last two weeks? What projects at work? What side tasks? What decisions? And then she helps me identify product-like tasks inside my projects. Like, if I ran a stakeholder workshop, that is facilitation and discovery. If I defined a campaign KPI and tracked it, that is metrics. If I created a content journey based on user segments, that is user thinking. We translate.
Anna: So she is reframing your work through a product lens.
Lisa: Exactly. And then we rewrite my CV and LinkedIn around that. Not lying, but translating. And then we run job search as experiments. That is her style. We create hypotheses and test by response rates and callbacks. It is very, um, scientific.
Anna: Can you give a concrete example of one of these experiments?
Lisa: Yes. We did two CV variants. Variant A was more marketing heavy but with product adjacent language. Variant B was more aggressive, like product orientation, highlighting workshops, requirements, roadmap contributions, even if small. And then we targeted different roles. For A, we applied to product marketing roles, like PMM. For B, we applied to junior PM roles or associate PM roles, and also internal product roles. We tracked response rates in a spreadsheet. Like, how many applications, how many callbacks, how many recruiter screens, how many rejections, and why.
Anna: Do you remember any numbers, roughly?
Lisa: Um, yes, roughly. Variant A got like, maybe 20 to 25 percent callback for PMM, like recruiter replies. Variant B for PM roles got like 5 to 10 percent, which is not amazing, but it was not zero. And then we adjusted keywords. Like, we realized I used marketing language that PM recruiters ignore. We added words like discovery, backlog, stakeholder alignment, experimentation, prioritization. And we tailored the top summary. And then the response rate improved slightly, like from 5 percent to maybe 12 percent for some roles. Not huge, but enough to say, okay, it is possible.
Anna: How did that feel, emotionally, to treat job search like experiments?
Lisa: It helped my anxiety a lot. Because instead of, I got rejected, I am not good enough, it becomes, okay, hypothesis failed, adjust variables. It is less personal. It also fits my brain because at BCG we love frameworks. So it gave me something to hold onto.
Anna: You mentioned coaching is expensive but effective for you. Can you talk about that?
Lisa: Yes. It is expensive. I pay, um, I will not say exact number, but it is a significant amount per session. For Germany, it is not cheap. And I hesitated. I was like, should I spend this money? But I see it as investment. And I already feel outcomes. Like, clarity, reduced anxiety, better narrative, and also tangible things like more interviews.
Anna: Can you share a moment from coaching that increased your trust, something memorable?
Lisa: Yes. There was a session where I talked about internal politics. I was scared to say it, because it can be sensitive. Like, I told her about a situation where a senior person kind of blocked me from joining a product initiative. Not officially, but by saying, we need you in marketing now, stay in your lane. And I felt small and angry and also ashamed. And in the session, she did two things. First, she explicitly stated confidentiality. She said, I want you to know, this stays here, I do not work with your company, I do not know these people, and I will not share anything. And it was not like legal talk, it was calm. And I felt, okay, safe.
Anna: And the second thing?
Lisa: Second, she showed competence. She did not go into drama. She asked me to map stakeholders like a product launch. Who has power, who has interest, who could sponsor, what is the incentive. She was like, this is not personal, this is system. And then she gave me a script, like an exact wording for a conversation with my manager. In German, actually. Like, she said, you can say, Mir ist wichtig, meine Entwicklung Richtung Produkt zu gestalten, und ich moechte konkrete Schritte vereinbaren. Something like that. And it worked. I used a version of it, and my manager responded better than I expected. That session made me trust her deeply, because she held my vulnerability and turned it into action.
Anna: What was vulnerable about it for you?
Lisa: I felt I was whining. In consulting culture, you are supposed to be solution oriented. So admitting I felt blocked felt weak. And also, there is fear of being naive. Like, maybe I misunderstand. She helped me see it clearly without making me feel stupid.
Anna: Was there a moment where you felt stuck and the coach helped you reframe?
Lisa: Yes. I remember one session where I said, I do not know if I am even allowed to want this. It sounds crazy, but I was like, who am I to think I can be a PM? And she stopped me. She said, okay, pause. Where did you learn that you need permission? And I was like, eehm, maybe family, school, German culture, like do not be too ambitious. And she reframed it as: you are already doing parts of product work, you just do not have the title. She asked me to list evidence. Like, concrete things I did. And then she said, your job is not to be chosen by the market, your job is to present evidence and learn. It sounds simple, but it hit me.
Anna: How did you feel after that session?
Lisa: Lighter. Also more confident. Not like, I am the best, but like, okay, I can try. It reduced anxiety.
Anna: Have you ever had an experience where you trusted the coach, but it was not effective in that moment?
Lisa: Yes, actually. Early on. In the first or second session, she wanted to go very deep into values, like what do you want in life, and I was in panic mode. I needed tactical help. I trusted her as a person, she was kind and smart, but I left the session thinking, okay, this was nice but I still do not know what to do next week. So it was trusted but not effective for my immediate needs. Then I told her, and she adjusted. She said, okay, we balance depth and action. That also increased trust, because she adapted.
Anna: That is a useful distinction. And on the other side, have you had something effective but not fully trusted? We will also talk about AI later, but within human coaching first.
Lisa: Within human coaching, maybe in the beginning I was like, is she really the right person? Like, she gave me a template for a networking message. It was effective, because people responded. But I still did not fully trust the method yet, because I was like, maybe this is too salesy. So I used it, it worked, but trust was still building. Now it is different.
Anna: What helps trust build over time for you?
Lisa: Consistency. She shows up. She remembers details. She tracks my progress. Also empathy without being fuzzy. Like, she can say, that sounds hard, and also, here is what we do. And confidentiality. And competence, yes.
Anna: And effectiveness, how do you evaluate that for your situation?
Lisa: Effectiveness is like, do I have more clarity? Did I make decisions? Did I take concrete actions? Do I get interviews? Do I feel less anxious? Do I have a narrative that feels true and strong? Also, at work, I see results. Like, I present myself differently in stakeholder meetings now, more like product thinking, and people respond. That is effectiveness too.
Anna: Thank you.
5) AI tools experience and trust 
Anna: Now I want to shift to AI tools. What AI tools do you use for career related things, and how?
Lisa: I use, um, mostly ChatGPT. And sometimes a tool like Notion AI for structuring notes. Also Grammarly, but that is more writing. For career, it is mostly ChatGPT. I use it for project audits and structure. Like, after a week, I dump what I did, and I ask it to categorize my tasks into product-like competencies. I also ask it to rewrite bullet points, or to generate a structure for a case story. But deep work is only with a human.
Anna: When you say deep work, what does that mean?
Lisa: Deep work is like, what am I actually afraid of? What is my real decision? Also, very tailored narrative. Like, how do I combine my BCG marketing story with product? AI can give generic, but it does not know my context fully. And also, I have privacy boundaries.
Anna: Can you give an example of a moment AI helped with project audit or restructuring accomplishments?
Lisa: Yes. There was a time I had to update my CV and I was overwhelmed. I had like, 12 projects, and BCG marketing projects are messy, like many stakeholders, and you do a bit of everything. I wrote a raw list of tasks, very unstructured. I put it into ChatGPT and asked: group these into themes, and suggest strong action verbs, and propose metrics placeholders. And it returned a neat structure. It was not perfect, but it helped me see patterns. Like, I realized I do a lot of experimentation, A B testing in campaigns, and also stakeholder workshops. And then I could bring that to my coach. So AI was effective as a first pass.
Anna: What made it effective there?
Lisa:  Time. I mean speed of it. Seconds? And structure. It reduced cognitive load. It was like, okay, here is a draft. And then I can edit.
Anna: And can you describe a moment AI failed for you?
Lisa: Oh yes. Hahaha&. Many. One specific: I asked it to help me craft a story for why I want PM. I gave it my background, and it produced a very bland narrative, like, I am passionate about products and I want to leverage my marketing skills. It sounded like everyone. And it also assumed I had direct product ownership, like it wrote, led cross functional team to deliver product roadmap, which is not true. So I was like, no, that is dangerous. Also, it missed nuance. Like, my reason is partly emotional, partly about impact, partly about identity. It cannot capture that without a lot of back and forth.
Anna: Was there any privacy boundary moment?
Lisa: Yes and no at the same time. I hesitate to paste internal information. BCG has strict confidentiality. Even if I anonymize, I feel uneasy. So I keep it generic. And that makes the output more generic. But when I talk about myself i dont care. 
Anna: How does that affect your trust in AI tools?
Lisa:It can lower it but depends on the task. Like, I trust it for structure,t for deep personal or confidential context sometimes but not like super strongly. Also, I sometimes worry about wrong assumptions. It can sound confident while being wrong. So I try to always verify.
Anna: Do you ever use AI during the coaching process?
Lisa: Yes, between sessions. Like, after a coaching call, I use AI to summarize my action items, or to draft an email to a mentor, or to brainstorm interview questions. But I do not replace the coach with AI.
Anna: What would make you trust AI more in this domain?
Lisa: Clear boundaries. Like, if it says, I cannot know your context, here are options. Also, better personalization without needing me to share sensitive data. And, eehm, maybe transparency, like why it suggests something. But honestly, performance matters most. If it consistently gives me useful outputs, I trust more.
Anna: Thank you.
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks
Anna: I want to compare human and AI through specific tasks. Let us take project audit first. If you had to choose, who is better for that, AI or your coach?
Lisa: AI is good for the first layer. Like, I dump tasks, it categorizes, it gives a structure. But my coach is better for meaning. She asks questions like, what did you decide, where did you influence, what was the impact. AI cannot ask the right follow ups in the same way, unless you prompt it very carefully. So, for project audit, I do both. AI for structure, human for interpretation.
Anna: What about rewriting CV and LinkedIn?
Lisa: AI can help with wording and variants. Like, give me three bullet options. That is helpful. But the coach is better for strategy. Like, what story do we want? What evidence do we highlight? How do we avoid overclaiming? She also knows market patterns. AI sometimes produces inflated language. In Germany, too much hype is a red flag. My coach catches that. She is like, do not sound like American sales. 
Anna: And job search strategy, like selecting roles, companies, and testing hypotheses?
Lisa: Coach. Because she helps me decide what to test first, and how to interpret results. AI can suggest a list of companies, but it does not know internal constraints, like which BCG unit is realistic for me, or which hiring manager values what. Also, in Munich, the product ecosystem is different from Berlin. AI can say, apply to startups, but my coach asks, do you want startup risk? What is your visa situation? What salary floor? Things like that.
Anna: And emotional support, confidence, dealing with anxiety?
Lisa: Human. No question. AI can say supportive words, but it does not feel real to me. I mean, no wait, let me rephrase. It can be comforting for a minute, like journaling. But trust is different. With my coach, I feel seen. And AI can be like it is processing me? Scanning my words? I dont know 
Anna: Can you explain what feeling processed means?
Lisa: Like, I input text, output comes. It is transactional. And sometimes it mirrors you, but you know it is a pattern. With my coach, she notices when I avoid something. She says, you laughed, why? She can sit with silence. AI fills silence with text. 
Anna: Any tasks where AI is clearly better?
Lisa: Brainstorming quickly. Like, give me 20 interview questions, or a checklist for product case interviews, or a structure for a portfolio. Also language polishing. And sometimes, it is effective because it is available at night. Like, at 23:00, when I am spiraling, I can dump thoughts. Not ideal, but it is there.
Anna: And any tasks where the coach is clearly better beyond emotional support?
Lisa: Negotiating identity. Like, the shift from marketing to product is not just tasks, it is who I am. Also politics. And accountability. I tell her I will do something, and then two weeks later she asks, did you do it? AI does not hold me accountable unless I set it up.
Anna: So there is a relational accountability?
Lisa: Yes.
Anna: If you think about a very concrete moment, can you describe a time you tried AI for something and it did not work, and then you brought it to the coach and it did work?
Lisa: Yes. I tried AI to craft a message to a senior person at BCG  to ask about internal transfer. AI gave me a very formal message, polite but generic. I sent something similar once and I got no reply. Then I told my coach, and she asked, what is your relationship, what is the context, what do you want exactly, what is in it for them. And then we crafted a message that was short, specific, and referenced a concrete shared project. And I got a reply within one day. So AI was ineffective because it lacked relational nuance. Coach was effective because she coached me on social dynamics.
Anna: That is helpful.
Anna: I want to do a quick short rating part, just to capture your current perceptions. It will take about a minute. On a scale from 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all and 10 is complete trust, how would you rate your trust in human career coaches, in general?
Lisa: In general, I would say 8.
Anna: What makes it an 8 and not a 10?
Lisa: Because coaches vary. There are many on the market, and some are not good, or they overpromise. So general trust is not 10. But I believe a good human coach can be trustworthy because of empathy, confidentiality, and experience. That is why it is high.
Anna: And trust in AI tools for career support, 0 to 10?
Lisa: Maybe 5.
Anna: Why 5?
Lisa: Because I trust it for structure and drafts, but I do not trust it for nuance, confidentiality, and sometimes accuracy. It can hallucinate, like assume things. So it is mid.
Anna: Perceived effectiveness of human coaching for your situation, 0 to 10?
Lisa: For my situation, I would say 9.
Anna: Why 9?
Lisa: Because it actually moves me forward. I have actions, I have interviews, I have confidence. The only reason it is not 10 is because it is expensive, and also because not every session is a breakthrough. Sometimes it is maintenance.
Anna: And perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, 0 to 10?
Lisa: 6.
Anna: Why 6?
Lisa: It helps a lot with speed and structure. It is effective for certain tasks. But it cannot replace the human work, so sex. 
Anna: Thank you, that is very clear.
7) Trust-effectiveness link
Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about human coaching, how does trust affect whether it is effective?
Lisa: Trust is like the door. If I do not trust the coach, I will not share the real problem. And then they cannot help. Like, if I hide the messy parts, like insecurity, politics, fear, then the coach can only work on surface things, like CV wording. So trust enables depth. And depth makes it effective.
Anna: What are the main drivers of trust for you in a human coach?
Lisa: Competence. Like, do they understand my context, corporate, consulting pace, German market. Empathy, but not pity. Confidentiality, explicitly stated and felt. Structure, like a process. And feeling seen, like she remembers details, she catches patterns.
Anna: And what are your indicators that coaching is effective?
Lisa: Clarity, decisions, action. Like, I decide, okay, I will target PMM roles for two months and build a portfolio, then reassess. Or I decide to have a conversation with my manager. Also external indicators, like callbacks, interviews, response rates. And internal indicators, like less anxiety, more confidence, better narrative. And even at work, like I take more ownership, I speak in a different way, and stakeholders respond.
Anna: Can you think of a moment when trust was present but effectiveness was low, besides the early values session you mentioned?
Lisa: Yes. There was a session recently where I was very tired. BCG had a big push, and I was burned out. I trusted her, but I could not implement anything. So the session felt like, okay, we talked, but nothing moved. And then she noticed and said, maybe the effective thing now is rest. So the definition of effectiveness changed. But in the moment, I was like, not effective.
Anna: That is interesting, effectiveness depends on capacity.
Lisa: Exactly.
Anna: And can you think of a moment where something was effective but you did not fully trust it?
Lisa: That is mostly AI. Like, AI gave me a very good structure for a portfolio outline. It was effective because it saved time and it looked professional. But I did not fully trust it because I was like, is this actually the right thing for German PM interviews? Or is it generic i dont know silicon valley stuff. So I used it, but I needed validation from my coach.
Anna: So you can separate usefulness from trust?
Lisa: Yes. Usefulness can be high even when trust is not high. It is like, I can take the output as a draft, but I do not let it decide for me.
Anna: In your experience, does trust always lead to effectiveness?
Lisa: Not always. It increases the chance. Like, if I trust my coach, I will do the work and I will share more. But effectiveness also depends on timing and method. Like if I am burned out, even the best coach will not create miracles.
Anna: And can something be effective without trust, or with low trust?
Lisa: Yes, for small tasks. Like, if a person gives me a good template, I can use it. But for big decisions, I need trust. Otherwise I resist. I either do not follow through or I keep it superficial.
Anna: How do you notice in yourself that trust is missing?
Lisa: I become performative. Like I speak in polished phrases. Very BCG. ] I present a story instead of the truth. And I do not mention the parts that feel messy. That is when I know trust is not there.
Anna: aha and in human coaching, what did your coach do that made you not feel you had to be performative?
Lisa: She normalizes. She says, it is okay to not know. And she also uses silence. Like, if I say something and then I laugh, she waits and says, what is behind the laugh. That is uncomfortable but it makes it real. And also she shares structure. Like, okay, today we do audit, then we do narrative, then we do next steps. So I feel held.
Anna: Does confidentiality come up explicitly in your coaching relationship?
Lisa: Yes. In the first session she explained how she handles notes, what she stores, that she does not share anything. And then later, when I brought up sensitive internal stuff, she repeated it in a natural way. Not like legal text, but like, this is your safe space, I do not work with BCG, nothing leaves here. That matters.
Anna: Is there anything that could break trust quickly for you?
Lisa: Hm i guess if she would judge me. Like, if she says, why are you complaining, or you should just be grateful. That would break it. Also if she would push me into a decision that benefits her, like keeping me as a client, or if she would promise unrealistic outcomes. And if she would share even a small detail that makes me feel exposed. But that has not happened.
Anna: When you think of AI tools, do you have trust drivers there too, or is it different?
Lisa: It is different. AI trust drivers are more like, does it give consistent output, does it respect constraints, does it avoid inventing. And also, does it keep my data safe, but I cannot really verify that. So I rely on my own boundary.
Anna: So you manage trust by controlling what you input?
Lisa: Exactly. Like i do not paste internal numbers. I do not paste names. I keep it general. And then I accept that the output is general.
Anna: That if you had to describe the trust effectiveness link in one sentence for your situation, how would you say it?
Lisa: Trust allows me to be honest, and honesty makes the coaching effective. For AI, usefulness is separate from trust, because it can be useful without being relationally trustworthy.
Anna: alright. Let us move to comparing human and AI directly via tasks.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction
Anna: Imagine you have three tasks this week like one doing a project audit of your last two weeks, two, rewriting a LinkedIn section to be more product oriented, and three, preparing for a tough career conversation with your manager. For each task, which support would you choose and why?
Lisa: Okay. Project audit, I would start with AI. Because I can dump everything and get structure fast. Then I bring it to my coach for refinement.
Anna: Why not directly with the coach?
Lisa: Because coach time is expensive and limited. And also, AI can do the messy first pass without me feeling judged. Then I come to the coach with something more organized.
Anna: And rewriting LinkedIn?
Lisa: Both. AI for options, like three versions, different tones. But the coach for strategy, like what positioning is credible for me in Germany and within BCG context. Also she catches when it sounds too inflated.
Anna: And the career conversation with your manager?
Lisa: Only human. I would not use AI for that beyond maybe drafting the email follow up. But the core conversation prep needs a human. Because it is emotional, political, and I need to anticipate reactions. AI can give generic scripts, but it cannot read the relationship dynamics.
Anna: What makes you say relationship dynamics is crucial?
Lisa: Because my manager is supportive but also under pressure. If I push too hard, she might shut down. If I am too vague, nothing changes. My coach helps me find the right balance. She asks, what does your manager care about, what can you offer, what is a small ask. That is nuanced.
Anna: If you think about hybrid models, some providers imagine AI as a coach that chats with you daily, and then a human coach occasionally. How do you react to that?
Lisa: My first reaction is, hmm, no. Daily AI coaching feels like noise. I would use it like a journal, but I would not call it coaching. Coaching for me is depth, not frequency.
Anna: What would be your ideal hybrid setup?
Lisa: AI as a preparation and follow up assistant, like collecting my data, making a summary, tracking my experiment metrics, reminding me of action steps. Then human sessions every two weeks for deep work, decisions, and accountability. That is ideal.
Anna: What would you want AI to do specifically between sessions?
Lisa: It could help me do a weekly audit. Like, what did you do, what are outcomes, what are blockers, what is next. And it could generate drafts: networking messages, CV bullet variants, interview question lists. Also maybe a small tracker: which roles I applied to, response rates, and patterns. Like, if PM roles at certain companies respond more, that is data.
Anna: And what do you not want AI to do?
Lisa: Interpret my emotions. Or give me therapy style responses. And I do not want it to pretend it understands my life. I want clear boundaries. Also I do not want it to store sensitive data. If it has memory, I would be cautious.
Anna: If we take a step further: imagine a system where AI suggests a career decision, like you should choose internal transfer over external. Would you accept that?
Lisa: No, I would not accept it as decision. I might accept it as a set of options. Like, here are pros and cons. But the decision has to be mine, and ideally discussed with a human, because it involves values and risk.
Anna: What kind of risk?
Lisa: Financial, because coaching and job search take time. Also identity risk. Like leaving BCG brand. And in Germany, job changes are sometimes judged. Not always, but there is still a cultural thing of stability. Also practical things like location. Berlin is exciting but chaotic, Munich is stable but maybe less startup. So it is not a pure optimization problem.
Anna: That is interesting. If you imagine AI tools becoming much better at personalization and context, would that change your preference?
Lisa: It would change it partially. If AI could truly understand my context without me sharing sensitive data, and if it could challenge me like a good coach, then maybe. But I still think human presence matters. Like, when I am stuck, I need a person to look at me and say, I see you, and I believe you can do it, and also, you are avoiding the real issue. AI can imitate that, but it will not feel real to me. So I think I will always want some human element.
Anna: What if the human element was a group format, like group coaching, and AI handles individual prep?
Lisa: That could work. Group coaching can be cheaper. And hearing other people also reduces shame. Like, you realize everyone is struggling. And AI could handle the repetitive parts. But I would still want at least some one to one time for my specific situation, because internal BCG politics are very specific.
Anna: Thank you. That covers hybrid preferences nicely. Let us go to the last blocks. We already discussed trust and effectiveness as separate, but I want to ask you to reflect on the link more directly for AI versus human, and then we close.
9) Closing 
Anna: As we wrap up, if you think about your own journey from marketing toward product, what do you think has changed most due to human coaching, and what has changed due to AI tools?
Lisa: Human coaching changed my identity narrative. Like, I stopped seeing myself as only marketing. I started seeing product thinking inside what I do. It also changed my behavior. I take more ownership, I speak differently. And it reduced my anxiety. AI tools changed my execution speed. Like i can create drafts faster, I can structure my thoughts, I can get unstuck on small tasks.
Anna: If you had to give one advice to someone similar to you, early career in Germany, wanting to switch into product, what would you say about trust and effectiveness in the support they choose?
Lisa: I would say, choose support that you trust enough to be honest. Because if you hide, you will not get the help you need. And effectiveness is not just motivation, it is concrete actions and feedback loops. If a coach or tool does not lead to actions, you will stay stuck. 
Anna: Anything you would like to add that we did not cover?
Lisa: No
Anna: Thank you, Lisa. I will stop the recording now.

2) Participant context and career situation

I started it because I thought I wanted research, like the classic path, you know, Bachelor, then Master, then maybe PhD, maybe a research institute. That was like the plan in my head. But then I started and I was like, no. Like, no wait, let me rephrase. Not that the topic is bad, but the environment and the daily life of research, it felt so slow and kind of isolated.


Anna: Aha, i understand. When you say slow and isolated, what do you mean by that?


Laura: Like, weeks of reading papers, meetings where everyone speaks in very careful language, a lot of, um, how to say ehm i dont remember the word nevermind. I realized I get energy from shipping something, from seeing impact, from being in a team that builds and moves. Research can have impact, obviously, but it felt like I was always preparing for impact.


Anna: So what is the decision you are facing now?


Laura: The main decision is basically do I finish the Master as it is, or do I kind of reframe it to support the pivot. I am attracted to startups, but I also see the risk, like stability, visa stuff does not apply to me because I have German citizenship, but still, rent in Munich is not funny. And also language expectations. Like my German is fluent, but in tech jobs a lot is English, and then some companies still want German for internal stuff. It is like, okay, where do I fit best.


Anna: If you think about the last 6 to 12 months, what was the most significant moment or turning point in your thinking?


Laura: There was one moment, yes. I was doing a research project, like a lab project, at a chair that works with simulations. It was not even a bad chair, the people were fine. But we had this meeting, and I presented some results, and the professor was like, yes, interesting, but now you need to validate it with more literature, and then maybe in three months we can think about a workshop paper. And I remember walking out and feeling physically heavy. I was like, is this my life. Another three months for a workshop paper that maybe five people read. And then, in the same week, I went to an event at Unternehmer TUM, and it was so different. People spoke about actual products, customers, costs, scaling. And I was like, oh. This is where my brain feels something good.


Anna: Tell me about that UnternehmerTUM event. What kind of event was it, and what did you take away?


Laura: It was at the Unternehmertum building, like this startup hub vibe. I think it was a talk plus networking. The topic was something like platform strategy or infrastructure, maybe not exactly those words, but it was about building reliable systems for growth. There was someone from a Munich startup, not like a huge сompany, more like Series A or something, and they explained how they migrated from a messy setup to a more structured platform approach. They talked about things like monitoring, incident response, and how that affects customer trust. And I was like, wow, this is engineering that sits directly next to business outcomes. Like, if you do it well, the company can move faster, and customers are happy, and you avoid downtime.


Anna: Did something specific stick in your mind from that talk?


Laura: Yes. They said something like, platform engineering is not about tools, it is about enabling teams. Like internal customers. And they described building a self service platform where dev teams can deploy without waiting for permission. And I was like, that is basically reducing friction, like a product, but interna. And I think that connected to me because I like systems. Aerospace is also systems, but in aerospace it is often slow with heavy compliance, long cycles. Here it is faster.


Anna: So after that, what did you do? Did you take concrete steps?


Laura: I did. i started exploring what roles exist, like cloud engineer, DevOps, SRE, platform engineer. I realized the titles are messy. And also  started learning more practically. I did a basic cloud certification prep, like AWS Cloud Practitioner first, and then I started looking at Solutions Architect Associate, but that one is heavy. And then I also tried some labs with Terraform and Docker. Not super advanced, but enough to see if I like it.


Anna: And where are you now with the Master? You said you are in the middle of a pivot.


Laura: I am still enrolled. I have finished most core modules. I chose some electives that are more software or systems oriented. I also took one entrepreneurship or management course, because I want to be closer to business. For the thesis, I am trying to find a topic that is not purely aerospace research, maybe something like cloud infrastructure for simulation pipelines, or reliability in engineering tools, something bridging.I am also applying for internships in startups, but it is competitive.


Anna: What makes it difficult right now for you?


Laura: Hm, uncertainty? Identity issues too. Like, my CV says aerospace. People assume I want aerospace. And then I say, no, I want platform engineering, and they are like, why. And sometimes I feel like I am betraying my past self, you know. But also I think it is normal to change. Also the job descriptions are crazy. Entry level but they want three years of Kubernetes, and then also German, and then also on call experience. I am like, okay, sure hahaha.


Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed during this period?


Laura: I dont know 


Anna: Like what did you want someone experienced to tell you?


Laura: Ah, okay, like maybe what is the path, what are the steps, how do I present the story. Also accountability maybe. But I hate the idea of someone telling me who I am. I need support but also that does not mean i am lost. 


Anna: You mean you want to be in charge? 


Laura: Yes. 


Anna: Understood. Let us move to career support in general.


3) Career support in general


Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?


Laura: To myself, like journaling. Can use AI, like ChatGPT, for structured reflection. Then sometimes I talk to friends who work in tech. I also talk to my boyfriend, but he is not in tech, so it is more emotional support. And I sometimes ask professors for practical things, but not career coaching, more like, can you recommend a course, can you connect me to someone. And also I go to events, like meetups, UnternehmerTUM, sometimes TUM career events, to just hear what exists.


Anna: When you talk to friends, what do you ask them?


Laura: Very concrete questions. Like, what do you actually do as a platform engineer. What skills do you use daily. How did you get your first job. How do interviews look. And I trust that more because it is grounded. Not like, follow your passion, and the universe will deliver. That kind of stuff makes me angry hahaha. 


Anna: What has actually helped, and what felt unhelpful or frustrating?


Laura: Helpful is when someone gives me a mental model or a concrete step. Like, do one certification, build one small project, write about it, apply to these roles, tailor CV like this. Also helpful is when someone says, this is hard, but normal. Unhelpful is vague advice or commodified empathy. Like, people who sell feelings. Also, anything that feels like influencer culture, like, buy my product, I will tell you your purpose. That is, um, no.


Anna: You mentioned professors. Have you had any mentoring type relationship?


Laura: In a way, yes. I had one lecturer who was very honest about industry. He worked in Airbus before academia. When I said I am thinking about leaving research, he did not judge, he said, okay, then you need to optimize for learning speed, go where you can do stuff. That felt like mentoring. But it was not a paid coach situation. 


Anna: If you had to list what you need most from any form of support, what are the top two or three things?


Laura: Structure, realism, and maybe a mirror. Like reflecting my own thoughts back so I can see them.


Anna: A mirror? interesting. We will come back to that. Now I want to focus specifically on human career coaching, even if you have not used it.
4) Human coaching experience and trust 


Anna: Have you ever used human career coaching consultations, for example university career services or private coaching?


Laura: No. And I do not want to. I know that sounds harsh. But I have a strong negative view.


Anna: That is okay. Can you tell me what your expectations are, and why you feel negative about it?


Laura: Eehm. I think coaching as an industry is full of nonsense. Like, people who have no real skills, but they learned how to talk, and they package common sense as a product. And in Germany especially, there is this thing where people use titles and certifications to sound serious. But then they tell you very generic things. And they charge like 150 euros per hour for talking. And I have seen friends being manipulated. Like, someone tells them, your fear is blocking you, invest in yourself, and then they buy more sessions. It feels like a scam ecosystem.


Anna: When you say manipulated, what happened to your friends?


Laura: One friend from my Bachelor, she was very anxious about jobs. She went to a coach she found on Better Go or something like that . The coach told her that she has limiting beliefs and she needs to commit to a package of three sessions, otherwise she will not change. And my friend paid, and after three sessions she felt worse, because she felt like she is failing the coaching too. And the advice was like, visualize your future, write affirmations. That is not what she needed. She needed interview prep and a plan. So I watched that and I was like, nope.


Anna: That sounds like a specific style of coaching. Do you make a difference between that and, for example, a coach provided by a university career center? Like a sound career coach, not just a coach?


Laura: Hmm. Maybe. I know TUM has career services, and those people are probably more grounded. I still have skepticism, but I can imagine they might help with CV formatting or general guidance. But I think the private coaching market ruined the concept for me. Also, I do not like the power dynamic. Like, I sit there and someone pretends to know my life better than me.


Anna: What do you think a human career coach would do in a session, if you imagine it?


Laura: They would ask me about my values, my strengths, maybe some tests. Then they would tell me I can do anything.


Anna: Jokes aside there can be like this sometimes yes. 


Laura: Yes. And then maybe they would help with LinkedIn. But I think for complex situations, they do not have the context. Like, they do not know the technical landscape, they do not know what a platform engineer actually does. Unless they are specialized. 


Anna: What would make you trust a human career coach, hypothetically? If you think of trust drivers.


Laura: Competence, obviously. Like proven experience in the domain. Not just talking. Also honesty about limits. If someone says, I do not know, that increases trust. Also not pushing packages. And maybe references. Like if someone I respect recommends them. But in general, my baseline is low.


Anna: What about affective aspects, like empathy, feeling understood?


Laura: I do not want empathy from a stranger that I pay. It feels fake. Like, I know they are being nice because it is their job. Maybe that is unfair, but that is how it feels. I prefer empathy from friends. For career decisions, I prefer competence and a plan.


Anna: You said earlier you need a mirror. Some people see a coach as a mirror. What makes that different for you?


Laura: Because the coach mirror comes with interpretation. And I do not trust their interpretation. If I want a mirror, I want something that reflects my words and helps me structure, but does not impose a narrative. That is actually why I like AI, because it is basically me and my answers and model of thinking. 


Anna: You said baseline trust is low. Can you give a number? I will ask you a few quick ratings later, but we can start here. On a scale from 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all and 10 is complete trust, what is your trust in human career coaches, in general?


Laura: For private career coaches, maybe 1. For something like university career services, maybe 3. But if you ask one number, overall, I would say 2.


Anna: And why 2, not 0?


Laura: Because I know there are probably some good people. And also sometimes even generic advice can trigger something. Like, you hear something obvious and you finally do it. So not zero. But yeah, very low.


Anna: aha cool thank you. We will revisit effectiveness separately. For now, I want to move to AI tools, where you said you have real experience.


5)  AI tools experience and trust


Anna: Which AI based tools have you used for career related questions, and what was your experience?


Laura: Mostly ChatGPT. Also sometimes Perplexity for searching, but the main is ChatGPT because it is conversational. I use it like a thinking partner. Like self coaching. I open it and I basically talk to it like I talk to myself, but it responds with structure.


Anna: Can you walk me through a concrete situation where you used it?


Laura: Yes. When I realized I might not want research, I was confused. I felt guilty, like I wasted time. So I wrote a long message describing my situation, what I liked, what I hated, what I feared. And I asked it to help me map options. Not like telling me what to do, but like making a decision framework. It responded with categories, like values, constraints, experiments. It suggested doing small experiments, like attending events, doing a small cloud project, talking to people. And that actually helped me because it made it actionable instead of emotional chaos.


Anna: What does trust mean for you when it comes to ChatGPT?


Laura: Trust is different. I do not trust it as truth. I trust it as a tool for reflection. So I trust that it will give me a structured response and not judge me. And I trust that it will be available and consistent. I do not trust that it is always accurate about facts. And I do not trust it with personal data, like very sensitive details. I keep some boundaries.


Anna: What boundaries for example?


Laura: I do not tell it names of companies I am applying to, or personal details about family, or things that could identify me. I keep it more abstract. And if I paste something like my CV, I remove contact data. And I do not tell it like private conflicts. Maybe some, but not too deep.


Anna: You said you use it for self coaching. How exactly do you do that? Is it like daily, weekly?


Laura: It depends. In intense phases, I use it almost daily. Like when I am applying for jobs, I use it to rewrite bullet points, to prepare for interviews, to draft messages, to simulate questions. In calmer phases, maybe once a week, like check in.


Anna: Can you give an example of a prompt, without quoting it directly, just describing what you asked?


Laura: Sure. I asked something like: here is my background in aerospace, here is why I want platform engineering, help me craft a narrative that does not sound random, and give me three versions, one for a startup recruiter, one for a corporate, and one for networking. And also highlight gaps and how to address them. That kind of thing.


Anna: And what did it produce that was useful?


Laura: It gave me a structure: past, pivot trigger, present actions, future goal. It also suggested specific phrases to connect aerospace systems thinking with platform reliability. That was helpful. It also listed gaps like lack of production experience, and suggested ways to compensate, like personal projects, open source contributions, internships.


Anna: What do you see as the main reasons you trust it more than a human coach?


Laura:  It is not trying to sell me anything.I am already paying for premium though hahaha. At least it feels like that. It  is consistent and fast also it does not judge. And what i like specifically is that it can adapt to my style. Like, if I say, be more direct, it does it. A human coach might be like, hmm, let us explore your childhood. No thanks.


Anna: And what makes you distrust it, or trust it conditionally?


Laura: It can write complete cringe. Like, it can confidently say wrong things. That is a problem. Also it can be basic. Sometimes it gives advice that is like the same that the selling coach gives like, network more, learn skills, be confident. And I am like, okay, thanks. Also privacy. And also it sometimes assumes things about me.


Anna: Can you describe a specific moment where it failed in a way that mattered?


Laura: Yes. I asked about a specific certification path and job requirements in Germany. It recommended a certain certification that is more US focused, and it also said some companies require it, but when I checked, it was not true. It sounded plausible, but it was not accurate for startups in DACH. And also once I asked about salary ranges, and it gave numbers that felt inflated i think. 


Anna: Did that reduce your trust?


Laura: It reduced trust overall but i already knew it is not to be trusted. So yes but no in the reflection function. Like, I still trust it to help me think, but I do not trust it to be a source of truth.


Anna: If we do a quick rating: on 0 to 10, what is your trust in AI tools like ChatGPT for your career related needs?


Laura: Hmm. I would say 7.


Anna: Why 7, not 9?


Laura: Because of hallucinations and privacy. And because it lacks real world accountability. Like, it can say anything, and nothing happens to it. So I keep it at 7.


Anna: Why 7, not 4?


Laura: Because it is actually useful and consistent. It has helped me a lot. So it earned trust through experience.


Anna: Great. Now I want to compare human and AI via tasks, because your thesis topic is about differences across tasks.


6) Human vs AI comparison via tasks


Anna: If you think about specific career tasks, which ones would you prefer AI for, and which ones would you prefer a human for, and why?


Laura: AI for brainstorming options, structuring plans, rewriting CV bullet points, cover letters, preparing interview answers, explaining concepts, giving learning roadmaps. Also for practicing, like mock interview questions. Human for specific mentoring, like someone who actually works as a platform engineer telling me what matters. Also maybe emotional support, but not from a coach, more from friends. And maybe for negotiation or reading a room?


Anna: Let us pick one task. Clarifying options. If you were deciding between staying in research and going to business, how would AI and humans differ?


Laura: AI helped me create a matrix. Like, criteria and scoring. Humans often give opinions. Like, do what makes you happy. Or they project their own fears. AI felt more neutral. But it can miss context, like cultural stuff, family expectations.


Anna: Family expectations, can you tell me a bit about that?


Laura: My parents are Spanish, but they live here. They are hardworking, and they value stable careers. Aerospace sounds prestigious to them. Research sounds prestigious too. Startup cloud engineer sounds like, what is that. So there is this pressure to not waste the degree. They are not controlling, but they worry. And also in Spanish culture there is a bit of, you have difficulties, you do not jump around too much. So part of me is like, I should just finish and go to Airbus. And part of me is like, no, I want something else. AI cannot fully understand that emotional layer, but it can help me articulate it.


Anna: And decision making? Do you think AI can support decisions effectively?


Laura: It can support the process but I don't think it can decide. But it can break down the decision. It can suggest experiments. A human can also do that, but again, depends on the person.


Anna: Can you give an example where AI gave you a plan you actually followed?


Laura: Hm. Yes. I asked for a 12 week learning plan for some role, assuming I have 10 hours per week, and I want to be ready for internships. It gave me weeks, topics, small projects. I followed it partly. Like not perfectly, but it made me start. Without that, I would just be overwhelmed and would look for info in websites? I dont know even how people did that before chat GPT


Anna: What about CV and applications?


Laura: AI is great there. Like, you paste a bullet point, it makes it stronger. But you need to check truthfulness, because it sometimes exaggerates. It can add things like led cross functional team, and I am like, no. So you need to control it.


Anna: You said human mentoring could be valuable. That is interesting given your strong rejection of coaching. Can you clarify the difference for you?


Laura: Yes. Mentoring is anchored in real experience. Like, a senior engineer telling me what they do. Coaching feels like someone selling a method. Mentoring is not trying to transform your soul. It is like, here is reality. And I respect that.


Anna: If you imagine a human career coach who is actually specialized, like someone who used to be a platform engineer and now coaches people into that field, would that change anything?


Laura: Maybe. It would increase competence trust. But I still dislike the vibe of coaching. I would rather just talk to an engineer. But, okay, I can imagine for some people it helps to have a structured human guiding them, especially if they lack self discipline.


Anna: So there is a thing. For you, if i understood correctly, the label coaching is the problem, and the market practices? 


Laura: I guess yes. 


Anna: Now I want to ask explicitly about trust and effectiveness as separate concepts. Sometimes people say, I trust something but it does not help, or I do not trust it but it still helps. Do you have examples of that?


7) Trust-effectiveness link


Laura: Yes. I think low trust but usefulI once attended a career talk, not at TUM, like a general career seminar. I did not trust the speaker because they were very salesy. But they shared one framework for telling your story in interviews, like situation, task, action, result. That was useful. Even though I disliked them, I took the framework and used it. So effectiveness can exist without trust. 


Anna: Aha. Thank you. And high trust but limited effectiveness?


Laura: Probably also yes with AI  i trust ChatGPT to brainstorm and not judge me. But it cannot fully capture emotions. Like, sometimes I ask, why do I feel stuck, and it gives a reasonable answer, but it does not feel like being seen by a human. It is like cognitive empathy, not real. So I trust it to be supportive, but it does not always change how I feel. It helps me think, but not always feel better.


Anna: So in your mind, trust and effectiveness are linked but not identical.


Laura: Seems to me like so yes. But like  if I do not trust something, I might not use it. But sometimes you still use it because it is convenient. Like, I do not trust social media influencers, but sometimes I still watch a clip and get that blush from a tiktok shop you know. And effectiveness is about what came after. Like outcome or end goal was reached or not. 


Anna: If you think about the way trust affects effectiveness for you, what is the mechanism?


Laura: If I trust the source, I invest more attention, I follow through. If I distrust, I keep distance, I might still take small pieces. So trust affects depth of engagement.


Anna: Thank you. Now I want to do the short micro poll ratings, just to anchor the conversation. We already did two, but we will do all four clearly. I will ask, and you give a number and a short explanation.


Laura: Okay.


Anna: On a scale from 0 to 10, your trust in human career coaches.


Laura: 2. Because I associate it with a market of vague advice and sales tactics. I think some are okay, but I start skeptical.


Anna:On a scale from 0 to 10, your trust in AI tools like ChatGPT for career support.


Laura: 7. Because it is consistent and helpful for structure, but it can hallucinate and there are privacy limits.


Anna: Perceived effectiveness of human career coaching for your situation, hypothetical, 0 to 10.


Laura: For me personally, maybe 3. It could help with generic things like CV review, but I do not think it would help with my real pivot decisions. Unless the person is very specialized.


Anna: Why 3, not 6?


Laura: Because I would resist it. I would sit there and internally argue. So even if it is good, it would not work on me. Also cost. I would feel annoyed paying for it.


Anna: Fourth: Perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, based on your experience, 0 to 10.


Laura: 8.


Anna: Why 8, not 10?


Laura: Because of factual unreliability and because it cannot replace real world mentorship and emotional depth. But it is very effective for planning, preparation, and clarity.






Anna: You mentioned earlier that you believe everyone should find their own path through difficulty? Can you explain that belief a bit?


Laura: Eehm, yes. I think struggle is part of building a real identity. If someone tells you what to do, you borrow their certainty. But it is not yours. And then when it gets hard, you collapse. Whereas if you fight through confusion, you build internal trust. That sounds dramatic. But I feel it. Like, I moved countries as a kid, I learned languages, I had to adapt. That taught me that discomfort is normal. So when I see coaching that promises easy clarity, I distrust it. Life is messy.


Anna: Wow that is cool.  Is there any scenario where you would accept paid human support?


Laura: If it is technical mentorship, like paying for a session with a senior cloud engineer to review my project or my approach. That is basically consulting. I would pay for expertise.


Anna: So it is not the money itself, it is what you pay for.


Laura: Exactly.


8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction


Anna: Imagine a hybrid model that combines AI for structured reflection and preparation, and then human experts for targeted sessions, for example to validate your plan, give industry context, or help with a key decision. How do you react to that idea?


Laura: That sounds more reasonable. Because AI can do the repetitive stuff, and human time is used for high value expertise. But I would want the human to be an insider, like actually working in the field, not a generic coach.


Anna: What would make you trust such a hybrid service?


Laura: Transparency about who the human is. Like their real job, background also clear boundaries what AI does, what human does. And no emotional manipulation. Like, do not tell me, invest in yourself or you are blocked.Also pricing that feels fair. Like pay per session, not a package trap.


Anna: What would make you not use it?


Laura: If it feels like coaching culture. Or if the AI part collects too much data. Or if the matching feels random. Like, if you match me to someone who is not relevant, I will lose trust immediately.


Anna: Would you want AI to be involved during the human session, like summarizing, creating action steps?


Laura: That could be helpful. Like after the session, AI could turn notes into a plan. But I would want control. And I would want to know what data is stored.


Anna: If you think about tasks, which tasks would you assign to AI and which to the human in this hybrid?


Laura: pre work, self reflection, draft CV, draft interview answers, learning plan, tracking applications to ai probably. To human: reality check, feedback on my narrative, technical roadmap validation, maybe mock interview with real feedback, and advice about market, like which startups or companies are good.


Anna: In such a model, where would trust matter most?


Laura: Trust matters most with the human, because they can influence my decisions and they are a real person with incentives. AI is more like a tool. I need trust in the system to not harm me, but it is different.


Anna: And effectiveness?


Laura: Effectiveness depends on whether it produces action and outcomes. Like, do I get interviews, do I feel clearer, do I build skills. AI already helps with that. Human could increase it if they are truly relevant.


Anna: If you had to design the perfect support experience for yourself, what would it look like in one paragraph?


Laura: I would have a space to think, like AI conversation, where I can dump thoughts and get structure. Then maybe once a month a session with a real platform engineer who reviews my progress and gives me realistic feedback. And maybe a community or meetup for networking, but not forced. Like optional.


Anna: Thank you. Before we close, I want to check if there is anything we missed about your experience with AI that feels important, especially around trust.


Laura: No 


Anna: Thank you. Great. Laura this is  the end of the interview. After this, I will stop the recording. If you have any questions later, you can message me in insta or whatsapp i give you

2)  Participant context and career situation


Anna: Where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing, or recently faced?


Oleg: So I am 25, I live in Germany now, for four years. I finished my Master two years ago, here in Germany. I work as a data analyst in a big transportation company. Like, buses, trains, logistics, mobility, that kind of thing. And I like data analytics a lot. I like when you take messy data and make something useful. But eehm, recently, I feel a bit lost about the next step.


Anna: When you say lost, what do you mean exactly?


Oleg: Like, I know I am good as an individual contributor. I can do all IT stuff, dashboards, stakeholder requests. But I want to develop towards management. I want to lead a team eventually, maybe manage an analytics unit. And I do not know i see some people become team leads, but it feels like договорняк как бы. Also there is German corporate culture, and you know.


Anna: Yes i know. What makes it feel like that договорняк you mentioned?


Oleg: There are like official career steps, job levels, all these frameworks. But in reality, you need sponsors, you need someone to say, okay, Oleg can lead. And I don’t know what exactly they look for. Sometimes I think, maybe I should switch to product analytics somewhere else, or maybe consulting, but I do not know. So I feel stuck between wanting leadership and not knowing how to move.


Anna: What is your current role like day to day?


Oleg: I am in a corporate analytics unit, like central reporting. We support different business areas, operations, punctuality, customer experience, finance controlling. So a typical week, I have stakeholder meetings, they want some KPI, some report for a board update, or for a performance review cycle. I work with data pipelines, but we also have data engineers. I mostly do analysis and dashboards. We use Power BI mainly, sometimes Tableau in some legacy teams, and I do Python for deep dives. And SQL daily, yes. A lot of SQL.


Anna: And what does leadership mean to you in this environment?


Oleg: Um, it is not only having people reporting to you. It is like owning a domain. Saying no to stakeholders. Helping juniors. Translating business questions into analytics. And also, eehm, protecting the team from chaos? Because in big company, everyone wants something yesterday.


Anna: What is the most significant career decision you have made in the last 6 to 12 months?


Oleg: Hmm. I think the biggest was whether to apply for an internal team lead position in another department. There was an opening, not in my team, but in a business unit analytics team. A colleague sent me the link and said, you should try. And I was like, wow, okay, but I am young, and also not native German speaker. And it was a leadership role. I was not sure if I am ready.


Anna: What did you do in the end?


Oleg: I did not apply. And I feel mixed about it. On one hand, maybe it was not the right role. It was very stakeholder heavy, and the team was under pressure. On the other hand, I think I blocked myself. Like страх, fear. And then I started to think, okay, how do I prepare for leadership if I always wait until I feel 100 percent ready.


Anna: What made it difficult to decide?


Oleg: Everything? Fear again.  Like, I did not know how they evaluate candidates. Also I didn’t know if my current manager would support me, because it would mean leaving. And I was afraid, if I apply and fail, it will look bad. Eehm, also I had a permanent contract recently, so I felt, okay, stability, but then taking a risk. I can't go back to Russia hahaha. 


Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed at that time?


Oleg: I needed someone to help me see the realistic plan. Like, what are the steps not just motivation. I needed plan and feedback whether i have that material no whether i am a  leadership material, what is missing. And also maybe emotional support, because I overthink a lot.


Anna: Okay. Thank you. Let us talk more generally now about career support.


3) Career support in general


Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?


Oleg: Usually people. I talk to friends, some are also in corporate. I also talk to my mentor sometimes. And sometimes I talk to my manager, but it depends, because manager is not always neutral. And I also use online resources, like articles sometimes forums but eehm, I filter a lot.

Oleg: Practical stuff. Like, someone telling me, in this company, you need to do this to get promoted. Or, talk to this person. Or, write your internal profile like this. Also, mock interviews helped. Also, sometimes just talking and hearing myself. Like, when you speak, you realize what you want.


Anna: And what feels unhelpful or frustrating?


Oleg: инфоцыганство hahaha.


Anna: hahaha.


Oleg: A Like, follow your dreams be confident. I mean its neat but doesn’t help. 


Anna: Yes. Do you use any tools, like structured exercises, career tests, anything like that?


Oleg: I tried once some career test  like personality, but it felt very hmm поверхностно. It gave me results like analytical, introvert, whatever, and I was like, yes, I know. So no. I prefer conversations.


Anna: You mentioned a mentor. Can you tell me how that started?


Oleg: Yes. So, I came to Germany for Master. I studied Data Science did internships, but I was not sure how to enter a big company. A friend from Russian community introduced me to a guy, older, like 35 maybe, who worked in the company where I am now. He was in analytics, but more senior. We had a call, like an informational interview. And he was very open, explained the hiring process, what they look for. And then later, when there was an opening, he said, send me your CV, I can refer you. So that became mentoring, I guess.


Anna: How often did you meet?


Oleg: At the beginning, maybe once per month, then less. Sometimes every two or three months. It was not formal coaching, more like mentoring and career consultation.


Anna: And you experienced it overall as positive, you said.


Oleg: Yes, overall positive. Without him, I think I would not get this job. Or it would be much harder. So I value it a lot.


Anna: Okay. Lets go deeper into that experience, especially around trust.


4) Human coaching experience and trust 


Anna: Thinking about your experience with mentoring or career consultations, can you describe one specific moment that increased your trust in that mentor?


Oleg: Hmm. There was a moment early, when I asked him about salary expectations. I was afraid to ask because it is a sensitive topic. And he said, okay, listen, this is confidential. He literally said, whatever you say, stays between us. And then he opened the internal salary range, like not exact numbers, but he explained what is realistic for a junior analyst, what is too low, what is too high. And he also said, do not undersell yourself just because you are new in Germany. That made a hell lot of trust for him. Because he showed he understands the context and he is on my side.


Anna: What did he do that made it feel confidential, beyond just saying it?


Oleg: He did not share details with other people. Like, later I met someone from his team, and they did not know my situation. He would say, do not mention my name when you talk to HR, just say you heard it from internal sources. He protected himself and me. That felt professional.


Anna: So confidentiality and professionalism.


Oleg: Yes. And also competence. He knew the process. Like, he said, you will have HR screening, then technical interview, then business case, then final with hiring manager. He described what questions they ask. And it was super accurate.


Anna: Can you share an example where mentoring helped you concretely?


Oleg: Yes. So, first, he reviewed my CV. He changed it a lot. He said, German corporate CV is different. You need structure, bullet points, not too creative. He also said, quantify impact. So we added numbers from my internship. Like, reduced processing time, improved report accuracy, whatever. Then he helped with interview prep. We did a mock interview on Zoom. He asked me typical questions, and he stopped me when I was too long. He said, be concise, answer with this and that method. And then he referred me internally. He submitted my CV in the internal system. That gave me interview faster, because in big company, HR gets thousands.


Anna: That is a very nice mentoring i say.


Oleg: Yes.


Anna: When you think about trust in that relationship, what were the main drivers for you?


Oleg: Competence is number one. Like, do they know what they talk about. Then shared context. He worked in the same company, so it is not abstract. Then honesty about limits. Sometimes I asked, should I negotiate hard, and he said, I do not know your hiring manager, I cannot promise, but generally this works. So he was not pretending. And also feeling seen, like he remembered my background, that I moved from Russia, my Master, my anxiety. He would ask, how are you doing with German, are you okay in the team. That creates trust.


Anna: Did you ever experience something like trusted but not effective, with a human coach or mentor?


Oleg: Yes, actually. I had one session with a university career counselor during Master. She was very kind. Like, очень добрая, very warm. I trusted her in the sense she was not harmful. But it was not effective. She did not understand data roles in German market. She gave me generic advice like apply more, tailor your cover letter, go to job fairs. It did not move me forward. So I trusted her intentions, but it did not lead to progress.


Anna: Aha. You trusted her as a person, but the outcome was limited.


Oleg: Aha.


Anna: Did you ever have the opposite, where something was effective but you did not fully trust the person?


Oleg: Hmm. With humans, less. Maybe with a senior colleague once. He gave me a very good tip for stakeholder management. Like, always confirm requirements in writing, and document assumptions. It was effective. But I did not fully trust him personally, because he is political you know. He is nice, but he has certain you know


Anna: Yes i know aha


Oleg: So I took the advice, but I did not share my real doubts. So effective advice, but low trust on deeper level.


Anna: So you can act on something even without deep trust, if it is useful and low risk.


Oleg: Yes, if it is low risk and verifiable. But for personal questions, I need more trust.


Anna: What makes you distrust a human coach or mentor?


Oleg: If they are too confident without evidence. If they push their agenda. Or if they share stories about other clients in a way that feels not confidential. Also if they do not listen and just follow a script. 


Anna: In your current company, do you have any formal career development support?


Oleg: We have HR programs, like trainings, leadership academy, mentoring program. But it is bureaucratic. You apply, you wait. And sometimes it feels like checkbox. Also we have performance review once per year, and mid year check. There is a works council and rules, so some things are structured. But the real development is through manager support and projects.


Anna: How do you experience that system, as someone aiming for leadership?


Oleg: It is okay, but slow. Like German corporates are slow. Decisions take time. And for leadership, they like stability. They want you to prove yourself. Which is fine. But for me, I want clarity and steps.


Anna: Okay. Let us shift to AI now. You said you use AI a lot for work, but not for self reflection.


5) AI tools experience and trust 


Anna: What AI tools do you use at work, and what for?


Oleg: I use ChatGPT sometimes, and also internal company tools. We have an internal AI assistant in the Microsoft environment. It can summarize meetings, draft emails, help with Excel formulas. I use it mainly for productivity. Also for code. Like, I write SQL queries, and I ask it to optimize, or to generate a quick Python snippet for a data transformation. And sometimes I use it to write narrative for dashboards, like executive summary.


Anna: Can you give a concrete example of a moment where AI helped you a lot at work?


Oleg: Yes. There was a project about punctuality KPIs. We had to calculate delays, but the data source had weird logic. Some trains are cancelled, some are partial, some are replaced by buses. The stakeholder wanted a simple KPI for monthly report. I had to join multiple tables, handle edge cases. I wrote SQL, but it was messy. I asked AI to help me structure the query. I described the tables and logic without sensitive identifiers, just generic names. It suggested using CTEs, separate steps, and it reminded me about handling nulls and duplicates. It saved me maybe, I do not know, two hours. And it also explained why. That was very helpful.


Anna: And what made you comfortable using it in that case?


Oleg: Speed and I could verify. I run the query, check results, compare with baseline. So trust is not blind. It is like, assistant.


Anna: Have you had a moment where AI failed or felt risky?


Oleg: Oh yes. Many. One time it hallucinated. I asked for a Python snippet to do time series decomposition. It gave me code with a library that was not installed, and also wrong function name. I copied, it failed. Not dangerous, but annoying. Another time, more risky, I asked something about our internal policy, like, can we use certain customer data for analysis. It gave a confident answer, but it was wrong. And that can be serious. Because compliance, GDPR, Germany is strict. So I learned: do not trust AI for policies. You need official documents.


Anna: So you calibrate trust depending on the domain.


Oleg: Exactly. For code, okay. For compliance, no. Also privacy boundaries. I never paste sensitive data. I never paste customer information. We have rules. Sometimes people do it, but I do not. It feels risky. Also company has guidelines: do not upload confidential data to external AI.


Anna: What are your trust drivers for AI tools, compared to humans?


Oleg: For AI, trust is more like i can verify and stuff. Transparency helps, like if it shows sources, or explains steps and i can see if it makes things up or not i just need to be careful. 


Anna: And with human coaches? Is it the same?


Oleg: Yes, but with AI it is even more important. Because it can sound very confident but be wrong.


Anna: Do you ever use AI for career related tasks, like CV writing, job search, or decision support?


Oleg: I used it a little for CV wording. Like, I wrote bullet points and asked it to make them more concise. But I did not use it for big decisions. For job search, not really.


Anna: Why not?


Oleg: I don’t really know how i mean it feels artificial for self reflection. Like, I do not want to talk about my doubts with a chatbot. Also privacy. Even if they say it is safe, I do not know. And also, I prefer human feedback. When I process thoughts, I need a real person reacting, maybe challenging me. AI can simulate, but it does not feel the same.


Anna: What is missing exactly, for you?


Oleg: Emotional safety. Like, with a person, you have relationship, you see they care, they remember you. With AI, it is like talking to a wall that answers nicely. Also accountability. If a mentor says, do this, and next time asks, did you do it, that pushes. AI can remind, but it is not the same social pressure. And also, I worry it will give generic answers. Like career is messy. AI does not know the internal politics, my manager, my environment.


Anna: Do you think it is mainly about empathy, or about context knowledge?


Oleg: Both. Context and empathy. For work tasks, AI is great because the context is the prompt. For life, the context is huge.


Anna: Okay. Now I want to ask you to compare human and AI in specific career support tasks.


6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks


Anna: If you think about different career tasks, like clarifying options, making decisions, planning strategy, preparing for interviews, or writing a CV, where would you prefer a human, where would you prefer AI, and why?


Oleg: For CV formatting and wording, AI can help a lot. It is fast. Like, rewrite bullet points, adjust tone. But I still check, because sometimes it makes claims too strong, like 200 percent improvement, nonsense. So effective, but not fully trusted. I treat it as draft generator.


Anna: And for interview preparation?


Oleg: Mixed. 


Anna: And for clarifying options, like whether to pursue leadership or switch track?


Oleg: Human. Definitely. 


Anna: What about planning concrete steps towards leadership in your company?


Oleg: Human plus some AI support. Like, human mentor can tell me: talk to these stakeholders, join this project, volunteer to mentor juniors. AI can help me write an email to a senior manager, or prepare a 1 page plan. But the strategy and other stuff, human.


Anna: Can you think of a recent concrete career task where you used AI, and what happened?


Oleg: Yes. Recently I prepared for a performance review. We have annual review, and you need to write self evaluation. I used AI to structure it. I wrote my achievements, and asked it to make it more clear, and align to competencies. It gave me a nice structure. That was helpful. But I did not put sensitive numbers. I wrote general. And still, I did not fully trust it, because it can exaggerate. I edited.


Anna: So in that case, it was effective as a structuring tool.


Oleg: Yes, very effective for structure and wording.

Anna: If you imagine a tool that is explicitly designed as a career coach chatbot, would that change anything?
