Anna: What would make you consider using it for deeper questions?


Oleg: If I could be sure about privacy. If it is certified, maybe. And if it can show why it recommends something. Like evidence. And maybe if it is connected to a human coach, like hybrid. Then I can use AI for preparation and reflection, but human for decision.


Anna: That leads nicely to the trust and effectiveness link. But before that, I want to ask a short micro poll. These are 0 to 10 ratings. It is quick, and then I will ask why.




7) Trust–effectiveness link (micro poll embedded)


Anna: On a scale from 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all and 10 is complete trust, how would you rate your trust in human career coaches or mentors, in general?


Oleg: In general, hmm, maybe 7.


Anna: Why 7, and not higher or lower?


Oleg: Because I think humans can be very helpful, and I trust the right person. But not all coaches are competent. Some are generic. Also humans can have bias. So I do not give 10. But I trust more than AI for personal topics. So 7.


Anna: Same scale, trust in AI tools for career support specifically.


Oleg: For career support, maybe 4.


Anna: What drives that 4?


Oleg: Because AI can be useful, but I do not trust it with high stakes decisions. Also privacy and AI can be wrong. For technical tasks, my trust would be higher, like 7 or 8. But for career, 4.


Anna: Now perceived effectiveness. For your personal situation, how effective is human coaching or mentoring, 0 to 10?


Oleg: I would say 8.


Anna: Why 8?


Oleg: Because my mentor actually helped me get the job, and also helped with internal understanding. It led to concrete actions. It increased my confidence. So it is effective. Not 10 because sometimes even mentor cannot solve the system. Like, internal promotion is still uncertain. And sometimes advice is limited.


Anna: And perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your personal situation, 0 to 10?


Oleg: For my situation, maybe 5.


Anna: Why 5?


Oleg: Because it can help with tasks like writing, structuring, generating options. That is effective. But it does not replace human for leadership decisions. So it is medium.


Anna: Thank you. Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness more directly. When you think about your experience, how does trust affect whether something feels effective?


Oleg: Trust is like, um, the entry point. If I do not trust, I do not use it deeply. Like with AI, even if it gives good advice, I might not act. Or I will double check so much that it loses value. With human, if I trust, I share more real information, like my fears, my doubts. Then the advice is better, more tailored. So trust enables depth, and depth enables effectiveness.


Anna: Can you give a specific example where trust increased effectiveness with your mentor?


Oleg: Yes. When I was preparing salary negotiation, I told him my real number, and my fear that they will reject me because I am foreign, and I needed visa stability. If I did not trust him, I would hide that. But because I shared it, he gave specific advice: focus on value, but also understand my risk tolerance. He said, okay, aim for X, but if they offer Y, it is still okay because you need entry. That was tailored. It was effective because it fit my real constraints.


Anna: And can you think of a case where you trusted but it was not effective, we touched one with the university counselor. Anything else?


Oleg: Another example: I had a friendly senior colleague, like a buddy. He listened, he was supportive, I trusted him emotionally. But when I asked about leadership track, he said, just wait, it will happen. And I was like, okay, but what should I do. He had no concrete steps. So trust, but low effectiveness.


Anna: And effective but not fully trusted, you mentioned AI for CV bullet points. Can you expand on that?


Oleg: Yes. AI can produce good bullet points quickly. That is effective. But I do not trust it fully because it can invent achievements. So I always verify. I treat it like speed, not authority. So it is effective, but trust is partial.


Anna: So effectiveness can exist without full trust if you can verify and the stakes are low.


Oleg: Yes 


Anna: Aha. If we break down trust drivers, for humans you mentioned competence, confidentiality, feeling seen, shared context, honesty about limits. For AI you mentioned reliability, verifiability, transparency, boundaries. Anything else?


Oleg: For AI, also consistency. Like, if it gives stable quality. And maybe integration. If it uses real data sources. For example, if it can cite German labor market, or internal job profiles. 


Anna: For humans, anything else?


Oleg: For humans, also values alignment. Like, if a coach values stability and I value growth, there is mismatch. And also, the ability to challenge me without being rude. Like, constructive.


Anna: When you think about effectiveness indicators, what tells you that a career support interaction was effective?


Oleg: For me, effectiveness is when I get my head cleared. Like, I decide something. Not just thinking. Also when I take action. Like, I schedule a meeting with my manager, I apply, I join a leadership training, I ask for a project. Also when I see outcomes: interview invitations, internal opportunities, better performance review, more confidence. Not only feeling good, but steps.


Anna: Do you think trust always leads to effectiveness?


Oleg: No. You can trust someone and still not get results, like we said. And you can get results without deep trust if it is technical. So trust helps, but it is not enough. Competence is key.


Anna: Does effectiveness build trust?


Oleg: Yes, definitely. If AI helps me reliably with code, I trust it more in that area. And if a mentor gives good advice, trust increases. But if one time it fails badly, trust drops quickly, especially for AI. For humans, maybe you forgive more if relationship is strong. But it depends.


Anna: That is interesting. Why do you forgive humans more?


Oleg: Because you assume good intentions. And you see they are limited. With AI, it is just output. If it is wrong, you feel, okay, it is unreliable.


Anna: Okay. Now I want to ask about your preferences and reaction to hybrid models.


8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction


Anna: Imagine a hybrid career coaching model where you have AI support for preparation, reflection prompts, structuring options, and follow up, and you also have access to human mentors or coaches for deeper conversations and key decisions. What is your first reaction?


Oleg: I mean sounds good i mean It sounds like the best combination. Because I do not want to spend human time on small tasks like rewriting text. That can be AI. But for leadership path, I want human.


Anna: What would make you adopt such a hybrid model?


Oleg: Quality of the human part. Like, who are the mentors. Are they real professionals. Also trust and privacy for the AI part .And prices matter. I am early career, I can’t pay a lot. .


Anna: In such a model, where would you want AI to lead, and where would you want a human to lead?


Oleg: I don’t really care the main is the result.


Anna: Okay. If the AI part asks you reflective questions, like what do you value, what do you fear, would you use it?


Oleg: Maybe a bit, but I would be cautious. It depends on how it is framed. If it is like journaling prompts, okay. But if it tries to interpret me, like you are this type, then no. I do not want психологический анализ from AI. I want a person.


Anna: What about if the AI part was designed to be very transparent, like it always says, I am not a therapist, I am a tool, and it shows you how it builds suggestions.


Oleg: Doesn’t matter how you frame it it is still same. 


Anna: How would you want the human coach to work with the AI output?


Oleg: It could be useful if I can bring a summary to the human. Like, AI helps me prepare: here are my goals, constraints, options. Then the human can go deeper. That saves time.


Anna: Would there be any red flags that would make you avoid a hybrid model?


Oleg: If it feels like the AI is replacing the human. Or if they push me to use AI only. Also if they collect too much data. Like, if they ask for my company name, salary, visa status, and store it. That would feel risky. Germany is sensitive about data I am now too hahaha. 


Anna: And what if the model matches you to a mentor based on your profile and goals?


Oleg: That is good, but I would want to understand the logic. Like, why this person. If it is a black box, I trust less. If it shows criteria, I trust more.


Anna: Do you think you would trust a mentor from your own company more, or an external one?


Oleg: Mixed. From my company, they have context, very useful. But also risk, because internal politics. 


Anna: If you had a human coach, what would you want them to help you with specifically, in the next three months?


Oleg: I want a plan steps to leadership. Like, identify skills gap. Identify projects to take. How to influence without authority. How to mentor juniors. And also how to talk to my manager about development. And maybe practice those conversations, because I get nervous about money talks.


Anna: If you had AI support, what would you want it to help with, in the next three months?


Oleg: Help me draft emails, structure my development plan, prepare for meetings, summarize feedback, and maybe simulate scenarios. Like, what if my manager says no. How to respond. But again, I would check.


Anna: Okay. We are close to the end. I want to ask a final closing block.

Anna: And the biggest difference in effectiveness?


Oleg: Human is effective for deep, messy problems and decisions. AI is effective for structure, speed, and low stakes tasks.

2) Participant context and career situation


Anna: Where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now, or recently faced?


Lena: Eehm, so I am finishing my Bachelor in Media Informatics. I am in Berlin now, but I am from Mainz originally. I moved here for the university and also for work, kind of both. And I work part time as a junior UX researcher, like Werkstudentin, at a mid size SaaS company. So I do research tasks, mostly around product discovery, usability tests, interviews. And the big question for me is like, what is my path after the Bachelor. Like, do I continue with UX research, do I go more into UX design, or do I go into product, like PM, product discovery, something like that. And also the question of where, like stay in Berlin, or maybe go back to Mainz area, or maybe somewhere else.  Or masters. I don’t know yet. But Berlin is kind of the obvious place for UX, I think.

But I also see that UX research roles are competitive and sometimes unclear. Like in Berlin, every startup says they want a UXR, but then they want someone who is also a designer and also a PM and also a data analyst. And I am like, okay, what are you actually hiring for. So that makes it stressful.

My German is great, I  mean am I German hahaha, my professional English is also great. But interviews, especially when you have to talk about impact, I freeze. And I see other people who are louder, and they can tell a story, and I am like, I did good work, but I cannot present it as a shiny story.

Lena: Yes, exactly. Like, I can do the research. But then, Bewerbungen, portfolio, case studies, interviews, I feel like I am weak. And I hate exaggerating, I hate the feeling of bragging.

And my current company is more structured. So I decided to stay. Also because I needed stability, like rent in Berlin is insane. And I have my thesis coming.


Anna: I see.  What made that decision difficult?


Lena: The fear of missing out, honestly. Like, the startup sounded like, wow, you can build everything, you can do huge impact. And in my current company, I am part time, and I do research, but I do not own the whole product. So I thought maybe I should go to the startup to have a stronger portfolio story. But also I was like, do I want to be in chaos while finishing my Bachelor. Probably not. And also the startup offered less money.

And also someone who understands the Berlin market, like what matters for UX roles here. I talked to friends, but they are also students, so they guess. I wanted someone who knows how hiring works.

And if I do it at university only, I have more freedom but maybe less practical connection.

Or if it is product, then I know what entry roles to target. Because right now, it is like a fog. I know what I like, but I do not know what to choose.

Unhelpful is like show your motivation, be confident. It is like, yes, but how.

Lena: Yes.

4) Human coaching experience and trust


Anna: You mentioned you had one session with the university career service. Can you walk me through that session, like what triggered  you to book it, and what happened?


Lena: Yes. So, I booked it around, I think, May last year. Because I was starting to think about applications for internships and also like, what after Bachelor. And I felt stuck with my CV. In UX, it is weird because CV is one thing but portfolio is another. So I booked a slot, it was like 30 minutes, online, with a career advisor from the university.


Anna: What did you expect going into it?


Lena: I expected they would understand at least basics of UX roles, like difference between UX design and UX research and product. And I wanted feedback on my CV, and also maybe direction, like, what titles to search for. I wanted someone to look at my experience and say, okay, this is your narrative.


Anna: And what happened in the session?


Lena: It felt very template. Like, she opened my CV, she said, okay, you should use this standard template, like Europass style, or like their uni template. And she said, put your skills here, put your education here, and keep it one page. And I was like, okay, yes, but I am already doing that. And then I said, I am working as junior UX researcher, and she asked, so are you more like marketing research. And I was like, no, product research. And she was like, ah okay. Then she gave advice like, use action verbs, quantify achievements, mention tools. Which is fine, but it was like reading a checklist.


Anna: Was there anything that felt personalized at all?


Lena: Not really. She did not ask about what kind of role I want, or what kind of company, or what my strengths are. She did not ask for my portfolio at all. I was like, should I show it, and she said, we do not have time, but generally include a link. And it felt rushed. And no follow up, like, if you change it, send it again, nothing.


Anna: What emotions did you have during or after?


Lena: During, I was like, okay, maybe I was expecting too much but i felt very sad afterwards. Because I paid with my time, and I got basically nothing.  But, to be fair, one thing was helpful.


Anna: What was helpful?


Lena: She said, for Germany, sometimes companies like to see a short profile summary at the top, like a 3 line thing, and that I can frame it as, media informatics student with UX research experience in SaaS, skilled in qualitative interviews and synthesis, seeking junior roles in product research. And that actually helped me because I had no summary before. So that was one useful tip.


Anna:  Aha. Anything else? 


Lena: Like, it was useful, but the overall feeling was not trust. It was more like, okay, this is a service, they do generic stuff.


Anna: When you think about trust in a human career supporter, what are the main drivers for you?


Lena: Understanding. Like they actually listen and remember details. Also domain like they know UX, hiring, what a portfolio is, what a case study is, what stakeholders are. Confidentiality also structure, like a process. And non judgmental tone, like not making you feel stupid.


Anna: If you imagine an ideal real coach, what would you want them to do with you?


Lena: Hmm. I would want them to ask me questions first, like what motivates you, what kind of tasks give you energy, what you hate, what environment. Then help me map roles, like UXR, UX designer, product, and what skills overlap, what gaps. Then help me build a narrative for applications. Like, not lie, but show my value. And maybe do mock interviews, because that is my weakness.


Anna: What would make you distrust a human coach?


Lena: If they push their own agenda, like, you should do this because it worked for me. Or if they do not understand the field, but still act confident. Or if they are judgmental. Also if it feels like they are selling something, like upselling packages.


Anna: Have you ever trusted a friend a lot, but it was not effective?


Lena: Yes, actually. My best friend from Mainz, I trust her completely, like emotionally. She listens, she is kind. But she is studying psychology, not UX. So she says things like, follow your heart, you will figure it out. And it helps emotionally, it reduces anxiety, but it does not give me concrete action. So high trust, limited effectiveness for career decisions.


Anna:  Got it, thank you. For you, effectiveness is what exactly, like how do you know support is effective?


Lena: Effective means I know what to do after, like I can decide something. Or I have a concrete plan, like, apply to these roles, change these portfolio parts, talk to this person. Maybe confidence also, like I feel less small. Or measurable results like interview invites. But not only that, because sometimes you do everything and still no invites in this economy. But at least I feel my narrative is stronger and I can act.


Anna: So for you effectiveness includes clarity, confidence, concrete actions, and sometimes outcomes like interview invites?


Lena: Yes.


5) AI tools experience and trust


Anna: Thank you. Now I want to talk about AI tools. You mentioned you use AI for summarizing notes and rewriting CV bullets, but you avoid using it for deeper self reflection because it feels too impersonal. Can you tell me about your experience, what tools you use and for what?


Lena: I mostly use ChatGPT, like the normal one. And sometimes the built in AI in Notion for summarizing. But mainly ChatGPT. I use it after user interviews, but not with raw transcripts, more like my notes. I write messy notes during interviews, and then after, I paste them and say, can you structure this into themes, pain points, quotes, and possible insights. And it helps me to get a first draft. 


Anna: Can you give me a specific example of when it helped?


Lena: Yes. Two months ago, we did interviews about onboarding for a feature. We spoke with like six users, small sample, but still. And my notes were messy, because I was facilitating and note taking. After, I had like pages of bullet points. I put them in AI and asked it to cluster into themes and also highlight contradictions. And it gave me a structured summary. And I was like, yes, that is exactly it. It saved me time. Then I refined it myself.


Anna: So it helped with speed and structure?


Lena: Yes, and also with wording. Sometimes I have the insight in my head, but I cannot phrase it professionally or just don’t want to do it and it helps me write a sentence that sounds clear.


Anna: And in CV bullets, how do you use it?


Lena: I write a bullet like, conducted interviews and made report. And then I ask AI to rewrite it into impact focused bullet points, like, led 6 user interviews to identify onboarding friction, resulting in prioritized recommendations for product team. Stuff like that. HR love KPI. 


Anna: And now the other side. Can you describe a moment where AI failed, or felt risky, or made you uncomfortable?


Lena: Not really. I mean yes sometimes it outputs something that is obviously fake. Like, increased conversion by 30 percent, or improved user satisfaction significantly. And I am like, I did not measure that. So it pushes you to exaggerate. And if you do not pay attention, you might include lies. But it’s common knowledge and one must pay attention to it themselves. I mean always recheck or read what it got you. 


Anna: Aha. Anything else?


Lena: Privacy. At work, we have user data. We do interviews. I cannot paste raw transcripts because there are identifying details. So I am careful. I only paste my own abstracted notes. But still, sometimes I feel uneasy, like, am I allowed to do this. Even if it is anonymized, it is still internal. So I keep it low stakes. Also, AI sometimes makes assumptions about roles. Like, it will say, as a UX designer you should do this, and I am like, I am a researcher. It confuses me. So I do not trust it fully, but that's okay for me. 


Anna: So you have usefulness, but not full trust?


Lena: Yes 


Anna: I understand thank you. When you think about trust in AI tools, what drives trust for you?


Lena: Verifiability. Like, I can check. If it summarizes my notes, I can see if it matches. Consistency, like it always gives a structured output. Controllability, like I can prompt it, I can say shorter, more formal, include this. And low stakes. If it is a draft, it is fine. I do not trust it for decisions about my life, but I trust it for drafts.


Anna: What would reduce trust?


Lena: When it is too confident and wrong. Or when it gives advice that is generic but sounds authoritative. Or when it uses data it cannot know. Also, if it feels like it is making me into a generic person, like, here is your career path, and it is not me.


Anna: You said it feels too impersonal for deeper self reflection. Can you unpack that?


Lena: Um. It is like, if I talk about insecurity, I want a human reaction. Like, a person who actually knows me, who can say, you are not crazy, or who can challenge me. AI can respond kindly, but it feels like a script. And also I do not want to dump my emotional stuff into a tool. It feels weird. Like, is this real, is someone reading it, I do not know. So I avoid.


Anna: So that is a mix of emotional need and privacy uncertainty?


Lena: Yes.


6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks


Anna: Thank you. I want to compare human support and AI support through specific tasks. I will name a task, and you tell me what you would use, and why. First task is clarifying options, like mapping UX research versus product versus design. What would you use?


Lena: For mapping options, I would maybe start with AI because it is fast. It gives a broad overview. But then for actually choosing, I would want a human, like someone who knows me and can ask questions, because AI can list options but cannot feel what fits.


Anna: What about rewriting CV bullets or cover letters?


Lena: AI, definitely, for the first draft. But I would want a human to review for tone and truth, and to make sure it sounds like me, not like AI. But if I do not have a human, I still use AI.


Anna: Next task is preparing for interviews, like telling your story, answering questions.


Lena: Human would be better, I think. Because interview is social. You need feedback on how you speak, and confidence. AI can simulate questions, but it cannot really see you. But maybe AI could help generate question lists.


Anna: Thank you. Managing anxiety about applying, like procrastination, imposter feelings.


Lena: Human. Or like friend, therapist, someone. AI is not good for that, for me. It can give coping tips, but it is not the same.


Anna: Structuring your thesis decision, like thesis with company versus university.


Lena: Hmm. I would use both. AI for a pros and cons list, like a framework. Human for the emotional part and context, like what my supervisor expects, what the company might do, what the risk is.


Anna: You mentioned earlier that the university career service did not look at your portfolio due to time. For portfolio strategy, who would you trust more, AI or human?


Lena: Human, if they understand UX hiring. Because portfolio is nuanced. AI can suggest a structure, but a human can say, this is convincing, this is not. Also they know what looks junior, what looks senior.


Anna: Let us go into a specific work episode. Can you describe a moment at work where you felt strong and competent?


Lena: Yes. There was one meeting, like, I think in October. We had done usability testing on a new flow for setting up integrations. And the PM and the designer were convinced that the flow was fine, like minimal issues. But in testing, users got stuck at a step because of terminology. They thought integration means something else. I had clips, like short quotes, and I synthesized it into a clear insight: the mental model mismatch. In the readout, I presented it and I was like, this is not a small UI issue, it is conceptual. And the PM actually changed priority. He was like, okay, we need to adjust the copy and add guidance. And later he told me, good catch, we would have shipped confusion.


Anna: What did you do in that moment that made it successful?


Lena: I mean I was prepared. I had evidence, like quotes, patterns, not just friction. I also explained the why, like mental model, not just, users are confused. I could answer questions like, how many users, what scenarios, is it edge case. And I felt like, okay, I can influence.


Anna: And a moment where you felt insecure, especially around selling yourself?


Lena: Job applications, always. But also even inside the company, sometimes. Like, when we have a meeting with leadership, and they ask, what is the impact of research, and I am like, I do not have numbers. And I see senior people speak confidently. And I feel small. And then I think, in interviews, it will be worse. Because you have to say, I am great, hire me. And I just cannot.


Anna: What is the inner dialogue in those moments?


Lena: It is like, you are just a student, you are not real. Like, you only did small projects. Others have real impact. And then I compare myself. Especially in Berlin, there are many people with shiny portfolios.


Anna: When you say shiny portfolios, what do you mean?


Lena: Like, beautiful case study websites, perfect storytelling, big brand logos. And they write like, drove strategic decisions, delivered measurable outcomes. And I am like, maybe they also exaggerate, but it still affects me.


Anna: Does AI play any role in that insecurity, like making it worse or better?


Lena: Better in the sense that it helps me write. Like, when I feel blocked, it gives me words. Worse in the sense that it can produce those shiny lines, and then I feel like, do I have to sound like that to be hired. It pushes the standard higher.
7) Trust–effectiveness link


Anna: Thank you.  Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about human support, how does trust affect whether it is effective?


Lena: Trust is like a gate. If I do not trust the person, I will not share the real problem. Like, I will keep it superficial. Then the advice is superficial. In the career service session, I did not trust she understood UX, so I did not talk about deeper confusion. I just asked about CV. So it limited what could be effective.


Anna: And when you do trust, what changes?


Lena: I can say, I am scared, I feel like an imposter, I do not know what to choose. And then a person can help with that. But if I do not trust, I stay in safe topics.


Anna: How about AI, does trust work the same way?


Lena: Similar but different. With AI, if I do not trust it, I still might use it for drafts, because it is easy. Like, I can use it pragmatically. So trust is not necessary for some usefulness. But for high stakes, like, should I choose product or research, trust matters because otherwise I will not act on it.


Anna: Can you give an example where AI was useful but you did not fully trust it?


Lena: As I said before CV bullets. It gives me a good wording, but I do not trust that the bullet is truthful or appropriate. So I check and edit. It is useful, but not trusted as final.


Anna: And can you give an example where a human was trusted but not effective?


Lena: My friend, like I said. She is very trustworthy as a person. But the advice is like, you will be fine, choose what feels right. It helps emotionally, but it does not create action. So I trust her, but the career problem remains.


Anna: If we zoom out, what makes you say something is effective, independent of trust?


Lena: I think effectiveness is outcome, like does it move me forward. But trust influences whether I can engage deeply enough to get that outcome. In low trust, you can still get a small tip, like the summary paragraph in the CV. That was effective in a small way. But the bigger questions need trust.


Anna: And for AI, could something be effective even if trust is low?


Lena: Yes like summarizing notes. Even if I do not trust it emotionally, it can still do the structure. But I would not let it decide.


8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction


Anna: I want to ask you now some quick ratings, like a micro poll. It is 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all, 10 is full trust. First, trust in human career coaches, in general, like your expectation, including your experience with the university career service. What number would you give?


Lena: Hmm. Maybe a 6.


Anna: Why a 6?


Lena: Because I think humans can be very good if they understand the domain and if they care. I believe that. But my only official experience was generic, and I know there are also bad coaches. So it is not like automatic trust. It depends. So 6 is like, open but cautious.


Anna: And trust in AI tools for career related tasks, 0 to 10?


Lena: For career tasks, maybe 7 for small tasks, but overall, I would say 5.


Anna: Can I ask you to please stick with one number for overall, and then explain.


Lena: Okay, overall 5.


Anna: Why 5?


Lena: Because I trust it to help with drafts, structure, brainstorming. But I do not trust it with personal context and decision making. Also privacy concerns. So it is like half.


Anna: Next, perceived effectiveness of human coaching for your situation, 0 to 10.


Lena: Effectiveness, I would say 6 again.


Anna: Why 6?


Lena: Because I can imagine it being very effective if it is the right coach. But the career service was not very effective. It gave one good tip, but not clarity. So I rate it moderate.


Anna: Perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, 0 to 10.


Lena: For my situation, maybe 6.


Anna: Aha so you have AI effectiveness similar to human.


Lena: Yes, because for practical stuff, AI helps a lot, like saving time. But for the big decision, it does not help. So overall it is moderate.


Anna: If we separate trust and effectiveness, can you see cases where trust is higher than effectiveness, or effectiveness higher than trust?


Lena: Yes. With my friend, trust higher than effectiveness. With AI, effectiveness sometimes higher than trust, like it can produce a good structure even if I do not trust it fully.


Anna: Great. Now I want to introduce a concept of a hybrid model. Imagine a service where you have an AI that helps you prepare, like clarifying your situation, structuring your thoughts, drafting CV bullets, and collecting your experiences into a narrative. Then you have access to a human coach or mentor for key moments, like decision making, mock interviews, and emotional support. How does that sound to you?


Lena: That sounds ideal, honestly. Because I do not want to pay a human for everything. Like, if I have to pay 100 euros per hour for someone to rewrite my bullet points, it feels wasteful. But I would pay for someone to help me with strategy and confidence.


Anna: What would you want the AI part to do, specifically?


Lena: I would want it to help me prepare. Like, ask me questions, extract my experiences, help me build a portfolio outline, help me write drafts. Also maybe track applications, like reminders, but that is more tool. And also give me prompts for reflection that are not too therapy. Like, what tasks energize you, what environment, but in a structured way. Maybe I would do that if it feels like a guided worksheet, not like emotional chat.


Anna: And what would you want the human part to do?


Lena: Validate, challenge, and personalize. Like, look at my portfolio and say, this is strong, this is weak, and why. Do mock interviews and give feedback on how I present. And also help me decide between paths. And if I cry, they do not freak out hahaha. 


Anna: What would make you skeptical about the hybrid model?


Lena: Data privacy and how the AI uses my information. Like, who owns it, where it goes. Also, if the AI gives generic outputs, it would annoy me. And also the quality of the human. Like, if it is just random mentors, not trained, then it could be risky.


Anna: If you had to choose, would you prefer one consistent human person, or rotating mentors?


Lena: Consistent. Because trust needs continuity. If I have to explain my story again, it is exhausting.


Anna: And would you be open to AI being present during a human session, like summarizing, or suggesting questions?


Lena: Maybe, if it is transparent. Like, if the AI is only taking notes and I can see the notes, and it is not like spying. It could be helpful. Because then I can focus on talking, and later I have a summary.


Anna: What kind of boundaries would you want clearly stated?


Lena: Like, AI is not making decisions for you. It is not therapy. It does not store sensitive data unless you choose. It does not use your data to train something without consent. And the human is bound by confidentiality. Like, clear.


Anna: If you think about your reluctance to do deep self reflection with AI, could a hybrid model change that?


Lena: Maybe. Because if I know a human will see it later, then it feels less like talking into the void. Like, the AI could collect my thoughts, and then the human can discuss. That feels less impersonal. But I still would not want to write very emotional stuff to AI.


Anna: Thank you. 


9) Closing


Anna: We are almost at the end. If you think about someone like you, early career in Berlin, UX leaning, what is one thing you wish career support services understood better?


Lena: That UX is not just a normal job application process. It is portfolio, storytelling, and ambiguity. And also that juniors need help translating real work into a narrative, without exaggerating. And that confidence issues are real, like introverts exist hello.


Anna: If you could design your perfect support journey in one sentence, what would it be?


Lena: A structured process that helps me turn my experience into a clear story, with practical steps, and a human who understands UX and makes me feel safe.


Anna: Anything else you want to add that we did not cover?


Lena: No. 


Anna: Thank you, Lena. This was very helpful. 


Lena: Okay. Thank you. It was actually nice to talk about it.


Anna: It was a pleasure interviewing you. Have a good evening.


Lena: You too. Bye.






Mehmet
1)  Opening and consent

Anna: Hi Mehmet. Thanks for joining. Before we start, do I have your permission to record? Everything is anonymous, you can skip any question, and you can stop anytime.
Mehmet: Yes, you can record. It is okay.
Anna: Great. And just to confirm, you are comfortable with the topic, it is about your experiences with career support, human coaching, and AI tools.
Mehmet: Yes, totally.
Anna: Is there anything you prefer not to discuss today?
Mehmet: Hmm. Not really. Maybe not the exact company name, because, you know, Germany and Munich is small world. But otherwise okay.
Anna: Perfect. We will keep it anonymous. If any detail feels too specific, just tell me and we can generalize it. And one more thing, I may ask follow-up questions to understand your experience better, but there are no right answers.
Mehmet: Yes, okay.
Anna: Great. Let us start.


2) Participant context and career situation

Anna: Where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now, or recently faced?
Mehmet: Okay. So, eehm, I am 26. I am from Turkey. I came to Germany, Munich, six years ago. I did my Master’s in Mechanical Engineering here in Munich. After graduation I started working as a project engineer. I am in automotive supply chain, like Tier 1 supplier side, but we are very close to OEM, so we deliver modules and we are always under OEM pressure, you know.
Anna: Mm-hm.
Mehmet: And my question now is, I feel stuck. Like I am doing execution, execution, execution. Deadlines, quality topics, meeting after meeting. And I want to move into, I would say, product management or operations strategy. Something where I am not only firefighting, but also shaping how we do things. But I struggle with language, like corporate German, and also framing. Because in engineering, you do a lot, but you cannot always explain it as impact.
Anna: When you say stuck, what does that look like day to day?
Mehmet: Day to day is, eehm, I have my project plan, I track open points, I coordinate with production, quality, logistics. There is always some issue. Like a supplier is late, or a component has a deviation, or OEM changes requirement, and we need to react. I am basically the person that pushes and aligns. In German they like to say Abstimmung, alignment. So I am like the ash. 
Anna: Okay. And what feels missing?
Mehmet: The missing is, I do not own the why. I own the how. Like, okay we need to deliver this variant, we need to meet SOP date, we need to reduce scrap, okay. But I am not in the discussion of, should we change the process, should we change supplier strategy, should we change product roadmap for the module. That is more like product or operations strategy. And I see those people, they speak differently. They speak with, you know, business words, and in meetings they are confident.
Anna: What do product management and operations strategy mean to you, concretely?
Mehmet: Product management for me means, you decide what features, what variants, you prioritize, you talk with customer, you build business case. It is not only technical requirement list. And operations strategy is more like, how do we run the factory or supply chain in a smarter way. Like standardization, make or buy, supplier development, risk management, maybe digitalization. Not just reacting, but planning.
Anna: Have you had a significant career decision in the last six to twelve months?
Mehmet: Yes. Actually the biggest decision was, do I stay in automotive at all. Because automotive in Germany is like, eehm, stress, and also now all this transformation, e-mobility, cost pressure, layoffs, hiring freezes. In my company it is stable, but you always hear rumors.
Anna: Mm.
Mehmet: So I had an offer from a smaller company, not automotive, like industrial machinery. But it was more technical. And my manager also offered me to take a new project, more critical, more visibility. I had to decide, do I take this and maybe get promoted in the same path, or do I pivot.
Anna: And what did you decide?
Mehmet: I stayed. I took the project. Because I thought, okay, if I want to move into product or ops, I need credibility and visibility. But also, honestly, visa situation. I am on Blue Card now. I got it after I started full-time. So stability matters. Munich rent is crazy.
Anna: So stability and building credibility. What made it difficult?
Mehmet: Difficult is, I do not trust that doing more execution will lead to strategy roles. Sometimes it feels like you become good at firefighting and they keep you there. And also language. Like, my English is fine. German is, I would say B1-B2, but corporate German is another world. When people speak in meetings, it is like, eehm, they speak politely but they are pushing. I struggle to do that.
Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed around that decision and around the current situation?
Mehmet: I needed someone to tell me, okay, what is the path, how to translate your experience. How to speak. How to position myself internally. Also maybe which certificates or steps make sense. And also, eehm, confidence. Because sometimes I feel like outsider. Turkish, accent, not perfect German, and in Munich corporate culture it is very, I do not know, polished.
3) Career support in general

Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Mehmet: I talk with my friends, mostly other internationals. Also my girlfriend, she is German, she helps with language and also she understands the culture. I sometimes talk to my manager, but I am careful, because you do not want to look like you are leaving.
Anna: Right.
Mehmet: I use online resources, LinkedIn, YouTube, sometimes those German career channels. And I use AI a lot, like daily. For emails, meeting prep, translation. And also for career conversations, like how to craft CV, how to answer interview questions. And I did human coaching, like a paid package.
Anna: What has actually helped so far, and what has felt unhelpful?
Mehmet: Helpful is AI for language, 100 percent. It makes me faster and more confident. Also, informational interviews helped, like I reached out to one person in my company who moved from project engineering to operations excellence. We had coffee. That was helpful because he gave realistic steps.
Anna: And unhelpful?
Mehmet: Unhelpful is generic advice. Like, network more, tailor your CV, show leadership. Okay, but how, you know. And sometimes human coach gave me that. Also, some German content online is too general, and it assumes you are German native, so they do not address the language barrier.
Anna: When you say you reached out internally, how did you do it?
Mehmet: Eehm, I wrote on Teams. Very polite. I used AI to write it in German. Like, Hallo Herr so-and-so, ich interessiere mich fuer Ihren Werdegang, koennten wir uns 20 Minuten austauschen. Something like that.
Anna: And how did that go?
Mehmet: It went good. He was friendly. We met in the cafeteria. He told me, you need to show impact, not tasks. And you need to volunteer for cross-functional initiatives. And he said German is important for leadership roles, but not perfect, just confident.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust
Anna: You mentioned you did a paid career coaching package. Can you tell me about it? What did the package include?
Mehmet: Yes. So I found it on Instagram, actually. The coach was German, she had like, eehm, HR background and coaching certificate. The package was six sessions, one hour each, over two months. And there was homework. She gave templates, like values exercise, strengths, and a CV template, and a story template, like STAR method but also German version. And she had accountability, like you need to send your homework before next session.
Anna: What did you like about it?
Mehmet: Structure. Because I am engineer, I like structure. It was like, session one clarify goals, session two strengths and achievements, session three target roles, session four CV and LinkedIn, session five interview prep, session six action plan. So I knew what is coming. Also the homework forced me to think, not only talk.
Anna: And what did not work?
Mehmet: The coach did not understand engineering reality. Like she would say, just highlight leadership, like leading projects. And I said, in my role, leadership is not official, it is informal, and also we have strict processes. She suggested things like, you should propose a new product idea to the OEM. And I am like, eehm, I cannot just propose product idea. We are supplier. There are contracts. There are change requests, it is very formal. Also she mapped roles in a too simple way. Like, she said, project engineer can go to product manager easily. But in automotive, product management is sometimes technical product management, sometimes it is commercial. And they want German, customer communication.
Anna: Can you recall a specific moment that was memorable, where you felt that mismatch?
Mehmet: Yes. One session she asked me to write my biggest achievement. I wrote about solving a quality escalation. We had a part issue from a sub-supplier, and OEM found it in incoming inspection, and they threatened to stop line, like line stop is huge. I coordinated a containment action, 24 hours, we did sorting, we negotiated with supplier, we updated OEM with daily status, and we avoided line stop. It was stressful but we succeeded.
Anna: That is quite an example.
Mehmet: Yes. And she said, okay, nice, but you should not talk about firefighting, you should talk about strategy. And I said, but that is the reality, this is what I did. And she tried to rewrite it like, I led strategic quality initiative. And I was like, no, it was not strategic, it was urgent. But it still shows skills. She did not see that.
Anna: So she wanted to force it into a strategy narrative that did not feel true.
Mehmet: Exactly. And then I started to feel, she is nice, and I trust she wants to help, but she does not understand my world. So I cannot fully use her advice.
Anna: When you say trust, what makes you trust a human coach, in general?
Mehmet: For me, trust is competence first. Like, they should understand engineering and corporate environment. Not necessarily mechanical engineering, but at least how projects work, constraints, stakeholders, timelines. Second is structure, like process. Third is accountability, like they follow up. And empathy without fluff. Like I do not want too much motivational talk. I want practical.
Anna: Mm-hm. Anything else?
Mehmet: Concrete examples. If coach says, I helped someone move from project engineer to product, and they can explain steps, then I trust more.
Anna: Did you feel any of that with your coach?
Mehmet: Structure yes. Accountability yes. Empathy yes, she was kind. But competence in engineering reality, not enough. So I would say, I trusted her as a person, but not fully trusted her advice.
Anna Thank you. So trusted, but not effective?
Mehmet: Yes, exactly. I liked her, I felt safe, I could talk, but the output was not so effective for my situation.
Anna: When you say safe, what created that feeling?
Mehmet: She was not judging, she listened, she kept confidentiality, she did not share anything. And she was professional, like on time, prepared. That creates trust.
Anna: Did you ever hold back anything because of trust issues?
Mehmet: Not really. I am not very private. I can tell. 
Anna: We will come back to privacy later.
Mehmet: Okay.
5) AI tools: experience and trust
Anna: Let us talk about AI tools. You said you use AI daily. What tools do you use, and for what?
Mehmet: I use ChatGPT and also sometimes DeepL for translation. But ChatGPT is more flexible. I use it for writing emails in German, like to be polite but also clear. I use it to prepare for meetings, like I put agenda and notes and ask, what questions will they ask, what risks. I use it to summarize long Teams chat or notes. And for career, I use it for CV bullet points, cover letter, interview questions, and also to practice German corporate phrases. Like, how to say I disagree politely. Claude code, Cursor, n8n and all other stuff.
Anna: Can you give me an example where AI clearly helped at work?
Mehmet: Yes. Last month we had a supplier delay, and we needed to inform OEM program manager. In German you cannot say, you are wrong, or we are late, like direct. You say, eehm, there is a deviation, and we are working on mitigation, and we propose next steps. I wrote a draft and it sounded too blunt. I asked AI, rewrite in German corporate tone, like with those phrases, wir moechten Sie informieren, wir bitten um Verstaendnis, wir sind im engen Austausch. And it created a very good email. My boss even said, good wording.
Anna: What was the risk in that situation?
Mehmet: The risk is, if the email is too direct, they escalate. If too soft, they think you are hiding. So you need balance. AI helped with that.
Anna: And can you share an example where AI failed or caused a risk?
Mehmet: Yes. I once asked AI to draft an email to HR about internal mobility, like I want to explore opportunities in product management. And AI wrote something too strong, like I am unhappy in my current role and I want to change quickly. And that is dangerous. Because in Germany HR is, eehm, they document things. So I did not send. But I realized, okay, AI does not know internal politics. Also sometimes it hallucinates, like it adds policies that do not exist. It wrote something like, according to company guideline 4.3, you can apply after 12 months. I was like, what guideline, hahahah. 
Anna: How did you catch that?
Mehmet: Because I know there is no guideline 4.3. And also it sounded too confident. So I checked our intranet. And it was different.
Anna: How do you generally verify AI output?
Mehmet: Depends. For translation and tone, I do quick check. I read it and if it sounds okay, I use it. For technical content, I verify more. Like if it summarizes technical discussion, I check key numbers, tolerances, deadlines. For HR or sensitive topics, I verify a lot, and I ask my girlfriend or colleague.
Anna: So verification level depends on stakes.
Mehmet: Exactly.
Anna: What makes you trust AI in these tasks?
Mehmet: Speed, first. It is immediate. Second, repeatability. I can ask again, change tone, shorter, more formal. Third, no judgment. I can ask stupid questions, like what does this German phrase mean, and it will answer. Fourth, convenience, 24/7. Sometimes at 23:00 I prepare something for morning meeting.
Anna: And what reduces your trust?
Mehmet: When it gives wrong facts, or when tone is off. Like too formal, or too American, you know. Or it creates something that looks real but is not. That is dangerous.
Anna: You also mentioned privacy. You said you know privacy is weak but you do not care much. Can you say that in your own words?
Mehmet: Yes. Eehm, honestly, I think privacy is kind of an illusion now. Like, everyone already has your data. Google, WhatsApp, company systems. People share everything on Instagram. So I feel like, what am I protecting? I am not a celebrity. I am not doing illegal things. So I do not care much. I assume everyone already knows everything. So I have nothing to lose.
Anna: Does that affect whether you trust AI?
Mehmet: Yes, because I do not worry. I just use it. I do not overthink. If I was very privacy person, maybe I would not paste emails or details. But I do.
Anna: Do you ever paste confidential company information?
Mehmet: Hmm. I try not to paste, like, very confidential numbers. But I paste context. Like, schedule, topics, general. But yes, I share a lot.
Anna: And you feel okay with that.
Mehmet: Yes. In my mind, if company wants to be safe, they should provide their own tool. If they do not, then, eehm, people will use what exists.
Anna: Interesting. If I push a bit, do you see any scenario where privacy would matter for you?
Mehmet: Maybe if it is about health, or like family. But career topics, I do not mind. And emails, I mean, they are already in systems.
Anna: So for you, privacy is not a strong component of trust.
Mehmet: Exactly. Trust for AI is more about, will it help me and not create problems.
Anna: Okay.
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks
Anna: I want to compare human and AI through specific tasks. Let us take one task like translating your execution experience into a product management narrative. If you had to do that tomorrow, what would you use, human coach or AI, and why?
Mehmet: Eehm. I would start with AI. Because AI can help me write bullet points and give me structure. Like, it can take my messy notes and make it into achievements. And also it helps with German phrasing. But I would want a human, ideally someone who understands engineering, to validate if the story is credible and if it matches PM expectations.
Anna: So AI for draft, human for reality check.
Mehmet: Yes. Because AI sometimes makes it too polished and not realistic.
Anna: Another task.Preparing for an internal meeting where you need to influence stakeholders, like quality manager, production, maybe purchasing. What do you use?
Mehmet: AI helps to prepare agenda and questions. Like, what arguments, what risks, what data. But human helps with politics. Like, who is sensitive, who needs to feel respected, what to say in the room. AI cannot know that.
Anna: And another task. Deciding whether to invest heavily in German language or instead focus on technical certifications for a pivot. How would you handle that?
Mehmet: That is more human. Because it is personal and also depends on my life. But I still use AI to brainstorm pros and cons. But decision is, eehm, I need someone to challenge me, to ask why. AI can ask, but it is not the same.
Anna: Why not the same?
Mehmet: Because with human, you feel accountable. Like if I tell someone, I will do German course, then next session they ask, did you do it. AI does not care.
Anna: What about CV rewriting, cover letters?
Mehmet: AI is very good, very effective. Human coach also can help, but AI is faster and cheaper. And I can iterate many times.
Anna: Interview practice?
Mehmet: Mixed. AI can simulate questions, but human can read my vibe, my confidence, and also tell me if my story makes sense. And especially in Germany, cultural style matters. Like not overselling, but also not under-selling. That is hard.
7) Trust–effectiveness link
Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness. In your experience, how does trust affect whether a coaching method is effective?
Mehmet: For human, trust is necessary. If I do not trust the coach, I will not tell the real issues. Like, maybe I am insecure, or maybe I have conflict with manager, or maybe I feel discrimination. If I do not trust, I will say only surface. Then coaching is not effective. So trust is like, access to truth.
Anna: And for AI?
Mehmet: For AI, trust is different. Because I can still use it even if I do not fully trust. Like, I can ask it to write an email. It can be effective, even if I do not trust it fully, because I will check it. So effectiveness can happen with partial trust.
Anna: That is an important distinction. Can you give an example of effective but not trusted?
Mehmet: Yes. Sometimes AI writes a perfect email, like very diplomatic. It is effective because stakeholder reacts well. But I do not trust that it is correct, so I read it carefully. I think, is this too much, is this wrong. So I use it but I have doubt.
Anna: And trusted but not effective, you already mentioned with the coach.
Mehmet: Exactly. I trusted her as person, but advice did not fit engineering reality. So not effective.
Anna: What counts as effectiveness for you, in this career support context?
Mehmet: Effectiveness is, I get clearer narrative about my work. Like, I can explain myself in interviews or internally. Second, I get interview invites or internal conversations. Third, I can take steps for internal mobility, like apply, talk to people, join initiative. Fourth, in meetings I feel more confident and I can influence better. Like, not just follow.
Anna: So effectiveness is not only feelings, but concrete outcomes.
Mehmet: Yes. Feelings are part, but I want outcomes.
Anna: Do you think trust always leads to effectiveness?
Mehmet: No. You can trust someone and still they cannot help. Like my coach. Or you can trust a human who is kind but not competent. And then you waste time. So trust is not enough. Competence and fit is needed.
Anna: And can effectiveness build trust over time?
Mehmet: Yes. If AI helps me many times, I trust more. Even if I know it can be wrong, I rely more. For human also, if coach gives me one good insight, I trust more.
Anna: You mentioned earlier you are not very concerned about privacy. Some people connect privacy with trust, especially for AI. In your case, do you see any downside in not caring?
Mehmet: Hmm. Maybe I am naive. But, eehm, I think if something bad happens, like data leak, then I will care. But until then, I do not. It is pragmatic.
Anna: Does that pragmatism affect your willingness to use AI for deeper career reflection?
Mehmet: Yes. I use it for deeper reflection too. Like, I ask, what are my strengths, what are possible career paths. I tell it my situation. I do not feel blocked.
Anna: Okay.
Micro-poll ratings 
Anna: I want to include a quick micro-poll, just to structure the comparison. On a scale from 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all and 10 is full trust, how much do you currently trust human career coaches?
Mehmet: Hmm. I would say 6.
Anna: Why 6?
Mehmet: Because I think humans can be helpful, and they can understand emotions and context. But many coaches are generic. And I do not know how to find a good one. So I cannot give 8 or 9.
Anna: Same scale, trust in AI tools for career-related support?
Mehmet: I would say 7.5.
Anna: Why higher?
Mehmet: Because I use it daily, and it helps. I trust it for language and structure. But I do not trust it for facts always, so not 9.
Anna: Now perceived effectiveness for your situation. Effectiveness of human coaching you experienced, 0 to 10.
Mehmet: For my situation, maybe 5.
Anna: Why 5?
Mehmet: It gave me structure and some clarity, but it did not move me closer to product roles. It did not change outcomes yet.
Anna: And perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, 0 to 10.
Mehmet: That is higher, maybe 8.
Anna: Why 8?
Mehmet: Because it improves my daily work and communication, and it helps me prepare career documents fast. It is very practical.
Anna: Great, thank you. 
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction
Anna: If you imagine an ideal setup, what would you prefer: only human coaching, only AI support, or a hybrid model?
Mehmet: Hybrid. Definitely.
Anna: What does hybrid mean for you?
Mehmet: AI for preparation, drafts, language. Human for big decisions, for accountability, and for industry reality check. And also for networking maybe. Like a human can connect you, or tell you where to look.
Anna: If I describe a model where you have an AI assistant that helps you clarify goals, prepare materials, and then you can book sessions with experienced insiders in your target field, how do you react?
Mehmet: That sounds good. Because my problem is, I do not know where to find a good coach. If it is insider, like someone who did product in automotive or operations, then I would trust more. And AI can help me not waste human time with basic things.
Anna: What would you be cautious about in such a model?
Mehmet: Quality control. Like, who are these insiders, are they really good. And also cost. In Munich everything is expensive. And maybe, eehm, matching. Like, if you match me with someone from marketing, it will not help.
Anna: So domain fit is key.
Mehmet: Yes. And also language. If insider speaks English, I can do it. But if they only speak German, it is hard. But maybe that is also training.
Anna: Would you want the insider to be very empathetic, or more direct and practical?
Mehmet: Practical and direct. Empathy is good, but not too much. Like, do not tell me, you are amazing, believe in yourself. Tell me, do this, do that, and why.
Anna: If you could design the first session with such an insider, what would you want to achieve by the end?
Mehmet: I want a realistic path. Like, in my company or in Munich market, what are the roles, what skills, what steps. And I want to know, should I focus on German, on certifications, on internal projects. And I want feedback on my story, like how to position my quality escalation example as leadership.
Anna: And what would AI do before that session?
Mehmet: AI would help me write my achievements, translate, and prepare questions. Also summarize my situation so insider can read and not spend time on basics.
Anna: You mentioned earlier you are not overly excited about human coaches, but still want to try a good one. Can you say more about that?
Mehmet: Yes. I think human coaches are fine, but many are like, eehm, same. They sell packages, they have nice websites, but I do not know if they can help an engineer. I am not against it. I just do not feel, wow, coaching will change my life. But if I find a coach who is credible, then I am interested.
Anna: How would you try to find that credible person?
Mehmet: That is the problem. I do not know. Maybe through recommendation. Or LinkedIn. Or platforms. But it is hard to evaluate before paying.
Anna: So trust formation is hard upfront.
Mehmet: Yes. You need signals. Like background, client stories, but those can be marketing.
Anna: In your experience, did AI play any role in helping you evaluate coaches?
Mehmet: Yes, I asked AI, what questions should I ask a coach before buying. It suggested to ask about experience, methodology, success examples. That was useful. But I did not do it strongly, because I am polite. 
9) Closing

Anna: We are close to the end. Is there anything we did not cover that feels important about your experience with human coaching and AI for career support?
Mehmet: Maybe one thing. AI helps me with German corporate language, but it also makes me dependent. Like, sometimes I think, can I write without it. And also, if everyone uses it, then the standard becomes higher. So you need it to keep up.
Anna: That is interesting. Like an arms race.
Mehmet: Yes, exactly. And for human coaching, I think it can be good, but it must be tailored to reality. For engineering, we need someone who understands constraints.
Anna: If you could give one advice to a human coach working with someone like you, what would it be?
Mehmet: Learn the engineering context. Ask about real projects and constraints. Do not force strategy words that are not true. Help translate reality into impact, not invent.
Anna: And one advice to an AI tool designer for career support?
Mehmet: Make it understand German corporate culture better. Like tone, indirect phrasing, and also industry context. And maybe include warnings when content is risky, like HR emails. Because sometimes AI is too confident.
Anna: Thank you. Final question, how are you feeling about your next step right now, like today?
Mehmet: Today I feel, eehm, more hopeful than six months ago. Because I see options. I started German course again, and I did two informational coffees. Small steps. But still, I feel stuck sometimes.
Anna: Thank you for being open, Mehmet. If you have any questions for me about the study, you can ask now.
Mehmet: No, all good. It was interesting to reflect.
Anna: Great. Then we stop here. Thank you again.






Sofia
1) Opening and consent
Anna: Hi Sofia, thanks for joining. Before we start, do I have your permission to record? Everything will be anonymised, and you can skip any question.
Sofia: Hi Anna. Yes, you can record. That is okay.
Anna: Great. Just to double check, if anything feels uncomfortable, you can pause me or say you want to skip. Sound okay?
Sofia: Yes. Sounds good. I am a bit nervous but, um, okay hahaha. 
Anna: Totally normal. We can take it slow. Also, I will ask about experiences with human mentoring or coaching and also AI tools for career support. There are no right answers, just your experience. Ready?
Sofia: Yes. Ready.
2) Participant context and career situation
Anna: To start, where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now, or recently faced?
Sofia: Eehm, so I am 27. I moved to Hamburg three years ago from Spain for my Master’s in Business Analytics. I finished last year. And now I work in e-commerce as a data specialist. In my company the title is Data Specialist, but it is basically marketing analytics, like growth marketing analytics.
Anna: Aha. 
Sofia: And, um, I like the analysis part a lot. Like, I genuinely enjoy when there is messy data and you have to make it make sense. But I am not super comfortable with the, how to say, the storytelling to stakeholders. Like presenting to product managers or marketing leads, or leadership, that makes me anxious.
Anna: Can you say a bit more about what kind of situations trigger that anxiety?
Sofia: Yeah. So, we have weekly KPI meetings. It is fast paced, like every week is campaigns, promotions, seasonality, and everyone wants answers fast. And I have dashboards and analysis, and sometimes I am asked to, like, explain why conversion dropped in a certain segment, or why CAC changed, or why ROAS is not meeting target. And I know the analysis, but when I have to explain it in front of people who are very confident and very quick, I get, um, in my head.
Anna: What goes through your head in those moments?
Sofia: Like, I am thinking, okay, do I sound naive? Do I sound like I do not understand business? Also language. I speak English at work mostly, but there is always some German words and some jokes and, like, these small nuances. My German is, eehm, B1 maybe, like I can handle daily life, but in meetings I do not jump in German. So sometimes I feel behind.
Anna: Thank you. What is a career decision you faced in the last, say, six to twelve months?
Sofia: Yes. So, around, maybe, September or October last year, I had this decision. I was doing mainly reporting and campaign performance, like weekly dashboards, and I felt I was becoming, um, a dashboard person. And I wanted to grow into a bridge role, like someone who can translate analytics to business decisions. Like analytics translator, analytics PM, strategy ops with data. But I was not sure how to move there from my current role.
Anna: So what did you consider doing?
Sofia: I considered applying internally to product analytics, because product analytics is closer to feature decisions. But then I was like, wait, I will still have to present to stakeholders, maybe even more. And then I considered asking my manager to give me more exposure, like not just sending slides, but presenting, leading parts of the meeting. That felt scary.
Anna: What did you end up doing?
Sofia: I did a bit of both. I did not apply internally yet. I had a conversation with my manager, and I asked for more stakeholder exposure, like I said, I want to own one KPI narrative end to end. Like not just numbers, but the story. And she said yes, but then I had to do it. 
Anna: How did that go?
Sofia: The first time was bad in my head, but it was okay in reality. Like I prepared too much. I wrote a script almost. And then in the meeting, the Head of Marketing, he is German, very direct, he interrupted me and asked, so what do we do now? Like, he wanted action. And I froze for one second. And then I said, okay, we test creative A versus B, we adjust bid strategy, and we check attribution window. But my voice, I could hear it shaking.
Anna: What did you take away from that moment?
Sofia: That analysis depth is not enough. People want decision speed. They want, like, a recommendation, even if it is imperfect. And I can do that, but I need confidence. And also I need to feel safe. Like if someone interrupts and it feels aggressive, I interpret it as I failed.
Anna: When you say safe, what would create that safety for you?
Sofia: Um, if someone signals, it is okay to think, it is okay to not know everything. Like, if my manager says, we are exploring, not defending. But in those meetings it feels like defending.
3) Career support in general
Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Sofia: Eehm, friends. I have two close friends in Hamburg, one is also in tech. And I call my mom. My mom is in Spain, in Valencia region, like near Alicante, and she is very supportive. She is like, Sofia, you are capable, you moved countries, you did a Master’s, you can do this. She is very, how to say, ground?
Anna: What kind of support does your mom give you, practically?
Sofia: She gives emotional reassurance. Like she listens. And she also gives like, values advice. Like, she asks, what kind of life do you want? Do you want stability, do you want growth, do you want people around you? She is not in analytics, so she cannot give specific career tips, but she makes me calmer.
Anna: Anything else besides friends and your mom?
Sofia: Sometimes online resources, like LinkedIn posts, but honestly it overwhelms me. Everyone is like, you must do this, you must do that. So I avoid. And then, yes, I have used AI sometimes, like ChatGPT, and now I use Cursor a lot for vibe coding, building small agents. But that is more for work and also, like, for career brainstorming when I am overwhelmed.
Anna: Before we go to AI, tell me about human support. Have you used any mentoring or coaching?
Sofia: Yes. I tried mentoring via a women-in-tech network in Hamburg. It was like a programme, they matched you with a mentor.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust
Anna: Tell me about that mentoring. What was the setup, and what were you hoping to get?
Sofia: It was supposed to be, like, six months. One call per month, plus maybe messages in between. I wanted guidance on how to grow from data specialist into a more business bridging role. And also confidence, like how to speak up, how to present. Like, not therapy, but more, um, career mentoring.
Anna: What happened in the beginning?
Sofia: In the beginning it was great. I got matched with a mentor, she worked in a bigger company, like a marketplace, in analytics leadership. She was also Spanish, actually, which was, like, wow, instant connection. We spoke Spanglish sometimes. And she was very warm. She asked me questions, she remembered details. That made me trust her fast.
Anna: What specifically made you trust her?
Sofia: Feeling seen. Like she did not treat me like a generic mentee. She asked about my team, the meeting culture, my language situation. And she was credible, like she had done the path. Also confidentiality. She explicitly said, what we talk about stays here. That made me relax.
Anna: Was it effective?
Sofia: Um, partly. It was motivating. After the first call I felt, like, I can do it. She gave me some frameworks, like, prepare three bullets: insight, impact, action. And she suggested I ask my manager for more exposure. That was actually useful.
Anna: And then you said it became inconsistent.
Sofia: Yes. So after maybe two calls, it became messy. She started cancelling. Like, one time she cancelled 30 minutes before because she had a leadership meeting. And I understand, but then we rescheduled and she cancelled again. And then the programme coordinators said, okay, you can switch mentor if needed.
Anna: How did that affect you?
Sofia: It broke the trust in reliability. Like I still liked her as a person. So, this is interesting, I trusted her in the sense I felt safe and I thought she is a good person. But it was not effective anymore because there was no follow through. Like no accountability, no continuity.
Anna: Did you switch mentors?
Sofia: Yes. They switched me to another mentor. She was German, very direct, very structured. She was like, okay, what is your goal, what is your plan, send me your CV, we will review. That was, um, in some way effective, because she pushed me. But I did not trust her emotionally.
Anna: What made it hard to trust her?
Sofia: She felt judgemental, maybe not intentionally. Like she would say, why did you not do this already? And I was like, eehm, because I am scared? Hahaha. But I did not want to say that. Also she did not remember details. It felt transactional.
Anna: So would you say that mentor was effective but not trusted?
Sofia: Yes, exactly. Like, she gave concrete tasks. She told me to write a stakeholder update email in a certain format. And it worked. My manager liked it. So effective. But I did not feel warm. I did not feel safe. So I did not share the deeper anxiety.
Anna: And the first mentor, trusted but not effective, at least later on?
Sofia: Yes. Trusted, but not effective because no continuity. I still think she is great, but it did not help my career problem in the end.
Anna: Did you have any other human support, like a paid coach, university career services?
Sofia: I used the university career service once in the Master’s. It was okay, but very generic. Like, they gave me CV tips, but not specific to analytics in Germany. And also, I did not fully trust them because it felt like they do it for everyone.
Anna: Did it help anyway?
Sofia: A little. Like, they helped me format the CV and explain German CV conventions. That was useful, even if I did not trust them deeply. So, maybe that is human help that is effective despite low trust. It was effective for the task, but I did not feel a relationship.
Anna: Thank you that is good for distinction.
5) AI tools: experience and trust

Anna: Now let’s talk about AI tools for career support. What have you used, and in what situations?
Sofia: Mostly ChatGPT, and also I use Cursor a lot. But for career support specifically, ChatGPT. When I am overwhelmed, like I have too many options and I cannot think.
Anna: Can you give me a concrete moment when AI helped?
Sofia: Yes. In November, I had a week where I felt, like, I am stuck. I was comparing roles like analytics PM, strategy and ops, product analyst, and I was like, I do not know what fits me. And I was also tired from work. So I opened ChatGPT and I wrote, I am a data specialist in e-commerce in Hamburg, I like analysis but fear presentations, I want a bridge role, what options exist and what steps to test them.
Anna: And what did it do?
Sofia: It gave structure. Like, it made a table. It suggested informational interviews, internal projects, asking to shadow product meetings, building a small portfolio, like case studies. And it suggested a weekly plan, like week 1 identify roles, week 2 talk to manager, week 3 update CV, etc. That calmed me.
Anna: Did you act on it?
Sofia: Yes, partially. I used it to draft a message to my manager, like a Slack message. I was like, can I take ownership of the KPI narrative for retention next month, and can we define success. It drafted something that sounded confident.
Anna: How did you feel sending that?
Sofia: Nervous but it helped. And then I double checked with my friend and my mom. My mom was like, it sounds professional, but make it more you, like not too robotic. 
Anna: So you use AI but validate with humans.
Sofia: Yes. For important decisions, I need human validation. Even if AI is helpful, I do not fully trust it.
Anna: What makes you not fully trust it?
Sofia: Context and accuracy. Like, sometimes it assumes US style, or it assumes I can just switch jobs easily. Or it suggests things that do not fit German culture, like being super assertive in a way that can be seen as arrogant here. Also it can sound too polished. And then I worry, if I speak like that, people will think it is fake.
Anna: Can you describe a moment AI failed for you?
Sofia: Yes. I asked it once, like, how to prepare for a stakeholder meeting with a German leadership team. And it gave me advice that was generic, like, be confident, tell a story, use visuals. And it suggested I open with a personal anecdote. And I was like, hmm, in my company, leadership does not want personal anecdotes. They want numbers.  So it felt wrong.
Anna: Anything else?
Sofia: Another time it suggested salary ranges that were, I think, unrealistic. It was like, you can earn X, and I was like, I do not know if that is true in Hamburg for my experience. So I did not trust it. But it still gave me, like, negotiation phrases. That part was useful.
Anna: That sounds like effective but not trusted.
Sofia: Exactly. Like I take the structure and the wording, but I do not trust the facts.
Anna: You mentioned Cursor and vibe coding. How does that connect to your work, and maybe also your confidence?
Sofia: Oh yes. So I am kind of obsessed. I use Cursor to build small agents, like, um, scripts that help me at work. For example, I built an agent that drafts SQL queries from a question. Like, I write, I need conversion by channel by week, excluding returns, and it drafts a query. I still review, but it saves time.
Anna: What else?
Sofia: I built a reporting narrative generator. So, every Monday I have to send a summary of campaign performance. I created a template where the agent reads the metrics from a CSV and writes a first draft narrative, like, this week ROAS increased due to X, but CAC rose in Y segment, recommended action is Z. It is not perfect, but it gives me a starting point.
Anna: Interesting. Any agents for meetings?
Sofia: Yes. I have a meeting prep checklist agent. I paste the meeting title and attendees, and it generates, like, what do they care about, what questions might they ask, what risks, what one slide summary. And also I made a stakeholder FAQ generator. It creates a list of likely objections, like, is this statistically significant, what about seasonality, what about attribution. That helps my anxiety because I feel prepared.
Anna: Does it reduce anxiety in presentations?
Sofia: A bit. It gives me confidence because I have answers ready. But also it can make me over prepare. Like, I have 20 possible questions and then I am more stressed. 
Anna: You also said you believe many problems can be solved with AI.
Sofia: Yes, I kind of do. Like, not emotions, but a lot of work problems, planning problems, writing problems. I love building agents. It makes me feel powerful, like I can create my own support system.
Anna: And privacy, how do you think about that?
Sofia: Eehm. I understand it matters. Like GDPR, and company data, and personal data. But honestly, sometimes I ignore it because it is easier.  Like, I try not to paste sensitive data, but I probably do more than I should. It is like, I know it is important, but I also want speed. And then I laugh it off like, okay, Sofia, you will go to jail. Hahahah. But yeah, I know it is not ideal.
Anna: So there is awareness, but convenience wins?
Sofia:  Aha 
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks

Anna: I want to compare human mentoring and AI support through specific tasks. Let’s take a few common career support tasks. First, clarifying options. If you feel stuck and you need to map options, who or what do you prefer?
Sofia: AI first. Because it is fast and non judgemental. I can dump everything, like, I am confused, I hate presenting, I like analysis, and it will not react like, what is wrong with you.It just structures.
Anna: And then?
Sofia: Then human, to validate. Like my friend or my mom, or ideally a mentor who is consistent.
Anna: Second task, making a high stakes decision, like applying internally or asking for a role change. Who do you rely on?
Sofia: Human more. Because I need reassurance and also context. Like, my friend knows my company culture. AI does not.
Anna: Third task, preparing for stakeholder presentations. Who helps more?
