Sofia: This one is mixed. AI helps with structure, like the storyline, and anticipating questions. But a human helps with delivery, like confidence, tone, and what is realistic in the room.
Anna: What about practical tasks like CV rewriting or drafting messages?
Sofia: AI is very effective there. It can rewrite, it can produce versions. But I do not fully trust it because it can sound too perfect, too American. So I edit. Also I sometimes ask the German mentor style person, like, is this wording okay. She is effective for that even if I do not trust her emotionally.
Anna: So you use both, depending on the task.
Sofia: Yes. AI for speed and drafts. Humans for context and emotional safety.
7) Trust–effectiveness link

Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness. In your experience, how does trust affect whether something is effective?
Sofia: Trust is like, um, permission. If I trust a mentor, I tell the real story. Like, I say, I am scared, I feel stupid, I do not understand the meeting dynamics. And then they can help. If I do not trust, I only talk about safe topics, like CV. Then it is less effective.
Anna: And for AI?
Sofia: For AI, trust is different. I do not need emotional trust. I need to trust accuracy and that it is not misleading me. If I do not trust it, I still might use it for structure, but I will not follow advice blindly.
Anna: Can you give an example where you trusted but it was not effective?
Sofia: Yes, the first mentor in the women-in-tech programme. I trusted her as a person. I felt warmth. But it became not effective because she was not reliable, and advice stayed vague, like, be confident, you can do it, and then no follow up. So I felt good but nothing changed.
Anna: And effective but not trusted?
Sofia: AI sometimes. Like the negotiation phrases. Or the plan for internal move. It was effective because it gave me steps. But I did not trust that it understood Germany or my company. So I double checked with humans. Another example is the second mentor, the German one. She was effective with concrete tasks, but I did not trust her enough to share the deeper anxiety.
Anna: You also mentioned human support that was effective despite low trust, like university career services.
Sofia: Yes. They helped with German CV format. I did not trust them as, like, knowing my life, but they were effective for that task.
Anna: When you think about perceived effectiveness, how do you define it for yourself?
Sofia: If it leads to action and better outcomes. Like, I feel clearer, I do the next step, I perform better in meetings, I get better feedback from my manager, or I get interview callbacks. Also confidence. Like, if I can present and not panic, that is effectiveness.
Anna: Does trust always lead to effectiveness in your opinion?
Sofia: Not always. I mean , trust can make me feel better but not change behaviour. Like, emotional comfort without structure is not enough. And effectiveness can happen without full trust, like a useful template. But for deep career decisions, trust is necessary, because I need to share real things.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction (including micro-poll ratings)

Anna: I want to do a quick micro poll, just short. On a scale from 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all and 10 is complete trust, how would you rate your trust in human coaches or mentors, in general, for your career situation?
Sofia: Um. I would say 7. Because I believe humans can understand. But I also had inconsistency. So not 9.
Anna: What drives that 7?
Sofia: Warmth, feeling seen, credibility, like they know the path. Also accountability. But I deduct points because reliability is not guaranteed and sometimes advice is generic.
Anna: Same scale, trust in AI tools for career support?
Sofia: Maybe 5.5. Hahaha. Is half allowed?
Anna: Totally fine. Why 5.5?
Sofia: Speed, structure, low judgement, repeatability. I can ask again and again. But questionable accuracy and context. And sometimes it sounds too confident and I get suspicious.
Anna: Now perceived effectiveness. Same 0 to 10. Effectiveness of human mentoring or coaching for your situation.
Sofia: I would say 6. Because when it works, it is amazing. But I had inconsistency. And also sometimes humans are supportive but vague.
Anna: And effectiveness of AI tools for your situation?
Sofia: I would say 7. Which is funny because trust is lower. But effectiveness is higher for some tasks.
Anna: That is interesting. Why 7?
Sofia: Because it helps me move when I am stuck. It gives me drafts, plans, options. It reduces overwhelm. But I still validate with humans.
Anna: That separation is exactly what I want to understand. Now imagine a hybrid model. For example, you have an AI that helps you clarify options, prepare messages, and build a plan. And then you have a human mentor or coach who steps in for key decisions, emotional support, and accountability. How does that sound?
Sofia: That sounds ideal. Like, AI as the always available assistant, and human as the real anchor.
Anna: What would make you adopt it?
Sofia: Reliability of the human part. Like consistent mentor. Also clear boundaries. Like, when does AI handle and when does human handle. And transparency about privacy. Even though I laugh it off, I still want to know. Like, what happens to my data. If it is clear, I feel safer.
Anna: What would make you hesitate?
Sofia: If it feels like AI is replacing the human, like I cannot access a person when I need. Or if the human is too expensive or too unavailable. Also if the AI gives wrong assumptions and pushes me in a direction that does not fit me.
Anna: In a hybrid model, what tasks would you assign to AI, and what tasks to a human?
Sofia: AI: brainstorming options, structuring my thoughts, drafting messages, CV versions, interview prep questions, meeting prep checklist, stakeholder FAQ, like all that. Human: role strategy, reading company politics, helping me with confidence and presentation delivery, and accountability, like, did you actually do the thing.
Anna: When you say accountability, what would that look like?
Sofia: Like, if I say, I will talk to my manager next week, the human asks me, did you do it, what happened, what do you need now. AI can remind me, but it does not feel the same.
Anna: Why not?
Sofia: Because I do not want to disappoint a real person. AI does not care.
9) Closing

Anna: We are almost at the end. Is there anything important about trust or effectiveness in career support that we did not cover?
Sofia: Maybe one thing. I think I am optimistic about AI. I genuinely think we can solve many problems with AI, especially work problems. Like I love building agents. But for career, it is not only logic. It is identity. It is fear. And then I need a human to say, you are not crazy, you are allowed to want this. AI cannot really give that, even if it tries.
Anna: That is helpful. If you could design the perfect support for someone like you in Hamburg in e-commerce analytics, what would it look like?
Sofia: It would be like, AI gives me a structure and helps me prepare. And then a mentor who understands both data and business, and who is consistent, and who helps me practice presentations. Like maybe role play. And also understands I am not native in German, so gives me language tips without making me feel stupid.
Anna: Last question. If you think back to your stakeholder anxiety, what would be one small improvement that would change a lot for you?
Sofia: Practice with someone safe. Like rehearsing the narrative. And having a clear, simple recommendation. Like not trying to be perfect. If I can say, here is the insight, here is the impact, here is what we do, then I feel more in control.
Anna: Thank you, Sofia. That was very insightful. After I stop recording, if you think of anything else you want to add, you can message me.
Sofia: Thank you. It was actually nice to talk. I feel


Jonas 


1) Opening and consent

Anna: Hi Jonas. Thanks for joining. Before we start, do I have your permission to record? Everything is anonymous, you can skip any question, and you can stop anytime.
Jonas: Yeah, um, yes. You can record. That is fine.
Anna: Thank you. Just to double check, you are comfortable that this is for a master thesis study about trust and perceived effectiveness in AI-based versus human career coaching, and we keep it pseudonymized.
Jonas: Yes. As long as it is really anonymized, I am okay.
Anna: It will be. If any detail feels too identifying, tell me and we can rephrase. Also, are there any topics you would prefer not to discuss?
Jonas:Eehm. I think I do not want to go into very detailed security stuff. I had one incident and it made me kind of, yeah, paranoid. But I can talk about it in general.
Anna: Perfect. We will keep it high level. And just so you know, I will ask about both human support and AI tools, but you decide what you want to share.
Jonas: Okay.
Anna: Great. Let us start with a bit of context.
2)  Participant context and career situation

Anna: Where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing, or recently faced?
Jonas: Okay. So I am Jonas, I am 28, and I work as a backend developer in a fintech scale-up here in Cologne. I studied Computer Science, Bachelor, also in NRW. I have, um, around five and a half years experience full-time, if you count internships it is more, but yeah. And the big question for me right now is, like, what is next. Because I am technically strong, that is my thing, and I like building systems, I like clean code, architecture, performance, reliability. But in our company there is this expectation that at some point you either go into a lead role or you kind of stay forever in the same level. And I do not want to stagnate.
Anna: When you say lead role, what does that mean in your company?
Jonas: That is already part of the problem. It is not super clear. We have titles like Senior Engineer, Staff Engineer, Team Lead, Engineering Manager. But the boundaries are fuzzy. Sometimes Team Lead is also writing a lot of code, sometimes it is basically a mini-manager who does Jira and meetings. And that is my fear.
Anna: What is the fear exactly?
Jonas: I fear becoming a manager with an office job, you know. Like, no creativity. Just meetings, status updates, people problems. And I do not mean that in a disrespectful way, but I enjoy craft. I enjoy building. If I imagine my week is all meetings and, like, spreadsheets and performance reviews, I feel, um, trapped. I need creativity. I like solving problems, designing things. Even debugging can feel creative, in a weird way.
Anna: Can you give me an example of when that fear shows up in daily work?
Jonas: Yes. Like, we have these planning meetings, refinement, retro, sync with product, sync with compliance, and then leadership sync. And sometimes I look at my Team Lead, and he is in meetings all day. And then he maybe reviews a PR at night. And he is stressed. And when I see that, I think, if this is the path, I do not want it. I do not want to be the person who cannot open an IDE during the day.
Anna: And what is the decision you faced in the last six to twelve months related to this?
Jonas: So in the last, um, maybe eight months, I had an offer from another company, not fintech, more like e-commerce infrastructure. They wanted me as Senior Backend Engineer with more ownership. And I seriously considered leaving. Because in our scale-up it is intense, and there is a lot of ambiguity. But then I decided to stay, because I negotiated internally to get more scope. Like, more ownership of a core service, and also a clearer path to some leadership that is more technical. That was the decision.
Anna: What made it difficult?
Jonas: Uncertainty. Like, what is better. In a scale-up, growth is fast, you learn a lot, but also you can burn out. And in fintech, the stakes are high. Production incidents are scary. Compliance, audits, security, it is not just, oh we deploy and see. It can be, if we mess up, there are real consequences. So the question was, do I stay in the intense environment but maybe grow faster, or do I go somewhere calmer. And also, I was not sure if I am actually good for leadership, or if I just like the idea of it.And there was money too, obviously.
Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed at that time?
Jonas: I needed someone to help me see the options clearly and not just emotionally. Like, to reflect, okay, what do you want, what are your values, what is realistic. And also very practical, like negotiation. I am not naturally a negotiator. I can argue about architecture, but not about salary. 
Anna: That is a common split. Before we go into support, can you describe your current work setup a bit. Team, domain, maybe tech stack.
Jonas: Sure. We are a fintech in payments. Like, we provide payment processing for small and mid businesses. There is also some lending products, but my area is payments and settlement. Backend stack is mostly Kotlin and Java, Spring Boot, some older services in Java 8, we are migrating slowly. We use Kafka for events, Postgres, Redis. Some things in AWS, but we are moving parts to a more controlled environment because of compliance. Observability is decent, we have Grafana, Prometheus, logging, alerts. And team setup, we have squads, like cross-functional with product and design. My squad is backend heavy because payments are complex.
Anna: And how is it in Cologne, work-wise. Hybrid, office, tech community?
Jonas: We are hybrid. Office in Cologne, but many people are remote, also Berlin, Munich, even some in Spain. Cologne tech scene is smaller than Berlin, but there are meetups. I go sometimes to a Kotlin meetup or general backend meetups. Fintech is there, also insurtech, but it is not like London. Still, you know people. Sometimes you meet someone and they are like, oh I know your company, you had that incident. 
Anna: You mentioned production incidents. Is that part of the leadership pressure?
Jonas: Yes. Because when things break, someone needs to coordinate, decide, communicate. And that is leadership. And it is stressful. But also, that can be meaningful, like you feel responsible. So there is attraction and fear.
3) Career support in general

Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Jonas: First, friends in tech. Like, peers. We talk. Sometimes my girlfriend, but she is not in tech, so it is more emotional support. Then sometimes seniors at work, if I trust them. I do not really use official career coaching. I never went to a paid career coach. And I do not use AI for career stuff. For work, yes. For career, no.
Anna: What has actually helped, and what felt unhelpful or frustrating?
Jonas: Helpful is when someone understands the context. Like fintech, scale-up, German market, salary ranges, and also company politics. Unhelpful is generic advice like, just be confident, just apply, just network. Like, thanks. I know. But how. Also sometimes people project their own path onto you. Like, they became manager so they think you must. But maybe I do not.
Anna: So you prefer practical and context-aware support.
Jonas: Exactly. And also honesty. If someone tells me, yeah you would be a great manager, but they do not really know me, that feels like empty motivation.
Anna :Do you use any resources, like blogs, books, forums?
Jonas: Some. I read about engineering career ladders. Staff Engineer path, that stuff. There is a book, Staff Engineer, and also blogs from big tech. But then I think, okay, that is Google, not a German fintech. So it helps, but limited.
Anna: What about internal resources, like HR, performance frameworks?
Jonas:We have a career ladder doc, but it is, um, aspirational. It says, as Team Lead you do mentoring, planning, stakeholder management. As Staff Engineer you do cross-team architecture. But in practice, it is messy. So it gives vocabulary, but not a clear plan.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust

Anna: You mentioned that you had informal mentoring from a senior engineer. Can you tell me about that experience?
Jonas: Yes. So, in my previous team, we had a senior engineer, I will call him Markus, not real name. He was, like, very calm, very solid. He had been in fintech before, also in a bank. And we started talking because I was struggling with scope. I was doing a lot of on-call, a lot of incidents, and also feature work. And I felt I was drowning. So I asked him, how do you handle this, and how do you position yourself.
Anna: Was it formal mentoring, or just informal?
Jonas: Informal. Like, coffee chats. Sometimes after a retro, we would stay 15 minutes. Sometimes we booked 30 minutes. It was not like a program.
Anna: Can you describe one specific episode where this mentoring built trust for you?
Jonas: Yes. There was a moment when I got my performance review, and I was told, you are doing great technically, but you need to show more leadership. And I was like, what does that mean. And also, my salary increase was lower than I expected. So I was angry, but also unsure if I am being unrealistic. I talked to Markus, and he did not just hype me. He asked very concrete questions. Like, what exactly did they say, what did you deliver, what is the market rate, what is your leverage. And he also shared his own numbers, like his salary range, which is kind of sensitive. That was a big trust moment, because he took a risk for me.
Anna: What did he do, behavior-wise, that made you trust him?
Jonas: He was honest and not ego-driven. He did not try to be the hero. He also said, I do not know everything, but here is what I would do. And he listened. He did not jump to solutions. Also confidentiality. He explicitly said, this stays between us, I will not talk to your manager. That matters. Because in a scale-up, gossip travels fast.
Anna: What advice did he give you in that salary and scope situation?
Jonas: He gave me a plan. First, separate emotions from facts. Second, write down my impact. Like, not just I wrote code, but I reduced incident frequency, improved latency, mentored juniors, improved test coverage. Third, decide what I want. Do I want more money, or more scope, or both. Fourth, schedule a meeting with my manager, not in a reactive way, but prepared. And he helped me with wording. Like, how to say, I want to grow into a lead path, and for that I need ownership, and compensation aligned with market.
Anna: And what changed afterwards?
Jonas: I negotiated. I got a better raise, not huge, but meaningful. And more important, we adjusted my role scope. I became the owner of a service, settlement reconciliation, which is critical. And they also reduced my on-call rotation frequency. So I got more time for deep work. That was a tangible outcome. And mentally, I felt more confident. Like, I can advocate for myself.
Anna: So that mentoring felt effective.
Jonas: Yes, for that task, very effective.
Anna: Were there any moments where you trusted him but it was not effective?
Jonas: Yes. He is very technical, and sometimes when I ask about, like, people leadership or stakeholder stuff, his advice becomes generic. Like, just communicate more, build relationships. He is not bad, but it is not detailed. So I trust him as a person and as an engineer, but not everything he says is actionable for me.
Anna: That is interesting. So trust and effectiveness are related but not the same.
Jonas: Exactly. I can respect someone and still not get what I need.
Anna: What are, for you, the main drivers of trust for a human coach or mentor?
Jonas: Competence, but competence in my context. Like, understands fintech constraints, understands German work culture, understands scale-up chaos. Honesty. If they are unsure, they say it. Confidentiality. If I share career fears, I need it to stay safe. Consistent follow-up. Like, if we talk and they remember next time, that shows they care. And also, not ego-driven. Some seniors love to talk and show how smart they are. That kills trust for me.
Anna: And what makes someone untrustworthy?
Jonas: If they are political. If they use mentoring to recruit you to their agenda. Or if they promise things. Like, do this and you will get promoted. Nobody can promise that. Or if they share other people’s private info, because then I think they will share mine too.
Anna: How did you feel during those mentoring conversations, emotionally?
Jonas: Safer. Like, less alone. In German I would say, erleichtert. Because you realize, okay, it is not just me. Also, it gave structure. In a scale-up, everything is urgent. Mentoring gave me a step back.
5) AI tools: experience and trust 
Anna: Now shifting to AI tools. What is your experience with AI tools, and how do you use them?
Jonas: I use AI a lot for coding. Like, daily. Not because I blindly trust it, but because it speeds things up. I use it for unit tests, integration test scaffolding, refactoring suggestions, generating boilerplate, sometimes explaining an unfamiliar library. Also for code review, like, can you spot potential issues. I also use it to draft documentation.
Anna: Which kinds of tools, in general terms? Like integrated in IDE or web-based?
Jonas: Both. In IDE, like a coding assistant. Also sometimes a chat interface. But I am careful. For work code, I do not paste sensitive stuff. We have policies. And personally, because of my privacy thing, I try to limit what I share.
Anna: We will get to privacy. Before that, can you give me a moment where AI really helped you on a technical task?
Jonas: Yes. Recently we had a performance issue in a Kafka consumer. Under load, we had spikes, and the consumer lag went crazy. I suspected some blocking call. I asked the AI to help me reason about a certain pattern in Kotlin coroutines, like where blocking might happen if you mix coroutine context with a legacy library. It suggested a few things, and one of them was correct: we had a hidden blocking call in a mapping function that did synchronous network calls. I had overlooked it. So it helped me look in the right place. Then I fixed it by moving that call to an async pipeline and adding caching. That reduced lag significantly.
Anna: So it was not just code generation, but also diagnosis.
Jonas: Exactly. Like a second brain, but not always right.
Anna: And can you share a moment when AI failed or was risky?
Jonas: Many. Hahaha. One example: I asked for a refactor of a piece of code that handles money amounts, like decimals, currency. It suggested using float. And I was like, no. That is dangerous. Another time it hallucinated an API that does not exist. Like, it confidently said, use this method, and it was fake. That is the problem. It can look correct but be wrong. In fintech, that is scary. Also security. It sometimes suggests patterns that are not safe, like logging too much, or ignoring validation.
Anna: How do you handle that risk?
Jonas: Verification. I treat it like a junior developer with confidence. I check documentation. I run tests. I reason myself. It is helpful for speed, but I do not outsource judgment.
Anna: How would you describe your trust in AI tools in technical contexts?
Jonas: Conditional. I trust it for ideas, for drafts, for boilerplate. I do not trust it for correctness without verification. And I definitely do not trust it for sensitive data.
Anna: You said you avoid using AI for career topics. Why?
Jonas: Two reasons. One is privacy. Career topics are personal. The second is, I have this feeling it reinforces what you already believe. Like confirmation bias. If I ask, should I become a team lead, it will give me a nice answer that fits my question framing. And if I am in a mood, I might just select the parts that comfort me. It is like a mirror. And I do not want that. I want friction, sometimes. I want someone to challenge me.
Anna: Can you give a concrete example where you noticed that effect?
Jonas: Yes. I tried once. Just once. I asked something like, I am afraid management will kill my creativity, how can I grow without becoming a manager. And it gave me a very well-written answer, like, you can become Staff Engineer, you can mentor, you can do architecture. And it felt good. But then I realized, it just validated my fear. It did not ask, maybe your picture of management is wrong, maybe there is creative leadership. It could have, but it did not. And I did not push it. I just took the comforting answer. That is exactly the risk. It is too agreeable if you do not structure it.
Anna: So it can be soothing, but maybe not corrective.
Jonas: Yes. And for career decisions, I need something that reduces self-delusion, not increases it.
Anna: That is a strong statement. Let us talk about the privacy concern you mentioned earlier. You said you had a past incident where your email was compromised.
Jonas: Yes.
Anna: Can you describe it at a high level, what happened and how it changed your behavior?
Jonas: High level. So, a few years ago, I noticed weird login notifications. Like, password reset emails I did not request. Then I realized my email account had been accessed. And because email is the key to everything, they tried to reset other accounts too. Some passwords were reused, which is stupid, I know, and so some accounts were accessed. It was not catastrophic, but it was invasive. I felt, um, violated. Like, someone is in your digital home.
Anna: That sounds intense. What did you do afterwards?
Jonas: I changed everything. Password manager, unique passwords, 2FA everywhere, also a hardware key for important accounts. I stopped trusting cloud services blindly. I started thinking in threat models, like, what happens if this is leaked. And I became very cautious about sharing personal info in tools that store data. That includes AI tools. Because I do not fully know where the prompts go, who can access, how long it is stored. Even if they say it is private, I am cautious.
Anna: So your trust drivers for AI include data boundaries.
Jonas: Yes. If an AI tool is local, like running on my machine, I feel more trust. If it is cloud, I assume it is stored somewhere. Also, verifiability. Like, can I see sources, can I see reasoning. And clear policies.
Anna: Do you use any local AI tools?
Jonas: A bit. For experimenting. Not super powerful compared to cloud, but for some tasks, yes. And for work, we have approved tools, but still, I am cautious.
6)  Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks

Anna: Now I want to compare human and AI via specific career-related tasks. I will name a task, and you tell me which you would prefer, and why. Does that work?
Jonas: Yes.
Anna: Task one is clarifying options. Like, you are unsure whether to pursue Staff Engineer, Team Lead, or change company. Who would you go to?
Jonas: Human. Because I need questions back. I need someone to explore my assumptions. AI can list options, but I can also Google that. Human can say, okay, but what do you actually want, and why. And they can notice contradictions.
Anna: What if the human does not know fintech?
Jonas: Then less useful. Context matters. But still, a good human can facilitate reflection.
Anna: Task two: planning concrete next steps for a lead path, like what skills, what projects, what timeline.
Jonas: This is mixed. AI could help structure a plan, like a checklist. But I would want a human to validate it in my company context. Because in a scale-up, formal checklists are sometimes fake. So I would use AI for a draft, but then human for reality check.
Anna: Task three: negotiating salary and role scope.
Jonas: Human, definitely. Because negotiation is social. I need to practice, role-play, get feedback on tone. Also, a human can share market knowledge. AI can give generic tips, but it cannot tell me what my manager is likely to react to.
Anna: Task four: CV rewriting or LinkedIn polishing.
Jonas: AI, actually. For that, it is fine. Because it is not deeply personal, and it is pattern-based. But I would still not paste everything. I would anonymize. AI can generate bullet points and wording. Human can also help, but AI is faster.
Anna: Task five: making a high-stakes decision when you are emotionally stressed, like you are burnt out and thinking of quitting.
Jonas: Human. Because I need emotional containment. AI cannot really do that. It can write empathetic text, but I do not feel it. And I do not want to share that level of vulnerability with a system I do not trust.
Anna: What about task six: technical career growth, like becoming a better engineer, learning architecture.
Jonas: Here AI is effective. Like, for technical learning, it is great. But again, verify. It can explain patterns, show examples. For that, I trust it more.
Anna: So there is a split between technical and personal.
Jonas: Yes. For me, AI is a tool for technical tasks. For career identity, leadership, values, I prefer human.
Anna: Can you think of a situation where AI was effective for you but you still did not fully trust it?
Jonas: Yes. Like the performance debugging I mentioned. It helped. It was effective. But I still do not trust it as a system with my sensitive info. So effectiveness in one domain does not automatically create trust in another.
Anna: And the opposite, trusted but not effective, you mentioned with Markus and generic advice.
Jonas: Exactly. I trust Markus. But he cannot always give me the detailed leadership model I need.
7) Trust–effectiveness link

Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about human mentoring, how does trust affect whether it is effective?
Jonas: Trust is like the entry ticket. If I do not trust the person, I will not share the real thing. I will talk about safe topics, like how to improve my CV, but not the deeper fears. And without the deeper stuff, advice is shallow. So trust enables depth. And depth is often where effectiveness comes from.
Anna: What does depth mean for you in this context?
Jonas: Depth is like, why do I fear management. Is it because I saw a bad example. Is it because I fear losing identity. Is it because I do not want responsibility. These are uncomfortable. If I trust the mentor, I can say, I am scared. If I do not trust, I will say, I am considering career options, which is a polite lie. 
Anna: And with AI, how does trust connect to effectiveness?
Jonas: Different. With AI, trust is more about, can I rely on output and can I control my data. For technical tasks, I can get effectiveness without emotional trust, because I can verify. For career tasks, if I do not trust privacy, I will not even use it. So it cannot be effective because it is not used. Or I will sanitize so much that it becomes generic, and then it is not effective anyway.
Anna: So trust can be a prerequisite for use.
Jonas: Yes. Especially in personal domains.
Anna: What are your indicators that support was effective, whether human or AI?
Jonas: Clarity. Like, I know what I want to do next. Concrete next steps. Like, I will take ownership of project X, I will mentor someone, I will talk to my manager with these points. Also confidence. Not fake confidence, but grounded. And results, like my negotiation outcome. Also skill growth, like I learn how to communicate better.
Anna: Do you think trust can ever be too high, like overtrust, leading to bad outcomes?
Jonas: Yes. If you trust a mentor too much, you can take their path as yours. Especially if they are charismatic. That is risky. And with AI, overtrust is super risky because it can hallucinate. So I think appropriate trust is key, not just more trust.
Anna: Interesting. In your case, you described skepticism and verification.
Jonas: Yes. I am not a trusting person with systems. With humans, I can trust, but it takes time.
Anna: If you imagine an ideal support for your team lead path question, what would it look like?
Jonas: It would be someone who has seen different models. Like technical lead, staff engineer, engineering manager. And they can describe daily life, not just title. Like, what do you do on a Tuesday. How much code. How much people. And they can help me experiment, like take a temporary leadership responsibility without fully committing. And they can challenge my assumptions about creativity.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction (plus micro-poll ratings)

Anna: Now I will ask you for some quick ratings, 0 to 10. It is not a test, just to structure the conversation. And I will ask you to explain. Is that okay?
Jonas: Yes.
Anna: First, current trust in human coaches or mentors for career topics, 0 to 10.
Jonas: I would say, um, 7.
Anna: Why 7, not higher or lower?
Jonas: Because with the right person, I trust quite a lot. Like Markus, I would say 8 or 9. But in general, I do not automatically trust a coach. There is a whole market of coaches, and I do not know their incentives. And also, some people are just not good. So overall, 7.
Anna:Second, current trust in AI tools, 0 to 10.
Jonas: Overall trust, like as a system, maybe 4.
Anna: What drives the 4?
Jonas: Privacy concerns and hallucinations. Also, I do not know boundaries. For coding assistance, I trust specific outputs after checking, but the system trust is low.
Anna: Third, perceived effectiveness of human coaching or mentoring for your situation, 0 to 10.
Jonas: For my situation, maybe 6.
Anna: Why 6?
Jonas: Because it depends on finding the right person. When it works, it is very effective, like my negotiation. But it is not always available. People are busy. And it can be generic. So I cannot assume it will solve my team lead question. But it can help.
Anna: Fourth, perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, 0 to 10.
Jonas: For career situation, like leadership path, maybe 3. For technical growth, it is like 8. But you ask for my situation career-wise, so 3.
Anna: What makes it 3, not zero?
Jonas: Because it can still help with structured planning, CV, phrasing, brainstorming lists. But I avoid it mostly, so the impact is limited.
Anna: Thank you. Now, I want to describe a hypothetical hybrid model. Imagine a career support platform where AI helps you prepare, like clarifying goals, drafting questions, structuring options, and then you have sessions with a human mentor for deeper discussion and decisions. After the session, AI helps with follow-up plans and reminders. What is your reaction?
Jonas: That sounds, um, actually reasonable. Because AI as preparation and follow-up is less risky. The human part is where trust and emotional depth happens. But the key is privacy and boundaries. Like, what data does the AI see. If it sees my full story, I am not comfortable. If I can control what I input, maybe.
Anna: What would you need to trust such a hybrid model?
Jonas: Clear data boundaries. Like, ideally, AI runs locally or you have strong guarantees. And transparency. Also, I would want the human to be independent, not like sales. And I would want to choose what the AI stores. Like, delete button. And explainability, at least some. Like, why it suggests something.
Anna: And what would make it feel effective?
Jonas: If the human mentor is good and context-aware, and the AI helps reduce admin work. Like, I do not want to spend my mentor time writing lists. I want to talk. If AI can organize my thoughts, that is effective. Also, if it helps me do micro-experiments, like tasks to try leadership in small steps.
Anna: Do you have any concerns about a hybrid model?
Jonas: Yes. That it becomes worse of both. Like, AI collects data, human is limited. Or the human relies on AI too much and stops thinking. Or I get a generic plan again. Also, there is a risk of false confidence. If the AI writes a beautiful plan, I might think it is reality, but in a scale-up, reality changes weekly.
Anna: So you want adaptability.
Jonas: Yes. And also, I want creativity preserved. Like, leadership that still allows building things. If the hybrid model pushes me toward a pure manager role, I would reject it.
Anna: How would you define good leadership, in practice, that would still feel creative?
Jonas: Good question.I think good leadership in engineering can be creative if it is about shaping systems and people growth. Like, you design the environment. You mentor. You make decisions under uncertainty. That can be creative. But the fear is that it becomes bureaucracy, like just managing Jira tickets. So good leadership is not just meetings, but direction. Technical direction and also culture.
Anna: In your company, what does leadership look like right now?
Jonas: It is mixed. We have some leads who are like technical leaders. They still code, they do architecture, they mentor, they push quality. And we have some who are, um, more like project managers. They coordinate, but they do not have technical depth. In a fintech, that can be dangerous, because decisions have security implications. So the good ones, they combine technical competence with communication.
Anna: Do you see a path to become that kind of lead?
Jonas: Maybe. But it is not clearly supported. That is why I am thinking a lot. Like, do I push internally to become Staff Engineer, which is more technical leadership, or do I try Team Lead but set boundaries, like I still code. Or do I leave to a company with a better defined ladder.
Anna:If you could ask one question to an ideal mentor, what would it be?
Jonas: I would ask, what does your week look like and do you still feel creative. And what did you sacrifice. Because I think every path has sacrifice.
Anna: That is very honest. Earlier you said you want friction, someone to challenge you. Do you think AI could be designed to challenge you instead of confirming?
Jonas: In theory, yes. Like, if it is instructed to argue both sides, or to ask hard questions. But I am not sure I would believe it. Because it is still a system. With a human, the challenge feels more real. Like, they see you and they push you. AI pushing me feels like text.
Anna: So the relational element matters.
Jonas: Yes. And accountability. If I tell Markus, I will do this, next time he asks. AI can remind me, but it is not the same social pressure.
Anna: Do you ever feel judged by humans, and does that reduce trust?
Jonas: Yes. That is another thing. If a mentor is judgemental, I shut down. Like, if they say, why are you afraid, just do it. Then trust drops. Markus was not like that. He was calm.
Anna: And with AI, do you feel judged?
Jonas: No, but I feel observed. Hahaha. Like, the system is watching. Not emotionally, but data-wise.
Anna: That is a good distinction.
Jonas: Yeah.
9) Closing

Anna: We are near the end. Is there anything you expected me to ask but I did not?
Jonas: Maybe, um, about how I choose tools. Like, I am not anti-AI. I like it for work. But for career, I want to keep it human. At least for now. And I think many engineers are like that. We use AI as productivity, but not for life decisions.
Anna: That is very helpful. If you had one recommendation for someone building career AI tools, what would it be?
Jonas: Respect privacy by design. Not as marketing. And make it less agreeable. Make it challenge you, like a good mentor. And be honest about limitations. If it is uncertain, say it.
Anna: Thank you. Last check, are you still comfortable with the recording and use for anonymized research?
Jonas: Yes.
Anna: Great. Thanks Jonas. I will stop the recording now.
Jonas: Okay. Bye.
Anna: Bye.


Alina
1) Opening and consent

Anna: Hi Alina, can you hear me well?
Alina: Yes, hi Anna. I can hear you. Can you hear me?
Anna: Yes, perfectly. Thanks for joining. Before we start, I want to confirm consent. Do I have your permission to record this interview? The recording is only for transcription, everything will be anonymised, and you can skip any question.
Alina: Yes, you can record. That is okay.
Anna: Thank you. And just to check, are there any topics you would prefer not to discuss today?
Alina: Eehm. Maybe, like, very specific details about my employer, like the name or internal processes. But in general, we can talk about the work.
Anna: Absolutely. We will keep it at a general level, and if something feels too specific, just say stop or we can rephrase. Also, if I ask something and you are not comfortable, you can pass.
Alina: Okay, yes.
Anna: Great. One more thing, because we will talk about AI tools and coaching, I might ask you to give quick ratings from 0 to 10 at some point. It is not a test, just a way to structure the discussion.
Alina: Okay. Sounds fine.
Anna: Perfect. Then we can start. Can you briefly introduce yourself, just in terms of your current situation?
Alina: Sure.
2) Participant context and career situation

Anna: Where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now, or recently faced?
Alina: Um, so I am 29, I live in Frankfurt, I moved here from Ukraine four and a half years ago. I work in a bank, in a compliance team. And my situation is, like, stable. I have a stable contract, stable life, I have my apartment, I have friends here. But I feel, eehm, bored. Like, it is not crisis, it is not burnout. It is more like, I wake up and I know exactly what I will do. And it is fine, but it is not, how to say, meaningful. So my career question is how to pivot from compliance into ESG or sustainability reporting, maybe ESG risk, something like that. I am trying to understand how realistic it is, and what steps actually make sense in Germany.
Anna: When you say bored, what does that look like in your day-to-day? Like, what is missing?
Alina: It is difficult because the job is important, like compliance is important, but it feels very, um, procedural. Like, checklists, documentation, approvals. A lot of writing memos, following internal policies, and being very careful. And I like careful. I studied law originally, so I am okay with details. But I feel disconnected from impact. It is more about preventing risk for the bank, which is logical, but it is not like building something or contributing to, I do not know, society. It is a bit dramatic, but you asked what is missing. It is meaning and growth. Growth also because compliance can be a bit, how to say, narrow. You can become senior, you can become team lead, but the tasks stay similar.
Anna: What is your current role, like broadly? What do you actually do in the compliance team?
Alina: I work in a team that supports business lines, like corporate clients and sometimes investment products, but not trading. We do compliance advisory. So we review certain cases, we answer questions from stakeholders, we check if communications are okay, we support audits, we coordinate with risk and legal. A big part is documentation. Like, everything must be documented, why we approved something, what was the basis. There are internal controls, four-eyes principle, sometimes escalations. And there is always the idea that regulators can ask later. So you need to be able to show the trail.
Anna: What is the biggest career decision you made in the last 6 to 12 months?
Alina: Hahaha. The biggest one is that I decided to stay in my bank for now instead of switching to another bank or consulting. Because I had this moment where I thought, okay, maybe the boredom is because I am in the wrong company, not the wrong field. So I interviewed a bit, like not a lot, but I talked to recruiters and did two interviews for a compliance role in another institution. And then I realised it is the same, just different logo. So I decided, okay, if I want change, it needs to be a direction change, not just a company change.
Anna: What made that decision difficult?
Alina: Eehm, security. Germany is expensive, Frankfurt also. And I am not German, so stability matters. Also my residency situation is stable now, but I still think about it. Like, I have a residence permit tied to employment. I am on a skilled worker path, and I can apply for permanent residency soon, but I need stable employment and language level. So part of the decision is, do not do something too risky until I have that. And also the bank environment, it is very conservative. If you move around too much, people ask questions, like, why? And I am not sure if this is in my head, but yeah.
Anna: Can you share a bit more about your move to Germany and the residency context, as much as you feel comfortable?
Alina: Sure. I moved in 2021. It was, eehm, partly personal, partly professional. I had a partner then, in Germany. It did not work out long term, but the move was already done. Also I wanted a more international career, and in Ukraine I studied law, but the job market in law is, how to say, very local. Germany felt like a place where I could restart. I first came with a job seeker plan, but then I got an offer in a financial services company, not my current bank. And later I moved to the bank, because Frankfurt has a lot of these roles and I wanted to be closer to regulation and more structured career path.
Anna: What is your German level now?
Alina: Officially B2, but in reality, like, I can work in English in my team. Our internal documents are German, but many meetings are English. However, to move internally, sometimes they want German. And in compliance, German is important because you write policies, you communicate with local stakeholders. So I use German daily, but I still feel insecure. Like, I can do it, but it costs energy.
Anna: And what triggered your interest in ESG or sustainability reporting specifically?
Alina: There was one moment, actually. We had an internal project about new regulatory expectations, and one of the topics was ESG risk integration. Not the deep technical part, but the fact that regulators expect banks to consider climate risk, transition risk, reputational risk. And there was a presentation, and I remember thinking, finally, something that connects regulation with real world outcomes. Like, if a bank finances something, it matters. And also, I started reading more about EU regulation, like CSRD, EU taxonomy, SFDR. And it felt like a field that is new enough that I can enter, but still serious, still compliance-adjacent. It is not like I want to become, I do not know, a marketing person. It is still structured, but with more meaning.
Anna: Can you tell me about one specific episode at work where you felt bored or disconnected?
Alina: Yes. It was, um, a case where we had to review a client communication. It was a standard letter, like informing about fee changes. And we went back and forth for days because of wording. Like, one sentence about risks had to be in a specific order, and we had to ensure the German was perfect. And I get why. But after the third iteration, I thought, this is my life? And it was not intellectually challenging, it was just cautious. And then I compared it with the ESG topics where there are open questions, like how to interpret requirements, how to build reporting processes, how to coordinate data across departments. That felt more like building.
Anna: What have you tried already to move toward ESG or sustainability reporting?
Alina: A few things. I did a short online course, like a certificate, not university, more like a professional training about CSRD basics and sustainability reporting. I also joined a couple of webinars from Big4, because they do a lot in this area. And I started networking a bit on LinkedIn, like following ESG professionals, reading posts, and I had two informational calls with people. One was in sustainability consulting, one was in a bank ESG risk function. And internally, I asked HR about internal mobility, like what options exist. But the internal move is tricky because ESG roles are limited and sometimes they prefer people already in risk management, or they want German C1, or they want someone who already worked with data and reporting.
Anna: What feels like the biggest friction right now in the pivot?
Alina: Credibility. Like, how do I translate my compliance skills into ESG narrative? I can say I know regulation, I can work with stakeholders, I can document. But ESG reporting also needs understanding of metrics, data, frameworks. And I do not have real project examples yet. So I feel like, eehm, I am in this loop where you need experience to get experience. And also, politics. In the bank, if you want to move, your manager has to support you. And my manager is nice, but of course, they do not want to lose a trained person. So I feel I need to be careful how I communicate.
Anna: What constraints do you have outside of work? Like personal constraints that affect your options.
Alina: I cannot take a big pay cut. I support my family sometimes. Not like full support, but I help. Also, I do not want to move to another city now. Frankfurt is my base, I built my life. And I need time for German. Also, I am a bit tired of instability. When I moved, it was a lot of change. Now I want growth, but not chaos. I mean, no wait, let me rephrase. I want change, but in a controlled way.
3) Career support in general

Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Alina: Mostly friends. I have a friend who is also in financial services, and we talk. I also talk to my sister, but she is not in Germany, so she supports emotionally. And online resources, like reading, LinkedIn. And then AI tools, I use them for drafting and understanding texts. But for career decisions, I still prefer humans. Because there is, um, fear. Like, if I say wrong thing, or if I make wrong move, it affects my residency, my stability. So I want a human reflection.
Anna: What has actually helped, and what felt unhelpful?
Alina: Helpful is when someone challenges me a bit, but realistically. Like, my friend says, okay, you say you want ESG, but what exactly? In a bank it can be different. And she asked me to list roles: ESG compliance, sustainability reporting, ESG risk. That helped. Unhelpful is generic motivation, like follow your passion. Because passion does not pay rent. Also, some online advice is very US-centric, like just network and you will get a job. In Germany, networking is helpful but it is not magic. And also, I noticed that many career coaches speak very generally, they do not understand regulated environments.
Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed most in the last months?
Alina: A roadmap. Like, steps. Not only emotional reassurance. Steps like, what skills are missing, how to get them, which internal stakeholders to speak to, how to present my story. And also confidence. Because I doubt if I am, how to say, allowed to pivot. Like, I am an immigrant, I already changed field once, from law to compliance. And now I want another change. So I need both structure and emotional grounding.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust

Anna: You mentioned you tried a coach once. Can you tell me about that experience? How did you find the coach?
Alina: Yes. It was last year, like maybe spring. I found her through a recommendation from a friend in Frankfurt. She is a career coach, but also she had worked in HR in a financial institution before. That sounded good. We had, I think, three sessions. First was like getting to know me, second and third were more about my situation.
Anna: What made you decide to try coaching at that point?
Alina: I was feeling stuck. Like, I was complaining to friends, and I realised they cannot solve it. And I was also feeling anxious, like, is my boredom ungrateful? Because the job is stable. So I wanted a neutral person. Also, in Ukraine, coaching is becoming popular, and I thought, okay, maybe it can help.
Anna: What were the sessions like, practically? Like Zoom, in person, what did you do?
Alina: It was Zoom. She sent me a short questionnaire before, like values, goals. In the session, she asked many questions. It was very, um, reflective. Like, what do you want, what is your ideal day, what energises you. She was warm. She listened. She repeated back things. There was a lot of space for emotions. I cried once. Not like dramatic, but I was overwhelmed.
Anna: What created trust for you in that coaching experience?
Alina: Confidentiality. She explained that everything stays between us, and she had a professional vibe. Like, she did not push me to buy ten sessions. She was also empathetic, but not pity. She validated that boredom can be a sign that you outgrow a role. Also, she understood the bank culture a bit. Not deeply, but enough to know that compliance is sensitive and that internal moves are political. That made me feel she is not naive.
Anna: Were there any moments where you felt, okay, I can really open up?
Alina: Yes. In the second session, I told her about my fear of being seen as unstable. Like, in Germany, I sometimes feel I have to be perfect. Like, be grateful, be stable, do not complain. And she said something like, it is okay to want more, and it does not mean you are ungrateful. And she also asked about my identity, like, who are you beyond your job. That was emotional, but it felt safe.
Anna: And what did you hope to get from coaching, specifically?
Alina: Action. Like, I wanted to leave with a plan. Maybe not full plan, but at least clear steps. Like, which roles to target, how to position myself, what courses matter, how to approach internal stakeholders. I expected some of that because she had HR background.
Anna: Did you get that?
Alina: Not really.I got emotional relief. I felt lighter. I felt less alone. And I had some clarity on values, like I care about impact and learning. But when it came to market steps, it was vague. She said, explore, talk to people, update your LinkedIn. But I was like, okay, how? Who? Where? What is a realistic ESG entry role? Should I do a certificate like GRI? Should I learn data? Should I go to consulting? She did not have concrete answers. And she also did not give frameworks, like a networking map. It was more, trust the process.
Anna: So in your experience, it was trusted but not effective in the way you needed?
Alina: Yes, exactly. I trusted her as a person. I felt safe. But effectiveness, for my situation, was limited. Because my problem is partly emotional, but also very practical, like German job market, regulatory field, internal politics.
Anna: What would have made it more effective?
Alina: Industry-specific competence. Like, if she knew ESG roles in banks, or at least knew how to research them with me. Also structure. Like, give me homework that is not only journaling, but tasks like identify ten target roles, identify five people to talk to, draft a pivot story, revise CV for ESG, do interview practice. And also maybe accountability, like check progress.
Anna: Was there anything that reduced trust in that coaching, even if you felt safe overall?
Alina: Eehm, not reduced trust, but I noticed that sometimes she used very confident statements without evidence, like, you will find something if you follow your values. And I was like, maybe, but the market is hard. So I started to separate emotional truth from market truth. But I did not think she had bad intentions.
Anna: If you think about trust drivers for a human coach in general, what are the main ones for you?
Alina: Confidentiality, like clear boundaries. Empathy, but professional. Competence in my context, like understanding regulated environments, immigration context, German market. Structure, like a process, not random talking. And feeling seen, like the coach remembers details and connects patterns. Also realism. Like, do not just motivate me, but tell me what is hard and how to approach it.
Anna: And what are your indicators that human coaching is effective?
Alina: Clarity and steps. Like, I leave with decisions. Also, real market signals, like interviews or feedback from people in the field. And confidence, but grounded confidence, not just hype. Also reduced anxiety, because I feel in control.
5) AI tools: experience and trust

Anna: You also mentioned you use AI tools. Can you tell me what you use them for and how often?
Alina: I use AI almost daily, but not always. Mostly for drafting German texts. Like emails to stakeholders, summaries, sometimes policy wording, but I am careful. Also for understanding regulations, like if I read a long document and I need to understand the structure, definitions, or differences between terms. I use it like a study buddy.
Anna: Which kind of tool is it? Like a chatbot interface?
Alina: Yes, like a conversational tool. I do not want to say the brand, but it is basically a chatbot. Sometimes I also use internal tools, like translation software, but the AI chat is the main one.
Anna: What makes you cautious, you mentioned confidentiality?
Alina: Yes. Banking compliance is very sensitive. You cannot put client info, you cannot put internal cases. Even internal policies, sometimes you should not share externally. So I never paste confidential details. I anonymise. I write like, imagine a bank has scenario X, what is the general principle. Or I ask it to improve German style without context.
Anna: Can you give a concrete example of how you use it for German drafting?
Alina: Sure. For example, I need to write an email in German to a business stakeholder about a compliance requirement. My German is okay, but corporate German is, how to say, heavy. Like, sehr geehrte Damen und Herren, but also not too formal, and you need this passive voice, and you need to sound firm but polite. So I write a draft in simple German, then I ask the AI to rewrite in professional corporate German tone. It gives me options. I then adjust because sometimes it is too much, too long, too fluffy. But it helps.
Anna: And for regulations, what kind of questions?
Alina: Like, explain the difference between two concepts. Or summarise a regulatory text into bullet points. Or give me a structure for a memo. Sometimes I ask, what are typical compliance controls for topic X. Not specific to my bank, but general. Or I ask it to translate a paragraph and explain terms.
Anna: Can you describe one moment where AI helped a lot?
Alina: Yes. There was a time we had to write a short internal note about a regulatory update. The source was in German, but the audience was mixed, some colleagues prefer English. I needed to summarise quickly. I asked AI to help me structure the memo: background, key changes, impact, next steps. It gave a clean structure. Then I filled in with my content. It saved time. Also, it helped me find the right German phrases, like angemessen, unverzüglich, solche words that appear often.
Anna: And one moment where AI failed or felt risky?
Alina: It failed when I asked about interpretation of a regulation. Like, I asked about a specific requirement, and it answered very confidently, like, you must do X. And I thought, okay, but is this true? I checked the source, and it was more nuanced. It was not exactly wrong, but it oversimplified. And in compliance, oversimplification is dangerous. Also, hallucination risk. Sometimes it invents an article number or a reference. And if you trust it, you can make mistakes.
Anna: How did that affect your trust?
Alina: It made me trust it conditionally. Like, I trust it for language and structure, but not for final interpretation. I always verify. So it can be effective, but not fully trusted.
Anna: If you think about trust drivers for AI tools, what are they for you?
Alina: Usefulness, like it actually helps. Consistency. If it gives good drafts. Also transparency about limitations. I like when it says, check sources. But sometimes it does not. And privacy. Like, I do not want my data stored. So I keep it generic. And accuracy, especially with regulatory topics.
Anna: And effectiveness indicators for AI in your situation?
Alina: Speed. Clarity. Better language. Better structure. Less mental load. It helps me operate in German corporate environment. It also reduces anxiety because I feel I can draft something and not sound stupid. But effectiveness is limited for deep career decisions, because it does not know my full context, and I do not want to share sensitive details.
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks

Anna: I want to compare human coaching and AI via specific tasks. I will name a few typical career support tasks, and you tell me which one you would prefer and why. Is that okay?
Alina: Yes.
Anna: First task: clarifying options. Like, what roles exist, what pathways exist.
Alina: AI is good for brainstorming options. Like, list roles in ESG in banking, list skills. It can give a broad map. But I would prefer a human to refine it for Germany and for my profile. Because AI can list everything, but it does not tell you what is realistic. And also, a human can ask me questions to narrow.
Anna: Second task: deciding between options. Like, choosing whether to pivot now or later, internal move or external.
Alina: Human. Because it involves risk, emotions, and also my residency context. I do not want to put that in AI. And a human can sense fear, and also challenge me. AI can be too neutral, like here are pros and cons, but it does not help me commit.
Anna: Third task: planning concrete steps. Like a 3-month plan or 6-month plan.
Alina: This is interesting. AI can help structure a plan, like a template. But a human is better for accountability and realism. So maybe hybrid. Like AI drafts the plan, human reviews and adjusts, and then human holds me accountable.
Anna: Fourth task: CV positioning and LinkedIn profile, specifically for an ESG pivot.
Alina: AI is helpful for wording, especially in German. Like translating my compliance experience into sustainability language. But again, a human with market knowledge is needed to tell me what recruiters want, what keywords matter, what achievements to highlight. So also hybrid.
Anna: Fifth task: networking strategy. Like who to talk to, how to approach.
Alina: Human, because of nuance. In Germany, networking is not like, hey, can I pick your brain. It can feel awkward. A human can help with scripts and with cultural tone. AI can write a message, but it might sound unnatural or too American. Though you can prompt it, but still.
Anna: Sixth task: interview practice.
Alina: Human. Because you need feedback on how you come across. AI can simulate questions, but it cannot fully read me. Also, in compliance and ESG, there is a lot of nuance, and a human can see if I am convincing.
Anna: Seventh task: emotional support, like anxiety, self-doubt.
Alina: Human. Always. AI feels, um, empty for that. It can say supportive things, but it is not the same as feeling seen.
Anna: And what about understanding regulations for a new field like CSRD or taxonomy?
Alina: AI as a starting point, but verified. And also human, like training or expert, because you need correct interpretation. For learning, AI is good. For decision-making, no.
7) Trust–effectiveness link
Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about human coaching, how does trust affect whether it is effective?
Alina: Trust is like the gate. If I do not trust the coach, I will not share the real constraints. Like my fear about residency, or my financial obligations, or my insecurity about German. And then the coach can only give generic advice. So trust enables depth. And depth makes it effective. Also, if I trust the coach, I will actually do the homework. If I think the coach is not competent, I will ignore it.
Anna: Can you connect that to your coaching episode? How did trust and effectiveness interact there?
Alina: Yes. I trusted her, so I shared emotions and fears. That was effective for emotional relief. But because she lacked market competence, the coaching was not effective for steps. So trust alone was not enough. It was trusted but not effective for the practical part. I would say, trust enabled the emotional work, but effectiveness for career outcomes needed competence and structure.
Anna: And for AI tools, how does trust affect effectiveness?
Alina: I use AI even when I do not fully trust it. Like, I know it can be wrong, but it is still effective for drafting. So that is, how you said, effective but not fully trusted. I trust it as a tool, not as an authority. I trust it to generate text options, not to decide. And because trust is partial, I always verify. That verification step reduces risk but also takes time. So the effectiveness is limited by trust, but not zero.
Anna: So in your words, trust and effectiveness are separable?
Alina: Yes. For humans, trust is more connected because if you do not trust, you do not open up, and then nothing works. For AI, you can use it pragmatically without trust, because it is like a calculator, but with words. But you still need some trust to rely on it without checking.
Anna: If you think about a situation where you would not use AI because trust is too low, what would that be?
Alina: Anything confidential. Like a real compliance case with client details. Or something that could be used against me. Like discussing internal politics or naming people. I do not want that anywhere. So for sensitive discussions, I prefer human, because confidentiality is clearer. Though even with humans, you need to trust them.
Anna: And what about effectiveness. What would make you say, this support was effective?
Alina: If I make progress. Like I have a clear target role, I take steps, I get interviews, I build credibility. Or even if I do not get interviews yet, I have real market feedback from informational calls. And I feel less anxious because I know what I am doing.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction

Anna: I want to ask you about a hybrid model. Imagine a career support service where AI helps you prepare, like clarify options, draft documents, track steps, and then a human expert steps in for sensitive discussions, decisions, and accountability. What is your reaction?
Alina: That sounds ideal, actually. Because AI can do the heavy lifting for drafts and structure. And humans can do the judgment and emotional part. Also it reduces cost maybe, because you do not need many human sessions for basic things. But the key is, the human must be competent in my domain. Like, an ESG person or someone who knows banking and Germany.
Anna: What would make you trust such a hybrid model?
Alina: Clear boundaries. Like, what data goes to AI, what stays private. Maybe an option to keep everything local, but that is technical. Also transparency: who are the human experts, what is their background. And a process. Like, step 1 AI intake, step 2 human session, step 3 AI follow-up tasks, step 4 check-in. That would make me feel safe. And also confidentiality policies, like GDPR, data handling. In Germany, people care.
Anna: What would make you not trust it?
Alina: If it feels like marketing. Like, we have AI and it will change your life. Or if the human is too generic. Or if the AI gives advice that feels wrong but confident. And if they push me to share too much personal data.
Anna: What tasks would you want AI to lead, and what tasks would you want the human to lead?
Alina: AI leads drafting, translation, structuring, brainstorming. Human leads decisions, career narrative, networking strategy with cultural nuance, interview practice, and emotional support. Also, like, helping me interpret market signals. AI can list roles, but human can say, for you, this role is realistic if you do X.
Anna: Let us do the micro-poll ratings now. I will ask four ratings from 0 to 10, and I would like you to explain each briefly. First, your current trust in human career coaches, in general, for your situation. 0 is no trust, 10 is full trust.
Alina: I would say 7.
Anna: Why 7?
Alina: Because humans can be empathetic and confidential, and they can adapt. But I do not fully trust because many coaches are not competent in my niche. So I trust humans as humans, but I need to check their expertise.
Anna: Second, your trust in AI tools for your career-related needs. 0 to 10.
Alina: Maybe 5.
Anna: Why 5?
Alina: Because I trust it for drafting and structure, but not for deep advice. Also confidentiality concerns. And accuracy issues. So it is useful, but trust is limited.
Anna: Third, perceived effectiveness of human coaching for your situation. 0 to 10.
Alina: For my situation, I would say 4.
Anna: What makes it 4?
Alina: My experience was emotionally helpful, but not actionable. So effectiveness for my actual goal, pivot to ESG, was low. Maybe if I find the right coach, it can be higher. But based on what I saw, it is not very effective.
Anna: Fourth, perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation.
Alina: That is higher. I would say 7.
Anna: Why 7?
Alina: Because it helps me daily with German, with structuring, with learning. It makes me more productive and confident in communication. It does not solve my career pivot alone, but it supports me in practical tasks.
Anna: Interesting. So you have higher effectiveness for AI than for the human coaching you experienced, but lower trust.
Alina: Yes, exactly. That is the paradox.
Anna: If you imagine the ideal support for your ESG pivot, what would it look like in, say, three months?
Alina: I would have a clear target role. Like, I decide, okay, I aim for sustainability reporting under CSRD in a bank, or ESG risk. And I have a plan: courses, internal project involvement, maybe a small internal task force, networking calls. I would also have my CV updated with an ESG narrative. And maybe I would have at least one interview or at least two strong informational calls that lead to opportunities. And I would feel less like, I am dreaming, and more like, I am executing.
Anna: What would be the first step you want help with right now?
Alina: Narrowing the target. Because ESG is broad. I need help to choose the path that matches my compliance background. And then I need help to create credibility, like projects or certifications. And also, German. I know it is not sexy, but it matters.
Anna: When you think about hybrid, would you want the human to be a coach, like reflective, or more like a mentor, like insider?
Alina: More like insider. I think coaching style is nice, but I already have self-awareness. I need someone who knows the field, who can say, in Frankfurt, in banks, this is how ESG roles look, these are the hiring channels, these are the skills. Also, someone who can tell me honestly if my story makes sense.
Anna: And how should AI be positioned in that model, so that you are comfortable?
Alina: As assistant, not as authority. Like, it helps you prepare for the human session, it helps you draft, it tracks tasks. But it should not pretend it knows better than the human. Also it should have clear disclaimers, like verify sources, but in a calm way. And you should be able to choose what you share.
9) Closing

Anna: We are coming to the end. Is there anything important about your experience with human coaching or AI tools for career support that we did not cover, that you think matters?
Alina: Maybe one thing. In regulated environments, trust is already part of the culture. Like, we are trained to be cautious. So when people say, just use AI, it is not that simple. It is not only fear, it is professional responsibility. So for me, AI is helpful, but I always have this compliance brain saying, check, verify, do not share. And with humans, trust is also not automatic. You need someone who understands that mindset, otherwise they think you are just anxious.
Anna: That is very helpful. If you could give one advice to a designer of a career support service for people like you, what would it be?
Alina: Make it specific. Like, do not say career coaching for everyone. Make pathways for regulated professions, for immigrants, for Germany-specific context. And combine practical steps with emotional support. Because we are not robots. Even in compliance, there is emotion. We just hide it. 
Anna: Thank you. Last check, do you have any questions for me about the research or the interview?
Alina: Not really. I am curious what you find. But no questions now.
Anna: Great. Thanks again for your time and for being open. I will stop the recording now.
Alina: Thank you, Anna. Bye.
Anna: Bye.


Pierre  
1. Opening and consent
Anna: Hi Pierre, can you hear me well?
Pierre: Yes, I can hear you. Can you hear me?
Anna: Yes, perfectly. Thanks for joining. Before we start, do I have your permission to record? Everything will be anonymised, and you can skip any question.
Pierre: Yes, you can record. That is fine.
Anna: Great, thank you. And just to check, are there any topics you prefer not to discuss today?
Pierre: Um, no, I think it is okay. Maybe no very specific company secrets, like supplier names, but career stuff is fine.
Anna: Of course. We will keep it at a general level. Also, if at any point you want to pause, just tell me.
Pierre: Okay.
Anna: Cool. Then we can start with a bit of context about you and your current situation.
2. Participant context and career situation
Anna: Where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now?
Pierre: Eehm, so I am Pierre, I am 27. I am from France, from Lyon originally, and I moved to Stuttgart two years ago. I did a Master in Industrial Design. After that I worked one year in France, in a small studio, and then I got this job in Germany in a hardware company. It is like, not a startup, it is more established. Mid size but very German in the way it works. I am in product design, but also I coordinate with suppliers. So my role is half craft, half, like, coordination and industrialisation things.
Anna: When you say hardware company, what kind of products are you working on?
Pierre: We do components for mobility and also some industrial devices. Not full cars, but parts that go into systems. For example, housings, interface elements, sometimes a complete device like a charging module. It is very, how to say, manufacturing heavy. Lots of tolerances, testing, certifications, and supplier chain.
Anna: And what is the career dilemma you are facing at the moment?
Pierre: The big dilemma is, do I stay in this corporate style environment and grow slowly, or do I switch to a startup. And if I switch, what startup, what role. Like should I be product designer in a startup, or should I go more into product management, or like design engineering. And also I have this fear, like, if I go to a startup, it looks cool now, but later, does it look serious. Does it give credibility for, like, a long career, especially in Germany where people like stability.
Anna: What has made this dilemma urgent for you now, as opposed to one year ago?
Pierre: Um, good question. I think it is the slowness. I enjoy the craft and the design work, like sketching, CAD, making prototypes, even small details like surface finish, textures, how a knob feels. I love this. But the process is very slow. So I feel blocked. And then I see friends in startups who ship things faster, they have more ownership. And I also feel my learning is not as fast now as I want.
Anna: Can you give me a concrete example of the slowness, like a story that really shows it?
Pierre: Yes. I have one story that is very typical. We had a new revision of a housing for a module. It was not a huge change, it was like a small change for a connector position, but it impacts the injection mold. So we had to go through internal approvals, then purchasing, then quality, then the supplier. The supplier says, okay, change request, we need a new insert. Then purchasing wants three quotes. Then quality wants a risk assessment. Then there is a meeting, and in the meeting everyone says the same thing, but nobody decides.
Anna: Mm.
Pierre: So it took, I think, maybe ten weeks just to approve a change that in a smaller environment would be, like, okay, we do it, we adjust, we test. And the funniest part is, at the end, we still had a mistake because the requirements document was not updated properly. So we lost ten weeks and still had an issue. And I remember sitting there like, why am I spending my time on chasing signatures.
Anna: What did that do to your motivation?
Pierre: It makes me feel, um, like I am not a designer but a process manager. And I do not hate it, I understand it is needed in hardware, because you cannot just change like in software. But the amount of bureaucracy is too much. And also Stuttgart is very automotive culture. Like everything is planned, conservative, risk avoidance. Sometimes it is good, but it can kill creativity.
Anna: Where do you feel you are strongest right now in your role?
Pierre: I think I am strong in concept development and also in translating between design and engineering. Like I can talk to engineers, I can understand constraints, and then still keep a design intent. And with suppliers, I can communicate, like, what is the key parameter, what is the must-have, what can be flexible. So I am not only aesthetic.
Anna: And what feels hardest?
Pierre: The politics and the waiting. And also, eehm, the feeling that my career path is not clear. Like in Germany, there is a clear ladder for engineers sometimes. For designers in hardware, it is less clear. You can become senior designer, but then what. And because I am not German, I also feel I need to prove credibility.
Anna: How is your German now?
Pierre: It is okay for work, like B1, maybe low B2. I can do meetings, but sometimes I miss nuance. When people speak Swabian, forget it. I can do small talk, but when it is conflict or negotiation, English is easier for me. But in this region, German is important, especially in supplier discussions.
Anna: You mentioned status and credibility. Can you say more about what status means for you in this decision?
Pierre: Um, yes. It is a bit stupid maybe, but, like, the name of the company on your CV. In France also, but in Germany maybe more, people look at it. If you are in a known industrial company, it signals stability. If you are in a startup, people ask, is it serious, did you really do something, or is it chaos.
Anna: Is that coming from your own view, or from other people, like family, peers, LinkedIn?
Pierre: All of it. My family, they are like, you moved to Germany, good job, stable, good salary, why you want to risk. And then on LinkedIn, you see people move to startups and it looks modern, but you also see people come back and say, it failed. And I am like, if I move now, in two years, will I be able to come back to corporate if I want. Or will they see me as unstable.
Anna: What would success look like for you, if things go well?
Pierre: Success is, I am doing meaningful product work, shipping real things, not just PowerPoints. And I have a clear story. Like, I can explain why I did this path. And I feel my skills grow. Also, I want to be in a place where decisions are made faster, but still with quality. And maybe long term, I could lead a design team, or be a product lead for hardware, like owning a product line.
Anna: What is the biggest career decision you have made in the last six to twelve months?
Pierre: The biggest decision was, I started actively exploring startups. Not only thinking, but actually doing something. I joined events in Stuttgart, I talked to people, I started a side project to update my portfolio.
