And I also had a conversation internally about rotation, like maybe moving to a more innovation focused team. But that one, hmm, it did not really move.
Anna: What made it difficult to decide?
Pierre: Uncertainty. Like, what is the right startup, what is the right role. Also salary risk. Stuttgart is expensive. And visas are not an issue because I am EU, but still, stability matters. And then fear of making a wrong move and losing credibility.
Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed while making that decision?
Pierre: I needed someone who understands design careers in hardware, and understands Germany. And someone who can be honest, like tell me, Pierre, your portfolio is good but you need this, or your story is weak here. I needed structure, like steps, not only motivation.
3. Career support in general
Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Pierre: Um, I talk with friends, mostly designers. Some are in Berlin, some in Munich, some in Paris. I also talk with one colleague at work, but carefully, because you do not want to look like you want to leave. I also look at online resources, like articles, videos, and sometimes I use AI tools for certain tasks. Not really for self reflection, more for brainstorming.
Anna: What has actually helped you in the past, and what felt unhelpful?
Pierre: Helpful is when people give specific feedback. Like, not, follow your passion, but, your portfolio needs more manufacturing constraints, show how you work with suppliers, show DFM. Also helpful is when someone challenges your assumptions. Unhelpful is general advice, like, just apply everywhere, or just go to Berlin, like it is simple. And also unhelpful is when people project their own fear. Like family says, startup is dangerous, but they do not know the ecosystem.
Anna: In Stuttgart specifically, do you feel there are support structures or networks that matter?
Pierre: Yes, there are meetups, but it is different from Berlin. Stuttgart has this manufacturing gravity, like automotive suppliers, hardware culture. There are accelerators, there are innovation hubs, but it is still connected to big companies. Even startups often work with corporates. So the culture is more B2B, more industrial. That affects the kind of roles.
Anna: How do you approach networking there?
Pierre: Slowly. In France I am more direct. Here, people want to know you over time. I go to events, I talk, but I also follow up on LinkedIn, like, danke for the conversation, and then maybe coffee. It is slow but it works.
Anna: Any professional services, like formal career coaching, university career center, anything like that?
Pierre: I did not do paid career coaching. But I had mentoring through an accelerator program, like a program connected to innovation, where they match mentors. That was the closest to coaching, and it was very helpful.
Anna: We will go into that. Before that, one more question. When you think about career support, what do you wish existed, like an ideal support?
Pierre: Something that combines honest human feedback with structured tools. Like, you can prepare with tools, gather options, but then a human helps you with nuance, and with the story. And also accountability, because I procrastinate sometimes.
Anna: Okay. Let us move into your mentoring experience.
4. Human coaching: experience and trust 
Anna: Tell me about the mentoring you had through the accelerator program. What was the situation and what did you work on?
Pierre: So, about one year ago, I joined an accelerator style program, not as a founder, more like as a participant in an innovation track. It was connected to hardware and mobility. They offered mentoring. I was matched with a mentor who had worked in product development, like design and product strategy, in Germany and also internationally. He was older, maybe in his 40s, and very direct.
Anna: How many sessions did you have?
Pierre: Three sessions over, like, two months. And some messages in between.
Anna: What made it work for you?
Pierre: The honesty. Like, first session, I showed my portfolio, and he said, eehm, your work is good, but your story is too safe. You show nice renders, but you do not show what you own. And he asked me, what do you want, like really. And I started with corporate polite answers, like, I want to grow, I want impact. And he interrupted and said, no, what do you want, specifically. And I was like, okay, I want to design products that ship, and I want to be in a faster environment. And he said, then your current narrative does not match.
Anna: That sounds quite confronting. How did you experience that?
Pierre: At first, I was defensive. I was like, who is he to judge. But then, I felt he was not attacking me, he was just precise. And he had credibility, because he knew the industry. He talked about supplier lead times, manufacturing gates, he understood. So I trusted him.
Anna: Can you recall a vivid moment, something he said that felt especially honest or helpful?
Pierre: Yes. There was a moment where I said, I want to go to a startup but I am afraid it looks bad later. And he said, you are mixing fear and strategy. He said, prestige is not a plan. And I was like, ouch. But he explained. He said, you need to define what skills you want, what story you want, and then choose environments that build it. Prestige will come from coherence, not from logos. He said something like, if you go to a small company and you can explain your impact, it is stronger than being a small person in a big logo.
Anna: What did that change for you after the session?
Pierre: I started to redesign my portfolio. Not redesign like visuals, but content. I added one case study that shows supplier coordination. Like, I show the iterations, the constraints, the decisions. I also started writing short explanations of trade offs. And I also changed how I talk about my role. Before I was like, industrial designer. Now I say, industrial designer in hardware with supplier coordination and production readiness.
Anna: Did you take any action beyond portfolio changes?
Pierre: Yes. He told me to have one informational interview per week with someone in a startup environment, but in hardware, not software. So I did that for maybe six weeks. And it helped. I talked to someone in an e-mobility startup, someone in a medical device startup, someone in a robotics company. And I learned, roles are very different.
Anna: What did you learn about roles that mattered to your decision?
Pierre: That startup product designer can mean many things. Sometimes it is mostly CAD and prototypes, very hands-on. Sometimes it is UX and interfaces, more digital. Sometimes it is a mix with product management. So I realized I need to be careful. I do not want to become only a graphic person. I want to stay close to physical product.
Anna: What were the main drivers of trust for you with this mentor?
Pierre: Competence, first. He understood my context, hardware, Germany. Second, honest feedback, like he did not just comfort me. Third, confidentiality. He made it clear he is not connected to my company, and what we talk stays there. Fourth, he saw me as a person. He asked about my move to Germany, language, family pressure. And also structure. He gave tasks, like do this by next time, and we review.
Anna: Was there anything that reduced trust at any point?
Pierre: Not really. Maybe sometimes he was too fast. Like he assumes you can just do things. But overall, trust was high.
Anna: Did you ever have an experience where you trusted someone, but it was not effective?
Pierre: Yes. A colleague at work, he is very kind, very supportive. He says, Pierre, you are talented, you should go for internal promotion. He listened, he cared, so I trust him emotionally. But it was not effective because his advice was like, wait, be patient, it will come. And maybe it is true in corporate, but it did not give me steps. It kept me in the same loop. So I trusted him, but it did not change anything.
Anna: That distinction is helpful. When you think of effectiveness for the mentoring, how would you define it? What were your indicators?
Pierre: Effectiveness is, after sessions, I had clearer targets. Like I could say, okay, I want hardware startup in mobility or medical, not just any startup. And I had concrete actions: update portfolio, do informational interviews, prepare a story. Also I felt more confidence because I had a plan. So effectiveness is clarity plus action plus confidence.
Anna: Did mentoring influence your actual decisions, like did you apply somewhere?
Pierre: I have not signed anything, but I did start applying. Like, I applied to two roles, and I got one first interview. It did not go further, but it was a good test. And I changed how I present myself, thanks to the mentor.
Anna: What happened in that interview, if you can share at a high level?
Pierre: It was a robotics startup. They asked, why you leave corporate. Before I would say, I want faster pace. Now I said, I want higher ownership and shorter feedback loops, and I have experience bringing design to production, which is valuable in hardware. And they reacted well. They said, okay, you understand constraints. So that was a result.
Anna: Sounds like the mentor influenced both your materials and your narrative.
Pierre: Yes.
5. AI tools: experience and trust 
Anna: Let us talk about AI tools. What have you used, and in what situations related to your career or work?
Pierre: I use AI mostly for ideation prompts and naming concepts. Like, if we have a new module and we want internal concept names, or if I am brainstorming themes for a design direction. Sometimes also for writing, like to clean up an email in English or German. But not so much for self reflection. Because I feel it flattens nuance.
Anna: Can you walk me through a concrete moment where AI helped you a lot?
Pierre: Yes. We had a workshop where we needed to propose three concept directions for a new device. It was like an industrial device that also has a user interface, like a small screen, buttons, indicator lights. And the brief was confusing. Many stakeholders, marketing, engineering, sales. Everyone wants different things.
Anna: That sounds familiar to me 
Pierre: Yes. So I used AI to generate, like, categories. I gave constraints: industrial environment, gloves, dust, need readability, and also brand values. And I asked, propose concept themes with adjectives and quick rationale. It gave me like, rugged clarity, calm precision, modular honesty, things like that. Some were generic, but some helped me create a structure. Then I took the best ones and made sketches. So it helped me go from chaos to a frame.
Anna: So it was more like a structuring and variation tool.
Pierre: Exactly. Speed and variation.
Anna: And naming concepts?
Pierre: Yes, naming is funny. We need internal names sometimes for prototypes. AI is good for that. Like you say, give me names inspired by Swabian engineering and simplicity, and it gives you lists. Some are ridiculous, but some work. And it saves time.
Anna: Can you think of a moment where AI did not help, or even made it worse?
Pierre: Yes. I tried to use it for career reflection once. I wrote a long prompt about my dilemma, corporate vs startup, fear of prestige, Stuttgart culture. And it responded with very generic advice, like, consider your values, make a pros and cons list, network, follow your passion. And I was like, yes, I know. It felt like a blog post. It did not catch the nuance, like the specific signaling in German hardware.
Anna: What nuance did you feel was missing?
Pierre: The cultural nuance. Like in Stuttgart, startups are often connected to corporates. So it is not like Berlin. Also the design nuance, like in hardware, you cannot just iterate weekly. And also personal nuance, like my identity as a French person here, language, credibility. AI makes it smooth, but too smooth.
Anna: How would you describe your trust in AI tools for these different tasks?
Pierre: I trust AI for speed and for brainstorming, like generating options. But I do not trust its taste. Like in design, taste matters. AI suggestions can be same-y, like average. And I do not trust it for high stakes decisions, because it does not know the real constraints, and it can be confidently wrong.
Anna: Do you have concerns about privacy or data when using AI?
Pierre: Yes, a bit. At work, we are careful. We do not put confidential details. For personal career, I also do not want to put too much personal detail. But my main issue is not privacy, it is quality and nuance.
Anna: Did you ever use AI for job applications, like CV wording?
Pierre: A little bit. I used it to rewrite bullet points, like make it more concise. That was helpful. But I had to correct, because it sometimes makes claims too strong. Like it says, led cross functional teams, and I am like, no, I collaborated, I did not lead.
Anna: That is an interesting trust boundary.
Pierre: Yes, it tends to exaggerate.
Anna: Can you think of an example where AI was effective for you, but you did not fully trust it?
Pierre: Yes. The naming example. We needed a name for a concept, internal only, but it mattered for the presentation because it frames perception. AI gave a name that actually worked, it fit the concept. We used it. It was effective. But I did not fully trust it because it felt shallow, like it did not understand the deeper design intent, it just combined words.
Anna: That fits the idea of effective but not fully trusted.
Pierre: Exactly.
Anna: And the opposite, trusted but not effective, you already mentioned your colleague.
Pierre: Yes.
6. Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks
Anna: I want to compare human mentoring and AI tools through specific tasks. I will name a task, and you tell me what you would prefer and why. First task: clarifying career options. Like, what roles, what industries, what paths.
Pierre: For clarifying options, I would start with AI, because it is fast. Like, give me a map of roles in hardware startups, differences between industrial designer, mechanical design engineer, product manager, design strategist, whatever. It can give a good first overview. But then, I would want a human to validate and contextualize. Because AI can list roles, but it cannot tell you how it really looks in Stuttgart, or in a specific company.
Anna: Second task: making a decision, like corporate vs startup.
Pierre: Human. Because that is not only information, it is identity and fear. A human can challenge you, and also read between lines. AI cannot see when I am rationalizing. A mentor can say, you are using prestige as an excuse, or you are avoiding risk. AI is polite, it does not confront you in the same way.
Anna: Third task: building a concrete plan for the next six weeks.
Pierre: Hmm. Hybrid. AI can help structure, like a checklist: update portfolio, target list, networking, interviews, timeline. But a human can prioritise. Like, Pierre, stop perfecting visuals, focus on story. And a human can hold you accountable.
Anna: Fourth task: rewriting your CV and cover letter.
Pierre: AI can help with language, especially in English or German. But I would not trust it fully. I would do AI draft, then human review, maybe a mentor or friend. Because AI can make it generic, like everyone.
Anna: Fifth task: portfolio critique.
Pierre: Human, definitely. Portfolio is taste, narrative, what to show, what not. AI can give general tips, but not a deep critique. A mentor who has hired designers is best.
Anna: Sixth task: ideation for a side project, like a portfolio piece.
Pierre: AI is very useful for ideation prompts, because it can push you out of your default. Like, propose project themes combining sustainability, mobility, circular design, whatever. But then I choose and shape it. So AI for sparks, human for meaning.
Anna: If you had to summarise, what is AI good at, and what is it bad at, for you?
Pierre: AI is good at speed, variation, structure, language cleaning. Bad at nuance, taste, context, and emotional truth. And also it can be too generic, like it averages.
Anna: And humans?
Pierre: Humans are good at seeing you, being honest, context, and credibility. But humans are slow, expensive, sometimes biased, sometimes inconsistent. And some humans are nice but not useful.
Anna: Aha, thank you 
7. Trust–effectiveness link
Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about human mentoring, how does trust affect whether it is effective?
Pierre: Trust is like the entry ticket. If I do not trust the mentor, I will not share the real fears, like the prestige anxiety, family pressure, or that I feel stuck. Then the mentor can only give surface advice, like improve your CV. So trust enables depth. And depth makes it effective.
Anna: What creates that trust, specifically?
Pierre: For humans, it is competence and honesty. Competence means they know the domain, like hardware careers, not just general coaching. Honesty means they tell you what you need to hear, not what you want. Also confidentiality. And feeling seen, like they remember details, they understand my context. And structure, like there is a process.
Anna: Does trust always lead to effectiveness?
Pierre: Not always. Like with my colleague, I trust him, but his advice was not actionable. So trust is necessary, but not sufficient. You can trust someone and still not get results.
Anna: And can you have effectiveness without trust?
Pierre: Yes, like AI naming. Or even with humans, maybe a recruiter gives you a tip that works, but you do not trust their motives, because they want to place you. Still, the tip can be useful. So effectiveness can happen without full trust, but it is limited.
Anna: How does trust affect your willingness to act on advice?
Pierre: If I trust, I act more. Because I believe the advice fits me. If I do not trust, I might still use it, but I treat it like input, not guidance. Like, I take it, but I do not commit.
Anna: And for AI, how does trust relate to effectiveness?
Pierre: For AI, trust is more like, do I believe it is accurate for this task. If I trust it for brainstorming, it is effective because I use it. But for self reflection, I do not trust it, so I do not use it, so it is not effective. Also, if it gives generic answers, trust goes down quickly.
Anna: That makes sense. Do you notice any pattern where AI becomes more trusted over time?
Pierre: Slightly, for specific tasks. Like, I learned what prompts work. So I trust it more in a narrow scope. But not as a partner. More like a tool.
Anna: If you imagine an AI tool designed specifically for career coaching, would that change trust?
Pierre: Maybe, if it is more specialized, like it knows the German market, roles, and it is transparent. But still, I would want a human for big decisions.
8. Preferences and hybrid model reaction
Anna: I want to include a short micro-poll, just quick ratings from 0 to 10. 0 means no trust at all, 10 means complete trust. First: your current trust in human coaches or mentors for career topics. What number comes to mind?
Pierre: For human mentors, I would say 8.
Anna: Why 8, not 10?
Pierre: Because it depends on the person. My mentor was great, but not everyone is. Some coaches are generic or too motivational. So I trust humans if they have competence and honesty. But I do not trust the category automatically.
Anna: Second: trust in AI tools for career related topics.
Pierre: Maybe 4.
Anna: Why 4?
Pierre: Because I trust AI for certain tasks, like brainstorming or language, but not for decisions. And I do not trust nuance. So overall, moderate low.
Anna: Third: perceived effectiveness of human mentoring for your situation right now, 0 to 10.
Pierre: Effectiveness, I would say 7.
Anna: Why 7?
Pierre: Because my mentoring helped, it gave me actions and clarity. But it was limited time, three sessions. And it does not remove the structural reality, like corporate slowness or market uncertainty. So it was effective, but not a magic solution.
Anna: Fourth: perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation right now.
Pierre: For my situation, maybe 5.
Anna: Tell me more.
Pierre: AI is useful as a support tool. It helps me create options, structure, write better. That is effective in small ways. But for the main dilemma, it is not deeply helpful. So it is in the middle.
Anna: Thank you. Now, let us talk about preferences. If you could design an ideal support setup for yourself, would you choose human only, AI only, or hybrid?
Pierre: Hybrid.
Anna: What would hybrid look like for you, concretely?
Pierre: I would want AI first for preparation. Like, I input my background, my constraints, and it helps me map options, prepare questions, generate drafts for CV, maybe even propose a plan. Then I want a human mentor to review, challenge, and adapt it to me. And then maybe AI for follow up, like reminders, templates, and tracking progress. Like, okay, you said you will do one informational interview this week, here is a message draft, here is a checklist. That would be great.
Anna: What would you not want AI to do in that hybrid model?
Pierre: I do not want AI to pretend empathy. Like, I hate when it says, I understand how you feel, but it does not. It feels fake. I would prefer it to be transparent, like, I am a tool, here are options. Also I do not want AI to make big recommendations like, quit your job. That feels dangerous.
Anna: What would you want the human to do that AI cannot?
Pierre: The human should do the deep conversation, the confrontation, the nuance, the reading between lines. Also taste and portfolio critique. And also credibility. Like if a mentor tells me, in Stuttgart hardware, this move makes sense, I believe it more.
Anna: Are there any situations where you would prefer AI over a human, even if a human is available?
Pierre: Yes. For quick tasks. Like I need a cover letter draft at midnight. Or I want to brainstorm side project ideas quickly. Or I do not want to bother a human with small questions. Also sometimes with a human, I feel judged. With AI, you can ask stupid questions.
Anna: Interesting. So psychological safety can be higher with AI sometimes?
Pierre: For small things, yes. Because it is not a person. But for big things, I want a person.
Anna: And when would you prefer human only, no AI?
Pierre: When it is about identity, like what kind of life I want, what kind of work environment. Also when I feel emotional, like fear. AI responses feel flat. A human can respond to emotion.
Anna: You said earlier you worry about startup credibility later. If a mentor says, this startup is good, it will not hurt your CV, would that reduce your anxiety?
Pierre: Yes, because it gives me an external validation. But I also know mentors can be biased. So I would want multiple signals. Still, it helps.
Anna: In your ideal hybrid, how would trust be built?
Pierre: For the human, through competence and honesty. For AI, through consistency and transparency. Like, it shows where information comes from, it admits limits, it does not hallucinate. And maybe it can show examples of people with similar profiles, anonymized, like paths. That would increase trust.
Anna: And how would you know it is effective?
Pierre: If I get clearer targets, like I can name three roles and three types of companies. If I improve my portfolio and get interviews. If I feel less stuck. If I can take actions weekly. And if I feel my story is coherent.
9. Closing
Anna: Before we close, is there anything important about your experience with human mentoring or AI tools for career support that we did not cover?
Pierre: Maybe one thing. In hardware, the stakes are different. Like, mistakes cost money and time. So career advice also needs to respect that. AI tools often feel like software mindset. But hardware is slower, and that is okay. So for me, the right support is not to say, move fast and break things. It is more like, move with intention.
Anna: That is a really clear point. Anything else?
Pierre: Also Stuttgart is special. It is not the most sexy startup city, but there are good companies. The path can be hybrid too. Like corporate innovation teams, or startups connected to corporates. So I am exploring that.
Anna: Great. Thank you, Pierre. Last check, are you still comfortable with everything we discussed being used anonymously for research?
Pierre: Yes, totally.
Anna: Perfect. Thanks again for your time. I will stop the recording now.




Noura

Noura: Yes. I am 23, and I live in Düsseldorf.
Anna: Perfect. Then we can start.


2) Participant context and career situation

Anna: To begin, where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing, or recently faced?
Noura: Okay. Um, so I am finishing my Ausbildung, and at the same time I do part-time studies, business administration. It is one of those dual-ish paths, but not the classic duales Studium, it is more like, Ausbildung in the company and then evening classes, weekend classes. So it is a lot. I work as HR coordinator in a mid-size company here in Düsseldorf. We are like, not a startup, more like, established, maybe around 700 employees in Germany, and then also some international locations.
Anna: And your Ausbildung is in which occupation?
Noura: It is Kauffrau für Büromanagement, but I am placed in HR. So my official apprenticeship title is office management, but practically I am in HR operations. I chose it because I wanted something stable and also because my grades after school were okay, but I was not sure yet what I want to do long-term. And the company offered me HR placement, so I was like, yes, this is interesting.
Anna: What does your current day-to-day look like in that HR coordinator role?
Noura: Eehm. A lot of scheduling. Like, interviews, onboarding appointments, Betriebsarzt appointments, trainings, and also contracts. Not writing the whole contract alone, but preparing templates, checking if all documents are there, sending them to the manager for signature, tracking if the person returns it. And HR system stuff, like data entry in our HRIS, updating addresses, adding working hours, sick leave notes, Urlaub, all that. Plus many emails. Stakeholders write to HR like, can you please urgently do this, and everything is always urgent. Also I support onboarding days. Like the first day, I prepare the welcome package, access badge, laptop request, and I coordinate with IT. And then sometimes I help with training logistics, like booking rooms or Teams links, sending reminders.
Anna: That sounds like a lot of operational pressure.
Noura: Yes. And because I am still Ausbildung, people sometimes treat me like, you are just assisting, but then they still expect perfect accuracy. Like, if one number is wrong in a contract, it is a big problem. And it is stressful because I am also studying. So I do work full-time, then I have two evenings of lectures, and sometimes Saturday. And then I try to also learn extra things because I want to move into people analytics or learning and development. So it is like, three jobs. 
Anna: You mentioned people analytics and learning and development. What drew you to those directions?
Noura: Um. People analytics first, because I noticed in HR there is so much talking and opinions. Like, managers say, oh we have too much sick leave because young people are lazy. And I am like, maybe it is the shift schedule, maybe it is workload, maybe it is leadership. And I like the idea that you can look at data and make a better argument. Also I like Excel. I know it is nerdy, but I like building tables, making it clean, seeing patterns.
And L and D, I am also interested because I like helping people learn, but I am insecure about facilitation. Like if I stand in front and teach, I get nervous. So L and D is attractive, but also scary. People analytics feels like, I can build credibility through numbers. But then I also feel like, do I really have the skills?
Anna: When you say you feel insecure about skills, what is the main insecurity?
Noura: That I lack real expertise or credibility. Like, I am HR coordinator, but I am not HR Business Partner. I do not do strategy. I do not sit in leadership meetings. So sometimes when I think, oh I want to do people analytics, I am like, who am I to say anything? I do not even have access to all the data because of Datenschutz and because I am junior. And then I see people on LinkedIn with titles like People Analytics Manager, and they have masters, they have statistics, they have Python, and I am like, I am just doing onboarding and contracts. So I feel behind.
Anna: What has been the most significant career decision in the last six to twelve months?
Noura: Hmm. The biggest one was deciding to not just stay in HR admin, but to actively ask for a project in analytics. Like, in summer, I had a moment where I was like, okay, I can finish Ausbildung and then just continue as HR admin, and it is stable. My parents would like that, stable job, good company. But I felt like, if I do not move now, I will be stuck. So I asked my HR manager if I can support a project about turnover and onboarding quality. It was not full people analytics, but it included some data. Like, we looked at how many people leave within the first six months, in which departments, and we tried to identify patterns.
Anna: How did it feel to ask for that?
Noura: Scary. Because I thought they will think I am annoying or I am overstepping. And also I was afraid they will ask me questions and I cannot answer. But actually she reacted okay. She said, yes, we can try, but keep it small and make sure it does not affect your operational tasks. So I did it on top. That is the story of my life.
Anna: What made the decision difficult?
Noura: Uncertainty. Like, is it realistic to go into people analytics without a full university degree first? And also fear of mistakes. If I do a report and it is wrong, it will be embarrassing. And pressure because of studies. And also sometimes family expectations, not heavy, but like, they want me to finish something and have security. My parents are from Syria, they came to Germany, they worked hard. So stability is important. They are proud that I have Ausbildung and I study, like, this is very German path, very respectable. But when I say I want to do something like analytics, they are like, okay, but what job is that exactly? They want a clear label.
Anna: And what are you aiming for in the next six to twelve months?
Noura: Finish Ausbildung, for sure. It ends in summer. And then I will continue my part-time studies, maybe I have like, one and a half years left. In the company, I want either a junior role with some development, like junior HR specialist with analytics tasks, or maybe I apply externally for a People Analytics Assistant role, something like that. But I am not sure. I also want to do at least one certification. I was thinking either Power BI or basic SQL. Because I already use Excel, but I want to upgrade.
Anna: So the main questions are, stay internal versus apply outside, and which focus, people analytics versus L and D?
Noura: Yes. Exactly. And also, how to be taken seriously.
3) Career support in general

Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Noura: Eehm. Friends a little bit, but my friends are also in Ausbildung or studies, so they are also confused. My family gives emotional support, like, they say you can do it, but they do not know HR market. At work, I sometimes ask one colleague, she is like HR generalist, she is friendly. But I also do not want to bother her too much.
Online resources, yes. Like blogs, YouTube, sometimes German resources like Karrierebibel, but it can be very generic. And then LinkedIn, I tried outreach to mentors. Mixed results. And AI tools, I use them a lot. Mainly for structuring learning plans and preparing interview answers. And also journaling with AI when I am stressed. It feels non-judgmental. I can say anything without worrying about social acceptance.
Anna: When you think about what has actually helped, and what has been unhelpful, what comes to mind?
Noura: Helpful is when it is concrete. Like, someone says, do this next, and explains why. Unhelpful is when it is motivational, like, believe in yourself, and then nothing else. Or when people judge. Like, if someone says, why did you not do university directly, why Ausbildung, like they imply it is less. That makes me shut down.
Anna: In your support sources, do you see any patterns about what you trust?
Noura: I trust when someone understands my context. Like, Germany context, Ausbildung, part-time studies. If someone is like, just do a master abroad, I am like, okay, but that is not helpful for me. And I trust when they do not talk down to me.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust
Anna: Let us talk about the human side first. You mentioned LinkedIn outreach to mentors. Can you walk me through what you did and what happened?
Noura: Sure. Um, I started last year, around October, I think. I was seeing many posts about mentoring, and I felt like, okay, I do not have a network. So I searched for people analytics roles in Germany. I filtered by Düsseldorf, Köln, sometimes Berlin because many roles are there. And then I looked for people who maybe also did Ausbildung or who wrote about non-traditional paths. Then I wrote messages like, hi, I am Noura, I work in HR, I am doing Ausbildung plus part-time studies, I am interested in people analytics, could I ask you two questions or could we have a short call.
Anna: How many did you reach out to, roughly?
Noura: Maybe like, 20? Over time. Not all in one day. Sometimes I wrote three, then I waited. Many did not reply.
Anna: Let us start with a positive interaction. Can you describe one contact that felt helpful and trustworthy?
Noura: Yes. There was one woman, she worked as People Analytics Specialist in a big retail company. Based in Köln. She replied after two days, which already felt like, wow. She said, yes, I can do a 20-minute call, but please send me your questions first, so we can focus. That felt professional. And also she wrote in German, but with a friendly tone, like, klar, machen wir.
Anna: What happened in the call?
Noura: We had a Teams call. I was nervous. She started with, tell me your story. And I explained, Ausbildung, HR coordinator, part-time studies. And I said I feel not credible. And she said, listen, this path is not a weakness. In Germany, Ausbildung is valued. And HR operations gives you understanding of processes and data quality, which is crucial for analytics. She made it sound like, you already have something relevant.
Anna: That sounds validating. What made it feel trustworthy beyond validation?
Noura: She was specific. She asked what HR system we use, what kind of data I touch, like, time tracking, absences, onboarding, recruitment pipeline. Then she said, okay, based on this, your first analytics projects can be descriptive, like dashboards for onboarding and turnover. She explained data access challenges. She said, in Germany, Datenschutz is big, so you need to learn how to phrase questions. Like not, give me individual data, but aggregated patterns, and always ask, what decision will this support. That was super practical.
Anna: Did she give you concrete next steps?
Noura: Yes. She said, for the next three months, pick one tool and go deep. For example, Power BI plus basic SQL. She even suggested a learning order: first understand data tables and joins in SQL, then practice with sample HR datasets, then build dashboards. She also said to do a small internal project and document it, like a mini portfolio. And she offered to review my plan in one month by message. That follow-up offer made me trust her more, because it was consistent.
Anna: What changed for you after that interaction?
Noura: I felt calmer. Like, okay, there is a path. And I asked my manager for that turnover project partly because of this call. Also I started learning SQL basics. I used AI to make a plan, but her advice gave the direction. She also told me which skills are actually used day-to-day, not just trendy. Like, she said, do not panic about Python at the beginning. Excel, SQL, Power BI, and communication is enough for entry.
Anna: Great. Now let us talk about a frustrating interaction.
Noura: Yes, there was one guy. He was like Head of People Analytics something. Very impressive profile. I wrote him a message, and he replied like, sure, send your CV, I can give feedback. I was excited. I sent it. Then he wrote back, very short, like, your profile is weak, you need a university degree, and HR admin is not real HR. It was harsh. And he said, also you should do internships in analytics, otherwise no chance.
Anna: How did that land with you?
Noura: Bad. I cried a bit. Not like, dramatic, but I felt ashamed. Because it confirmed my fear. And the tone felt judging. He did not ask questions. He did not understand that I am already working full-time. He just compared me to a standard path. Also he never answered again after that. Like, he dropped the bomb and disappeared.
Anna: That is a strong example. What did it do to your willingness to seek human mentoring?
Noura: It made me hesitant. I was like, okay, maybe I should not ask people. It felt like, if I am not already perfect, they will judge. And also, ghosting is common. Some people say, yes, let us schedule, then they never send times. Or they reply with generic lines like, keep learning, network more, and that is it. That feels like, not effective.
Anna: When you think about trust drivers for humans, based on these experiences, what stands out?
Noura: For humans, trust is about feeling safe. Like, I can share insecurity without being laughed at. Confidentiality also, like, I do not want my colleagues to know I am planning to leave. With a LinkedIn mentor, it is weird, because you do not know them. But still, you want discretion. Empathy, but also being taken seriously. Practical knowledge, like understanding Germany HR market, privacy rules, what skills matter. And consistency, like if they say they will follow up, they do.
Anna: And what about distrust drivers?
Noura: Judgment, obvious. Also when they are too vague. Or when they push their own agenda, like, they want to sell coaching or courses. And also mismatch, like they are in Berlin startup world and they cannot relate to Düsseldorf corporate HR.
Anna: You mentioned earlier that sometimes someone can be kind but not helpful. Do you have an example?
Noura: Yes. There was a woman, she was super kind. She wrote long messages like, you are doing great, you are strong, I believe in you. Very sweet. But when I asked, okay, how do I move into people analytics, she said, just follow your passion, apply and it will happen. That was trusted in a way, because she was safe and kind. But it was not effective. I ended the chat feeling warm, but still lost.
Anna: That is a clear trusted but not effective example.
Noura: Yes, exactly.
5) AI tools: experience and trust
Anna: Let us shift to AI tools. You said you have experience using AI for learning plans and interview answers, and also for journaling when stressed. Can you describe how you use it in practice?
Noura: Sure. I use, um, like conversational AI tools, mostly ChatGPT. Sometimes also the one in Microsoft, but mostly ChatGPT. I use it in English, sometimes in German. My English is okay, especially for work topics. German is my everyday language, but for structured stuff, I sometimes prefer English because the output feels more direct. Arabic I speak with my family, but I do not use it for career stuff.
Anna: And what are the typical tasks you use AI for?
Noura: Learning plan: like, I say, I have 6 hours per week, I want to learn SQL and Power BI, I have HR background, make me a 8-week plan with topics and small exercises. Or I say, explain joins like I am beginner, and give me practice questions. Also for interviews: I paste a job description and my CV, and ask for possible interview questions and STAR answers. Then I practice.
And journaling: when I am stressed, I write like, I feel overwhelmed, I feel stupid, I feel like I am failing. And it responds in a calm way. It asks me questions. It is non-judgmental. That is the key. I can say anything without worrying about social acceptance. If I tell my friend, I feel stupid, they might say, no you are not, stop. But with AI, I can be messy.
Anna: Can you describe a specific stress moment where you used AI journaling, and what it did for you emotionally?
Noura: Yes. There was a day in November. I had work, and then lecture, and then I got an email from a manager saying, why is the contract wrong, this is unacceptable. And it was a small mistake in start date format, but he wrote very angry. I felt my stomach drop. And then after lecture, I came home at like 22:30, and I was shaking. I was like, I cannot do this, I am not good enough.
So I opened AI chat and wrote everything. Like, I am overwhelmed, I make mistakes, people think I am incompetent, I want to quit. And it responded like, it reflected my feelings, and it said, this is a stressful environment, mistakes happen, and it asked me to separate facts from interpretations. It asked, what exactly happened, what is the evidence that you are incompetent, what would you say to a friend. It felt like guided journaling.
Anna: And what changed after that?
Noura: I felt relief. Like, I could breathe. It made me calmer. Not magically happy, but calmer. And then it helped me write a professional reply to the manager. Like, short, acknowledging the correction, and then I fixed it. So it was emotional regulation plus practical.
Anna: When you think about trust in AI, what makes you trust it, and what makes you not trust it?
Noura: Trust drivers for AI: it is always available. It is non-judgmental. It helps structure. It can break down a big thing into steps. It also does not get tired of my questions. For example, joins, I ask five times, it still answers.
But trust issues: accuracy varies. Sometimes it is too confident. Like it says something and it sounds right, but it is not. And privacy uncertainty exists. I do not paste very sensitive company data. I do not put names or internal numbers. But still, you wonder, where does it go.
Anna: Do you have an example of a moment AI helped with a learning plan, something concrete?
Noura: Yes. In December, I wanted to learn SQL, but I was overwhelmed by all resources. I asked AI: I am HR coordinator, I want people analytics, I have basic Excel, no coding, 6 hours per week, make me a plan. It gave me weekly topics: week one, select statements, filtering, sorting. Week two, group by and aggregates. Week three, joins. Week four, subqueries. Then it suggested small HR examples, like, employee table, absence table. That made it feel relevant.
Then I followed it and I created a small fake dataset in Excel, and I practiced queries. It gave me exercises like, find departments with highest turnover. That was fun. It also suggested to track progress and do mini projects. So I built a small dashboard in Power BI with a sample dataset. That gave me confidence.
Anna: And a moment AI did not help?
Noura: Many. One moment: I asked for a plan, and it was generic. Like, learn statistics, learn Python, learn machine learning, build portfolio, network, apply. It was like, everything. And I was like, okay, this is not realistic. Another moment: I asked about German HR data privacy rules, like what is allowed for analytics, and it gave a confident answer that sounded like legal advice. And I was like, hmm, I am not sure. It did not mention Betriebsrat involvement properly. So I realized, okay, this can be wrong. It can also make assumptions. Like it assumes you can access data easily, but in my company, access is restricted.
Anna: So effective sometimes, but you double-check.
Noura: Yes. It can be effective but not fully trusted. Like, it gives useful structure, but I verify.
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks
Anna: I want to compare human versus AI through specific tasks. Let us take CV and interview preparation first. For that, what feels more useful, human support or AI?
Noura: AI is more useful for speed. Like, I can paste my CV and job description and get questions, and it gives me drafts. It also helps me phrase things in a more professional way, especially in English. But I do not fully trust it, because sometimes it exaggerates. It adds skills I do not have. So I have to correct.
Human is useful for realism. Like, the mentor from Köln told me, do not try to be everything, focus on what is credible. That is human judgment. But I do not always have access to humans. So AI wins for availability.
Anna: How about deciding between people analytics and L and D? Which helps more?
Noura: For deep decision, human helps more, if the human is good. Because they can ask questions, they can understand values, and they can challenge gently. AI can ask questions too, but it sometimes feels scripted. Still, AI is good for exploring. Like, I can say, give me pros and cons, give me day-to-day tasks, give me possible entry roles. That is helpful.
Anna: And for learning plans?
Noura: AI is very effective for learning plans. It is like a personal tutor. But sometimes it is too broad. Human can also help, but most humans will not sit and build a weekly plan with you. So AI is better.
Anna: For emotional support, like stress and confidence?
Noura: This is interesting. AI journaling is surprisingly good for immediate calming, because it is non-judgmental. Human support is deeper, but also risky. Like, with humans, you worry about being judged. With AI, you can say anything. But then human can give real empathy, like tone, facial expression, and also you know they are a person. AI empathy feels like, simulated. Still, it works on me sometimes.
Anna: For navigating workplace politics or stakeholder issues?
Noura: Human is better, because they know context. AI can give templates, like how to write an email, how to set boundaries. But humans can say, in Germany, with Betriebsrat, you must consider this. AI can miss nuance.
Anna: Do you feel you trust humans more overall, or AI more overall?
Noura: Hmm. Overall I trust humans more for high-stakes decisions, but I trust AI more for private messy thoughts. Because humans can judge.
7) Trust–effectiveness link (including micro-poll ratings)

Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about human support, like mentors or coaches, how does trust affect whether it is effective?
Noura: Trust is like, the door. If I trust, I share the real problem. Like I can say, I feel not credible, I am afraid. If I do not trust, I will just ask surface questions, like, what course should I do. Then the advice is surface. So trust enables depth, and depth makes it effective.
Anna: And if you do not trust, what happens?
Noura: I filter myself. I try to look strong. Then I do not get help with the real thing. So it is less effective.
Anna: How about AI? How does trust affect effectiveness there?
Noura: With AI, if I do not trust accuracy, I do not follow the advice. I might still use it as brainstorming. Like, it can be effective as a mirror, even if I do not trust it as truth. But if it makes a wrong assumption, it can confuse me. So trust in AI is more like, trust in reliability and boundaries. If I know it is good for structuring, then it is effective for that. If I mistakenly trust it for legal topics, it can be harmful. So I keep it in its lane.
Anna: That is a nice way to put it, keep it in its lane. I would like to include a quick micro-poll. I will ask for ratings from 0 to 10. 0 means no trust at all, 10 means complete trust. First, your current trust in human coaches or mentors for career support, generally. What number comes to mind?
Noura: Um. Maybe 6.
Anna: Why 6?
Noura: Because I had one good experience, which raises it. But many people ghost or judge, or give generic advice. So I cannot say 8 or 9. But it is not 2, because humans can be very helpful if the match is right. So 6.
Anna: Next, your trust in AI tools for career-related support, 0 to 10.
Noura: I would say 7.
Anna: Interesting, slightly higher. Why 7?
Noura: Because it is consistent in being available and non-judgmental. And for structuring and interview prep, it is usually helpful. But I do not give it 9 because accuracy varies and privacy is uncertain. So 7.
Anna: Now perceived effectiveness. For your situation specifically, how effective has human support been, 0 to 10?
Noura: Hmm. Maybe 5.
Anna: Why 5?
Noura: Because the good mentor gave me direction, but overall, I did not get ongoing support. And some interactions made me feel worse. So the net effect is medium. If I had a good coach consistently, it could be higher.
Anna: And perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, 0 to 10?
Noura: I would say 8.
Anna: Why 8?
Noura: Because I actually use it weekly, and it gives me concrete things: learning plan, practice, interview answers, email drafts, journaling. It reduces stress. It helps me make progress. But I keep one point off because sometimes it is wrong or too generic, and I need to double-check.
Anna: Great. Thank you. You already gave one example of trusted but not effective, the kind mentor with vague advice. Can you give an example of effective but not fully trusted, besides the legal topic?
Noura: Yes. Interview answers. AI can generate very strong answers, like perfect STAR stories. It is effective as a template. But I do not fully trust it, because it can make me sound like someone else. And sometimes it adds metrics that I cannot prove. So it is useful, but I have to edit. So it is effective, but not trusted as final truth.
Anna: That makes sense.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction

Anna: Now imagine a hybrid model where AI supports you for preparation, reflection, and structuring, and then a human mentor or coach steps in for key decisions, context, and accountability. How does that sound to you?
Noura: Honestly, that sounds ideal. Because I want the speed and privacy of AI, but I also want human validation and reality check. Especially for credibility questions. Like, am I on the right track, am I overselling, is this realistic in Germany.
Anna: What would you want the AI part to do, and what would you want the human part to do?
Noura: AI part: learning plan, practice questions, explain concepts, help me draft applications, help me reflect when stressed, help me prepare for interviews. Also help me organize options, like list roles, required skills, timeline.
Human part: feedback on my plan, like, is it too ambitious, is it missing something. Also help me with identity and confidence, but in a real way. And networking guidance. Like, in Düsseldorf HR market, which companies have people analytics teams, how to approach them. And also accountability, like, check in with me, did you do the plan, adjust.
Anna: What would make you trust a human in such a hybrid model?
Noura: Feeling safe, confidentiality, empathy, being taken seriously, practical knowledge, and consistency. And also transparency about their background. Like, are they really experienced in people analytics or L and D, or are they general career coach who knows nothing about HR. I want someone who understands HR.
Anna: And what would make you trust the AI part more?
Noura: Clear boundaries and transparency, like what it is good at and what not. Also privacy clarity. And maybe sources. Like, if it says, in Germany, HR analytics often requires this, and it links to something credible, that would help. Also if it remembers my context correctly. Sometimes it forgets, and then it gives advice that does not fit.
Anna: How do you feel about the idea that AI could match you to a human mentor based on your goals and preferences?
Noura: That would be great, because LinkedIn outreach is random. You do not know who will reply, and you cannot see if they are actually supportive. If there was matching, like, you can choose someone with a similar path, or someone who is not judgmental, that would help trust.
Anna: What are your concerns about a hybrid model?
Noura: Cost. Because I am early career, I do not have money for expensive coaching. Also quality control. Like, how do you ensure the human is good and not just motivational. And time. If the human is not responsive, it ruins it. Also data privacy: if AI collects my information and shares it with a human, I would want control. Like, I want to decide what is shared.
Anna: If you could design the ideal experience, how would it work?
Noura: I would start with AI onboarding, like I explain my situation, my constraints, Ausbildung, part-time studies, Düsseldorf, HR coordinator, goals. Then AI suggests a few paths and learning plan. Then it suggests three mentors that fit. I choose one. Then I have a short call with the mentor, like 30 minutes, to validate plan and set goals. Then AI supports me weekly with tasks. Then every month, a human check-in, like, adjust and reflect. That would be perfect.
Anna: Would you prefer the human check-ins to be video, or chat, or voice?
Noura: Video, at least sometimes. Because trust builds with seeing the person. But sometimes chat is okay for quick questions. A mix.
Anna: If you had to choose only one, only AI or only human, which would you pick right now?
Noura: Right now, only AI. Because I actually have access and it helps me. Human is too uncertain. But ideally, hybrid.
Anna: That is very clear.
9. Closing

Anna: Before we end, is there anything important about your experience with career support, trust, or effectiveness that we did not cover?
Noura: Maybe one thing. Um. In Germany, there is this feeling of career ladder and titles. Like, people want you to have the correct degree for the correct job. And that makes me insecure. So any support that understands that, and helps me translate my path into credible story, that is what I need. AI helps me write the story. Human helps me believe it.
Anna: That is a strong closing point. If you think about the next month, what is one concrete step you want to take?
Noura: Finish my SQL basics module, like, consistent. And also maybe ask at work if I can get access to some aggregated data for another small analysis, like training participation. Something small.
Anna: Great. Thank you, Noura. I will stop the recording now. And thank you again for sharing, especially the difficult parts. 
Anna: I am glad. Have a good evening.
Noura: You too. Bye.




Slava
1) Opening and consent
