2) Participant context and career situation

Anna: To start, where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now?
Slava: Okay. So, I am 30. I am from Poland originally. I moved to Germany eight years ago, first to Dresden actually, and then Leipzig. Now I am in Leipzig since, I think, six years. I work in logistics operations for a big retail chain. Like, everyone knows it, but I will not say the name. It is large, nationwide. I work in a distribution center that supplies stores in the region.
And my role now is operations manager, like a logistics operations manager. I manage multiple shifts, multiple areas, like inbound, outbound, picking, also some returns. It is, um, high pressure.
My main question right now is: do I continue in operations and go up, like senior operations, site lead path, or do I try to move into consulting or strategy, like internal strategy team or external consulting. And it sounds, like, clean when I say it, but emotionally it is not clean at all. 
Anna: What makes it not clean emotionally?
Slava: Because operations is my identity now. I am good at it. I have a reputation. Like, if you ask people, they say Slava delivers. And I do. But I am also close to burnout. And I can feel it. And the idea of consulting is like, maybe more intellectual, maybe more control, maybe more future options. But also it is like fantasy and reality mixed. So I am in this tension.
Anna: When did this tension become sharp for you? Was there a trigger moment?
Slava: Yes. In the last, maybe, nine months. It got really sharp in, I would say, spring and summer. Because we had, like, a reorganization. And we also had higher sick leave. Krankenstand was crazy. And we had pressure from headquarters because costs. Also, service level had to stay high. So basically the same output with less people and less overtime. That is always the joke, right.
Anna: You mentioned you are close to burnout. How do you notice it in yourself?
Slava: Sleep. First sleep. I wake up at 3:40 or 4:10 and my brain is already running. Like I open eyes and it is already a shift plan in my head. Or it is like, what if the sorter stops, what if the last mile carrier rejects volume, what if we miss the cut-off. And then I cannot fall asleep again. Then irritability. I snap. Not always at people, but like internally I am angry. Also with my partner I am less patient. Small things annoy me, like the dishwasher not loaded correctly. It is stupid.
Anna: Oh 
Slava: And then constant firefighting. Even when I am not at work, I check my phone. There are WhatsApp messages and Teams messages. I try not to, but I do. Weekend messages. I have this feeling like I am always responsible. Like a weight.
Slava: Also physical. I have shoulder tension, headaches, and I started to get like stomach issues. Not dramatic but you feel it. My partner says, you are not present. You are there but not there.
Anna: That sounds heavy. In the last 6 to 12 months, what was the biggest career decision you made?
Slava: The biggest decision was whether to accept a promotion to take over another area, like a bigger scope. It was like, okay, you become responsible for outbound plus last mile coordination, not only warehouse outbound. That means more stakeholders, more stress, but also more visibility. And I said yes. And now I am like, why did I say yes. But also, I learned a lot.
Anna: What made that decision difficult at the time?
Slava: I felt like if I say no, it means I am not ambitious. And I have this thing, maybe from Poland, like, you come to Germany, you want to prove yourself. You want to show you are not the immigrant guy who is lucky to have a job. You know. So I said yes. Also, money. It was not huge, but it was something. And status. And also, I had a good relationship with my boss. He was like, I believe in you. So I did not want to disappoint. But the difficult part was I already felt tired. Like, I already felt, okay, I am at 80 percent capacity. And then I took 120 percent scope. That is not smart.
Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed during that decision?
Slava: Someone to help me think with distance. Like, is this good for you, not only for career. And also someone who understands operations, because many people just say, take the promotion, it is good. But they do not understand what it means, like shift calls at night, escalation, works council topics, staffing chaos.
Anna: When you say you did not have that person, does that relate to mentoring or coaching experiences?
Slava: Yes.


3. Career support in general

Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Slava: Eehm. Usually, long walks. That is my main. I walk along the canal, Karl-Heine-Kanal, or in Auwald. I just walk, like one hour, two hours, sometimes more. And I think. It is like processing. Second, I talk to my partner. She is German, from Saxony, and she is in healthcare, like not HR. But she is good at listening. And she tells me when I am making stupid assumptions.And she helps me calm down.
Anna: Anything else? 
Slava: I use AI tools, a lot, but more for planning, scenario analysis, writing. Not for self reflection. I do not like to tell a chat my feelings. It feels, I do not know, sterile.
Slava: Also friends. But friends are not really helpful for career strategy because they are either in similar stress or they just say, it will be fine. Which is supportive but not helpful. I mean, no wait, let me rephrase. Friends help emotionally, but not with clarity.
Anna: Do you use any formal career services, like university services, networks, professional coaching?
Slava: Not now. I used to, like, early on I had one mentor. That was the only structured thing. After that, nothing structured. I sometimes watch videos, podcasts, like consulting content, or operations leadership content. But it is not like a person.
Anna: What has actually helped in the past, and what felt unhelpful or frustrating?
Slava: Helped: my early mentor, and also some managers who gave direct feedback. Like, German directness sometimes is painful, but it can be helpful. Unhelpful like, find your passion. Or, just apply. Or, work life balance is important. Like, ja, danke. That does not solve anything. Also, sometimes HR internal programs are more like checkbox. They say you can move internally, but then there is no real path.
Anna: If you think about the kind of support you wish you had now, what is missing?
Slava: A mentor who understands business roles and also understands psychology. Like someone with psychologist background who works in HR, or a full time career mentor who has business context. Because I need both. I need someone who can help me regulate, like, stress, emotions, and also someone who can say, okay, consulting is like this, strategy is like this, your skills transfer like this, here is a plan. And I do not know where to find that.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust

Anna: You mentioned one powerful mentoring relationship early in your career. Can you tell me that story? How did you meet, what made it powerful?
Slava: Yes. It was when I moved to Germany. I started in warehouse, like entry level. I was doing picking, then I became team lead. I was hungry, like, I wanted to learn everything. I was also insecure because my German was not good. I had like B1, maybe B2, but in logistics slang it is different. My mentor was a manager in the site, not my direct boss, but like one level above. He noticed me because I was always asking questions, and also I was doing like small improvements. Like I made a simple Excel to track some errors and root causes. Nothing crazy. But he saw it. He invited me for coffee. And at first I thought, okay, I am in trouble.  Because in warehouse, if a manager calls you, it is usually not good. But he said, no, I just see potential.We met every two weeks. Sometimes in the canteen, sometimes outside. He would ask me, what do you want, what do you struggle with. And he was very direct. He said, you are fast and smart, but you are also, how to say, too eager to please. And he said, you need to learn boundaries, and you need to learn stakeholder management, not just doing work.
Anna: What did he do that created trust for you?
Slava: First, confidentiality. He explicitly said, what we talk stays here, unless you want me to escalate something. That was big for me. Because I was afraid to look weak.Second, he understood the context. He knew operations. He knew the pressure. He knew KPIs. So when I said, I feel overwhelmed, he did not say, take a vacation. He said, okay, what is the load, what can be delegated, what is system problem. Also he challenged me. Not only empathy. He would say, you complain but you also create your own chaos. And it was true. He made me see patterns. Also, he remembered details. Like, next time he asked, how was your night shift, what happened with that supervisor. Feeling seen. That is trust for me.
Anna: Do you remember a specific moment or feedback that stayed with you?
Slava: Yes. There was one moment, I had a conflict with a German supervisor. He thought I was too pushy, too direct. I thought he was lazy. It was messy. I came to mentor and I was like, he does not respect me because I am Polish. I was angry. And my mentor listened. Then he said, okay, maybe. But also maybe you are communicating like in Poland, more like, do it now, and in Germany, people want more context, more agreement. Especially with works council culture. He said, if you want respect, you need to speak in their logic. Use facts, use process, not emotion. And he taught me how to have that conversation. He even role played with me, like, what to say, how to say. And after I did it, the relationship improved. It was not perfect, but it was better. And I learned something: trust is not only being nice, it is being helpful in a concrete way.
Anna: What changed in your career after that mentoring relationship?
Slava: A lot. I got moved to a better role. I became shift manager earlier than I would. And I also got confidence. Like, I started to feel I belong. And also I learned to think bigger, not only day to day.
Anna: And then you said after that, nothing structured. What happened?
Slava: He left the company. He moved to another city. And after that, the culture changed. New leadership. And I also moved to Leipzig. In Leipzig, I had good bosses but no one like that. Also I became more senior, so people assume you do not need help. But actually you need more.
Anna: Today, when you think of having a mentor or coach, what would you expect? And what would make you trust them?
Slava: I would expect structure. Like, not just random talks. A process. Maybe goals. Accountability. Trust drivers for human: confidentiality, feeling seen, empathy plus challenge, and industry understanding. Also not judging burnout. If someone is like, you are weak, then no. I need someone who can say, burnout risk is real, but also, you have choices. And I want someone who understands Germany context. Like, works councils, Betriebsrat, sick leave norms, and also internal mobility. Because sometimes advice from US content is like, just quit. That is not realistic.
Anna: Have you ever had a human support that you trusted, but it was not effective?
Slava: Yes. My partner, sometimes. I trust her fully. She is very supportive. But if I ask, should I move to consulting, she cannot answer. She can help me calm down, but she cannot give steps. So I trust, but not effective for that specific task. And also friends. They are loyal, but they do not move me forward.
Anna: That distinction is helpful. Anything else you want to add about what you do not want in a human coach?
Slava: I do not want someone who is too soft and just validates. I also do not want someone who is like a guru. I want practical. And I want someone who can handle complexity. And I want boundaries, like, what they can do, what they cannot do.
 5) AI tools: experience and trust

Anna: Let us shift to AI tools. You said you use AI heavily for planning, scenario analysis, and writing reports. Can you walk me through how you actually use it in your work?
Slava: Yes. So I use AI almost daily. For example, peak season planning. We have like Easter peak, summer peak, and then Christmas, Black Friday, all that. We need to forecast volume, staffing, overtime, temp agency. I take historical data, like shipments, order lines, pick rate, absenteeism. I put it in my own spreadsheets. Then I use AI to help me think through scenarios. Like, if volume increases 12 percent, and sick leave stays at 8 percent, what staffing do I need to maintain OTIF. Or if we cannot get enough temps, what levers do we have. AI helps me structure options and risks. Also risk logs. Like, we do weekly risk register. AI helps me write it cleaner, more executive language. Because operations people write messy. I am included. And report writing. Every week I send an update to regional director. It must be crisp. AI helps with rewriting, making it more structured, less emotional.
Also incident postmortems. When a sorter breaks and we miss cut-off, we do a postmortem. AI helps me create a template: timeline, root cause, corrective actions, preventive actions.
Anna: Can you give one moment where AI helped a lot?
Slava: Yes. Last year, like October, we had a problem with last mile carrier capacity. They suddenly reduced trucks due to driver shortage. And we had store deliveries at risk. My boss asked me to prepare an escalation message to headquarters and also propose mitigation plan, within like two hours. I had all the messy notes. I used AI to structure it: situation, impact, options, recommendation, request. It produced a very clear message. I still edited, but it saved me, honestly. And the tone was better than my angry tone.  Also it suggested a mitigation I did not think: shifting some volume to alternative cross-dock in the region, even if cost is higher, for only two weeks. We did something similar. So it helped.
Anna: And one moment where AI failed, or felt risky?
Slava: Yes. Two things. First, hallucinated numbers. I once asked it to summarize a dataset and I gave it like a table. And it confidently said, absenteeism decreased by 3 percent, but actually it increased. It misunderstood. And if I would copy paste that, I would look stupid. So I learned: never trust numbers unless I verify. Also confidentiality and senses. We have works council topics, like shift patterns, overtime, performance. If I put too much detail into AI, it is risky. Also HR sensitivity. For example, I wanted to write a note about a supervisor performance issue. AI suggested language that was too direct, like, terminate or replace. In Germany, you cannot just say that. Also it can create legal issues. So I do not use AI for personal HR cases. I keep it for general structure and anonymized.
Anna: So when you talk about trust in AI, what drives it for you?
Slava: Speed, structure, low effort. It is always available. Also it is consistent. And it does not judge. Like, if I ask stupid question, it does not laugh. But emotional safety is limited. And nuance is limited. Also, it can be overconfident. So trust is conditional. I trust it to help me draft, structure, brainstorm. I do not trust it as final truth.
Anna: You said you do not use AI for self reflection. Can you explain why? What do long walks and partner talks give you instead?
Slava: Yes. AI feels like talking into a mirror that answers. But it is not a human. For self reflection, I need silence. I need my body to calm down. When I walk, my nervous system goes down. I breathe. I notice things. I remember what actually matters. And with my partner, I get real feedback. She can see my face. She can say, you are lying to yourself. AI cannot do that in the same way. Also, there is intimacy. Like, I can be weak. I do not want to be weak with a tool. Even if it is safe, it feels weird. Also I do not want to create habit of outsourcing my feelings. I want to process them myself. Maybe it is old school.
6. Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks

Anna: I want to compare human and AI via concrete tasks. I will name a task and you tell me which you would prefer and why. First: writing a report for senior leadership about weekly performance and risks.
Slava: AI first. Because it can structure fast. I still verify. But AI is effective there. Human mentor would be slower, and also they would not be in my inbox at 23:00. AI is. 
Anna: Second: scenario planning for peak season staffing and capacity.
Slava: AI plus me. Like, AI helps generate scenarios, but I need domain knowledge. A human could help if they have operations background, but it is rare. Most coaches do not know picking rate and cut-off times. So AI is more practical.
Anna: Third: deciding whether to move from operations into consulting or strategy.
Slava: Human. 100 percent. AI can give pros and cons, but it cannot feel my situation. It cannot challenge my avoidance. It cannot hold me accountable.
Anna: Fourth: preparing a CV and cover letter for consulting roles.
Slava: AI for drafting and structure, human for positioning. Like, AI can make it pretty, but human mentor can say, this is the story, this is credible, this is what consulting wants. AI can guess, but it is generic.
Anna: Fifth: practicing an interview, especially case interview style.
Slava: Hybrid. AI for practice questions and feedback quick. Human for real pressure, like, interrupting, probing, seeing if I am confident. Also human can tell if I sound like operations guy who is hiding insecurity.
Anna: Sixth: managing your burnout risk and boundaries at work.
Slava: Human. Because I need empathy plus challenge. AI can suggest time management tips, but it will not handle the messy reality, like my boss calling, or works council conflict, or guilt. Human can.
Anna: Is there any area where you feel AI is effective even if you do not fully trust it?
Slava: Yes. Report writing. Sometimes it produces a very strong narrative. It sounds confident, like consultant language. It is effective. But I do not fully trust it. I verify every claim. So it is like, effective output, but trust is partial. I rely on it as a tool, not as authority.
Anna: And is there any area where you trust a human but they might not be effective?
Slava: Yes, like I said, supportive people who listen but do not give steps. Also, some managers who are nice but they do not have time. They say, yeah, you can move internally, and then nothing happens. I trust they mean well, but it is not effective.
7) Trust–effectiveness link

Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about human mentoring or coaching, how does trust affect whether it is effective?
Slava: Trust is like entry ticket. If I do not trust, I will not say the real thing. I will say the safe version. Like, I am busy. Not like, I am scared, or I feel trapped, or I fantasize about leaving because I hate this. If I do not trust, then the coach works on surface.When I trusted my mentor early on, I told him, I feel like outsider, I feel angry, I feel insecure. And then he could help. So trust enables depth. Depth enables effectiveness.
Anna: What are your indicators that mentoring or coaching is effective for you?
Slava: Concrete steps. Not only feelings. Like, after a session, I want a plan: this week do X, next week do Y. Also reduced overwhelm. Like, I feel lighter, I can breathe. Better decisions. Like, I stop looping in my head and I choose. Also energy. If coaching is effective, I have more energy, not less. And for transition, effectiveness would mean: credible plan to move, maybe applications sent, interviews, or at least informational interviews. Not only dreaming.
Anna: And how does trust affect effectiveness with AI tools for you?
Slava: Different. With AI, trust is more like reliability. If I see it makes mistakes, I trust less, so I verify more. Effectiveness can still be high because it saves time, but trust changes how I use it. Also if I do not trust it with confidentiality, I will not put sensitive info. So it limits usefulness. For example, I cannot ask it, how to handle this specific works council conflict, because I cannot share details. So trust boundaries reduce effectiveness.
Anna:Can you name the main trust drivers for AI in your case, and the main trust drivers for humans, clearly separated?
Slava: Okay. For humans feeling seen, confidentiality, empathy plus challenge, industry understanding, no judgment about burnout, and structured accountability. Like, they keep me on track. For AI speed, structure, low effort, availability. Also it is consistent. But trust is limited by overconfidence, hallucinations, and confidentiality risks. And lack of emotional nuance.
Anna: And effectiveness indicators, also separated, for your situation?
Slava: Human effectiveness would be: clarity on direction, concrete transition plan, better boundaries, less burnout, and progress like interviews.AI effectiveness is: better planning, faster writing, clearer reports, scenario options, and reducing cognitive load in operations tasks.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction (with micro-poll ratings)

Anna: I want to include a short micro-poll, just to structure the comparison. I will ask four ratings from 0 to 10, and I want you to explain briefly. First: your current trust in human coaches or mentors for career topics, 0 to 10.
Slava: Um. I would say 7 out of 10. Because I had one very good mentor, so I know it can be great. But I also know many coaches are generic. And I worry about people who do not understand operations. So I am open, but not blindly.
Anna: What would move it from 7 to 9?
Slava: Clear proof of understanding my context, like retail logistics, KPIs, shift reality. And confidentiality. And also if they have psychologist background, because then I feel they can handle stress topics without making it weird.
Anna: Second rating: trust in AI tools for career related or work related support, 0 to 10.
Slava: For work tasks, like planning and reports, trust is 6. For career guidance, trust is lower, maybe 4 or 5. But overall if you force me one number, I say 6 out of 10.
Anna: Why 6?
Slava: Because it is useful and often correct in structure. But I have seen mistakes. And the confidence is dangerous. Also privacy. So I trust it as assistant, not as expert.
Anna: Third rating: perceived effectiveness of human mentoring or coaching for your current situation, 0 to 10.
Slava: If I had the right person, it could be 9. But in reality, given I do not have it now, I would say 7. Like, I believe it is effective, but access is the problem.
Anna: What makes you believe it could be 9 with the right person?
Slava: Because of my early mentor. I saw how one person can change your trajectory. Also because my problem is not only technical. It is identity and stress and choices. Humans are better for that.
Anna: Fourth rating: perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your current situation, 0 to 10.
Slava: For my operations tasks, 8. For the transition question, maybe 5. Overall, if one number, I say 7.
Anna: So trust in AI is 6, effectiveness is 7. Trust in humans is 7, effectiveness is 7, with potential higher. Is that fair?
Slava: Yes. That is fair. AI is like, effective worker. Human is like, potential deeper shift.
Anna: Now I will describe a hybrid model very briefly, and you tell me your reaction. Imagine a career support service where AI helps you prepare, structure your thoughts, draft CV and messages, and track tasks between sessions. Then you have sessions with a human mentor who is matched to you based on background and style, and they focus on deeper decision making, accountability, and emotional regulation. How does that land for you?
Slava: Honestly, that is exactly what I want. Because I already use AI for prep. But I do not have the human part. The matching is key. Like, I do not want random coach. I want someone who understands business roles, maybe consulting, maybe HR, and ideally psychology background. Because if I am close to burnout, I need someone who can spot patterns, like perfectionism, guilt, over responsibility. Also I like the idea AI tracks tasks. Because I am good at work tasks, but for my own career I procrastinate. So if AI says, hey, you said you will message two people on LinkedIn, did you do it, it could help. But it must not be creepy. It must feel like tool, not surveillance.
Anna: What would make it feel creepy?
Slava: If it collects too much. If it stores sensitive personal notes. Also if it is not transparent. Like, who sees what. If the human mentor can see my AI conversations, I need control. Because sometimes I might write things I do not want to share.
Anna: How would you prefer the division of labor between AI and human in such a model?
Slava: Preparation, brainstorming, structure, drafts, reminders, maybe practice questions.For human decision sessions, value clarification, burnout boundaries, and reality check about consulting. Like, consulting is not vacation. Also human should help with network strategy. Like, who to talk to, how to position my story.
Anna: You said earlier you do not know where to find that kind of mentor. What have you tried so far?
Slava: I tried LinkedIn. But it is noisy. Everyone is like coach. Hard to tell quality. I also looked at internal programs, but they are more leadership training, not career transition. I asked one colleague who moved to consulting. He gave me tips, but it was one conversation. Not ongoing. I also considered paying for coaching, but I do not know how to choose. Like, I fear spending money and getting generic advice.
Anna: What signals would you look for to evaluate a mentor or coach?
Slava: Case examples. Like, have they helped people transition from ops to consulting. Also their questions. If first call they ask deep questions, not selling. Also if they can explain their method. Like, how they work. And if they can talk about confidentiality clearly. Also vibe. Like, do I feel safe and also challenged. Not only friendly.
9) Closing

Anna: We are close to the end. If you imagine yourself six months from now, what would be a good outcome, realistically?
Slava: Realistic good outcome: I have a clear plan. Either I commit to operations but with boundaries, maybe internal move to less firefighting role, like process improvement, continuous improvement. Or I commit to consulting path and I have already done like, five informational interviews and maybe applied to some roles. Also I would like to feel healthier. Like, sleep better. Not checking phone every weekend. That would be success.
Anna: If you could give advice to someone designing career support, based on your experience, what would you emphasize?
Slava: Do not underestimate burnout and emotional load in operations. People think it is just logistics. But it is people, safety, pressure, and you feel responsible. Also, do not make advice generic. People need context. And give structure, like steps, not only inspiration. And for AI, be transparent about limits and privacy. Otherwise people will not trust with important topics.
Anna: Anything you wish I asked but did not?
Slava: Maybe about consulting fantasy. Like, I know it is also pressure, travel, long hours. But I still want it because it feels like growth. I guess that is enough.
Anna: We can end on that. Thank you, Slava. Your examples were very concrete and helpful. I will stop the recording now.
Slava:Thank you. It was good to know you. Okay i

2) Participant context and career situation
Anna: Where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing, or recently faced?

I have, like, two years of work experience in Berlin, and before that some internships. In my current job I started as like partnerships associate, and now I am like BD manager, but in a startup that title means nothing. It means you do everything.
Anna: Mm.
Silvia: And my main question is, I need to sharpen my positioning. Like, what am I, actually. Am I BD, am I product, am I operations. Because in our company, the lines are super blurred. And I feel ambitious, I want to grow, but I also feel insecure because I am not technical enough. And climate-tech is very technical, or at least it can be. So I am trying to figure out where I can be strongest.
Anna: When you say sharpen your positioning, what does that mean for you, concretely?
Silvia: Like, if I need to apply somewhere, or if I want to ask for a different scope internally, I need to say, ok, I am a BD person. Or, no, I am more like product-ish. Or, I am like ops, I make things work. And right now my story is too broad. It is like, I do sales, I do partnerships, I do marketing sometimes, I do a bit of customer success, and then I join product calls and talk about features.
Anna: Mm. So it is not clear what the core is.
Silvia: Exactly. And it sounds nice, but it also sounds like, she is random. Like, she is not deep.
Anna: And what triggered that question recently? Was there a specific moment?
Silvia: Yes. There was like a re-org moment. In December, our founders decided we should focus on enterprise accounts, longer sales cycles, more consultative selling. We are in carbon accounting software, for supply chain emissions, mostly Scope 3. So it is complex. And they said, ok, Silvia, you will own partnerships for DACH and also start leading enterprise outreach for, like, industrial clients.
Anna: That is a big shift.
Silvia: Yes. And it was exciting, because it is like, wow, they trust me. But it also was scary because I was like, I do not speak German perfectly, and also enterprise means you need credibility. Like, you talk to sustainability managers, procurement, sometimes CFO. And then the product is technical, you need to explain methodologies, data integration, APIs, ERP systems. And I was like, hmm, ok, am I the right person. 
Anna: What happened that made you feel not technical enough?
Silvia: There is one moment I remember. We had a customer demo with a logistics company. The head of sustainability asked about how we map emission factors and how we deal with missing supplier data. And then someone from their IT joined and asked about integration with SAP and data security, like where the data is hosted, encryption, access rights.
Anna: Mm.
Silvia: And I tried to answer, but I was not sure. I gave like a high-level answer, and then our engineer jumped in, and he was super precise. Like, yes, we use this, we do that, we have a certain architecture. And the vibe changed, you know. It was like, ok, the technical guy is the credible one. And I felt like, I am just the person doing the talking.
Anna: That sounds uncomfortable.
Silvia: Yes. And then after the call, the founder told me, you did well, but you need to be more confident. And I was like, yes, but confidence comes from knowledge. It is not just like, be confident.
Anna: Mm. So your insecurity is tied to competence, not just mindset.
Silvia: Exactly. And also, I think I compare myself to people in Berlin who are like, product managers, ex-consultants, or people from TU Munich, you know, very technical. And I am International Relations. Sometimes I feel like, what am I doing here.
Anna: How do you measure ambition for yourself?
Silvia: Eehm. Good question. I measure it like, I want to have more impact and also more responsibility. I want to lead projects, maybe lead a team. I want to be in rooms where decisions happen. And I also want to earn more, honestly. Berlin is expensive now.
Anna: Sure.
Silvia: And I want to stay in climate-tech because it matters, like, it is not just selling random software. But I also do not want to become like this, how to say, performance machine, where I am always optimizing, always chasing titles.
Anna: So there is tension between ambition and authenticity.
Silvia: Yes. Exactly. Like, I want to win, but I also want to feel like myself. 
Anna: And in terms of your situation in Germany, do you have any visa constraints, or is it straightforward?
Silvia: I am EU, so it is straightforward. That is one privilege. I do not have Blue Card stress. But I have like, life stress. Berlin stress.
Anna: Understood. And your German level?
Silvia: I think B1, maybe low B2 in reading. Speaking is the problem. In meetings I can do some German, but for enterprise sales, it is tough. Sometimes I switch to English, and then the German person switches too, but you feel like, hmm, maybe I am losing something.
Anna: Ok. Thanks. That is helpfut. Let us talk about where you get career support in general.
3) Career support in general
Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Silvia: Eehm. Friends first. Like other internationals in Berlin, people working in startups. Also some Italian friends, because they understand my background. Then sometimes my boyfriend, but he is in tech, so he gives a technical perspective.
Anna: Mm.
Silvia: And I also use online resources. Like newsletters, podcasts, LinkedIn posts. Sometimes I talk to people in my network, like informational chats. Berlin is very network-y, you can just DM someone and say, coffee.
Anna: Does that work for you?
Silvia: Sometimes yes. Sometimes it is superficial. Like, people give advice but they do not know your context. So you get very generic things. But the network effect is real. If you meet one person, they introduce you to another, and then you see patterns.
Anna: Any formal services, like university career services or mentors?
Silvia: Not university, because my Master was in Italy and I did not really use career services. In Berlin, I did group coaching, like a program for women in startups. It was paid, but not super expensive.
Anna: Ok.
Silvia: And I sometimes use AI tools. Like ChatGPT. Mostly for work, not for like, should I change my job. More for outreach messages, market research summaries, like competitor mapping.
Anna: What has actually helped you, and what felt unhelpful or frustrating?
Silvia: Helped: talking to peers. Like people one or two steps ahead. They say, this is normal, you are not crazy, and also they share very practical things, like, how they position their role, how they negotiate salary, scripts for difficult conversations. That is super helpful.
Anna: And unhelpful?
Silvia: When advice is too generic. Like, follow your passion. Or be confident. Or, build your personal brand. And you are like, ok, but what do I do on Monday.
Anna: Mm. Thank you. Let us talk about your experience with human coaching, specifically the group coaching you mentioned.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust
Anna: Can you describe that group coaching experience? What was it, and what did it look like in practice?
Silvia: Yes. It was like an eight-week program, once a week, two hours. Zoom. There were maybe twelve women, mostly in startups, roles like marketing, BD, product, operations. The coach was a woman, German, maybe in her 40s, with like coaching certification.
Anna: And what was the goal of the program?
Silvia: It was career clarity and confidence. Like positioning, boundaries, leadership presence. And also peer accountability. We had like homework, journaling prompts, and then we shared in the group.
Anna: What did you like about it?
Silvia: The peer effect. One hundred percent. Like, hearing other women say they feel impostor syndrome, they feel not technical enough, they feel behind. It normalised my feelings. And also, peers had examples. Like one woman was in a climate-tech scaleup, and she explained how BD works there, how they work with product. It was like, wow, ok, there are options.
Anna: Can you share a specific moment where peers helped you?
Silvia: Yes. There was a session on self-advocacy. I shared that our founders want me to do enterprise outreach, but I feel insecure. And I said, I do not know how to ask for support without sounding weak. And then two peers jumped in. One said, you can frame it as, to deliver better results, you need access to technical knowledge, so you propose a weekly session with an engineer to learn the product deeper. And another said, you can ask for a clearer role definition, like, what is success in three months.
Anna: That sounds practical.
Silvia: Yes. And then they even gave me wording. Like, I mean, no wait, let me rephrase. They gave me a script. Like, in German and English. One person said, you can say, I want to take full ownership, and to do that I need these resources. It is not weakness, it is professionalism.
Anna: And did you use that?
Silvia: Yes. I actually did. The next week I spoke with the founder. I said, I want to own enterprise, but I need a technical buddy. And now I have a weekly session with our solutions engineer. It is like thirty minutes. That came from peers, not from the coach.
Anna: Interesting. So the outcome was a concrete change at work.
Silvia: Yes. Exactly.
Anna: And how did the coach contribute?
Silvia: Eehm. She facilitated. She created a safe space. She had some frameworks, like values, strengths, vision. But sometimes it felt generic. Like, she would say, ok, write down your values, and then align your career. And I was like, yes, that is true, but it is also like, ok, I have values, now what.
Anna: Can you remember a moment where the coach felt generic or secondary?
Silvia: Yes. There was one session where I said, I am unsure if I should move toward product or stay in BD. And she said, maybe you can do an ikigai exercise, like what you love, what you are good at, what the world needs, what you can be paid for.
Anna: Mm.
Silvia: And I was like, ok, yes, but that is like, the internet. And she did not really ask deep questions. It was like, ok, do the exercise, and you will know. But I did the exercise and I still did not know, because I like many things.
Anna: So it was nice but not specific.
Silvia: Exactly. She was kind. She was trustworthy in the sense she was respectful and confidential. But not effective in that moment, because she did not get into my concrete context, like climate-tech BD, technical insecurity, Berlin startup reality.
Anna: That is helpful. You are already distinguishing trust and effectiveness.
Silvia: Yes. Trust was high in terms of safety, but effectiveness was medium.
Anna: Let us unpack trust in human coaching for you. What makes a human coach or a group setting trustworthy?
Silvia: For humans, trust is like, do I feel seen, not judged. Emotional safety. Confidentiality. Also, lived experience matters. Like, if someone has been in a similar role, BD in a technical company, then I trust more. And peer credibility, like hearing from peers who actually do the thing. That was huge.
Anna: So peers were like a credibility source.
Silvia: Yes. Because they do not pretend. They say, I did this, it worked, or it did not. And you believe it because it is messy.
Anna: Anything that reduces trust in human coaching?
Silvia: If it feels like they have a one-size-fits-all method. Or if they push you to conclusions. Or if they do not remember what you said before. Like if I share something vulnerable and next time they forget, I feel like, ok, I am just another client.
Anna: In group coaching, did you worry about confidentiality?
Silvia: A bit, yes. Because you do not know people. But we had rules, like what is said here stays here. And it felt ok. I did not share super sensitive details like salary numbers. But I shared enough.
Anna: And did you feel seen in that group?
Silvia: Yes, by peers. The coach was ok, but peers really saw me. They said, oh, you always downplay your competence. Or, you are always translating technical stuff into human language, that is a skill. And I was like, oh, that is true.
Anna: That is interesting. So trust drivers can come from different actors in a group setting.
Silvia: Exactly.
Anna: Ok. Before we move to AI, I want to capture one more concrete episode. Biggest career decision in the last six to twelve months. What was it?
Silvia: Hmm. I think it was whether to stay in this startup or take a role in a bigger climate-tech company, like a scaleup, where the role is more defined. I got approached by a recruiter in September. It was a partnerships role, but more like account management, less chaotic.
Anna: And what made it difficult?
Silvia: The uncertainty. In Berlin startups, you can grow fast, but you can also burn out. In a bigger company, you learn processes, but you might get stuck. And I also felt like, if I leave now, I am running away from my technical insecurity instead of solving it.
Anna: What did you decide?
Silvia: I stayed. And then the re-org happened, so now it is like, ok, you wanted growth, here it is.
Anna: That is how it goes.
Silvia: Yes.
Anna: Ok. Now let us shift to AI tools.
5) AI tools: experience and trust
Anna: You said you use AI mainly for work, like outreach messages and market research summaries. Can you walk me through how you actually use it?
Silvia: Sure. For outreach, I do cold emails and LinkedIn messages, mostly. Our product is carbon accounting for supply chains. We target sustainability managers, procurement, sometimes operations. And each persona cares about different things.
Anna: Mm.
Silvia: So I use AI to draft an initial message, then I customise. I might say, ok, write a LinkedIn message to a head of sustainability in manufacturing, mention recent EU regulations, like CSRD, and connect to Scope 3 reporting, and be friendly but not too salesy.
Anna: And then you edit.
Silvia: Yes. I always edit, because otherwise it is too generic, too enthusiastic, like, hope you are doing great, and I am like, no, Germans do not do that. 
Anna: Fair.
Silvia: And also, I feed it context. Like, I paste their company description, or I summarise their latest sustainability report, and I ask for a message that references something specific. It saves time.
Anna: Any follow-up sequences?
Silvia: Yes. Like, I ask it to propose a three-step follow-up sequence. For example, first message is value proposition, second is a short case study, third is a question. It helps structure.
Anna: And market research summaries?
Silvia: That is also huge. Like, I say, summarise the carbon accounting software landscape in Europe, main competitors, positioning, typical pricing models, and key decision criteria for buyers. Then it gives me a map. But I have to be careful because it sometimes invents things.
Anna: Can you give an example of when it invented things?
Silvia: Yes. It once said a competitor had a partnership with a big consulting firm. And I was like, wow, that is important. Then I searched and it was not true. It was like hallucination. That was a trust hit.
Anna: How did you notice it was wrong?
Silvia: Because I knew the competitor a bit. And also it cited like, sources, but when I checked, the sources were not real. And then I was like, ok, I cannot rely on it for factual market intelligence unless I verify.
Anna: So you have a verification habit.
Silvia: Yes. For market facts, yes. For message drafts, less risky.
Anna: Ok. Tell me about a moment AI helped a lot.
Silvia: One moment was when we were launching a new feature: supplier engagement module. We needed to explain it in a simple way to prospects. I asked AI to help craft a value proposition for three personas: sustainability lead, procurement, and IT. It gave me different angles. For procurement, it focused on supplier compliance and risk. For IT, it focused on integration and data governance. That helped me create a better outreach. And we had better reply rates that week, like, not crazy, but noticeable. I think because messages were more relevant.
Anna: So effectiveness there was in speed and structure and persona adaptation.
Silvia: Exactly. And also, it helps with language. Like, I am Italian, my English is good, but sometimes I want a crisp sentence, and it helps.
Anna: And a moment where AI did not help, apart from hallucinated facts.
Silvia: There was a moment where I asked it to help me position myself. Like, I tried once. I said, I am in climate-tech BD, I am not sure if I am more BD or product. Help me decide.
Anna: And what happened?
Silvia: It gave me a very structured answer. Like, here are pros and cons, here is a decision matrix, here are steps. And it felt impressive, like, wow. But I also felt like, it is pushing me into optimization mode. Like, choose the best option, maximise. And I was like, but I do not want to optimise. I want to know what I actually want.
Anna: Mm.
Silvia: And then I felt anxious. Because it was like, ok, do these ten steps, build a portfolio, talk to five people, do a SWOT. And I was like, oh my god, more tasks.
Anna: So it increased the pressure.
Silvia: Yes. And also it did not know me. It did not ask about my values in a deep way. It was like, do a values exercise, which is again generic. So I stopped. For personal decisions, I avoid AI. Because I fear it will push me into being a productivity robot, not a human.
Anna: That is an important boundary.
Silvia: Yes. I use it for external-facing tasks. Messages, summaries. But not for, who am I.
Anna: You also mentioned that wanting question, like what you truly want, feels more suitable for a psychologist or coach, or something you figure out yourself.
Silvia: Yes. Exactly. Because wanting is emotional, messy. AI is too clean. It gives you an answer, but maybe it is not your answer. It is like, it can be effective in output, but not authentic.
Anna: That is a good phrase. Effective output but not authentic.
Silvia: Yes.
Anna: Let us talk specifically about trust in AI. What drives trust for you, and what reduces it?
Silvia: Trust drivers: speed. It is always there. Structure. It can take messy information and create a clean outline. And for outreach, it can generate variants fast, which feels like, ok, I can iterate.
Anna: And what reduces trust?
Silvia: When it invents facts. When it sounds too confident. Also privacy, but for me less, because I do not put very sensitive things. But still, I do not put internal numbers. And the other thing is, it can lead to sameness. Like, if everyone uses it, everyone writes the same messages.
Anna: So you fear losing uniqueness.
Silvia: Yes. And losing my voice. That is like an emotional trust issue. Like, can I trust it to represent me.
Anna: That is a different trust dimension than factual accuracy.
Silvia: Exactly.
Anna: Ok. Now I want to compare human and AI via specific tasks. But before that, we will embed some micro-poll ratings. It will be quick. Is that ok?
Silvia: Yes.
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks
Anna: I will ask you to rate from 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all and 10 is complete trust. First: your trust in human career coaches, based on your experience, including the group coaching nuance.
Silvia: Hmm. I would say, maybe 7.
Anna: 7. Why 7, not higher or lower?
Silvia: Higher because I can feel safe, like confidentiality, being heard. And in group coaching, peers add credibility. Lower because the coach can be generic, and also, I have not had like one-on-one coaching with someone who really knows my domain. If I had a coach who did climate-tech BD, maybe it would be 9.
Anna: Ok. Next your trust in AI tools for career-related support, 0 to 10.
Silvia: For career-related, like decisions, I would say 4. For work tasks, maybe 8, but you asked career support, so 4.
Anna: 4. What drives that?
Silvia: I trust it to generate structure and drafts, but I do not trust it with my identity questions. And I do not trust it with facts unless I verify. So overall, 4.
Anna: Next: perceived effectiveness of human coaching for your situation, 0 to 10.
Silvia: Hmm. Maybe 6.
Anna: Why 6?
Silvia: Because it helped through peers, accountability, and I made a concrete change at work. But the coach itself was not super effective. And group coaching is not personalised. So 6.
Anna: And perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, 0 to 10.
Silvia: For my situation, I think 7. But again, not for personal decisions, more for tasks. So maybe 7.
Anna: Interesting. So AI effectiveness higher than AI trust.
Silvia: Yes. Because it can produce a strong message, like objectively effective, but I might not trust it emotionally. Or I feel it is not me.
Anna: That is exactly the distinction we are exploring. Ok, thanks. Now we will go deeper with tasks. I will name a task and you tell me which you prefer, human or AI, and why.
Silvia: Ok.
Anna: Task one: drafting outreach messages to prospects. Human coach, peer, or AI?
Silvia: AI. Definitely AI.
Anna: Why?
Silvia: Speed. And you can do variants. And it is not emotionally risky. If AI gives me a bad message, I just change it. Also, a human coach would be too slow and expensive for that.
Anna: And what would make AI more trustworthy for outreach?
Silvia: If I can control the tone. Like, prompt it with my style and constraints. And if it does not make wrong assumptions about the prospect. Sometimes it assumes they care about something they do not.
Anna: Can you give a concrete example?
Silvia: Yes. I once asked it to write a message to a procurement lead. It said, you are probably under pressure to comply with CSRD. But procurement is not directly responsible. So it was like, slightly wrong. Then the message feels off.
Anna: Ok. Task two: summarising a market or competitor landscape.
Silvia: AI, but with verification. Like, I use it as a starting point, but then I check.
Anna: What do you check?
Silvia: Competitor names, features, pricing, partnerships, any claims. I cross-check with websites, news, sometimes G2 reviews.
Anna: Task three: preparing for a difficult conversation with your founder about your scope, like BD vs product vs operations.
Silvia: Hmm. That is more human. Or peers.
Anna: Why?
Silvia: Because it is emotional and political. AI can give me scripts, but it cannot read the room. Peers can tell you, watch out, the founder will react like this, or the culture is like that.
Anna: Does AI help at all there?
Silvia: Maybe for structuring my points. Like, ok, list what I want, list what I offer, list what support I need. But I do not want it to tell me what I should want.
Anna: Task four: clarifying what you want long term, like the wanting question.
Silvia: Human. Or myself.
Anna: What kind of human, specifically? Coach, therapist, mentor, friend?
Silvia: Eehm. I think a coach or therapist. Coach for career structure, therapist for deeper patterns. Friends also help, but they are biased. A coach at least is neutral. But the coach needs to be good.
Anna: What is good?
Silvia: Someone who asks real questions, not frameworks. Someone who can hold silence. And who can challenge me gently. And ideally someone who understands startup roles.
Anna: Ok. Task five: building confidence in technical meetings.
Silvia: That is tricky. I think human plus practice. AI can help me learn concepts, like explain what Scope 3 is, or explain what an API is, but confidence comes from using it.
Anna: So AI can support learning.
Silvia: Yes. Like, I ask it, explain SAP integration like I am five. That helps. But then in a meeting, you need to speak.
Anna: Task six: writing a personal narrative for LinkedIn or for interviews, like your positioning.
Silvia: Hmm. Hybrid. AI can help with drafts and structure. Human can help with authenticity and truth.
Anna: That is a good segue into trust and effectiveness. Let us go there.
7) Trust–effectiveness link
Anna: When you think about your experiences, how does trust affect whether support is effective, for humans and for AI?
Silvia: For humans, trust is like the condition for depth. If I trust the coach, I share the real insecurity. Like, I say, I feel stupid in technical meetings. If I do not trust, I will say something superficial, like, I want to grow. Then the coaching is superficial, and effectiveness is low.
Anna: Mm.
Silvia: And in the group, trust was created by rules and by seeing others be vulnerable. Then I felt, ok, I can share. That made it effective because peers gave real advice.
Anna: So trust enabled effectiveness.
Silvia: Yes, for humans. For AI, it is different. Trust is not needed for it to produce an effective output. Like, it can write a strong message even if I do not trust it. But then, whether it is effective for me depends on whether I use it and whether it aligns with me.
Anna: Can you give an example of effective but not trusted?
Silvia: Yes. I had one outreach message it generated that was really good. Very crisp, very relevant, and it got a reply. But I felt weird, like, is this my voice. It felt slightly manipulative, like too polished. And I was like, ok, it works, but I do not trust it to represent my values. So it was effective in outcome, but not trusted.
Anna: That is very clear. And the other direction: trusted but not effective.
Silvia: That is the coach moment. She was nice, safe, I trusted her as a person, but her advice was generic. Like, do ikigai. So I trusted her, but it did not move me.
Anna: If you think about effectiveness indicators for you, what counts as effective support?
Silvia: Clearer narrative. Like, I can say what I do and why it matters. Confidence in meetings. Concrete experiments, like trying a project with product, or setting boundaries with founders. Better outreach results, like reply rate, meeting booked. Clearer boundaries, like what I will not do. And reduced anxiety. Like, sleeping better.
Anna: And how do trust and effectiveness interact in your day-to-day?
Silvia: With AI, I use it because it is effective for tasks, even if trust is partial. With humans, if trust is not there, I do not use it, so effectiveness cannot happen. So trust is like a gate for humans. For AI, trust is like a filter, not a gate.
Anna: Oh wow.
Silvia: Yes. Because I can still use AI pragmatically, like a tool, without trusting it emotionally. But for a coach, you cannot.
Anna: Ok. If you look at your earlier ratings, human trust 7, human effectiveness 6, AI trust 4, AI effectiveness 7. Does that pattern make sense to you now?
Silvia: Yes. It makes sense. Because humans are more trustworthy emotionally, but not always effective practically. AI is effective practically, but not trustworthy for identity.
Anna: In your opinion, what would increase trust in AI for career decisions?
Silvia: Hmm. That is hard. Maybe if it had boundaries, like it clearly says, I am not here to tell you what to want. I can help you explore options. Also, if it asked better questions, not just give answers. And if it was more transparent, like, why it suggests something.
Anna: So a more Socratic approach.
Silvia: Yes. And maybe if it was integrated with a human, so you do not feel alone with it.
Anna: Ok. That brings us to preferences and hybrid models.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction
Anna: Imagine a hybrid model: AI helps you prepare, structure, draft, and explore options, and then a human coach or mentor steps in for high-stakes decisions, values, and accountability. How does that sound to you?
Silvia: That sounds good. That is like, the ideal.
Anna: What feels good about it?
Silvia: Because it matches how I already behave. I use AI for fast tasks and structure. Then I go to peers for real talk. If a platform made that seamless, it would reduce friction.
Anna: What would make you sceptical?
Silvia: If it is not clear when AI stops and human starts. Like, if they pretend AI is a coach. That would annoy me. Also, privacy. If my AI chat is shared with a human without my control, I would not like that. And also, I fear the hybrid could still push me into optimization. Like, you do AI exercises, you get tasks, you become like, more productive, but not necessarily happier.
Anna: So you want it to support choice, not optimisation.
Silvia: Yes. Choice and clarity. Not hustle.
Anna: If you could design your ideal hybrid journey for your current question, BD vs product vs ops, what would it look like?
Silvia: Eehm. First, AI helps me map the roles. Like, what does BD look like in climate-tech, what does product look like, what does ops look like, day-to-day, success metrics, typical career paths.
Anna: Ok.
Silvia: Then AI helps me list my experiences and translate them into skills. Like, I already do some product discovery because I talk to customers, I translate feedback to engineers. That could be product. Or I coordinate partnerships, that is ops.
Anna: Mm.
Silvia: Then, with a human, I would talk about what I enjoy, what drains me, what I want to become. And also, the human can challenge my insecurity, like, are you really not technical enough, or are you just comparing yourself.
Anna: Would you prefer one-on-one or group?
Silvia: For this, one-on-one. Group is good for normalising, but for a decision, I want personal attention.
Anna: What kind of person would you trust most as a human in that hybrid?
Silvia: Someone who did BD or product in a technical startup. Ideally climate-tech, but at least B2B SaaS. And someone who understands Berlin culture, like ambiguity. And someone who does not judge my background. Like, International Relations is not a weakness.
Anna: And how would you want AI positioned in that model? As a tool, as a coach, as a copilot?
Silvia: Tool or copilot. Not coach. Coach implies emotional guidance. Copilot is like, you still fly the plane.
Anna: Nice. Any concerns about reliance, like overusing AI?
Silvia: Yes. I can see myself going into a loop. Like, prompt, refine, prompt, refine. It feels productive, but you are not deciding. So the system should maybe limit it or encourage reflection.
Anna: Encourage reflection how?
Silvia: Like, after giving options, ask, which one feels energising. Or, what do you fear. But not in a fake way. And maybe it should say, stop now and talk to a human.
Anna: So nudges.
Silvia: Yes. But not too paternalistic. Berlin people hate being told what to do. 
Anna: Fair.
Silvia: We are all like, do not control me. But also, please help me. 
Anna: That is very Berlin.
Silvia: Yes.
Anna: If you think about trust in a hybrid model, what are the key trust conditions?
Silvia: For AI part: transparency, boundaries, no hallucinations, and control over my data. For human part: confidentiality, being seen, and domain credibility. And also, a clear explanation of how the human is chosen, like matching logic. If it is random, I would not trust.
Anna: Ok. If you had to summarise your preference in one sentence, what would it be?
Silvia: AI for execution and structure, humans for meaning and courage.
Anna: Meaning and courage. Woah.
Silvia: Because decisions need courage. And courage comes from feeling supported, not from a perfect spreadsheet.
Anna: Mm. Ok. We are close to closing. I will ask a couple of final questions.
9) Closing
Anna: Looking forward, what is one concrete next step you want to take in the next month regarding your positioning question?
Silvia: I want to do a small experiment. Like, I want to take ownership of a product-related project. We have a feature request pipeline, and I want to lead one initiative from customer insight to shipping, with product and engineering. Just one.
Anna: That would give you information.
Silvia: Yes. It will show if I like product work, or if I hate it. And also, it will increase my technical confidence because I will work closer with engineers.
Anna: What support do you need to do that?
Silvia: Time and permission. And someone to mentor me, like the product lead. And I need to manage my own anxiety, like, not thinking I am stupid.
Anna: If you had a good coaching support, human or hybrid, what would success look like after, say, three months?
Silvia: I would have a clear narrative: I am a BD person with product mindset, or I am transitioning to product, or I am ops. And I would feel calmer. Like, ok, I am not behind. I am on a path. And I would feel more confident in technical meetings, like I can ask questions without shame.
Anna: Anything else you want to add that we did not cover?
Silvia: Maybe one thing. In climate-tech, there is also this moral pressure. Like, you want to do good, and sometimes you feel guilty if you focus on career and money. And coaching that ignores that feels incomplete. AI also ignores that. It is like, optimise your career. But in climate-tech, you also want alignment with values.
Anna: Thank you. So the value layer matters.
Silvia: Yes. Because otherwise you can become cynical. And then you leave climate-tech.
Anna: Ok. Thank you, Silvia. That was very good to talk to you. I will stop the recording now. Thanks again for your time.
Silvia: Thank you. It was actually nice to talk. And good luck with your thesis.
Anna: Thank you so much. Bye.
Silvia: Ciao.










Mihir

2) Participant context and career situation

Anna: To start, where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now?
Mihir: Um, okay. So I am 26. I am from India. I have been in Germany for, like, two and a half years now. I am based in Munich. I am doing my Master’s in Electrical Engineering, but with a track in embedded systems and robotics, like more software plus hardware. I am also working as a working student in a startup.
Anna: Mm. And when you say career decisions or questions, what is the biggest one for you right now?
Mihir: The biggest is, what role do I actually want and can I get in Germany. Because I feel I am doing many things. At the startup I do embedded stuff, some firmware, sometimes testing, sometimes a bit of data logging and like Python scripts. But I do not know if I should aim for embedded software engineer, or robotics engineer, or maybe something like systems engineer. And then also the whole German job market has these titles, like Entwicklungsingenieur, Softwareentwickler, Systemingenieur, and they all sound similar but then requirements are different. So, eehm, I am confused.
Anna: When did that confusion become more intense? Was there a moment in the last months?
Mihir: Yes. Actually last summer, like around June or July 2025, I had to decide about my thesis topic and also whether to extend my working student contract. My startup wanted me to stay, but it is a bit, how to say, unclear. Like I learn a lot, but I do not know if it is the right brand for my CV. And then my university supervisor was like, pick a thesis that matches your target job. And then I was like, but I do not know the target job. So it became a loop.
Anna: A loop, yes. What did you decide in the end?
Mihir: In the end, I chose a thesis topic that is kind of in the middle. It is about real-time sensor data processing on microcontrollers, with some edge AI concepts, but not too heavy. My thinking was, okay, embedded plus some ML buzzword helps. But then I also feel, maybe I am doing it for the job market, not because I love it. And then I feel guilty. It is a lot.
Anna: When you say you feel guilty, can you say more? Guilty about what?
Mihir: Guilty because I feel I am not having a clear passion. Like in India, also with my family, it is like you choose a track and you become that. Here I see people very focused. Like, my classmates, some are like, I am robotics, I do ROS2, I build robots, that is it. Some are like, I am power electronics, I do SiC, that is it. And I am like, I can do many things but I cannot say one sentence what I am. And then on LinkedIn everyone is like, I joined this company as full-time, I got Blue Card, I bought a car, whatever. And I am still studying. And I am 26. And in my head it is like, bro, what are you doing. It makes me very low sometimes.
Anna: Thanks for sharing that. When you feel low, what does that look like day to day?
Mihir: Um. I sleep late because I overthink. Then in the morning I am tired. I still go to work because I have to. And then I do okay at work, because tasks are clear. But when it is about future, like applying, planning, then I freeze. And sometimes I just scroll job ads and then I get more stressed. Or I compare salaries. Or I watch videos like, how to get a job in Germany as international student. And then it is too much. It is not like I cannot function, but it feels heavy. Like a constant pressure.
Anna: That pressure, is it mainly about time, money, visa, or identity, or all of it?
Mihir: All. Money because Munich is expensive. I share a WG. Rent is crazy. Visa because I have student visa. I know after graduation I can get job-seeker, and then Blue Card path, but it still feels like a ticking thing. Also identity because I feel, I came here to build something. I wanted a proper career. And I am still not there.
Anna: What is your German level right now?
Mihir: Like B2 maybe. I can do daily things. I can speak in supermarket, Bürgerbüro, but job interviews in German, no. And job ads are sometimes in German. Or mixed. So I use AI to translate and explain. That is a big part.
Anna: We will go deeper into that later. Before that, tell me a bit about your working student role. What kind of startup is it, and what do you actually do there?
Mihir: Okay. It is a small startup, maybe 25 people. They build a hardware product for industrial monitoring. Like sensors plus edge device. I am in the engineering team. My tasks, for example, one month I worked on firmware for a microcontroller to handle SPI communication with a sensor. Then we had issues with timing, so I had to debug with logic analyzer. Then I also wrote a small Python tool to parse logs and produce graphs, like check packet loss. Sometimes I also support QA by writing test scripts. So it is many hats.
Anna: And how do you feel about that?
Mihir: Mixed. On one side, I love that I learn. Like it is hands-on. It is not like only theory. On the other side, when I try to write my CV, it becomes messy. Because the job ad wants, like, embedded C, RTOS, communication protocols, but also wants requirements engineering, or wants safety standards, or wants AUTOSAR. And I am like, I did a bit of everything, but not deep in those. And then I think, am I behind.
Anna: You said you sometimes use AI for confidence-building, like am I behind. Is that connected to this?
Mihir: Yes. Very connected. Because when I see job ads, I feel not enough. And then I ask AI, like, if I have these skills, is it normal, am I behind, what can I do in 3 months. Sometimes it helps, sometimes it makes it worse.
Anna: Okay. We will come back. One last thing in this section. What kind of plan do you imagine would calm you? If you picture an ideal structured plan, what is in it?
Mihir: Hmm. Like a timeline. Like, okay, next 8 weeks, do this project, update CV, apply to 10 roles per week, practice interviews twice a week, learn German one hour per day. And also milestones, like by March, have one strong portfolio project, by April, have 5 interviews, by May, finish thesis. Something like that. And also decision points, like if embedded roles are not working, then pivot to data engineering or something. But with logic, not just random.
Anna: Aha. Got it.
3) Career support in general

Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Mihir: First, my friends. Mostly Indian friends also in Munich. We are all like in the same boat. And sometimes German classmates. But with Germans I feel, they are kind, but they do not fully understand visa pressure, you know. Then I also talk to my girlfriend in India, on video call. She is supportive, but she cannot help with Germany specifics. And then I use online resources, like YouTube, LinkedIn posts, sometimes Reddit, but it is too much noise. And I use AI tools, like ChatGPT, also sometimes Gemini, but mostly ChatGPT.
Anna: Any university career services?
Mihir: I tried one workshop at university. It was okay, like basic, how to structure CV, what is Anschreiben, things like that. But it was very general. And also mostly in German. So I felt, okay, nice, but not enough.
Anna: What has actually helped you, and what felt unhelpful or frustrating?
Mihir: Helpful is when someone tells me concrete reality. Like, in Germany they like this format, like this evidence, they ask this in interviews, like they want projects. Also helpful is when someone reads my CV and says, this is confusing, cut this. Unhelpful is generic motivation. Like, keep going, you will be fine. Because I know they mean well, but it does not reduce my uncertainty. It is like a band-aid.
Anna: Can you give me an example of high support but not effective? Like someone you trust, but it did not help?
Mihir: Yes. My close friend here, he is like my brother. He listens. He tells me, you are smart, you will get job, do not worry. And I trust him, like emotionally. But after talking, I still do not know what to do next. So I feel supported, but not effective. And then sometimes I feel worse because I wasted time talking instead of doing. But also, I need that sometimes. So it is complicated.
Anna: That is really clear. Supportive but not directional.
Mihir: Yes, exactly.
Anna: And in terms of paid support, have you ever tried a paid career coach?
Mihir: No. I have not. I thought about it, but I have no idea where to find the right one. And I also worry about money and scams. Like, there are many coaches on Instagram or LinkedIn. Everyone is a coach. How to know who is legit. And also, I feel I need it urgently, but I do not know how to choose.
Anna: When you say urgently, what makes it urgent right now?
Mihir: Because I feel time is running. I am 26, still student. I moved from role to role. In India I did internship, then I worked one year, then I came to Germany, then I did working student. So it feels like I am always junior. I want to finally start a real career, like a full-time role with a plan. And also visa and money. So I feel urgency.
Anna: Okay.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust

Anna: You mentioned you had a mentor via a student association. Can you tell me about that experience? How did you meet, and what was the context?
Mihir: Yes. So there is a student association in Munich, like for international students in engineering. They have mentorship program. I joined because a friend told me. It was like, you fill a form, they match you with a mentor. My mentor was an Indian guy, but he has been in Germany for, like, 10 years. He works at a big automotive supplier as embedded engineer. So he knows the system.
Anna: How did the first meeting go?
Mihir: We met in a cafe near Sendlinger Tor. It was very Munich. He was like, okay, tell me your story. And I started talking, very messy. Like, I did this, then that, then Germany. And he listened. And then he asked very structured questions. Like, what do you want, what are your constraints, what do you enjoy, what are your strengths. And then he also explained how hiring works in Germany. That was the big value.
Anna: What did he explain that was new for you?
Mihir: Many things. Like, he said in Germany, HR screens first, they look for keywords, but also they look for stability. They do not like too chaotic story. He said, your CV must show a narrative. He also explained that for embedded roles, you need to show you worked close to hardware, not only Python. And he explained Blue Card basics. Like, he said do not panic, but you need a salary threshold and a degree. He said also, in interviews they will ask about your projects in detail, and they want you to explain your decisions, not just list tools.
Anna: Do you remember a specific moment in that conversation that changed something for you?
Mihir: Yes. He looked at my CV and he said, your CV is like a shopping list. He said, you wrote everything you touched, but you did not write what you achieved. And he took one bullet, like I wrote, implemented firmware in C. He said, no, write, implemented firmware module for sensor communication, reduced latency, improved stability, something like that. And then he asked me, did it improve anything? And I was like, I do not know, it just worked. And he said, go back, measure, or at least describe impact. That was like, okay, German style. Evidence.
Anna: What made you trust him?
Mihir: Hmm. Competence, first. He clearly knew the market. He used German terms, like he talked about Tarif, about Betriebsrat, about what working student means, like Werkstudent, and he understood. Second, he was honest. He did not sugarcoat. He said, Munich is competitive, do not apply randomly. Third, he understood international student constraints. He knew visa, language anxiety. And also he was respectful. Like, he did not judge me for being confused. So I felt safe.
Anna: Did you feel confidentiality with him? Like you could share things?
Mihir: Yes, because it was one-on-one and he also said, whatever we discuss stays here. It was informal but he said it. So I felt okay to say I am stressed.
Anna: After that mentor meeting, what changed in your behavior?
Mihir: I changed my CV format. I made it one page, more focused. I made sections like projects, skills, and I removed many random things. I also started tracking applications in a spreadsheet, like he suggested. And I started preparing for interviews by doing STAR method. He told me about that. So I had more structure.
Anna: Did it solve the confusion about which role fits you?
Mihir: Not fully. It helped me understand the process. But role identity is still open. He gave some suggestions, like, based on my interest, embedded software is good, but he also said, do not chase buzzwords. But I still feel uncertain.
Anna: That is interesting. So high trust, and useful, but not fully effective for the deeper question.
Mihir: Yes. Exactly. Because he was one person, and also he has his own bias. He is in automotive. He thinks embedded is the best. Maybe he is right, but maybe not for me.
Anna: If you imagine a paid career coach, what would you expect from them that your mentor did not provide?
Mihir: I expect more systematic. Like not only one meeting. I want a process. Like first, assess my strengths, values, constraints. Then explore roles. Then choose direction. Then make plan, with weekly check-ins. And accountability. And also someone who knows the German market but also can understand international student reality. And also, I want them to push me, not only comfort me. Like, you said you will apply to 10 jobs, did you do it. But in a kind way.
Anna: What would make you trust a paid coach?
Mihir: Hmm. Proof of competence, like their background. Maybe they worked in hiring or in industry. Also testimonials, but that can be fake. Maybe if they understand my context quickly, like they ask good questions. Also transparency, like what they can and cannot do. And confidentiality. And also, realistic guidance. Like not, you will get job in 2 weeks. That would make me distrust.
Anna: Have you had any interactions with someone who claimed to be a coach, and it felt off?
Mihir: Yes, on LinkedIn. Someone messaged, like, I can help you get job in Germany, pay me 500 euros, I will rewrite your CV. It felt like spam. He did not ask anything about me. So I ignored.
Anna: So for you, trust is tied to personalization and realism.
Mihir: Yes.
Anna: Okay.
5) AI tools: experience and trust

Anna: Now let us talk about AI tools. You said you use AI for learning, debugging, and explaining German job ads. Can you tell me about the first time you used AI for a career-related thing?
Mihir: I think first time was in 2023, when I came to Germany. I was applying for working student roles. I saw job ads in German. Like, they had many words I did not understand. Like, Kenntnisse in, Erfahrung mit, wünschenswert, Vorteil, and all these. So I copied the job ad into ChatGPT and I asked, explain this in English and tell me what are must-have and nice-to-have. And it gave me a structured breakdown. That was very helpful.
Anna: Can you give me a vivid example, like a specific job ad or requirement where it helped you decode expectations?
Mihir: Yes. There was one job ad for Embedded Software Working Student. It said something like, Erfahrung in hardwarenaher Programmierung, Kenntnisse in Echtzeitbetriebssystemen, gute Deutschkenntnisse wünschenswert, and then it had like, CAN, LIN, SPI, I2C. I was like, okay, I know SPI, I2C, a bit of CAN from university, but not deep. And German part made me panic. So AI explained, hardwarenah means close to hardware, real-time OS is like FreeRTOS, and wünschenswert means desirable but not mandatory. It also told me, highlight SPI and I2C projects, and for German, mention that you are learning and comfortable in English. That reduced stress.
Anna: So speed and translation reduced anxiety.
Mihir: Yes, because it made it clear. Like, okay, I do not need to be perfect German. At least for working student, maybe English okay.
Anna: What about using AI for learning and debugging in your technical work?
Mihir: That is daily. Like, I am working with C on microcontrollers. Sometimes there is a bug, like a pointer issue, or timing issue. I paste a simplified code snippet, and ask, what could cause this behavior. It gives ideas. Or for Python scripts, it helps me fix errors fast. Also for concepts, like interrupts, DMA, concurrency, it explains in simple terms. I use it like a tutor. And I trust it more there, because I can test the output. If it says something wrong, I will see.
Anna: So you can verify.
Mihir: Yes. In career advice, it is harder to verify.
Anna: You also mentioned you sometimes use AI for confidence-building, like am I behind. Can you describe a moment when you did that?
Mihir: Yes. One night, I was scrolling LinkedIn. I saw someone from my university, same age, got a full-time role at a big company. And then I felt like, I am failing. So I opened AI and I wrote like, I am 26, still studying, is it too late, am I behind compared to others in Germany. And AI replied like, no, everyone has different path, it is normal, you have international experience, do not compare. It felt good for 5 minutes. But then it felt generic. Like a fortune cookie.
Anna: Did it help in any practical way?
Mihir: Not really. It calmed me, but not grounded. Then I asked, okay, give me a plan. And it gave a plan, but it was too optimistic. Like, apply to 50 jobs per week, build two projects, learn German to B2 in 2 months. And then I felt worse because it was unrealistic. So sometimes AI increases anxiety.
Anna: That is a really concrete example. What do you think made that plan feel untrustworthy?
Mihir: Because it did not consider my actual time. I have thesis, work, cooking, life. It was like, just do everything. Also, it did not ask me questions first. It assumed. And it gave too many options. Like, you can be embedded, you can be data, you can be product, you can do PhD. And I was like, no, I need fewer options, not more.
Anna: So for trust in AI, speed and availability are positives, but accuracy and fit to your context are concerns.
Mihir: Yes.
Anna: Are there moments where you do not trust AI, but you still use it because it is useful?
