Mihir: Yes. For example, CV templates. Sometimes I ask AI to rewrite a bullet point. I do not fully trust it because it can exaggerate or use fake metrics. But it still gives a good structure. Like it transforms boring sentence into impact format. Then I edit. So low trust but still useful.
Anna: That is a good separation. You do not fully trust the content, but you trust the format help.
Mihir: Exactly.
Anna: What are your trust drivers for AI specifically? If you list them.
Mihir: Speed. Availability at night. Ability to explain and translate. Also, it is non-judgmental. Like, I can ask stupid questions. And it can structure information. Like it can turn a mess into bullets.
Anna: And what reduces trust?
Mihir: Hallucinations. Like sometimes it says things with confidence that are wrong. Also generic advice. Also privacy, like if I paste my CV with personal data. I try to remove, but still. And also emotional flattening. Like it responds like a bot, even if it is trying to be nice. It does not feel like real understanding.
Anna: When you say hallucinations, have you caught a specific one?
Mihir: Yes. One time I asked about Blue Card requirements. It gave salary threshold numbers, but they were inconsistent. Like one message said one number, then later another. Then I checked other sources and it was confusing. So I realized I cannot rely on it for legal stuff. I can use it to understand concept, but not exact.
Anna: Okay.
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks

Anna: Now I want to compare human support and AI support through specific tasks. I will name a task, and you tell me what you would prefer and why. First task clarifying career options, like what roles exist and which fits you.
Mihir: For options, I use AI first. Because it can list many roles and explain. Like, difference between embedded engineer and firmware engineer and systems engineer. That is helpful. But then I need a human to narrow down based on me. Because AI can list, but it does not know my personality. A human can ask, what do you enjoy, do you like working alone, do you like debugging, do you like customers, and then guide.
Anna: Second task making a decision, like choosing a direction for the next year.
Mihir: Human. Because decision is emotional and also has risk. I want someone to challenge me and also understand my constraints. AI can give pros and cons, but it feels like a spreadsheet. Human can see my fear and maybe call it out.
Anna: Third task: planning concrete next steps, like a weekly plan, checklists, milestones.
Mihir: This is interesting. I think AI can draft a plan fast. Like, it can give a template, weekly schedule, application pipeline. But I need human to make it realistic and hold me accountable. Because AI will not check if I did it. Also human can adjust based on real life.
Anna: Fourth task rewriting CV and cover letters.
Mihir: AI is strong for language and structure. But human is better for truth and positioning. Like, AI can make it sound good, but sometimes too generic. A human who knows German hiring can say, this is not credible, or this story is confusing. Also mentor can say, do not write this because Germans interpret it differently.
Anna: Fifth task preparing for interviews.
Mihir: Both. AI can simulate questions, like give me typical embedded interview questions, and I can practice. It can also help me structure answers. Human is better for feedback on my communication, like how I sound, my confidence, and also the cultural part. Like in Germany they want concise answers, not too much self-promotion but also not too shy. A human can tell me.
Anna: Sixth task emotional support, like when you feel behind.
Mihir: Human. Even if it is not effective in plan, it is real. AI can give reassurance, but it feels hollow. But also, sometimes I do not want to burden friends, so I use AI for venting. It is like, okay, at least something. But not the same.
Anna: That is very nuanced. You are using both, but for different functions.
Mihir: Yes. And also because human costs money and time. AI is free and instant. That matters.
Anna: Can you describe a specific episode where AI helped you in a technical or career task more than a human could at that moment?
Mihir: Yes. At work, we had a bug in firmware. It was like, sometimes sensor read fails. My colleague was busy. I asked AI, and it suggested that I should check if the chip select timing is correct and if there is a race condition with interrupt. It gave me a checklist. I tested, and actually there was an interrupt that was messing the timing. So AI helped me faster than waiting for colleague. That felt very effective. And trust was okay because I verified.
Anna: And a specific episode where a human helped more than AI?
Mihir: The mentor meeting, definitely. Because he understood my whole context. He told me realistic steps. And also he gave me confidence grounded in experience. Like, he said, I have seen many people like you, it works, but you must focus. That felt different than AI saying, you can do anything.
Anna: And an episode where AI increased anxiety?
Mihir: The night with the plan. Also sometimes when I ask, what is best career for me, it gives too many options and I spiral. It is like opening 20 tabs.
Anna: And an episode where a human was kind but not effective?
Mihir: My friend I mentioned. Also my mom. She is like, beta, do not worry, everything will be fine, just study. In Hindi she says, sabbbbbb jhe. It is sweet. But it does not help with Germany job market. 
7) Trust–effectiveness link

Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about human mentoring or coaching, how does trust affect whether it is effective?
Mihir: Trust is like, it decides how honest I can be. If I do not trust, I will only show the nice story. Like, I will say, I am fine, I just want CV tips. But the real issue is, I am scared and confused. If I trust, I can say, I feel behind, I do not know my direction, I am panicking. Then the person can help with the real problem. Also, if I trust their competence, I will follow their advice. If I doubt, I will not act. So trust affects action.
Anna: What are the main drivers of trust for you in a human mentor or coach?
Mihir: Competence, like they know German hiring, they know engineering roles. Empathy, but not pity. Understanding international constraints, like visa, language, working student. Confidentiality. Structure, like they have a process, not random talking. And also feeling seen, like they remember details, they connect patterns. Like, if I say, I always avoid applying, they notice avoidance and help.
Anna: And for AI, how does trust affect effectiveness?
Mihir: For AI, trust is more like, do I believe the information is correct and relevant. If I trust it is accurate, it is effective. If I suspect it is hallucinating, I will double-check and then it is less effective because it adds work. But sometimes even if I do not trust fully, it can still be effective as a tool, like templates. So effectiveness can happen with partial trust. But for deep decisions, low trust means I will not rely on it. I will only use it as a brainstorming tool.
Anna: You just said something important. Sometimes low trust, still useful. Can you explain that with one more example?
Mihir: Yes. Cover letters in German. My German is not enough. So I ask AI to draft an Anschreiben. I do not trust the exact wording because it can sound too formal or weird. But it gives me a base. Then I edit with my friend who knows German better. So I use it despite low trust.
Anna: And opposite direction, high trust but not effective. We talked about supportive friends. Is there any case where you trusted a mentor or human, but it did not lead to results?
Mihir: Hmm. Actually yes. After my mentor meeting, I felt high trust. But I did not follow everything. Like, he told me to apply regularly and track. I did for two weeks, then thesis got heavy, I stopped. So effectiveness was limited not because his advice was bad, but because I did not implement. And also because he was not there to follow up. So trust was high, but outcome not fully.
Anna: So there is also an accountability component.
Mihir: Yes. Trust alone is not enough. I need structure and accountability. That is why I think a paid coach could help, if they do weekly check-ins.
Anna: If we separate trust and effectiveness, how do you personally define effectiveness in career support? What are your indicators?
Mihir: Effectiveness is when after the support, I have clarity and next steps. Like, I know what role I am targeting, what companies, what skills to highlight. Also my applications become stronger, like more interviews. Interview performance improves, like I answer better. And also reduced overwhelm. Like I feel calm because I know what to do. And confidence grounded in evidence, like not just feeling good, but actually seeing progress.
Anna: You mentioned reduced overwhelm. What reduces overwhelm for you concretely?
Mihir: Checklists. Like, today do this. And also having fewer decisions. If someone says, for next month, focus only on embedded roles, do not also apply to data. That reduces overwhelm. And also a timeline. If I see, okay, thesis ends May, apply from March, interview April, then I feel less panic.
Anna: Great.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction

Anna: I want to embed a quick micro-poll now. I will ask you to rate from 0 to 10. Zero means no trust or not effective at all, ten means fully trust or extremely effective. And then you explain. Okay?
Mihir: Okay.
Anna: First, trust in human coaches or mentors for career guidance. What number?
Mihir: I would say 7.5. Maybe 8 if it is a good mentor like mine.
Anna: Why 7.5 or 8, not higher?
Mihir: Because humans can be biased, or they can not understand my niche. Also, some coaches can be scammy. So I trust conditionally. But when I find a good one, I trust a lot.
Anna: Second, trust in AI tools for career guidance. What number?
Mihir: For career guidance, maybe 6. For technical learning, higher, like 8. But for career, 6.
Anna: Why 6?
Mihir: Because it is helpful and fast, but accuracy and context is uncertain. And it can give generic advice. So I trust it as an assistant, not as authority.
Anna: Third, perceived effectiveness of human support for your situation right now.
Mihir: Effectiveness, I think 7. Because mentor helped, but I need more continuous support. If I had a coach, maybe it could be 9, but I have not tried. So based on my experience, 7.
Anna: And perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation right now.
Mihir: Hmm. Maybe 6.5. It helps a lot in small tasks, like translating job ads, drafting, learning. But for the big picture, it does not solve.
Anna: Great. Thanks. Now, I will describe a hybrid model briefly. Imagine a service where AI helps you explore options, translate job ads, draft CV bullets, and create a structured weekly plan. Then a human coach steps in for deeper sessions: choosing a direction, reviewing your story, practicing interviews, and accountability check-ins. How does that sound?
Mihir: That sounds like exactly what I need. Because AI can do the fast work, and human can do the deeper work. Also it could reduce cost, because human time is expensive. So if AI does preparation, human session is more focused. That sounds good.
Anna: What would you need to trust such a hybrid model?
Mihir: Transparency. Like, what does AI do, what does human do. And data privacy, like who sees my data. Also quality of the human coach, like matching. Because if I get wrong coach, then it fails. And also boundaries, like AI should not give legal advice about visa with exact numbers, but it can explain concepts. So clear boundaries.
Anna: Matching is a big one. What would make you feel the matching is good?
Mihir: If the coach understands international student path and engineering. Like, not a general life coach. Also maybe someone who worked in Germany hiring. And also personality fit. Like, I want structured, not too fluffy. I want someone who gives plan. So if the system asks me questions and matches based on that, I would trust more.
Anna: And what would make you suspicious or reduce trust in a hybrid model?
Mihir: If it feels like marketing, like promises too much. Or if the AI outputs are clearly generic. Or if they hide that AI is used. I prefer honesty. Also if they push me to buy more sessions. Like upsell. That would reduce trust.
Anna: If you had to choose today, for the next two months, what would your ideal support mix be?
Mihir: I think, AI daily for small tasks, like job ads, learning, drafting. And then a human coach maybe every week or every two weeks for direction and accountability. And maybe one mentor in industry for real insight. Like, if I can talk to someone at a company I target. That would be ideal.
Anna: And if we go deeper, what would you want the coach to deliver by the end of those two months, as concrete outputs?
Mihir: Output one: clear role direction, like embedded software engineer focusing on industrial or automotive or robotics. Output two: CV and LinkedIn story aligned. Output three: portfolio project with documentation. Output four: interview prep plan and practice sessions. Output five: application pipeline system. And maybe German learning plan for job context, like key phrases.
Anna: Oh wow. That is very structured. 
Mihir: Yes. Because structure calms me.
Anna: Mee too. When you imagine having that structure, what changes emotionally?
Mihir: I feel less like I am floating. Like, right now I feel like I am swimming with no shore. If I have a plan, I can breathe. Even if plan is hard, at least it is clear. Then I feel, okay, I am not wasting time. Then I can sleep better.
Anna: That is a strong image. Swimming with no shore.
Mihir: Yes. It is like that.
Anna: One last thing in this section. You said you are not against career coaches and you feel you need one urgently, but you have no idea where to find the right one. What would make it easier to find the right one?
Mihir: A place where coaches are vetted. Like, not random. Also where I can see, okay, this coach knows engineering, knows international students, knows Germany hiring. And reviews that are real. And maybe a first short call to check fit. And clear pricing. Because hidden pricing is suspicious.
Anna: Great.
9) Closing

Anna: We are coming to the end. I want to check if there is anything important we did not cover about your experiences with human support, AI tools, trust, or effectiveness.
Mihir: No.
Anna: Thank you, Mihir. That was very helpful and very clear. I will stop recording now. Any final questions for me?
Mihir: No questions. Thank you. It was actually nice to talk. 
Anna: Thanks. Take care.
Mihir: You too.




Katharina 
1) Opening and consent

Anna: Hi Katharina. Thanks for joining. Before we start, do I have your permission to record this interview for research purposes? Everything will be anonymised, and you can skip any question.
Katharina: Yes, you have my permission to record. And thank you for saying I can skip. That already feels, um, safer. 
Anna: Great. And are you comfortable doing this mostly in English, with occasional German words if that feels more natural?
Katharina: Yes, totally. I work in English anyway. And sometimes I just say Gehalt or Jahresgespraech because it is faster.
Anna: Perfect. Just to set expectations, the interview is about your experience with career support, including human coaching and AI tools, and especially how you think about trust and perceived effectiveness. There are no right or wrong answers. If something feels too personal, we can move on. Does that work?
Katharina: Yes. I will try to be honest. Sometimes I notice I start to sound like I am presenting. So if I do that, tell me.
Anna: That is helpful. I may ask you to slow down or give an example, but I will not push you to share anything you do not want to. Could you start by introducing yourself briefly and what you do?
Katharina: Sure. I am Katharina, 28, born in Germany, and I live in Hamburg. I work as a product marketing specialist at a B2B software company. We are SaaS. We sell a platform that helps mid-market companies manage procurement workflows and supplier onboarding. So it is, like, supply chain and operations focused, not shiny consumer stuff. My role is product marketing, so positioning, messaging, launches, sales enablement, customer stories, sometimes competitive analysis, and internal stakeholder management, which is basically half the job. 
Anna: Great thank you. Let us start with your current career situation.
2) Participant context and career situation

Anna: Where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing, or recently faced?
Katharina: Um, I would say I am at this point where I am good at my job, and people trust me operationally, but I feel stuck. Like a plateau. The workload is high, I am involved in everything, but the path forward is unclear. I am not sure if I should aim for a PMM Lead track, like leading a small team or owning a bigger portfolio, or if I should pivot into product management. And then, connected to that, I avoided addressing a salary mismatch for too long. So my questions are: what is my next role, and how do I advocate for myself without burning bridges.
Anna: When you say you feel stuck, what does that look like day to day?
Katharina: It looks like this constant sprint mode. We have releases every two weeks, but real launches are more like monthly, and then bigger themes quarterly. And it is always messy because product changes scope late, sales wants certainty early, and marketing wants a clean story. I am in the middle. I do messaging docs, I build decks, I do training sessions, I write release notes that are actually product announcements, I coordinate with customer success for webinars, I chase people for approvals, I negotiate claims with legal, and I am also supposed to do research like win-loss and persona updates. There is never a quiet week. And because there is so much to do, I feel like I cannot step back and think strategically about my own career. I keep telling myself, later.
Anna: What was the most significant decision in the last 6 to 12 months?
Katharina: Investing in coaching. Like paying real money and committing time. That was the biggest decision because it meant I am admitting I cannot just power through. And it also meant I committed to two uncomfortable things: actually addressing salary, and exploring roles beyond my current company, including product roles. Before that, I was talking about it, but not doing it.
Anna: What made it difficult to make that decision?
Katharina: A few things. One is I am used to solving problems alone. Another is I was worried it would be a waste of money, like some fluffy advice. And also, time. Because the workload is high, so scheduling five sessions feels like another project. And honestly, I had a bit of shame. Like, why do I need help for something adults should figure out. But then I was also not sleeping well, and I had this feeling of, if I do not change something now, I will just slowly become bitter.
Anna: When did you notice the plateau? Was there a moment, or was it gradual?
Katharina: It was gradual. But there were two moments. One was a performance review cycle where my manager said, you are doing great, you are reliable, everyone likes working with you. And I was happy for five minutes, but then I realised, there is no concrete plan. No clear next step. Just more work. The second moment was when a new product manager joined and I was basically onboarding them on messaging and customer context, and they had a higher salary than me, which I found out indirectly. And I was like, wait. I am doing senior work, but I am paid like an earlier-career specialist.
Anna: That brings us to salary mismatch. Could you describe what you mean by mismatch, and how you avoided addressing it?
Katharina: So, I joined three years ago at 52k base. At the time I thought it was okay. Hamburg is expensive, but I was also coming from a smaller marketing job, and I was just happy to get into B2B SaaS. Over time my scope grew a lot. I was owning launches for two product areas: supplier onboarding and approval workflows. I also started mentoring a working student, I built the competitive battlecards, and I was running monthly enablement sessions. But my salary increases were small and very standard. Like 3 percent, then later a small bump when my title changed to specialist. But the market moved. Recruiters started reaching out with ranges like 65 to 75 for PMM roles. And then I compared with peers. Not exact, because Germany is weird about salary conversations, but I had enough signals that I was below the band for my impact.
Anna: And how did you avoid addressing it?
Katharina: By telling myself stories. Like, now is not the right time. The company is investing, budgets are tight. Or, there are salary bands, so nothing can change. Or, I should first prove myself in this one more launch. There is always a next milestone. Also, I told myself, if I ask, I will be seen as difficult. Like the annoying person who makes things about money. And I did not want that identity.
Anna: Under those stories, what was the fear?
Katharina: Fear of rejection. Fear of being told no, and then having to face what that means. Because if they say no, then either I accept it and feel undervalued, or I have to consider leaving, which is also scary because changing jobs is energy, interviews, uncertainty. And I was already tired. So avoidance felt like self-protection.
Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed at that point?
Katharina: Structure and accountability. And also someone who can hold the emotional part without making it therapy. Like, I did not need to talk about my childhood. I needed to talk about why I avoid money conversations and how to still do them. And I needed someone who knows the German corporate context, like salary bands, how performance reviews work, what is realistic in a negotiation.
Anna: What was your work background before this role?
Katharina: I did a Bachelor in business administration, and during that I had internships in marketing, mostly B2B. Then I worked two years in a smaller company in Hamburg, like a services company, doing marketing generalist work. Then I moved into SaaS. So in total I have around five to six years of work experience if you count internships, but three years in this specific PMM type role.
Anna: And in your current company, what is the structure like? Is it a startup, scaleup, corporate?
Katharina: It is like a scaleup. Around 250 people. Headquarters in Hamburg, but we have offices in Berlin and Munich too. Product and engineering are mostly Hamburg and Berlin. We sell to mid-market, and the sales cycle is longer, like three to six months. So PMM is heavy on enablement and proof points.
Anna: Okay. Let us talk about career support in general.
3) Career support in general

Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Katharina: Usually friends first. Some friends from university, some friends in tech. Then sometimes colleagues I trust, like one product manager who is very straightforward. I also read stuff online, like PMM career ladders and salary reports, but those can be generic. And I have used AI tools lightly, mainly for writing, like CV bullets or interview question lists.
Anna: What has actually helped, and what felt unhelpful or frustrating?
Katharina: Helpful is when someone gives structure and also asks uncomfortable questions. Like, what do you actually want, and what are you avoiding. And also, someone who understands the context. Like B2B, cross-functional politics, German salary norms. Unhelpful is generic encouragement. Like, you are great, you deserve more. It feels good, but it does not change behaviour.
Anna: Can you give an example of trusted but not effective support?
Katharina: Yes. My best friend from university. I trust her completely. She is kind, she listens. But she works in public sector communications. Her advice is shaped by that environment. She told me, just wait for the next review, they will recognise you, and do not push too much. And I felt emotionally supported, but it was not effective. It kept me passive. She did not challenge my avoidance because she hates conflict too. So it was trust without effectiveness.
Anna: And the opposite, effective but not trusted?
Katharina: AI is the obvious one. It can give me a pretty good template for a negotiation email or a structured interview answer. That is effective for that narrow task. But I do not trust it with sensitive context, like exact salary numbers or internal politics. So I do not share the real inputs, which means it cannot help with the deeper part.
Anna: If you think about your company environment, do people talk openly about career development?
Katharina: They say the right things. Like, we care about growth, we have frameworks. But in reality, it is mostly driven by business needs. Promotions happen when a team needs it or when someone threatens to leave. That is my cynical view. There are also good managers, but there is a lot of ambiguity.
Anna: What did you try before coaching to address the plateau?
Katharina: I tried working harder, honestly. Taking more ownership. I also tried asking my manager for a growth plan, but it stayed vague. Like, keep doing what you are doing, take more strategic initiatives. But no concrete milestones. I also tried doing informational chats internally with product managers, but those were informal and I did not follow through. And I read a lot of content about product management versus product marketing, but reading does not make decisions.
Anna: Okay. Let us move into your human coaching experience.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust

Anna: You mentioned a structured five-session coaching package with a certified coach. Can you describe what it was, how you found it, and what made you commit?
Katharina: Yes. I found the coach through a recommendation from a colleague, actually. She said the coach is certified and also used to working with people in tech. I checked her website, which looked serious, not too salesy. The package was five sessions, each 75 minutes. Three were online on Zoom, one was in-person in Hamburg, and the last one was online. There was also email support between sessions, like accountability check-ins. I committed because I realised I was spending energy avoiding decisions. And the cost, ironically, made it real. Like, if I pay, I will show up.
Anna: What was the cost range, if you feel comfortable sharing?
Katharina: It was around 1,100 euros for the package. So not cheap. I hesitated. But then I thought, I spend money on other things that do not change my life. And this might.
Anna: Tell me about the first session. What happened, and what was the early trust moment for you?
Katharina: The first session was very structured, which I loved. She started with confidentiality boundaries explicitly. She said, everything you share stays here, and she explained the only exceptions, like if there is a serious risk situation, but for career topics it is confidential. She also asked me what I need to feel safe in coaching. That surprised me. No one in work asked me that. And she outlined her process transparently. She said, we will clarify your situation, define a goal, identify blockers, build a plan, and practise the key conversations. It felt like a process, not a vibe.
Anna: When she asked what you need to feel safe, what did you answer?
Katharina: I said, I need confidentiality, obviously, and I need clear structure. And I said, I need challenge without shame. Like, you can confront me, but do not make me feel stupid. And she repeated it back, like, challenge without shaming, got it. And then she said, if at any point you feel shame in the room, tell me, because shame blocks learning. That was like, wow. That made me trust her quickly.
Anna: How else did trust form early?
Katharina: Competence. She understood the German corporate context. Like, she asked about salary bands, job levels, performance review cadence. She knew how managers talk here. And she did not jump into advice immediately. She asked questions first. That made me feel she is not projecting. Also, she was consistent. She ended the session with clear homework and clear next steps. That reliability builds trust for me.
Anna: What were your trust drivers for a human coach, if you list them out?
Katharina: Confidentiality, structure, competence in my market, empathy, challenge, consistency, and being seen. Like remembering details, catching patterns, not just listening passively.
Anna: Did you have any doubts about her early on?
Katharina: A little. I was worried it would be too psychological and not practical. Because I am a practical person. But she balanced it. She would explore the emotional blockers and then translate into action. That is what made it work.
Anna: Can you describe a moment where she challenged you without shaming, and it changed your behaviour?
Katharina: Yes. In session two, I was talking about salary in a very analytical way. I was describing benchmarks, role scope, market ranges. And she stopped me and said, you are speaking like a consultant. Where are you in this story. What are you actually afraid of. I got quiet. I felt exposed. But her tone was calm, not accusing. And then she said, I am not asking to judge you, I am asking because fear is driving your actions more than data. And I admitted, I am afraid they will say no and then I will feel small, and I will have to decide whether to leave. That admission changed the work. After that, we did not pretend it is just a rational negotiation. We worked on the fear response too.
Anna: What was it like to say that out loud?
Katharina: It was uncomfortable. I felt like, oh my god, I am 28 and I am scared of a conversation. But also relieving. Because it was the truth. And once it was spoken, it felt less like this shadow that controls everything.
Anna: How did it change your behaviour after that session?
Katharina: It shifted me from endless preparation to action. Because before, I would say, I need more data, more proof. After that, I realised, no amount of data will remove fear. So we practised the conversation and planned timing. Also, I started noticing avoidance in other areas. Like I avoid asking product for clarity because I do not want conflict. And I started being more direct.
Anna: You mentioned concrete outcomes: a target role list, negotiation script, two mock interviews, and a six-week plan with weekly accountability. Could you walk me through each outcome and what it looked like?
Katharina: Sure. We basically treated it like a mini project.
Katharina: Outcome one: target role list. We defined two tracks. Track A was product marketing path, aiming for Senior PMM or PMM Lead. Track B was product management, but realistically associate PM roles, because I do not have direct PM ownership. For each track, we created a list of companies and roles, including salary ranges, location constraints, and what skills I need. It was a spreadsheet. Very German.
Katharina: Outcome two: negotiation script. We built a structure for the salary conversation: opening, rationale, ask, silence, handling objections, next steps. We wrote example lines, and we practised saying them out loud without softening.
Katharina: Outcome three: two mock interviews. One mock interview was tailored to a PMM lead style role. The other was tailored to an associate PM role, with product case questions and prioritisation. We recorded them, and I reviewed my answers.
Katharina: Outcome four: six-week plan with weekly accountability. She created weekly themes and tasks, and I had to send a Friday update. It was simple but it worked.
Anna: Let us go deeper into the target role list. What did you actually put on it?
Katharina: We listed about, um, 18 roles total. For PMM, we listed companies like mid-size SaaS in Hamburg, Berlin remote-friendly companies, and a few larger ones with PMM teams. I avoided mega corporations because I do not want pure politics. For product, we listed associate PM roles in B2B platforms, because my domain knowledge is supply chain and procurement. We also listed internal options like moving into a product operations type role, or taking ownership of a roadmap area informally. But we were honest that internal pivots are slow.
Anna: What criteria did you use to decide what is a good role?
Katharina: We used criteria like: learning curve, autonomy, manager quality, workload realism, and salary. Also how cross-functional the role is. I like cross-functional, but I want healthier boundaries. And location, because I live in Hamburg and I do not want to relocate. I could do occasional travel, but not moving.
Anna: What did you learn from comparing PMM lead versus product management?
Katharina: I learned that my desire for product management is partly a desire for more influence, and partly an escape fantasy. Like, if I become PM, then I will be respected and paid more, and my work will be more strategic. But that is not automatically true. Product can be just as chaotic. And PMM lead path is more realistic short-term because I already have strength there. The coach helped me see that I can also negotiate influence within PMM, like owning strategy, not just execution.
Anna: Now negotiation script. Can you share one or two example lines you practised, without quoting them as a script, just describing them?
Katharina: Yes. We practised starting with a calm frame, like saying I want to discuss compensation aligned with my scope and market benchmarks. Then stating my target number clearly, like targeting 65k base, based on comparable PMM roles in Hamburg. And then anchoring it in impact, like the launches I owned, enablement outcomes, adoption metrics. And we practised a line for salary band objections, like asking what band my role is mapped to and what is required to move within it or to the next level. That line was powerful because it shifts from personal pleading to process.
Anna: How did you practise? Was it just repeating, or role-play?
Katharina: Role-play. She played my manager, and she pushed back. Like, she said, budget is tight, or we can revisit later, or you are doing great but. And I had to respond without collapsing. We also practised silence. She literally made me sit in silence after stating the number. That was hard because I fill silence with explanations.
Anna: What was your default pattern in those role-plays?
Katharina: Over-explaining and apologising. Like, I would say, if possible, or I know it is a lot, or I understand. And she stopped me. She said, you can be polite without shrinking. That was a key learning.
Anna: Tell me about the actual salary conversation in real life. What happened?
Katharina: Okay. So I scheduled a meeting with my manager, framed as development and compensation alignment. I did it after a successful launch, not during a crisis week. The meeting was 45 minutes. I started with a summary of my scope changes over the last year, like owning two product areas, enablement, mentoring. Then I said I want to align my compensation with that scope and the market. I stated the target number, 65k base. My manager did not react dramatically. He said, thank you for being direct, and he asked me what data I used. I shared two sources, like recruiter ranges and an internal level mapping we have.
Anna: How did he respond regarding bands?
Katharina: He said there are salary bands and my current salary is within the band for my level, but near the lower end. He also said moving to 65k might require a level change, like senior specialist. He suggested a step approach: an immediate adjustment plus a plan for level change in the next cycle. And he asked if I would be open to taking on a more strategic initiative, like owning messaging for a new module and mentoring a junior person formally.
Anna: How did you feel in that moment?
Katharina: Relief, because he did not reject me. Also a bit annoyed, because it felt like, of course I am already doing more, but now it is formalised. But I stayed calm. The coach helped me not take it personally. We agreed on next steps: he would talk to HR and come back in two weeks. And we scheduled a follow-up.
Anna: And what was the outcome?
Katharina: The outcome was, I got an increase to 58k immediately, effective next month. And we set a target for moving to senior specialist by the next review, with a band that would put me around 62 to 64, depending on performance and budget. So I did not get 65 right away. But it was a meaningful correction. Also, the conversation itself was a huge win, because I stopped avoiding it. And now I have a documented plan.
Anna: That sounds realistic. How did you interpret that result emotionally?
Katharina: Mixed. Part of me was like, finally. Part of me was like, I should have asked earlier. And part of me was like, maybe I should still explore external options because internal movement is slow. The coach helped me hold that complexity without spiralling.
Anna: Let us talk about the mock interviews. What happened in the first mock interview? What questions were hardest?
Katharina: In the first mock interview for PMM lead, she asked questions like: describe a launch that did not go well, how do you measure PMM impact, how do you handle conflict between product and sales, and tell me about a time you influenced stakeholders without authority. Those were hard because I have examples, but I would ramble. I would go into details about stakeholder drama and forget to state results. Also, I realised I under-sell my impact because I say we, not I.
Anna: And what improved after the second mock interview?
Katharina: After the second, I was more structured. I used a simple structure: context, action, result, learning. I also got better at naming metrics. Like, for a launch, I mentioned adoption rate, sales cycle influence, demo conversion, or enablement completion. Even if metrics are imperfect, stating them shows you think about outcomes. And I stopped apologising in my answers. That was big.
Anna: For the associate PM mock interview, what was challenging?
Katharina: Prioritisation. She asked a case like: you have three feature requests, sales wants A, customers complain about B, engineering wants C, how do you decide. And I could reason, but I felt insecure because I do not own a roadmap now. We reframed my PMM experience as relevant because I have customer insight, competitive context, and cross-functional coordination. I learned to speak in trade-offs, not just messaging.
Anna: Now the six-week plan. Can you describe what you actually did week by week, not just the plan on paper?
Katharina: Yes. Week 1, I gathered data and wrote a baseline. I collected job descriptions, salary ranges from recruiter conversations, and I mapped my current responsibilities. I also tracked my time for one week to see where I actually spend time. 
Katharina: Week 2, I did role exploration. I had two informational chats. One with a PMM Lead in a Berlin SaaS company, and one with an associate PM in Hamburg. I also talked to an internal PM about what skills I would need. Week 3, I updated my CV and LinkedIn. I rewrote bullets to focus on impact, like improved enablement adoption, reduced sales cycle confusion, and led messaging for two modules. I also created a small portfolio doc with two launch case studies, one successful, one messy, showing what I learned.
Anna: Oh wow and next.
Katharina: Week 4, negotiation prep. This was the hardest week because we had a big release and there was chaos. I missed one accountability update because I was exhausted. But then I recovered. I practised the salary script twice, once with the coach and once alone. I also wrote down my fear responses, like if they say no, what do I do.
Katharina: Then week 5, execution. I had the salary conversation with my manager. And I submitted three applications, two PMM roles and one associate PM. I also reached out to a recruiter contact.
Katharina: And week 6, iteration. I reviewed what worked, adjusted my plan, and kept tracking metrics. I also had the follow-up salary meeting. And I did one more interview prep session because I got a screening call.
Anna: You mentioned weekly accountability. What did that look like, concretely?
Katharina: Every Friday by 6 pm, I sent a short update email or message. Five bullet points: what I did, what I avoided, what I learned, what is next, and what support I need. The coach replied with one or two questions. Sometimes she just asked, what is the smallest next action you can do in 15 minutes. That question is annoying but effective. It breaks the procrastination.
Anna: Did you ever feel resistance to the accountability?
Katharina: Yes. Sometimes I felt like, I am already overloaded, now I have homework. And sometimes I felt childish, like I need someone to check on me. But then I noticed it works. And the tone was not policing. It was supportive, but firm.
Anna: This is a good moment to ask about micro-poll ratings. I will ask four quick ratings, 0 to 10, and I want your reasoning. First: trust in human coaches, in general.
Katharina: In general, I would say 7 out of 10.
Anna: Why 7?
Katharina: Because I believe humans can create real relational safety and challenge. But quality varies a lot. Some coaches are too fluffy or too generic. And trust depends on competence and boundaries. So I trust the potential, but not automatically the person.
Anna: Second rating: trust in AI tools for career support.
Katharina: 4 out of 10.
Anna: Why 4?
Katharina: Because I trust it for low-stakes tasks like rewriting text or generating a checklist. I do not trust it for sensitive context or high-stakes decisions. Also, I do not fully trust privacy, and I do not trust that it understands my specific situation. It can sound confident while being generic.
Anna: Third rating: perceived effectiveness of human coaching for your situation.
Katharina: 8 out of 10.
Anna: Why 8 and not higher?
Katharina: Because it created concrete outcomes and behaviour change, but it did not solve everything. I still have workload constraints. And some uncertainty remains about whether I pivot to product. Coaching helped me act and reduce avoidance, but it is not magic.
Anna: Fourth rating: perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation.
Katharina: 5 out of 10.
Anna: Why slightly higher than trust?
Katharina: Because effectiveness for me includes speed and output. AI can create a useful output even if I do not trust it deeply, because I do not share sensitive inputs. So it is effective in narrow tasks, like templates, bullet rewriting, interview question lists. But it is not effective for deep behaviour change.
Anna: Great. Let us continue with AI tools in more detail.
5) AI tools: experience and trust

Anna: What AI tools have you used, and for what career-related tasks?
Katharina: Mostly ChatGPT. I also tried one CV tool that claims it optimises for ATS, but it was not great. With ChatGPT, I used it to rewrite CV bullets, like turning my messy sentence into an impact bullet. I also used it to brainstorm interview questions for PMM roles and to draft a follow-up email after an interview. And one time I asked it for a negotiation script structure.
Anna: What did you like about it?
Katharina: Speed and structure. Like, if I am staring at a blank page, it gives me something to react to. It also helps me reduce perfectionism because I can iterate faster. And it does not judge. If I ask a basic question, it does not roll its eyes. 
Anna: And what did you dislike or distrust?
Katharina: Lack of context and relational safety. It cannot sense when I am avoiding. It cannot hold discomfort. It also does not know my company culture. And I do not want to type in sensitive details like internal politics or exact salaries. So I self-censor. That limits usefulness.
Anna: Do you think trust in AI could increase for you, and under what conditions?
Katharina: Maybe, if there were very clear data boundaries and if it was transparent about uncertainty. Like, if it says, I am not sure, here are options, rather than sounding confident. And if it could remember context safely, but that is also scary. Because remembering context means storing data. So I am conflicted.
Anna: Can you describe a specific moment when AI was effective for you, even if you did not fully trust it?
Katharina: Yes. The negotiation email template. I wanted to request a meeting with my manager without making it sound like a threat. AI suggested a calm structure: frame it as development and compensation alignment, propose a time, and mention you want to prepare and share context. That was effective. But I did not include my salary number or detailed frustrations. I kept it generic.
Anna: And a moment when AI output was not effective?
Katharina: When I asked for salary benchmarks in Hamburg for PMM. It gave ranges that felt too broad and not grounded. And it sounded very certain. That made me cautious. I then used actual salary reports and recruiter conversations instead. So it reminded me: AI can be wrong in a confident tone.
Anna: How do you define trust for AI, compared to trust for humans?
Katharina: For AI, trust is more like reliability and predictability. Does it give a coherent structure, does it not hallucinate too much, does it respect boundaries. For humans, trust includes emotional safety, confidentiality, and being seen. It is more relational. AI cannot do that in the same way.
Anna: And how do you define effectiveness, separately?
Katharina: Effectiveness is: did it help me move forward. Did it create clarity, action, measurable outcomes, confidence, reduced avoidance. For AI, effectiveness might be faster drafting. For human coaching, effectiveness is behaviour change, like actually having the salary conversation.
Anna: You said earlier that coaching felt like a psychologist for career questions. Could you expand on that in relation to AI?
Katharina: Yes. The coach noticed patterns. Like when I intellectualise or when I avoid. And she could challenge me gently. That is psychological. AI can reflect language patterns a bit, but it does not feel like a real mirror. And also, I do not want AI to be my therapist. That feels wrong. I want it to be a tool.
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks

Anna: I want to compare human coaching and AI via specific tasks. I will list tasks and ask which one you prefer, and why. Task one: clarifying options and direction, like PMM lead versus product management.
Katharina: Human. Because direction is tied to values and fears. AI can list pros and cons, but it cannot ask the right uncomfortable question in a human way. My coach could say, you say you want product, but are you escaping something. That question mattered.
Anna: Would you use AI at all for that task?
Katharina: As a support tool. Like generating a competency matrix: what skills PMM lead needs, what skills associate PM needs. Or drafting questions to ask in informational interviews. That is great. But the decision-making and meaning part, human.
Anna: Task two: planning concrete steps and maintaining momentum.
Katharina: Hybrid. AI can generate templates and reminders, but human accountability was what made me actually do it. I do not feel accountable to AI. I can ignore notifications. With a human, I felt, I committed, and she will notice.
Anna: Task three: CV and application writing.
Katharina: AI is good for wording. Human is good for narrative. So again hybrid. AI can rewrite bullets, but a human can help me choose what story to tell. Like, am I positioning myself as PMM lead, or as a pivot to product. That is strategy.
Anna: Task four: interview preparation.
Katharina: Both. AI can generate question lists and help structure answers. Human can simulate pressure and give feedback on my presence and confidence. My coach caught things like I shrink my voice when talking about money or impact. AI would not catch that.
Anna: Task five: salary negotiation.
Katharina: Human. Because it is emotional. I needed role-play and someone to push back realistically. AI can give scripts, but I would not share the real fear, and I would not practise the awkward parts. And the awkward parts are the point.
Anna: You already gave examples of trusted but not effective and effective but not trusted. I want to make sure we capture those clearly. Could you restate one example of trusted but not effective, and one example of effective but not trusted?
Katharina: Trusted but not effective: my university friend. I trust her fully, but her advice was generic and kept me passive. Effective but not trusted: AI negotiation template. It produced a useful structure, but I did not trust it with sensitive context, so it stayed shallow.
Anna: Do you have an example where you trusted a human coach but something was not effective, at least in a small way?
Katharina: Hmm. Yes. Sometimes I trusted her process, but a specific exercise did not land. Like she asked me to visualise an ideal day in five years. I found it too abstract. I trust her, but that exercise did not directly help. We moved on quickly. So trust does not guarantee every tool is effective. But overall it was.
Anna: And do you have an example where AI was effective but you felt it was not safe or you would not rely on it?
Katharina: Yes. When I asked for possible interview answers about conflict management, it gave a nice structure. But I would not copy it directly because it can sound generic and not truthful. And I would not want to reveal my real internal conflicts to it. So I used it as a base, but not as a truth source.
Anna: In your B2B PMM reality, what are the biggest tensions you manage between stakeholders?
Katharina: Product wants to ship, and they hate marketing language. Sales wants clear claims and competitive differentiation. Customer success wants stability and fewer surprises. Marketing wants consistency across channels. And leadership wants results. PMM is always negotiating priorities. Like, product says, we cannot commit to dates, and sales says, we need dates. I end up translating. That translation work is not always visible, but it is critical.
Anna: How does that context affect what you want from coaching?
Katharina: I needed someone who understands that my stress is not just workload, it is relational complexity. I am constantly managing expectations. Coaching helped me set boundaries, like saying no to last-minute requests, and also helped me frame my impact in that messy environment.
7) Trust–effectiveness link

Anna: Now I want to focus directly on the link between trust and perceived effectiveness. In your experience, how does trust affect whether coaching is effective?
Katharina: For me, trust is the gateway. If I trust the coach, I admit the real thing I am avoiding. And that real thing drives behaviour change. Without trust, I stay on the surface. Like, help me write a better CV. With trust, I can say, I am terrified of being rejected, and I feel guilty asking for money. That changed what we worked on. And because we worked on the real blocker, the coaching became effective.
Anna: You said trust formed early because of confidentiality boundaries, transparent process, and challenge without shame. Can you describe that early trust moment in more detail, like what she said or did, and what you felt?
Katharina: In the first session, she explained confidentiality very clearly, and she asked me what safety means for me. That alone was huge. Then she said something like, we will go at a pace that is challenging but not overwhelming, and you are in control. She also set boundaries for herself, like she is not my friend, she is my coach, and the container is the session. That professionalism felt safe. I felt, okay, this is not gossip, this is not judgement, this is work.
Anna: How did that trust translate into actions or outcomes?
Katharina: Because I trusted her, I did the homework. I sent the Friday updates. I practised the script even when it felt cringe. And I admitted when I avoided something. Like week 4, when I missed the update, I told her I felt ashamed. If I did not trust her, I would have disappeared. That continuity created momentum, which created outcomes.
Anna: Let us go back to salary avoidance. What story did you tell yourself, and what fear was underneath?
Katharina: The story was: it is not the right time, budgets are tight, salary bands mean it is fixed, I should just be grateful and prove myself more. Underneath was fear of hearing no and feeling undervalued. And fear of having to choose, stay or leave. And also fear of being seen as greedy or difficult.
Anna: What triggered the shift from avoidance to action?
Katharina: Two things. One was external validation: recruiter ranges and seeing that my market value is higher. That made it concrete. The second was the coach asking me to name the fear. Once I named it, I could not hide behind spreadsheets. It became a behavioural issue, not a data issue.
Anna: You said coaching is high-impact when it includes structure plus relational safety. Could you explain what each part contributed?
Katharina: Structure gave me a path and reduced overwhelm. It turned vague anxiety into weekly steps. Relational safety allowed me to be honest about what I avoid. Without safety, structure becomes a checklist I can fake. Without structure, safety becomes a nice conversation that does not change my life. The combination is what made it high-impact.
Anna: You also said it feels like a psychologist for career questions, not therapy. How does that fit into trust and effectiveness?
Katharina: It fits because psychological insight is needed for behaviour change. But it needs boundaries. Therapy can go into family dynamics and deeper trauma. Coaching stayed focused: workplace patterns, decision-making, communication. That boundary made me trust it, because I did not feel it would spiral into something too heavy. And that focus made it effective, because we stayed on actions.
Anna: You now track outcomes. How does tracking connect to trust and effectiveness?
Katharina: Tracking makes effectiveness visible. Otherwise it is just feelings. I track applications sent, interviews, salary conversation milestones. I also track a weekly avoidance score, like did I do the hard thing. Seeing numbers motivates me. And it also reduces shame. Like, if I did not apply this week, I can see why, maybe workload. It becomes data. That is something my coach modelled. So trust enabled me to be honest, and then tracking made progress measurable.
Anna: Could you give me the specific metrics you track and how often?
Katharina: Weekly, I track: number of applications submitted, number of networking reach-outs, number of interview invites, and number of interview prep sessions. For salary, I track: meeting scheduled, meeting done, follow-up scheduled, decision received. And I track my mood, like 1 to 5, and sleep quality, because career stress affects my mental stability. It is simple, not a full mental health app. Just awareness.
Anna: How has tracking affected your motivation?
Katharina: It helps me not catastrophise. Like, if I feel stuck, I can look and see, actually I had two interviews this month. Or I made one salary conversation happen. That builds confidence. It also shows me when I am avoiding. If a week is empty, I can ask, what happened. And then I can adjust.
Anna: If trust is the gateway, what would happen if trust was low with a coach?
Katharina: I would perform. I would talk about safe topics. I would not admit fear. And then the coaching would be less effective, because it would stay at surface tactics. It might still help a bit, but not the high-impact kind.
Anna: And with AI, you described a different pattern. How does the trust-effectiveness link look there?
Katharina: It is more transactional. AI can be effective without deep trust because I keep it in a safe zone. I do not share sensitive stuff, so it cannot help with that, but it can still help with drafting. So trust limits the scope. And because the scope is limited, effectiveness is limited too. That is basically the relationship.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction

Anna: Imagine a hybrid model where AI supports preparation and follow-up, like structuring goals, drafting documents, and tracking tasks, and a human coach focuses on deeper conversations, accountability, and high-stakes moments. What is your reaction?
Katharina: That sounds ideal. Because AI can reduce admin and make things efficient. I do not need a human to rewrite a bullet point ten times. But I need a human to challenge avoidance and to role-play difficult conversations. And I need accountability from a person. I can ignore an app, but I do not want to ignore a human I respect. 
Anna: What would make you trust a hybrid model?
Katharina: Clear boundaries. Like, what data is stored, what is shared with the coach, what stays private. Also, transparency about what AI does and does not do. If AI is positioned as advice, that is risky. If it is positioned as structure and drafting support, okay. And the human needs to own the ethical part and the nuance.
Anna: Where would you prefer AI to lead, and where would you prefer the human to lead?
Katharina: AI can lead in templates, tracking, reminders, and generating question lists. Human should lead in values clarification, identity questions, emotional blockers, negotiation practice, conflict handling, and interpreting context. Also, anything involving risk, like leaving a job or confronting a manager.
Anna: If you had to design your ideal career support experience, what would it look like in practice?
Katharina: It would start with two human sessions to clarify direction and blockers. Then an AI tool would help me set up a plan and track tasks. Then weekly, a short human check-in for accountability and reflection. And AI would help in between with drafting and prep. Also, I would want the human coach to be trained and certified, not just a random influencer. And I would want the whole thing to feel professional, not like motivational content.
Anna: What concerns would you have about a hybrid approach?
Katharina: Misalignment. Like if AI suggests something that conflicts with the coach, I would be confused. Also privacy. And the risk that AI makes it feel too transactional, losing the relational safety. If the system pushes me to input sensitive feelings into a tool, I would resist. I want my emotions to be held by a human, not by a chatbot.
Anna: If you think about your own timeline, would AI have helped you more at the beginning or later?
Katharina: Later. At the beginning, I needed relational safety to admit what I was avoiding. AI could not give me that. Once I had momentum, AI could support maintaining it.
Anna: You mentioned mental stability and career satisfaction. How do you connect that to seeking help?
Katharina: People spend most of their time at work. If work is constantly stressful and you feel stuck, it leaks into everything. For me, I noticed I was irritable, I slept badly, I avoided social plans because I was tired. Coaching helped me feel more stable because I had a plan and I took action. That reduced the background anxiety. So seeking help is not weakness. It is, um, maintenance. Like mental hygiene. And I want to be careful because it is not therapy, but it still affects mental stability.
Anna: That is clear. Let us close soon, but I want to make sure we captured the specific concrete episodes the study needs. I will ask a few targeted questions. First, the early trust moment. You described confidentiality and process. Was there a specific moment you remember thinking, okay I can trust this person?
Katharina: Yes. It was when she said, tell me what you need to feel safe, and then she repeated it back without judgement. And then when I said, I am ashamed about the salary topic, she did not reassure me in a fake way. She said, shame is common here, and we will work with it. That made me feel normal, not broken.
Anna: Second, the challenge without shaming moment. You described her calling out that you were speaking like a consultant. Did that feel risky?
Katharina: Yes, but in a good way. It felt like she sees through my defence. But her tone was respectful, not mocking. If she had laughed or pushed too hard, I would have shut down. Instead, I felt invited.
Anna: Third, negotiation script practice. Besides stating the number, what was the hardest part?
Katharina: Silence and handling pushback. Like when my manager says, budgets are tight. My instinct is to retreat. Practising responses helped, like asking for process clarity, asking what would be required, agreeing on a timeline. Also, not taking it personally.
Anna: Fourth, mock interviews. What question made you stumble the most, and what changed?
Katharina: The question about measuring PMM impact. I started talking about assets, like decks and webinars. Then she stopped me and said, those are outputs, what are outcomes. That changed my framing. After practice, I could talk about adoption, pipeline influence, and reduced sales confusion.
Anna: Fifth, the six-week plan. What did the weekly accountability feel like emotionally? Motivation, pressure, both?
Katharina: Both. Motivation because I wanted to report progress. Pressure sometimes, but it was healthy pressure. It kept me honest. Also, the coach did not punish me when I failed. She used it as information. That reduced fear.
Anna: Great. I think we have it. Let us move to closing.
9) Closing

Anna: Is there anything else you want to add about trust and perceived effectiveness, maybe something people misunderstand?
Katharina: No, not really. 
Anna: Thank you. Final check are you comfortable with the topics we covered today and the level of detail?
Katharina: Yes. It is anonymous I mean.
Anna: Great. Then I will stop the recording here. Thank you for your time, Katharina.
Katharina: Thank you. It was. 


Amir 
1) Opening and consent

Anna: Hi Amir, thanks for making the time today. How are you?
Amir: Hi Anna. I am good, eehm, a bit tired because I had a lab meeting this morning, but yeah, good.
Anna: Makes sense. Before we start, I want to confirm consent. Do I have your permission to record this interview? Everything is anonymized, and you can skip any question at any time.
Amir: Yes, you can record. That is okay.
Anna: Thank you. And just checking, are there any topics you would prefer not to discuss today?
Amir: Um, I think it is fine. Maybe nothing super personal, like family details. But career stuff, identity stuff, that is okay.
Anna: Absolutely. We will stay within what feels comfortable. If anything feels too personal, just tell me and we move on.
Amir: Cool.
Anna: Great. Let us begin.
2) Participant context and career situation
Anna: To start, where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing right now, or recently faced?
Amir: Okay. So, I am 24, I am from Iran, and I have been in Germany four years now. I am based in Cologne, in Köln. I am finishing my Master in Human-Computer Interaction. It is like, eehm, between computer science and psychology, you know. My current situation is I am writing my thesis and also working part-time as a working student in UX research.
Anna: What kind of company is it?
Amir: It is a SaaS company, B2B, like a platform for internal workflows, kind of. Not like a sexy consumer app. But interesting problems. The team is small. There is one lead UX researcher and then me and one UX designer, and product managers, engineers, you know. I do mostly research ops and some interviews and surveys.
Anna: And what is the biggest career decision you have faced in the last six to twelve months?
Amir: The biggest one was probably choosing my thesis scope and also, like, choosing what kind of first full-time role I should optimize for. Because the thesis topic can lock you in a bit, at least in your narrative. Like if I do something very academic, then I can say I want research track, PhD, blah blah. But if I do something more applied, then it is easier to say I want product research. And then there is the visa thing. I am on a student residence permit now. I need the job after graduation. So the decision is not purely what I like, it is also what is feasible.
Anna: What made that difficult?
Amir: Um. Multiple constraints. First, the job market right now is weird. Like, internships and working student roles are still okay, but junior full-time roles in UX research are not so many. Especially if you need visa sponsorship, you know. Second, language. My German is okay for daily life, like I can handle Bürgeramt, I can order food, I can do small talk. But in professional settings, research interviews in German, that is hard. So I ask myself, do I focus on English-speaking roles, or do I push German fast. And third, identity, like, what kind of life I want. Stability or autonomy. Like I enjoy structured work, but also I hate feeling stuck.
Anna: When you say identity, what does that mean for you in this context?
Amir: It is like, who am I trying to become in the next two years. Like, do I want to be a very serious researcher, publish papers, conferences, CHI, that world. Or do I want to be more product, shipping, fast feedback loops. And then there is the immigrant lens, like, do I want to stay in Germany long term, apply for Blue Card later, or do I keep the option to move, maybe Netherlands, maybe Canada, I do not know. It is not like I can just pick a vibe. There are consequences.
Anna: What kind of support did you feel you needed while making those decisions?
Amir: I needed, um, two things. One is clarity. Like not motivational talk, but clarity. And the second is emotional regulation. Because I can think a lot. I overthink. I can build a whole decision tree and still feel anxious. So I needed someone or something that can help me name what I am actually afraid of, and then structure it.
Anna: That is helpful. And your thesis, what is it on right now?
Amir: So, my thesis is on how people use AI tools for reflective practices in work contexts. Like, not purely productivity, but reflective thinking, learning from experience. It is like, I studied journaling, reflection prompts, and then how AI changes the affordances. I am doing interviews with early-career people actually. So it is meta.
Anna: That is quite aligned with your own habits then.
Amir: Yes, exactly. That is also why it was hard, because it is personally relevant and I can bias myself. So I had to be careful.
Anna: We will come back to that when we talk about AI tools. Before that, tell me a bit about your background. What did you study before the Master?
Amir: In Iran I did undergrad in industrial engineering, but I focused on systems design and some programming. Then I came to Germany, I did a bridge year, language, and then I started the HCI Master. I also did some student projects in interaction design, usability testing, and like, one project with Deutsche Bahn data, but more conceptual.
Anna: And student life in Cologne, how is it for you?
Amir: Cologne is good. It is open, people are friendly compared to some other cities. There is like, this vibe, more relaxed. People say, Köln ist ein Dorf, you see the same people. For me, student life is also like working student life. Because I work 20 hours. So it is not like partying all the time. But I have routines, like I go to a cafe near Zülpicher Platz, I study, I meet friends. It is okay.
3. Career support in general
Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Amir: Um. First, I use AI tools a lot, but not for CV mainly. More for reflection. Second, I talk with two friends. One is also international, from Turkey, doing data science. Another is German, doing product management. We do like, reality checks for each other. And third, sometimes I talk to my supervisor at university, but that is more academic direction.
Anna: What kind of support do you get from your workplace?
Amir: From work, not official. There is no mentoring program. But my lead researcher is supportive. She gives feedback, like, how to frame my impact, how to communicate. But she is busy. And also I do not want to be too needy. So I use her for concrete questions, like, what skills matter, what portfolios matter, how to prepare for interviews.
Anna: Have you used university career services in Germany?
Amir: Once. At the university we have career counseling, like a workshop. I attended a seminar about Bewerbungen and networking. It was helpful for basics, but it was very generic. Also it was in German. I understood, but I was like, okay, this is not my main problem. My problem is not how to format a CV, it is like, what am I optimizing for, and what trade-offs I accept.
Anna: What has actually helped you in the past, and what felt unhelpful?
Amir: Helpful is when someone helps me reduce the problem into something actionable. Like, you have three options, define evaluation criteria, run small experiments. Unhelpful is when people just say, follow your passion, or, everything will work out. That makes me angry, honestly. Because it ignores the visa reality, and the job market reality.
Anna: So you value realism and structure.
Amir: Yes. But also I need emotional space. It is not only structure. It is like, structure plus permission to be uncertain.
4. Human coaching: experience and trust
Anna: Have you ever tried human career coaching, like a private coach, mentor, or career counselor, in a one-to-one format?
Amir: I tried one session, actually. It was through a program at the university, like alumni mentoring. It was not exactly coaching, more mentoring. One session, one hour.
Anna: What was it like?
Amir: It was limited. The person was nice, German, he worked in HR at a big company. He asked me about my background, and then he gave advice like, apply widely, build network, improve German. It was not wrong. But it did not go deep. Also I felt I had to perform confidence. Like, I had to show I am motivated, I am proactive. And maybe it is cultural, but I did not want to share my doubts, like, I am scared I will not get a job and then I have to leave. Because then I feel weak.
Anna: What do you think made it hard to go deeper?
Amir: Two things. First, lack of continuity. One session, there is no trust built. Second, I did not know if he really understands my context. Like, he is German, he never had visa pressure. He can say, just take your time. For me, time is not neutral. And also, I did not know what he expects. In Germany, people can be direct, but also professional. I did not want to overshare.
Anna: When you think about trust in a human coach or mentor, what would make a human feel trustworthy for you?
Amir: Competence, like understanding UX research and the job market, not only general advice. And also, confidentiality, like they explicitly say, this stays here. And the vibe. Like, if I feel judged, then no. If I feel they are trying to push me into a standard path, also no. And also, I need them to ask good questions, not only give advice. If they only give advice, then it is like, okay, you are just a blog post.
Anna: And what would make a human untrustworthy?
Amir: If they are too confident without understanding my situation. Or if they project their own story. Like, I did this, so you should do this. Also if they treat me like a problem to fix. Or if they are, um, biased. Like, I had one experience in a networking event where someone kind of assumed, because I am international, that I will take any job. That makes me close.
Anna: If you had to put a number on it, a micro-poll, 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all and 10 is full trust, what is your current trust in human coaches for career topics?
Amir: Eehm. I would say 6.
Anna: And why 6?
Amir: Because I know humans can be very insightful and empathetic, and they can understand nuance. But for me, the access is limited, the match is uncertain, and I do not know if they understand visa constraints. So I trust the potential, not the default.
Anna: And perceived effectiveness, same scale 0 to 10, how effective do you think human coaching would be for your situation right now?
Amir: Maybe 5.
Anna: Why lower than trust?
Amir: Because even if I trust them, it may not translate to action, due to cost, time, or mismatch. Like, I can have a nice talk and feel supported, but then what. Also I think for my kind of problems, a lot is internal decision making. A coach can help, but they cannot remove the constraints. So effectiveness is moderate.
Anna: That distinction is important. Thank you.
5. AI tools: experience and trust
Anna: Now I want to go deeper into AI tools. You mentioned you use AI as a structured reflection partner, almost like guided journaling, three to four times per week. Can you describe what that looks like in practice?
Amir: Yes. So I have a routine. Usually in the evening, after work or after studying. Like 20 to 40 minutes. I open the AI chat, and I start with a check-in. I literally write, current state, and then I list, energy level, mood, main concern. It sounds like therapy, but it is not therapy. It is like, data plus feelings.
Anna: What happens after the check-in?
Amir: Then I paste my personal prompt. I have a template. It says something like, mirror my assumptions, list possible biases, ask me two clarifying questions, and then propose decision principles, not decisions. That last part is important. I do not want it to decide for me. I want it to help me create principles.
Anna: Can you give an example of the prompt template, without exact wording if you prefer?
Amir: Yeah, roughly it is: First, restate what I said in a structured way, like bullet points. Second, identify my hidden assumptions. Third, identify cognitive biases, like loss aversion, catastrophizing, whatever. Fourth, ask me questions that challenge me. Fifth, propose a framework, but keep it light, not too many. And then I ask it to propose two or three decision principles that I can reuse.
Anna: How did you develop that routine?
Amir: It evolved. At first I used AI like everyone, for quick questions. Then I noticed I am using it to think. Like I can type and it reflects. And then I realized, if I want it to be consistent, I need to design the interaction. HCI mindset, you know. Like, I can create an interface behavior through prompts. So I iterated. I removed parts that make it preachy, and I added parts that make it reflect uncertainty.
Anna: When you say you use it three to four times per week, is it scheduled or more reactive?
Amir: Both. I try to do it Sunday evening, like planning week. And then one or two times midweek when I feel stuck. And sometimes after something triggers me, like a rejection email, or a weird meeting. Then I use it to process.
Anna: Walk me through a full session from start to end, including how you save outputs.
Amir: Okay. So, step one: I open a note on my laptop where I write a quick raw dump, like, what happened, what I feel. Step two: I paste a cleaned version to AI. Step three: I ask it to mirror and find assumptions. Then it responds. I read it slowly. Sometimes I feel resistance, like, no, that is not it. Then I reply with corrections. Step four: I ask it to generate principles or trade-offs. Step five: I choose one principle that feels true, and I write it in my personal decision log.
Anna: You have a decision log.
Amir: Yes. In Notion. Very German of me. It is like, date, topic, options, principle, next step. And I tag it, like visa, career, identity, relationships. Then, over days, I cross-check. Like, if on Monday I think I want stability, and on Thursday I think I want autonomy, I compare. I ask, is this mood-driven or value-driven.
Anna: How do you cross-check with AI across multiple sessions?
Amir: I do two things. One is consistency check. I ask AI, based on what I wrote last time, what changed. But I do not fully trust its memory, because it may not have the context unless I paste it. So I paste the key points. Second, I do triangulation. I talk to a friend, or I write alone without AI. If the principle still holds, then I accept it.
Anna: What does conditional trust mean for you in this AI context?
Amir: It means I trust it as a thinking tool, not an authority. So I trust the process when it helps me see patterns, but I do not trust the content as truth. Like, if it says, the job market is great, I would laugh. It does not know my reality. But if it says, you seem to prioritize safety but also fear stagnation, that can be true. So I trust it for mirroring and structuring, not for facts or predictions.
Anna: What makes it feel trustworthy in those moments?
Amir: Internal coherence. Like if it can restate my situation accurately. If it catches contradictions gently. If it shows uncertainty, like, this could be one interpretation, not definitive. Also, if it helps name emotions without making me feel stupid. Like, it can say, it sounds like grief or fear, and I am like, yes. And it does not require me to perform confidence. With humans, I often perform. With AI, I can write, I am scared, and it is fine.
Anna: That non-judgmental aspect seems important.
Amir: Very important. Because I come from a culture where you have pride, you do not show weakness too much. And also as an immigrant, you always want to appear capable. The AI is like, okay, you can be messy here.
Anna: Can you share a vivid example of a value conflict session, like stability versus autonomy, or prestige versus learning versus visa security?
Amir: Yes. There was one in October. I had two opportunities. One was an interview process with a big company, like a well-known German brand, maybe automotive supply chain. It was a UX research internship that could lead to a job. It would be stable and looks good. The other was a smaller startup in Berlin, remote-first, doing a product I find interesting. They offered a working student role now and maybe a junior role later, but uncertain.
Anna: And what was the conflict?
Amir: The conflict was prestige and visa security versus learning and autonomy. The big company is like, you get structure, you get brand, maybe easier to justify for visa, and more stable salary. But the work might be slower, more bureaucracy, more German language, also. The startup is like, fast learning, more autonomy, English, but risk. And I was stuck. I was ruminating. Like, if I choose wrong, I waste time. That is catastrophic for me.
Anna: What did you ask the AI in that session?
Amir: I started with, I feel like I am optimizing three different things and they conflict. Then I used my prompt, mirror assumptions, biases. The AI mirrored and said, you assume stability equals safety, but you also assume autonomy equals identity fulfillment. It listed biases like, status quo bias, because I am leaning to big company because it feels safe. Also scarcity mindset, because visa makes me fear losing options.
