And then it asked questions like, what does stability mean concretely, is it contract length, is it learning structure, is it social belonging.
Anna: What did you notice emotionally as you read that?
Amir: I noticed tightness. Like in my chest. Because it made it real. It was not abstract anymore. I realized I am afraid of being forced to leave. And also I realized I am afraid of losing myself in bureaucracy. So it is like two fears.
Anna: And what decision principle came out?
Amir: The principle was something like, in the next two years, prioritize options that increase visa safety while still giving me skill growth that is transferable. That is a compromise principle. It is not prestige. It is not pure learning. It is, increase safety and transferability.
Anna: How did you apply that principle?
Amir: I used it to decide my thesis framing too. I chose a thesis that is relevant to industry, like AI-supported reflection in work contexts, but still has research rigor. So I can apply for UX research roles and also for research assistant roles. And then, for the job decision, I did not fully decide only by that principle. I still talked to my friend. But it reduced my anxiety.
Anna: That sounds like a moment where AI truly helped.
Amir: Yes. It created a coherent self-narrative. Like, I am not inconsistent, I am balancing constraints. It made me feel less shame. Like, I am not cowardly for wanting stability. It is rational.
Anna: Could you describe another moment where AI helped you take a concrete step?
Amir: Yes. In December I got a rejection from a company after a good interview. I was devastated. I wrote to AI, I feel like I will never get a job, I am behind. Then it did something useful: it reframed it as evidence that I can pass interviews, and it helped me create a post-mortem template. Like, what went well, what could improve, what is in my control. Then I did that, and I sent a message to the interviewer asking for feedback, in German. I would not do that without the emotional regulation. Because I would just hide.
Anna: So it reduced rumination and supported action.
Amir: Exactly.
Anna: Now, can you give me an example where AI did not help?
Amir: Oh yes. Many. One is when I get into over-intellectualizing. Like, I ask it to generate frameworks, and then it gives me five frameworks, and then I feel more overwhelmed. It is like, I wanted clarity and I get a menu. Another is when it gives confident but shallow answers, like, you should network, you should set goals. That is like, okay, thanks. And sometimes it can create false certainty. Like, it can write a very coherent narrative that feels good, but it might be too neat. And that can be dangerous. Because life is messy.
Anna: Can you recall a specific session where it felt safe but was not effective, meaning trusted but not effective?
Amir: Yes. There was a session after a conflict with my team. I felt misunderstood in a meeting. I used AI to vent. It mirrored my feelings very nicely, it validated, it named emotions. I felt calm. So I trusted it emotionally. But then it did not produce a next step. I ended the session feeling soothed but still stuck. It was like a warm blanket but no map. So trusted, yes. Effective, not really, because I did not act.
Anna: That is a clear distinction. Now the other direction: effective but not fully trusted. Do you have an example where the output was useful but you still did not fully trust it?
Amir: Yes. Sometimes it generates a decision principle that is very elegant. Like, prioritize learning velocity over brand. And I am like, that sounds cool, very Silicon Valley. It can be effective in giving me a language to think, but I do not trust it because it might be biased toward certain narratives, like hustle culture. So I take the structure but I validate with my real constraints. I ask, does this fit visa, finances, mental health. So it is effective as a tool, not trusted as a worldview.
Anna: What about privacy discomfort, does that ever show up?
Amir: Yes. That is another limit. Sometimes I write something and then I think, okay, where does this go. Like, I am not writing illegal things, but still, it is personal. And then I get uncomfortable. So I sometimes abstract. I do not write exact company name, I write my workplace. I also do not write exact numbers. And sometimes I choose to journal offline instead. So the trust is conditional also because of privacy.
Anna: If you had to rate your trust in AI tools for these kinds of career and identity reflections, 0 to 10, what would you say?
Amir: I would say 8.
Anna: Why 8 and not 10?
Amir: Because there are still hallucinations and overconfidence. Also privacy. Also it can be generic. But overall, for my use case, it is consistent and helpful.
Anna: And perceived effectiveness of AI tools for your situation, 0 to 10?
Amir: I would say 8 as well, maybe 8.5 but okay, 8.
Anna: Why?
Amir: Because it helps me create coherent self-narratives and decision frameworks, and it reduces anxiety. But it cannot replace real-world constraints. It also cannot network for me, it cannot sponsor visa. So effectiveness is high in thinking, medium in execution.
Anna: You said you do not use AI mainly for CVs. Can you explain why?
Amir: Yes. For CVs, I can do it myself, and also I do not fully trust it to understand German job culture. Also CV is like surface. My main struggle is not wording. My struggle is decision making and identity. So I use AI where it is strongest for me: structured reflection, asking questions, mirroring. For CV, I prefer to ask my lead researcher or friends.
Anna: How does your HCI mindset shape how you interact with AI?
Amir: I think of it as an interface. Like, the output is influenced by my input. So I try to design prompts like affordances. Like, I want it to ask me questions, so I tell it to ask. I want it to show uncertainty, so I ask it to present alternatives. Also I notice when it is leading me. Like, it can subtly push a narrative. So I try to make it symmetrical, like, show me multiple interpretations, show bias, do not moralize.
Anna: Do you ever feel you get dependent?
Amir: That is a fear. Sometimes I think, if I always process with AI, will I lose my own ability. But then I see it more like training wheels. It helps me learn how to structure. And sometimes I stop using it for a week to see. I can still think. So I do not feel addicted, but it is a tool I like.
Anna: When you stop for a week, what changes?
Amir: I ruminate more. I talk more to friends. But friends are busy. And I also feel more alone. AI is always available. That is a huge thing. For international students, loneliness is real. And AI fills some gap, not socially, but cognitively and emotionally.
Anna: That is an important point.
Amir: Yes. It is like, not companionship, but containment. Like a container for thoughts.
6. Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks
Anna: Now I want to compare human versus AI via specific career-related tasks. I will name a task, and you tell me which you would prefer and why. First task: clarifying career options, like industries or roles.
Amir: For clarifying options, AI is good for brainstorming and mapping. Like, it can list industries, roles, skill overlaps. But I do not trust it fully for local specifics. So I use AI to generate the space, then I validate with humans or job boards. Humans are better for realistic insider knowledge, like, in Cologne this company hires, or, this role expects German. AI can be too abstract.
Anna: Second task: making a high-stakes decision, like choosing between offers.
Amir: For high-stakes, I want hybrid. AI helps structure criteria and emotions. Human helps reality check and maybe emotional support. If I had a coach I trust, I would use them there. But because I do not have one, I use friends.
Anna: Third task: planning concrete next steps, like a job search strategy for three months.
Amir: AI is great for planning, like, weekly plan, track applications, build portfolio. But again, it can be generic. So I use it to propose a plan, then I edit. Human can hold accountability better. AI cannot really shame you, which is good and bad. It is good because no judgment, but sometimes I need pressure.
Anna: Fourth task: preparing for interviews.
Amir: AI for mock questions and structuring answers, yes. But for vibe and cultural nuance, humans. Like in Germany, the way you talk about yourself is different. AI can help, but a human recruiter or friend helps more.
Anna: Fifth task: dealing with anxiety or self-doubt after rejections.
Amir: AI, definitely. Because I can be messy, I can write at 1 am. It helps me name emotions. Humans I do not want to bother always. Also humans can give you their own anxiety. AI is stable.
Anna: Any task where you strongly prefer human?
Amir: When it is about social context, like office politics, or negotiating salary, or reading between lines. AI can give advice, but humans know reality. Also when it is about belonging, like, am I welcome in this team. AI cannot feel the vibe.
Anna: Any task where you strongly prefer AI?
Amir: When it is about internal conflict, identity, values. Because humans can judge, even if they do not want to. AI is safer. Also it is structured. Humans can be chaotic.
Anna: That is a nice contrast.
7. Trust–effectiveness link
Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness in your experience. When you think about AI tools, how does trust affect whether it is effective?
Amir: Trust is like, permission to use it deeply. If I do not trust it, I will not tell it the real thing. And then it will give generic advice. So trust increases depth of input, which increases quality of output. Also if I trust it as a process, I will follow the steps, like check assumptions, generate principles. That makes it effective.
Anna: And when trust is low, what happens?
Amir: I become defensive. Like, it says something and I am like, no, you do not understand. Then I stop. Or I use it only for shallow things. Then it is not effective for deep decisions.
Anna: Can you think of a moment where the AI output could have been effective but you did not trust it enough to use it?
Amir: Yes. When it gave me advice about negotiating a contract. It was probably good advice, like, ask for clarity, ask for written terms. But I did not trust it because I felt it does not know German legal culture. So I did not act. I asked my friend instead. So even if it was effective in theory, lack of trust reduced effectiveness.
Anna: And the other way: can trust itself make something feel effective even when the practical outcome is limited?
Amir: Yes. Like the warm blanket session. I trusted it emotionally, it felt safe. That reduced stress, so in that sense it was effective for emotional regulation, but not effective for action. So it depends what effectiveness means. If effectiveness is less anxiety, then it was effective. If effectiveness is decision, then no.
Anna: When you think about human coaching, how does trust affect effectiveness there?
Amir: Even more. Because with humans, if I do not trust, I will not share real fears. I will perform. Then the coach will coach the performance, not the reality. So trust is a gate. But also, with humans, trust takes time. With AI, trust is more immediate because it is non-judgmental by default, at least in feeling.
Anna: Interesting. What are your indicators that something is effective, either AI or human?
Amir: Clear decision principles. Like a sentence I can reuse later. Also a coherent self-story, like, I can explain to myself why I choose something. Also concrete next steps. And less anxiety, like my body feels calmer. And better conversations with humans. Like, I can talk to my lead researcher and not be chaotic, because I already structured my thoughts with AI.
Anna: That last point is important. Can you give an example?
Amir: Yes. I had a meeting with my lead researcher about my future. Before the meeting I used AI to clarify what I want to ask. I wrote, I want feedback on my readiness for junior role, and what gaps. The AI helped me generate a structured agenda. Then in the meeting, I was clear. She responded better. So the AI indirectly improved a human interaction.
Anna: So effectiveness can be downstream.
Amir: Exactly. It is like a scaffold.
8. Preferences and hybrid model reaction
Anna: Now I want to ask about your preferences. If you could design an ideal support system for career decisions, what would it look like?
Amir: It would be hybrid. AI for preparation, reflection, and follow-up. Human for pivotal moments. Like, AI helps me clarify what I feel and what I want. Then human helps with contextual advice and accountability. Then AI helps me integrate after the human session, like, what did I learn, what principle, what next steps.
Anna: What would you want the AI to do, specifically, in a hybrid model?
Amir: First, help me articulate the problem before I talk to a coach. Like, a pre-coaching intake, but intelligent. Second, help me generate questions for the coach. Third, after the coach, help me translate insights into actions and principles. Also keep a memory of my principles over time, but with privacy controls.
Anna: And what would you want the human to do?
Amir: Validate reality. Like, tell me what is realistic in Germany, in UX research. Also give me emotional presence when it is heavy. AI is safe but it is not real empathy. And the human can hold me accountable. Like, you said you will do this, did you do it. AI can remind, but it is not the same.
Anna: If you heard about a platform that combines AI-guided exploration with access to human insiders or coaches, how would you react?
Amir: I would be interested, but I would ask, how do you match me with the human. Because trust depends on fit. If you match me with someone who does not understand international constraints, I will not trust. Also I would ask, what happens to my data. And I would want the AI to be transparent about limits. Like, do not pretend to be a therapist. Also, I would want pricing that makes sense for a student.
Anna: What would make you trust such a hybrid platform?
Amir: Clear boundaries. Like, what AI does, what human does. Also evidence that the insiders are real, verified. And maybe some kind of quality signal, like reviews, but also not fake. And also, the platform should not push me to a path that benefits them. Like, conflict of interest.
Anna: If you had to choose one mode for the next six months, only AI or only human, what would you choose?
Amir: Only AI, honestly, because of access and my habit. But I would miss the human reality check. So I would probably build it through friends.
Anna: That is clear.
Amir: But if I had a really good coach who understands my context, then maybe only human. But that is hypothetical.
Anna: What is your biggest hesitation about AI in career coaching contexts?
Amir: Overconfidence and generic advice. And privacy. Also, if it becomes too persuasive, like it pushes a narrative, then it can manipulate. I do not like that. I want autonomy.
Anna: And your biggest hesitation about human coaching?
Amir: Cost. And judgment. And mismatch. And sometimes humans can be biased, consciously or not.
Anna: Do you think your preference will change as you become more stable, like after you get a job?
Amir: Yes. I think when visa pressure reduces, I may be more open to exploring identity without urgency. Then human coaching could become more valuable. Right now I need speed and structure. Later I may need depth and relational support.
9. Closing
Anna: We are coming to the end. Is there anything important about your experience with AI or human career support that we did not cover?
Amir: I guess no we are good. 
Anna: Thank you. And final check, are you okay with everything we discussed being used anonymously for research purposes?
Amir: Yes, that is fine.
Anna: Great. Thank you for your time, Amir. I really appreciate your openness.
Amir: Thanks Anna. It was interesting. Also for my thesis, it is funny.
Anna: Yes. Okay, I will stop the


Laura


1) Opening and consent

Anna: Hi Laura. Thanks for joining. Before we start, do I have your permission to record? Everything will be anonymised. You can skip any question, and you can stop anytime.
Laura: Yes, I consent. You can record.
Anna: Thank you. Just to confirm, this is for a master thesis about trust and perceived effectiveness in AI-based career coaching versus human coaching, with early-career people in Germany. I will ask about your experiences and perceptions. Any topics you prefer not to discuss?
Laura: Um, no, it is fine. Maybe just no very detailed numbers about my salary. But otherwise okay.
Anna: Perfect. We can keep it high level. And if anything feels too specific, we can move on. Are you comfortable speaking mostly in English?
Laura: Yes. My English is fine. And sometimes I might put a German word because Frankfurt is like that. 
Anna: Totally fine. Okay, let’s start.
2) Participant context and career situation

Anna: To begin, where are you currently in your career, and what career decisions or questions are you facing or recently faced?
Laura: Eehm. So I am 27. I am from Spain originally, and I have been in Germany for six years now. I live in Frankfurt. I work in a bank as a data and finance analyst. It is a bit of a hybrid role, like I sit between finance controlling and data analytics, so I do reporting, dashboards, forecasting, and also like ad hoc analysis when stakeholders ask something last minute.  And the big decision I faced, like in the last six months especially, was whether I stay in finance and go deeper, like try to get promoted, or if I move to a climate mission startup, like a climate fintech or a climate analytics company, or if I apply for a Master track or even PhD track in something like sustainable finance or climate economics.
Anna: And when you say big decision, what made it feel high-stakes for you?
Laura: Um, multiple things. One is that Frankfurt banking culture is intense. Like the prestige logic is real. It is like, if you leave, people assume you could not handle it or you are not ambitious. And I am an international, even if I am EU, I still feel that pressure to prove myself. Another thing is money and stability. I have a good salary, I can live alone, I can save, I can travel to Spain. If I go to a startup, the pay might be lower, and also the risk is higher, like they can fail. And then the third option, academia, it is like identity. I have this voice in my head, like you are smart, you should do research, you should do a PhD, you should do something meaningful and deep. But then I also know academia is stressful and uncertain. Funding, supervision, and like, you can feel very alone.
Anna: You mentioned you have been in Germany six years. What was your path to Frankfurt and to banking?
Laura: I did my Bachelor in Spain in Business Administration and Economics. In my last year I did an Erasmus in the Netherlands, and I liked the international environment. Then I applied for a Master in Germany. I did a Master in Finance and Data Analytics, like a program that was half finance, half data. It was in a different city first, not Frankfurt. After that I got an internship in Frankfurt and then a full-time offer. And Frankfurt is very, you know, bank-heavy. So it kind of happened.
Anna: What team are you in now, broadly? Like investment banking, retail, risk, something else?
Laura: Risk. Not the trading side. More like risk management and portfolio monitoring. I work with credit risk data, and also climate risk started to appear. So I do things like stress testing data preparation, and management reporting. It is not like front office glamour, but the pressure is still high because it goes to senior management. And because risk is like, no mistakes. In German they say, Fehlerkultur, but in banking it is like, no, there is no Fehlerkultur. 
Anna: That is a vivid line. What are your day-to-day stressors in that environment?
Laura: Deadlines. Like, there are cycles, month-end, quarter-end, and then special requests. And you cannot say no, because the stakeholder is like a director or someone. And also the culture is very, how to say, risk-averse and formal. So even if you do good work, you feel like it is never enough. And I have perfectionism. So I will check things five times. And then I also sometimes feel lonely because most people are German or have their network already. My German is okay, like B2-ish, but in meetings it is often German, and then they switch to English for me, and I feel like, ugh, I am the reason the meeting is slower. Even if nobody says it, you know.
Anna: That feeling of being the friction.
Laura: Exactly. And then also my family is in Spain. So if I have a hard week, it is not like I can just go for dinner with my parents. I have friends, but adult life, everyone is busy. So yes. So the decision felt high-stakes because it touches money, identity, mental health, and also like my future story. Like, who am I.
Anna: When did the decision become urgent?
Laura: Around September last year, so like four months ago. There was a reorg announcement. Not layoffs, but reorg. And my manager basically told me, there is an opportunity to take more responsibility, but it will be more pressure. And at the same time I was getting messages on LinkedIn from climate startups because my profile had climate risk keywords. And then, um, I had this moment where I thought, if I say yes now, I lock myself in for at least two more years, because leaving after getting responsibility looks bad. And I was already tired. So it became urgent.
3) Career support in general

Anna: When you need career support, who or what do you usually turn to?
Laura: Hmm. It changed. Earlier, like during my Master, I used university career services a bit, like CV review. Not so much for deep career decisions. Then at the bank, I sometimes talk to colleagues, like informal mentoring. And I talk to friends, like friends in consulting, friends in tech. And my sister in Spain, she is a psychologist, so she is good for emotional support, not career advice, but she helps me see patterns. And then, yes, I use AI. Like, very actively. Especially in this decision period, it became a big thing.
Anna: When you say career support, what do you mean you need? Is it information, structure, reassurance, something else?
Laura: All of it. But I think the hardest part is rumination. Like I can research forever. The internet gives you infinite stories. But then you still cannot decide. So support for me is, one, structure, and two, emotional containment, like someone to hold the anxiety without making it worse. And three, reality checks, like what is realistic in Germany, what is realistic in Frankfurt.
Anna: What has actually helped you historically, and what felt unhelpful?
Laura: Helpful is when someone asks me good questions. Like, not advice immediately. Like, why do you want this, what are you afraid of. Unhelpful is generic advice, like follow your passion. Or people projecting. Like a friend who hates corporate will say, just leave, corporate is evil. And a colleague who is very corporate will say, startups are chaos, you will regret. That is unhelpful.
Anna: And where does AI fit into that ecosystem for you?
Laura: In normal times, AI is like productivity. Like help me draft an email, summarise a report, or explain something technical. But in that three-week period, it became almost like a coach. Not in the emotional way, but in the decision structure way. And I did not expect that.
4) Human coaching: experience and trust

Anna: Let us talk about human coaching first. Have you ever had career coaching, like a professional coach, or something similar?
Laura: Not really professional. I had one short session with a coach that my bank offered, like an internal program, but it was more like career development talk. It was 45 minutes. And she was nice, but it was very surface. And then I had informal mentoring with a senior colleague, like coffee chats. But not like a structured coaching relationship.
Anna: Can you tell me about that one session? What was it like?
Laura: It was on Teams. The coach was external but hired by the bank. She asked about my goals, and I said, I am not sure if I want to stay. And she immediately tried to put it into a framework, like strengths, opportunities. Which is okay, but I felt she did not understand my context. Like Frankfurt risk teams have a very specific culture. And also she did not know me, so in 45 minutes, it is hard. I left feeling, okay, she is supportive, but I still do not know what to do. So it was trusted in the sense she was kind, but it was not effective for my situation.
Anna: That is an important distinction. We can come back to that. What about informal mentoring? Any episode that stands out?
Laura: Yes. I had a coffee with a director in another team, a woman, and she was very honest. She told me, if you want to move internally to a more climate-related risk team, you need sponsors, you need visibility, and you need to play the game a bit. And I appreciated that because it was concrete and realistic. I trusted her competence because she knows the bank. But also, she is inside the system, so she also had bias, like she wants people to stay.
Anna: When you think about trust in a human coach or mentor, what drives that trust for you?
Laura: Competence and context understanding. Like do they understand Germany, the labour market, also cultural things. Second is confidentiality. Like, if it is internal coaching, I am never 100 percent sure it is confidential. Third is empathy, but not like pity. More like, they can sit with ambiguity. And also structure. Like they have a process. If it is just vibes, I do not trust it.
Anna: And what would make you distrust a human coach?
Laura: If they push their agenda. If they talk too much about themselves. If they make big claims, like I can guarantee you will find your purpose in two sessions. That feels like marketing. And if they are not transparent about pricing and boundaries. Also if they judge. Like, if they say, why are you so ungrateful, you have a good job. That would shut me down.
5) AI tools: experience and trust

Anna: Now let us go into AI tools. You already mentioned a three-week period. Can you walk me through how that started?
Laura: Yes. It started very practically. I had a Sunday where I could not relax. I was on the couch, and I was scrolling LinkedIn and reading about climate jobs and reading about PhDs. And my brain was like a browser with 50 tabs. And I thought, okay, I need to do something structured. And I opened ChatGPT. I had used it before for work, but not for life decisions. And I wrote something like, help me structure a decision between three paths: staying in banking, moving to a climate startup, or doing a Master or PhD. And it responded with a framework, like criteria, scoring, scenario planning. And I was like, okay, this is actually what I need.
Anna: How often did you use it during those three weeks?
Laura: A lot. Like daily. Some days maybe one hour total, some days just ten minutes. But it was consistent. It became like, after work, I open it, I dump my thoughts, and I ask it to reflect back. And I did it for three weeks intensively, then it reduced.
Anna: What made it feel coaching-like for you, as opposed to just a tool?
Laura: Two things. One, it forced me to make trade-offs explicit. Like, I could not just say, I want stability and meaning and money and learning. It was like, okay, choose weights. And that was painful. And two, it mirrored my avoidance. Like it would say, you are listing pros and cons but you are not stating what you value most, or you are using uncertainty as a reason to delay. And I was like, ugh, that is true. It felt like someone calling me out, but without judgement. That is coaching-like.
Anna: Can you describe the decision model you built, in concrete terms?
Laura: Yes. So I defined five criteria. Learning and growth, values alignment, residency and stability, financial runway, and mental health. And then I assigned weights. I did not do it alone, I did it with AI. It suggested a starting point, then I adjusted.
Anna: What weights did you end up with?
Laura: I ended with learning at 25 percent, values alignment at 25 percent, stability at 20 percent, financial runway at 15 percent, and mental health at 15 percent.
Anna: And what did stability mean for you specifically, given you are an EU citizen?
Laura: Good question. It is not visa in the strict sense. It is more like, job security, ability to pay rent, and also my long-term plan to maybe apply for German citizenship. Like, I know as EU I can live here, but citizenship is still a thing I considered for identity and for like, feeling settled. Also stability is like, probation period risk. In a startup you have probation and it can end fast. In a bank it is stable. In academia, it is like, contracts, stipends, very uncertain.
Anna: Okay. And then how did you use those criteria?
Laura: I built a scoring table. Like each path gets a score from 1 to 10 on each criterion, then multiplied by the weight. But the AI also pushed me to do scenarios, not just one score, because each path has multiple versions.
Anna: Yes, tell me please about the scenarios.
Laura: For staying in finance, I had three scenarios. One was stay in my current team and take that extra responsibility. Two was move internally to a climate risk related team, like within the bank, if possible. Three was stay but negotiate boundaries, like less overtime, which is hard but possible. For climate startup, I had, one, join an early-stage startup with lower pay but high mission and learning. Two, join a scale-up, like Series B, more stable, but maybe more corporate already. Join a climate consultancy or climate data vendor, like still mission but more stable. For academia, I had, one, do a Master in sustainable finance, like a second Master, which is expensive and time. Two, apply directly for a PhD in Germany, maybe funded. Three, do part-time research while staying in the bank, which is like unrealistic but I included it.
Anna: And did you actually research real programs and real startups, or was it hypothetical?
Laura: Both. The scenarios started abstract. Then in week two, it became concrete. I started listing specific companies and specific programs. But I was careful, I did not put confidential bank information. And I also did not put full company names sometimes. I would write like, climate fintech, Frankfurt, 50 people. Because privacy.
Anna: What were your trust drivers for AI in this process? What made you trust it enough to use it as a decision partner?
Laura: Structure and consistency. Like it does not get tired. It can hold the whole model and remember the weights. Also the ability to mirror assumptions. Like it would say, you assume startup equals meaning, but is that always true. And forcing explicit trade-offs. And no judgement. With humans, I sometimes feel judged, like, why would you leave a good job. With AI, I can say anything, even contradictions, and it just works with it.
Anna: And what were distrust drivers?
Laura: Mistakes. Sometimes it would say things with confidence that were not true, like a statistic or a program detail. So I learned to verify. Also generic advice. Sometimes it would become like LinkedIn motivational. And missing context, like it cannot feel the Frankfurt culture. It can approximate, but not fully. And privacy concerns. Like, where does this data go, who sees it. So I never shared too personal or identifiable details. And also biased framing. Sometimes it pushes optimisation logic, like maximise learning, but humans are not spreadsheets. So I had to correct.
Anna: Can you give an example of something that was effective but not fully trusted?
Laura: Yes. It suggested salary ranges for climate roles and also suggested which German cities have more climate startups. It was useful as a starting point, but I did not trust it. I went to reality checks, like Glassdoor, LinkedIn, talking to people, and also job ads. So it was effective in giving me hypotheses, but trust was partial.
Anna: And an example of something you trusted but it was not effective?
Laura: Hmm. In AI? Or in general?
Anna: In general, either human or AI, as long as you make it explicit.
Laura: Human. My friend in Barcelona. I called her crying, like not crisis, but overwhelmed. And she was very supportive. She told me, you are brave, you will figure it out, you deserve happiness. I trusted her emotionally. It felt very good. But after the call, I still had no plan. So it was trusted but not effective for decision execution.
Anna: That is very clear. Now, can you describe how the AI outputs evolved over the three weeks? Like, what did you produce?
Laura: Week one was frameworks and criteria. Like, define values, define weights, build a scoring table. Week two was scenarios and risk analysis. It asked me to list risks, and then mitigations. It also helped me draft outreach messages to people. Week three was the decision memo. I wrote a one-page memo. The AI helped me structure it. It was like a real consulting deliverable, which is funny. 
Anna: Tell me about that memo. What did it contain?
Laura: It had a headline decision statement at the top, like, I will move to a climate mission-driven role in the next three months, targeting scale-ups, while keeping a backup plan of internal move if offers do not materialise. Something like that. Then rationale, like values and learning and mental health. Then risks, like financial risk, skill gap, identity risk, like leaving prestige. Then mitigations, like keep savings, upskill in climate data, talk to mentors, set timeline, and also like, if no offer by date, reconsider. And then next steps, like outreach list, applications, and a weekly plan.
Anna: And what was the final decision, in the end?
Laura: I chose the climate startup path. Specifically, I joined a climate risk analytics scale-up. It is not like 5 people in a garage. It is around 120 people, offices in Germany, and they sell software to banks and insurers. So it is climate mission but still connected to my finance background. And I accepted an offer starting in March. So yes, that is the final.
Anna: Congratulations. What made that the winning option compared to staying or academia?
Laura: The weighted model showed it clearly. Values alignment and learning were high, mental health was better, stability was okay because it is a scale-up not super early. Financial runway was lower than bank, but still acceptable. And the biggest thing was, I realised I was using academia as an escape fantasy. Like, oh, if I do a PhD, I do not have to decide between corporate and mission because I can be intellectual. But the AI kept asking, what is your actual goal. Do you want research, or do you want meaning. And I realised I want meaning and impact, not necessarily academia.
Anna: That moment sounds important. Can you describe a specific moment when AI surfaced an avoidance for you?
Laura: Yes. There was a prompt it asked me. I asked it to help me decide, and it said something like, what are you protecting by not deciding. And I wrote, I am protecting my identity as someone who made the safe choice and the prestigious choice. If I leave, I lose the story. And then it asked, what is the cost of keeping that story. And I wrote, burnout, loneliness, cynicism. And when I saw it in text, it was like, okay, I cannot unsee it. That was a real coaching moment.
Anna: And a moment where AI did not help?
Laura: Yes. Sometimes it pushed premature clarity. Like I would write, I feel scared, and it would respond with, then choose the option that maximises your weighted score. And I was like, yes, but I am a human. It could not hold the fear. It can label it, but not contain it. And sometimes it created false certainty. Like, you could get an offer in four weeks, just do these steps. And in reality, hiring cycles are slow. So I had to ground it.
Anna: During those three weeks, did you also talk to humans? Or was it mostly AI?
Laura: I did both. But AI was like the backbone. Humans were like emotional support and reality check. I talked to my sister, I talked to two friends, and I talked to one colleague who had moved from bank to fintech. And those conversations were important. Like, my sister helped me not panic. My friend helped me feel less alone. And the colleague helped me with practical things like, negotiate contract, what to ask. So it was hybrid already.
Anna: You said you used AI intensively for three weeks. Did you use it in a specific way, like journaling, or like Q and A?
Laura: Both. I had sessions where I just brain-dumped. Like, here are my thoughts, reflect them back, ask me questions. And then I had sessions where I asked for deliverables. Like, build a decision matrix, draft a message, draft a memo, generate scenarios. And I also used it to rehearse conversations, like with my manager.
Anna: Did that rehearsal actually influence what you said in real conversations?
Laura: Yes. I had to tell my manager that I am exploring. I did not want to say, I am leaving, too early. But I wanted to set boundaries. The AI helped me phrase it in a professional way. And I did it. And it reduced my anxiety because I had rehearsed.
Anna: That is an effectiveness indicator in itself. We will come back.
6) Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks

Anna: I would like to compare human and AI support via concrete tasks. I will name a task and you tell me what works better, and why, based on your experience. First task clarifying options. Like, generating possible paths.
Laura: AI is better for breadth. It can generate many options quickly, and it does not get bored. Humans are better for filtering based on knowing you. But if the human does not know you well, AI is more useful.
Anna: Second task making trade-offs explicit, like choosing criteria and weights.
Laura: AI, surprisingly. Because it forced me to write numbers. Humans sometimes avoid numbers because it feels too rational. But in my case, the numbers helped me see what I actually value.
Anna: Third task reality check about the labour market in Germany, like what is realistic in Frankfurt, hiring cycles, cultural things.
Laura: Humans. Definitely humans. Because they have lived experience. AI can summarise general patterns, but Frankfurt banking is very specific. Like the prestige and risk culture. Humans can tell you, yes, your manager will react like this. Or, in startups, probation is like this. That was human.
Anna: Fourth task emotional support when you feel overwhelmed.
Laura: Human. No question. AI can say supportive words, but it feels like, eehm, empty. Not always, but often. Human voice, human presence, it calms your nervous system.
Anna: Fifth task building an action plan, like timelines, outreach list, risk mitigations.
Laura: AI is very good. Because it can turn chaos into steps. And then humans can validate the steps. So AI creates the plan, human makes it realistic and also holds you accountable.
Anna: Can you give an example of the outreach list you mentioned?
Laura: Yes. I created a list of around 18 people. Some were LinkedIn connections, some were second-degree, some were alumni. I grouped them by category, like people in climate risk, people in climate fintech, people in academia. And I wrote short messages. The AI helped me draft the messages in a way that is polite in Germany. Like, not too direct, not too long. And then I actually sent them.
Anna: How many responses did you get?
Laura: Maybe 10 responded. And I had 6 calls. And two of those calls directly led to interview processes. So the plan was executed, not just theoretical.
Anna: That seems like strong perceived effectiveness.
Laura: Yes. It reduced rumination. Instead of thinking, I was doing.
Anna: Another task is  preparing for a difficult conversation, like with your manager or with HR.
Laura: AI again. It is like a simulator. You can practice, you can ask, what might they say, and how do I respond. It gave me scripts. But I still adapted in real life.
Anna: And task of exploring deeper values and identity, like who you want to be.
Laura: This one is mixed. AI helped because it asked structured questions and reflected patterns. But humans help because they know my history and they can challenge me emotionally, like, you always do this. AI can too, but it is more cognitive.
Anna: When you think about trust in these tasks, does your trust differ by task?
Laura: Yes. For emotional tasks, I do not trust AI fully. For structure tasks, I trust AI more. But I trust it with conditions. Like I trust it to help me think, not to tell me the truth. If that makes sense.
Anna: Yes. Trust as appropriate reliance, not blind belief I agree.
Laura: Exactly.
7) Trust–effectiveness link

Anna: Now I want to explore the link between trust and effectiveness. In your experience, how does trust affect whether something is effective?
Laura: Um. Trust is like permission. If I trust the tool or the person, I will share the real problem, not the polite version. And then the output is better. With AI, I trusted it enough to be honest about my fears and contradictions. But I also limited what I share because of privacy. So trust is partial. And effectiveness was still high because for my decision, I needed structure more than deep emotional disclosure. If I needed therapy-level support, trust would be different.
Anna: So effectiveness can be high even with partial trust, depending on the task.
Laura: Yes. Exactly. For example, I did not fully trust it on facts, like salary numbers. But it was still effective because it made me clearer and it pushed me to take action. So trust and effectiveness are related but not identical.
Anna: And what about the opposite? Can you have trust but low effectiveness?
Laura: Yes. Like the coaching session at the bank. I trusted she was kind. I trusted she meant well. But it was not effective because it was generic and not contextual. Also sometimes friends. I trust them, but they cannot give structure or they just say, you will be fine. That is emotionally nice but not effective for decision.
Anna: How did your trust in AI develop over the three weeks? Was it immediate or gradual?
Laura: Gradual. The first day, I was skeptical. I thought, it will give generic advice. Then it surprised me with good questions. Then I started using it more. Then I had one hallucination moment, like it told me something about a German program that was wrong. And that reduced trust. But I adapted by verifying. So trust became like calibrated. Not more or less, but smarter.
Anna: Can you describe that hallucination moment?
Laura: Yes. I asked about a specific scholarship option for a Master in Germany. It gave me a confident answer about eligibility and deadlines. And then I checked and it was wrong. And I felt, okay, this can mislead. In high stakes decisions, that is dangerous. So I learned to not rely on it for factual details without checking.
Anna: So your trust drivers became, it is good for reasoning, not for authoritative facts.
Laura: Yes. Reasoning and structure, yes. Facts, verify. And also privacy, be careful.
Anna: When you reflect on perceived effectiveness, what are your indicators? How did you know it was effective, not just engaging?
Laura: Clarity. Like, I could say, this is my priority order. I could articulate trade-offs. Decision confidence. Not like 100 percent, but enough. Action plan execution. Like, I actually did the outreach and interviews. Reduced rumination. Like, my mind was calmer. And better conversations with humans. Because I came to them with a structured story, not chaos. That made humans more helpful too.
Anna: That last one is interesting. AI enabling better human conversations.
Laura: Yes. Because humans get tired if you are messy. Like, you call a friend and you are like, I do not know, I do not know, and after 20 minutes they are like, okay. But if you say, I have three paths, here is what I value, can you help me reality-check this one risk, then the conversation is productive.
Anna: That is a very concrete mechanism.
Laura: Yes.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction

Anna: I will now ask you to do a short micro-poll, just quick ratings. On a scale from 0 to 10, where 0 is no trust at all and 10 is complete trust, how would you rate your trust in human career coaches, broadly?
Laura: Hmm. For human career coaches, I would say 6.5. Maybe 7. Because I think humans can be very good, but I have not had enough experience to trust blindly. And also there are many coaches with different quality.
Anna: What drives that 6.5 or 7, and what prevents it from being higher?
Laura: Drives it higher is empathy, context, and accountability. Prevents it is uncertainty about competence and agenda. Like, are they actually good, or is it marketing. And also cost. Like if you pay a lot, you want results, but results are not guaranteed. So trust is cautious.
Anna: Same scale, trust in AI tools for career support.
Laura: For AI, I would say 7.5. But it is weird. Because I trust it in a different way. I trust its process, not its truth. So I give it 7.5 because it was consistent and helpful, but it is not like I trust it with my life.
Anna: What keeps it from being higher?
Laura: Hallucinations, privacy, and lack of real-world accountability. Like, it can say anything. There is no consequence. A human coach has reputation. AI does not care. Also it can miss emotional context.
Anna: Now perceived effectiveness. On 0 to 10, how effective do you think human coaching would be for your specific situation, that three-path decision?
Laura: If it is a good coach, it could be 8. But based on my actual experience, I would rate 5.5. Because I did not have a strong human coaching relationship. So in practice, it did not move the decision.
Anna: And perceived effectiveness of AI tools for that situation?
Laura: For my situation, I would say 9. Honestly. It enabled the final decision and action plan. It did not just make me faster, it made me clearer.
Anna: What would have made it a 10?
Laura: If it could hold emotions better. Like, if it could help with fear without becoming generic. And if it could guarantee factual accuracy or show sources. Then I would trust more and it would be even more effective.
Anna: Thank you. That took about a minute, perfect.
Anna: Now, thinking about your preferences, if you could design the ideal support model for a person like you in that situation, would it be human-only, AI-only, or hybrid?
Laura: Hybrid. Definitely hybrid. AI for preparation, structure, and follow-up. Human for emotional containment, accountability, and maybe like one or two key sessions to challenge blind spots.
Anna: Can you describe what hybrid would look like, concretely, like over a few weeks?
Laura: Yes. Week one, AI helps you map options, values, criteria. Then you meet a human coach to talk about emotional stuff and identity, like what is behind the decision. Then AI helps you build scenarios and an action plan. Then a human helps you reality-check and also practice conversations. Then AI helps you with execution, like reminders, drafting outreach, tracking progress. And then a final human session to integrate and reflect.
Anna: How do you feel about the idea of a platform that combines human mentors or coaches with AI support, like AI to structure and humans to support emotionally?
Laura: It makes sense. Because the problem is, humans are expensive and not always available. And sometimes you do not need a human for everything. Like, I do not need to pay someone to build a matrix. But I would pay for someone to help me sit with fear and to see patterns. So hybrid is efficient.
Anna: What would you need to trust such a hybrid platform?
Laura: Transparency about what AI does and what humans do. Data protection. Like, where do my inputs go. Also quality control of the human side. Like, who are these mentors, are they trained, do they have ethics. And also boundaries. Like, do not pretend AI is a therapist. Be honest. If the platform is honest, I trust more.
Anna: What would make you hesitate?
Laura: If it feels like a black box. Or if it pushes me to share too much personal data. Or if the human is not good. Like one bad mentor can ruin trust. Also if the AI outputs are generic, then why use it. It needs to be personalised, but that conflicts with privacy. So that is a tension.
Anna: Yes, that trade-off. Personalisation versus privacy.
Laura: Exactly. And also, hmm, in Germany, people are sensitive about Datenschutz. I am Spanish, but living here, you absorb it.
9) Closing

Anna: Before we wrap up, is there anything you wish you had known before starting that three-week decision sprint?
Laura: I wish I knew that clarity is not a feeling, but rather a result of action. Like, I thought I need to feel sure, then act. But actually I acted, then I felt more sure. And AI helped me act. So I wish I had started earlier.
Anna: Any final thoughts on trust and effectiveness that you think are important?
Laura: No. 
Anna: Thank you, Laura. I will stop the recording now. 






Tobias


1) Opening and consent

Anna: Hi Tobias, can you hear me well?
Tobias: Yes, I can hear you. Um, yes, loud and clear.
Anna: Great. Thanks for joining. Before we start, a couple of quick points. This is a research interview about trust and perceived effectiveness in different kinds of career support, including human coaching and AI tools. It is voluntary. You can skip any question, and you can stop at any time. I will keep your identity anonymous and use a pseudonym. With your permission, I would like to record this for transcription. Is that okay?
Tobias: Yes, that is okay. Recording is fine.
Anna: Thank you. And just to double check, do you have any questions before we begin?
Tobias: Not really. I read the short info you sent. It is fine.
Anna: Perfect. I will start the recording now. Okay. So, to warm up, could you introduce yourself a bit. Whatever feels relevant for your career situation right now.
Tobias: Sure. Eehm, I am Tobias, well, pseudonym Tobias, I guess. I am 28. I was born in Germany, grew up in Baden Wurttemberg, and I live in Stuttgart. I work as a junior mechanical engineer in a mid size automotive supplier. Um, and I would say I am at that stage where I have, like, enough experience to see how things work, but not enough seniority to really influence it. That is kind of the whole issue.
Anna: Mm. Thank you. And you are comfortable speaking mostly English, with some German words if needed?
Tobias: Yes. I will probably mix sometimes, like, the German company words. Like Zielvereinbarung and so on.
Anna: Totally fine.
2) Participant context and career situation

Anna: Let us start with your context. What exactly do you do in your job day to day, and what kind of company is it?
Tobias: Okay. So, we are a supplier, not Tier 1 like the huge ones, but also not a tiny workshop. Maybe, like, 800 people. We do components for thermal management and some structural parts. I am in the development department, Konstruktion, but it is not pure CAD all day. It is, like, a mix. Um, I do design changes, tolerance analysis, and also support testing. Like, if a part fails in testing or if we have a customer issue, then we do root cause analysis, and I have to coordinate with testing, materials, sometimes with suppliers.
Anna: So it is design, plus troubleshooting, plus coordination.
Tobias: Exactly. And supplier coordination is the part I did not expect so much. Because at university it is, like, oh you design a thing. In reality it is, you design a thing, but then you talk to three people, and the supplier says, we cannot do that radius, and purchasing says, cost too high, and the customer says, we need it yesterday.
Anna: That sounds very familiar for automotive.
Tobias: Yes. And Stuttgart is, you know, very automotive. Everybody either works directly for OEMs or for suppliers, or your neighbor does. So the culture is quite, um, how to say, traditional. Hierarchy, titles, people care about who studied where, what your grade was, who is Dr. Ing. There is a lot of respect for expertise, which is good. But also it can be very rigid.
Anna: When did you start in this role?
Tobias: I joined the company about two and a half years ago. Before that I did my master at Hochschule, actually in the region, and I did internships, Werkstudent, at another supplier. So I did not come completely blind. But still, first full time job, first time being part of a project for real.
Anna: If you think about the last 6 to 12 months, what has been the biggest career decision or turning point for you?
Tobias:  Honestly, it was the decision to pay for a private career coach, and then to stop after the first session. And then, to replace that with, um, two things. One is using AI tools for reflection and preparation, and the other is asking a senior colleague to mentor me informally. That is the biggest shift. Because before, I thought, okay, I need a professional coach, like a structured thing. And now I am, like, skeptical, and I do it differently.
Anna: We will definitely go into that. Before we go there, can you describe what feeling stuck meant for you. Like, what did the recognition gap look like?
Tobias: Yes. So, the simple story is, I work a lot, I deliver, and I am still seen as, like, junior helper. That is the vibe. It is not that people are mean. It is more subtle. For example, in project meetings, I bring solutions, but then a senior engineer repeats it, and then it becomes the solution. Sorry, that sounds bitter, but it is true.
Anna: No need to filter. That is useful detail.
Tobias: Another thing is performance reviews. We have the annual review, Jahresgespraech, and there is this rating system. And you have Zielvereinbarung, and you get feedback like, you did a good job, keep going, but nothing concrete. I asked for more responsibility, like owning a module, or being the main contact for a subproject. And my manager said, yes, maybe next year, first prove yourself. And I was thinking, I am already proving myself for two years.
Anna: So, vague feedback, no clear pathway.
Tobias: Yes. And also compensation, like salary band. There are bands. And promotions are cautious. It is not like startups where you change title every year. Here it is, wait, wait, wait. Which is okay. But I wanted at least a development plan. Like, tell me what to do. And instead it was, be patient, and be visible. And I hate the word visible. Because it feels like politics.
Anna: Mm. What did you interpret as the main reason you were under recognized. Was it technical skill, communication, politics, or something else?
Tobias: It is a mix. Technically I am good, but maybe not, like, brilliant genius. But I think that is okay. I think the bigger issue is, I am not the loud guy. In meetings, I speak when I have something. I am not doing this, look at me. And in that culture, the loud ones are sometimes seen as more senior. Also, I do a lot of support work. Like, fixing issues, helping others, doing the unpleasant tasks. And that is important, but it is invisible. It is like, when everything works, nobody notices.
Anna: That is a very clear picture. In your mind, what were the options you were considering, before you booked coaching?
Tobias: I had three options. One, stay and just endure and hope it gets better. Two, move internally, like to testing or project management, maybe to get different visibility. Three, leave the company, maybe even leave the supplier world, or go into consulting, which everyone suggests in Stuttgart if you are ambitious. Like, oh you should do Beratung, you can earn more, and you see many projects.
Anna: And which of those felt attractive, and which felt wrong?
Tobias: Consulting felt wrong emotionally. Like, I respect it, but it is not me. I like building stuff, understanding a product deeply, improving it. The idea of, like, flying Monday to Thursday, making slides, telling others what to do, it felt like, not authentic. But I still had the thought because people kept saying it. Internal move was interesting, but also risky because then you lose technical path. Staying was safe but depressing. So I wanted clarity.
3. Career support in general

Anna: Before you went to private coaching, what kind of career support did you use in general. Mentors, friends, anything formal?
Tobias: Mostly informal. Friends from university, we talk. Sometimes my girlfriend, but she is in a different field, so it is more emotional support. I also talked to one former professor once, but it was a short call. And inside the company, there is something like mentoring program, but it is very, hm, formal, and you need approval. And I did not want to signal, like, I am unhappy. Also, you know, in German companies, people talk. Confidentiality is not always real, even if they say it.
Anna: That is important. So you did not use internal HR career services.
Tobias: Not really. HR is, in my perception, not for you, but for the company. That sounds cynical, but that is how it feels. They can help with forms, but not with, like, your personal strategy.
Anna: If you imagine ideal career support, what would it look like for you?
Tobias: I wanted someone who understands the industry context, like automotive supplier, and also understands personality differences. Like, not everyone should be extroverted sales person. I wanted a structured approach, but not a template. Like, ask me questions, understand my situation, and then help me plan. Also, I wanted confidentiality, and I wanted someone who can challenge me without shaming me.
Anna: Challenge without shaming. Can you unpack that?
Tobias: Yes. Like, it is okay if someone says, Tobias, you avoid conflict, you need to practice. That is challenge. But if someone says, Tobias, you are too sensitive, everyone feels that, just toughen up, then it feels like, you are not okay as you are. That is shame. And then I close.
Anna: That distinction will matter later, I think.
Tobias: Yes.
4)  Human coaching: experience and trust
Anna: Let us go into the private coaching experience. How did you find the coach, and why did you choose them?
Tobias: I found him on LinkedIn. He had a lot of content, like, posts about career acceleration, engineering to leadership, that kind of thing. He positioned himself as former consultant, now coach, and he had testimonials. He also had a website, very polished. I was, like, okay, he is professional. And he offered a package, like, three sessions, plus some worksheets, and support by email.
Anna: What made you believe he would fit you?
Tobias: He said he worked with engineers. And he spoke about structure, like, we build a strategy, we clarify your value proposition, we plan next steps. That sounded good. And he had this thing, like, engineers are undervalued because they do not market themselves. That hit me. I was, like, yes, that is me.
Anna: How much was the package, roughly?
Tobias: It was around, um, 900 euros for three sessions. Something like that. It felt expensive, but I told myself, okay, investment.
Anna: What were your expectations for session one?
Tobias: I expected that he would ask about my background, my job, what I want, what frustrates me. I expected, like, listening, and then maybe a framework. I also expected some empathy, not therapy, but, like, understanding. And I expected confidentiality.
Anna: Okay. Walk me through session one, in detail. Where were you, what was the format, what happened?
Tobias: It was on Zoom. I was at home, in my small office corner. I even took notes. I was nervous. He joined, he was very energetic, like, hi Tobias, great to meet you, we will make you unstoppable. That kind of vibe. And I was, like, okay, a bit much, but fine.
Anna: What happened next?
Tobias: He started with, like, a pitch. He talked for maybe ten minutes about his method. Like, I have a proven system, engineers go through these steps, and then they either move to consulting or leadership. He used those words a lot. Proven, system, method. And I was thinking, okay, but what about me.
Anna: Did you feel space to interrupt?
Tobias: Not really. He was talking fast. I tried to say, yes, I work in a supplier, and he was like, yes, yes, automotive, I know, very hierarchical, and then he continued. It felt like he already knew the story.
Anna: Was there a specific moment when trust dropped sharply for you?
Tobias: Yes.  It was when I described my performance review. I said, I feel under recognized, I do a lot, and the feedback is vague, and I feel frustrated, and also I feel, like, maybe I am too quiet, but I do not want to play politics. And he said, literally, everyone feels that. Everyone thinks they are under recognized. It is not special. You need to stop complaining and start acting.
Anna: How did that land in your body, in that moment?
Tobias: My stomach dropped. Like, physically. I felt heat in my face. And my throat got tight. I remember I stopped breathing for a second.It felt like, okay, I made myself vulnerable, and he slapped it away. And then I became very careful.
Anna: What did you do after that in the conversation?
Tobias: I said, okay, yes, maybe. And I started to speak more generally. I did not share, for example, that I had some conflict with a senior colleague, or that I sometimes feel anxious before reviews. I was not going to give him that information after that.
Anna: So you moved from specifics to safer surface.
Tobias: Exactly. Psychological safety went down. I did not use those words then, but that is what it was.
Anna: What did he do after saying that?
Tobias: He went into, like, narrative. He asked, what is your long term goal. I said, I want to be a senior engineer, maybe lead small team, but also stay technical. And he said, okay, then the fastest route is consulting. Because in consulting you learn to communicate, you get visibility, you get salary, and then you can go back to industry at a higher level. He said it like it is obvious.
Anna: And how did you respond?
Tobias: I said, I am not sure consulting fits me. I like engineering. And he said, that is just fear. Engineers always say they like engineering, but what they really want is recognition and money, and consulting gives that. And then he did something that felt judgmental. He said, you are the typical introverted engineer who hides behind technical details, and you need to become more of a leader type.
Anna: That felt like a personality judgment.
Tobias: Yes. Because it was said with this, like, smile, like, I see through you. And I was thinking, you do not know me. Also, introverted is not a problem. And he used it like a diagnosis. Like, here is your defect.
Anna: Did you say anything back?
Tobias: Not really. I laughed awkwardly. Like, hahahah, maybe. But inside I was, like, no. And then I started to distrust his intentions. I thought, he wants to sell a narrative that fits his method, not my situation.
Anna: You used the word intentions. What did you think his intention was?
Tobias: It felt salesy. Like, he has a product, and the product is, push engineers to consulting or leadership branding. And I was not a person, I was a case. Like, case number 47. He even used examples, like, I had a client, mechanical engineer, moved to consulting, doubled salary. And I was, like, okay, but that is not necessarily my goal. Also, it felt like he was proud of that path, because he was consultant before. So he was biased.
Anna: That is very clear. Did he offer anything concrete in that session that you could take away?
Tobias: Yes, and that is the annoying part. He gave a framework that is actually not stupid. He said, you need to define your value proposition in three parts. Your technical skill, your business impact, and your leadership behaviors. And then you need to collect evidence for each, like projects, metrics, examples. He also suggested a structure for talking to your manager. Like, do not ask, can I get promotion. Instead, say, here is what I delivered, here is how it links to company goals, here is what I want next, and what support I need.
Anna: That sounds reasonable.
Tobias: It is reasonable. If someone I trust would say it, I would do it. But because he said it after invalidating me and labeling me, I did not want to engage. It felt like, if I follow his framework, I accept his worldview. And I did not want that.
Anna: That is a perfect example of separating usefulness from trust. Low trust, some usefulness, but you could not use it fully.
Tobias: Yes. I even received a PDF after the call, like worksheets. And I opened it once, and I felt annoyed. I closed it.
Anna: What happened at the end of session one? Was there a plan for session two?
Tobias: He proposed homework, like, fill in the value proposition, and next session we refine and maybe discuss consulting options. And he said, if you are serious, you need to commit. And I said, yes, okay, I will do it. But inside I was already out.
Anna: Did you communicate your discomfort to him?
Tobias: No. And that is also part of the problem. I did not trust him enough to say, hey, what you said hurt. I did not think it would be safe. I imagined he would say, see, you are sensitive. So I just avoided.
Anna: What did you do between session one and the next scheduled session?
Tobias: I did not do homework. I procrastinated. I felt stress when I saw the calendar invite. Like, I had this tight feeling. And then I wrote him an email, like, I need to pause for now due to workload. Something polite. He responded fast, like, okay, but remember, success requires consistency. It was like a motivational poster.
Anna: And you stopped attending.
Tobias: Yes. I did not go to session two. I lost the money basically. And that also made me angry at myself. Like, why did you do that.
Anna: If you look back, what are the key reasons trust ruptured so quickly?
Tobias: Three things. One, dismissal. Everyone feels that. It killed the feeling of being taken seriously. Two, pre made narrative. Consulting is the solution. He did not explore alternatives. Three, judgment. Labeling my personality in a negative way. And also, maybe a fourth, lack of boundaries. Like, he did not ask about confidentiality, he did not ask what I am comfortable sharing. He just assumed.
Anna: If we zoom out, what does trust mean for you in a human coach relationship. What are the drivers?
Tobias: Being listened to, actually listening, not waiting to speak. Confidentiality, like clear statement. Non judgment, like you can challenge but not shame. Adapting to context, like automotive supplier reality, not generic Silicon Valley story. Transparent process, like, today we do this, why we do it. And boundaries, like, what is coaching, what is not. Also not being too salesy. Like, if I feel I am a revenue stream, I cannot trust.
Anna: That is a strong list. What are distrust drivers, basically the opposite?
Tobias: Templates. Invalidation. Salesy vibe. Pushing consulting. Personality judgment. And also over confidence. Like, when someone says, I have the proven method, then I think, okay, you will not listen.
Anna: After that experience, how did it affect you emotionally or behaviorally when seeking support?
Tobias: I became more cautious. Like, I did not want to talk to another coach. I also became more careful at work. Because it confirmed this fear that if you share frustration, people will dismiss you. So I kept it inside more. But also, weirdly, it made me clearer about my boundaries. Like, now I know what I do not want.
Anna: Mm. That is an after effect that can be both negative and informative.
Tobias: Yes.
Anna: One more detail on this. You said the advice was not necessarily wrong, but distrust prevented depth and follow through. Can you give one example of something you did not tell him because of distrust, that could have mattered?
Tobias: Yes. I did not tell him about a specific incident. There was a project where a test failed, and I found the root cause and I proposed a change, and it solved it. But in the meeting, my manager presented it as the team, and my name was not mentioned. And I was really upset. I wanted to talk about that, because it is about recognition, and also about how to communicate. But after he dismissed me, I was not going to share that. That story would require vulnerability. And I knew he would probably say, everyone has that, just be louder. And I did not want that.
Anna: That makes sense. Okay.
5) AI tools: experience and trust

5A. AI as structured reflection partner after the bad coaching
Anna: After you stopped the coaching, you mentioned you switched to a mix of AI reflection and a trusted senior colleague. Let us start with AI. What tools do you use, and for what exactly?
Tobias: Mostly ChatGPT. Sometimes also another tool integrated in the browser, but mainly ChatGPT. I use it in German and English. And I use it not for technical design, like not for CAD obviously, but for thinking and communication.
Anna: Can you describe a typical use case?
Tobias: The most common is preparation for conversations. Like, if I want to talk to my manager about development, I write a rough description of my situation, and I ask, help me structure this. What is a clear agenda. What are questions I can ask. How can I phrase it in a calm way. Because I tend to either be too emotional inside or too passive outside. AI helps me find a middle.
Anna: So it is like a rehearsal.
Tobias: Yes, rehearsal, and also clarity. It asks me questions sometimes if I prompt it that way. Like, what do you actually want. What are your constraints. And I answer, and then I see patterns. It is like journaling but more structured.
Anna: Do you use it for decision trade offs too?
Tobias: Yes. Like, should I stay and try internal move, or leave. I can list pros and cons. But I try not to let it decide. It is more, it helps me see the decision more clearly.
Anna: Do you use it for concrete documents?
Tobias: Sometimes. Like, drafting an email to a senior colleague, asking for advice. Or drafting a development plan. Or rewriting bullet points for my internal profile. We have internal systems, like, you need to write competencies. I hate writing that. AI helps.
Anna: How does trust play out for you with AI tools. Do you trust them?
Tobias: It is different. I trust it as a tool, not as a person. So trust means, is it useful, is it consistent, does it keep my data private, things like that. But emotionally, it cannot shame me. That is important. Like, AI does not say, you are too sensitive. Unless you prompt it badly. 
Anna: That is an interesting point. So psychological safety is higher because there is no judgment vibe.
Tobias: Yes. I can type something stupid, and it will respond. It might be wrong, but it is not judging. So I am more open. But I also do not fully trust its advice, because sometimes it is generic, or it says confident things that are not correct.
Anna: Can you give an example where it was not correct or not helpful?
Tobias: It once suggested, like, in German companies you can just ask HR for promotion criteria and they will provide it. That is, in my experience, naive. HR will give you generic guidelines, but the real criteria are political. So I learned, do not take it literally. Another example, it sometimes suggests, just set boundaries and say no. But in a project context, you cannot just say no. You need a strategy. So I have to adapt.
Anna: So AI is supportive, but not a magic solution.
Tobias: Exactly. It is like a calculator for words. But you need to know what you want.
Anna: Do you have privacy concerns?
Tobias: Yes. I never put confidential company data. I never put names. I abstract. Like, I say, mid size supplier, manager, senior colleague. And I also do not paste internal documents. I keep it general. So trust is conditional.
Anna: How did you start using AI in this way. Was it directly after the coaching?
Tobias: Pretty much. I was angry and I needed to process. I did not want to talk to friends too much because I felt ashamed, like, I wasted money. So I wrote it into AI. Like, here is what happened, help me understand. And it gave me language. Like, it said, this sounds invalidating, and you experienced a trust rupture. And I was like, yes, exactly. It gave me words.
Anna: That language helped you make sense of it.
Tobias: Yes.
Anna: Do you ever miss the human element with AI. Like empathy, accountability?
Tobias: Accountability, yes. AI cannot make me do the thing. It can suggest. But if I am scared to talk to my manager, AI cannot look at me and say, okay, now do it. And empathy, it can simulate, but it is still, you know, not real. Sometimes I want someone to say, I see you, and mean it. AI cannot mean it.
Anna: That is a nice way to put it.
Tobias: But still, I prefer it over a bad coach. 
6. Human vs AI comparison via specific tasks

Anna: I want to do a more concrete comparison. I will name a task, and you tell me whether you would prefer a human coach, AI tool, or a mix, and why. Ready?
Tobias: Yes.
Anna: Task one. Clarifying what you want from your career in the next one to two years.
Tobias: Mix. AI helps me list options and clarify values. Human, like a mentor, helps me reality check. Because AI can be too generic. Mentor can say, in this company, that path is possible or not.
Anna: Task two. Preparing for a conversation with your manager about recognition, promotion, or responsibilities.
Tobias: AI first, then human if available. AI helps structure and write. But a human who knows the politics is crucial. My senior colleague can tell me, do not phrase it like this, because manager will hear it as complaint. Or, mention this project because it is visible. AI cannot know that.
Anna: Task three. Emotional processing, like frustration, feeling undervalued.
Tobias: Honestly, AI is easier for the first draft, because no judgment. But a human friend or mentor is better if I trust them. The problem is, trust. With the coach, I did not trust, so it was useless. With AI, it is safer, but also shallow sometimes. So again, hybrid.
Anna: Task four. Understanding the career ladder and what skills matter for recognition in automotive supplier culture.
Tobias: Human, definitely. Someone inside or with deep industry knowledge. AI can give general ideas, but it does not know the informal parts. Like, who sponsors you, how you get on certain projects, how titles work. That is very specific.
Anna: Task five. Building a concrete plan with steps you actually execute.
Tobias: That is interesting. AI can produce a plan, but I might not execute. Human can create accountability. But again, only if I trust them. My senior colleague, because he is not selling me something, he is just helping, that creates accountability. So human mentor plus AI plan drafting.
Anna: Great.
7) Trust–effectiveness link

Anna: Now I want to explicitly talk about the link between trust and perceived effectiveness. In your story, it sounds like distrust prevented depth and follow through. Can you describe that mechanism in your own words?
Tobias: Yes. When I distrust the person, I do not share real information. I share a safe version. Then the advice is based on wrong or incomplete input. So even if the advice is good, it is not tailored. Also, I do not feel motivated to act on it, because acting feels like accepting their authority. And if I think their intentions are not aligned, I resist. So the effectiveness drops. Not because the advice is wrong, but because I do not engage.
Anna: That is very precise. If you compare that to higher trust situations, like with your senior colleague, what changes?
Tobias: I share more specifics. Like, I can say, I feel angry because my name was not mentioned. And he can respond with nuance, not dismiss. Then his advice fits. And because I trust him, I am willing to try. And then I get results. Like, I actually had better conversations.
Anna: So trust increases willingness to act, which increases perceived effectiveness.
Tobias: Exactly.
Anna: Can you give one example of something you actually executed because of AI preparation, and one example because of the senior colleague?
Tobias: AI example. I used AI to draft an agenda for my performance review follow up. Like, I asked my manager for a short check in, not the annual, but a mid year. I prepared three points. One, recap achievements with examples. Two, ask for criteria for the next level. Three, propose a next responsibility, like owning a small module. I wrote it in a calm, structured email. Before, I would write something messy or not write at all. I sent it. My manager agreed to a meeting. That happened.
Anna: And the senior colleague example?
Tobias: He helped me choose which achievements matter. I was listing technical details, like I improved tolerance stack. He said, nobody cares. He said, translate it to impact. Like, reduced rework, avoided delay, improved customer satisfaction. He also told me, mention the customer escalation you solved, because that is visible. And he told me how to say it without sounding arrogant. So in the meeting, I used that. And my manager reacted better. He actually said, okay, I see you want to move forward, let us define a plan.
Anna: That is a concrete outcome.
Tobias: Yes. It is not promotion yet. But it is movement.
8) Preferences and hybrid model reaction

Anna: You already described a hybrid approach. Can you tell me more about the senior colleague mentorship. How did that start, and what makes it trustworthy for you?
Tobias: He is a senior engineer, maybe 42, in a different team but close. We worked together on a project. He is respected, but he is not political in an aggressive way. He is calm. After my coaching disaster, I was venting a bit at lunch, carefully, and he said, if you want, we can talk. And I hesitated, because I did not want to appear weak. But he said, listen, everyone has this, but the difference is how you navigate. And he did not say it like dismissal. He said it with empathy, like, I had it too.
Anna: The same phrase, everyone has this, can be dismissal or connection, depending how it is said.
Tobias: Exactly. That is the point. The coach said it like, shut up. The colleague said it like, you are not alone. Big difference.
Anna: What are the boundaries of that mentorship?
Tobias: It is informal. We meet maybe once a month, sometimes just coffee. I do not dump everything. It is focused. I bring one topic. Like, how to talk to manager, how to move internally, what training is useful. He also said clearly, I cannot make decisions for you, and I cannot promise promotion. That transparency builds trust.
Anna: And confidentiality?
Tobias: I trust him more than internal programs because he is not HR. He is not reporting. But still, I am careful. I do not mention names when it is sensitive. But I think he respects it.
Anna: If you imagine a hybrid model, where AI helps you prepare, and then a human coach or mentor supports key decisions, how does that sound?
Tobias: That sounds ideal, if the human part is ethical and adaptive. Like, I would use AI to organize my thoughts, then the human would help contextualize and challenge me. Also, the human could help with accountability. But I would need to feel that the human is not pushing a template.
Anna: If a human coach could demonstrate clear boundaries, process, and adaptation, would your skepticism reduce?
Tobias: Yes, but slowly. I would probably ask more questions upfront now. Like, what is your approach, how do you handle confidentiality, how do you adapt. I would test. I am skeptical of marketing.
Anna: What would be a green flag in the first 10 minutes of a first session?
Tobias: A green flag is, they ask, what do you want from this, and they actually listen. They summarize my situation accurately. They do not jump to solution. They ask permission for sensitive topics. They explain the process, like today we map your situation, then we decide next steps. And they do not make me feel weird for being quiet.
Anna: And a red flag?
Tobias: Too much talking from their side. Too much success stories. Too much, I have the system. And any comment that labels my personality as a problem.
9) Micro-poll ratings

Anna: I want to do a very short micro poll, just quick numbers, and then I will ask you to explain. On a scale from 0 to 10, where 0 is not at all and 10 is fully, how much do you currently trust human career coaches in general?
Tobias:Right now, maybe 4.
Anna: 4 out of 10. What drives that number?
Tobias: I know there are good coaches. Friends had good experiences. So it is not zero. But my experience made me cautious. And I think the market has many people selling templates. So trust is low until proven.
Anna: On the same 0 to 10 scale, how much do you trust AI tools for career support?
Tobias: I would say 7.
Anna: 7. Why higher?
Tobias: Because the risk of judgment is lower, and it is consistent. It does not have ego. But it is not 10 because it can be wrong, and privacy is a concern.
Anna: Next, perceived effectiveness. For your situation specifically, how effective was the human coaching you experienced?
Tobias: For my situation, maybe 2.
Anna: 2 out of 10. What makes it that low?
Tobias: Because I did not continue, I did not do the work, I did not trust, so it did not change anything. The only effectiveness was, it taught me what I do not want, but that is not what I paid for.
Anna: And for AI tools, perceived effectiveness for your situation?
Tobias: I would say 6.
Anna: 6. What makes it a 6 and not higher?
Tobias: It helped me take steps, like preparing conversations. It gave me structure. But it cannot fully understand context and politics, and it cannot replace a human ally. Also, sometimes it is too generic.
Anna: Perfect. That is the micro poll. Thank you.
10) Closing
Anna: We are coming to the end. Is there anything important about your experience that we did not cover, something you would want a researcher to understand?
Tobias: Nothing from my side.
Anna: Thank you. 
Tobias: Thank you. It was actually helpful to talk about it.
Anna: I am glad. Have a good evening.
Tobias: You too. Bye.
